{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Rmd Files will No Longer be Rendered as Markdown on Github","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/10/rmd-github/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>Recently I requested Github to undo <a href=\"https://github.com/github/markup/pull/343\">the feature</a> of treating R Markdown as Markdown on Github, i.e., rendering R Markdown as if it were plain Markdown when you browse such files in a Github repo. I guess some users are going to hate me for this, so I want to explain the rationale in this post.</p>\n\n<p>Four years ago, a couple of R users, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/_inundata/status/510448862880727040\">including Karthik</a>, requested Github to add this feature. Since then, I have found it more bothersome than convenient, and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/662332967532957700\">eventually thought it was a mistake</a>. There are rarely things that are absolutely good or bad. It is always about trade-offs. Rendering Rmd as Markdown has the one and only advantage: it makes the text of Rmd files more human-readable, because reading a rendered output file (an HTML page) is more pleasant than reading the (Rmd) source file.</p>\n\n<p>The question is whether the disadvantages outweigh the advantage, and obviously I do think so. There are two drawbacks of rendering Rmd as Markdown on Github.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"r-markdown-is-not-100-markdown\">R Markdown is not 100% Markdown</h2>\n\n<p>R Markdown is a source format, which looks like Markdown, but it is not equivalent to Markdown. The bottom line is that the source is the source. If it needs to be rendered (to HTML), it has to be rendered in the way that it is supposed to be rendered (via the R package <a href=\"https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com\"><strong>rmarkdown</strong></a>). Rendering <code>*.Rmd</code> as <code>*.md</code> on Github loses critical information, which can be highly confusing. In particular, the code chunk headers are lost, and the chunk headers can be critical for the purpose of learning. For example, when you see a nice R Markdown output page somewhere, and you may want to know how it was generated. Then you go to the Github repo, open the <code>*.Rmd</code> source file, and you cannot see the full source code in the default normal view.</p>\n\n<p>There have been people who struggled with trying to show other people the verbatim source Rmd file. One day I saw an instructor posted an Rmd example on Github, and tweeted about it with the Github raw link to the Rmd file. Then it seemed she was not happy with the raw link, because it pointed to a plain text page. Then she indented the whole content of the Rmd file by four spaces, which would make it easier for people to see the Rmd source from the normal Github view, but the Rmd source was essentially wrong. In short, between a nice view and the correct Rmd source, you could only choose one.</p>\n\n<p>Later I emailed her and she told me her students were often confused, too:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>[&hellip;] that is sad! I was teaching R Markdown the other day with a github repository full of .Rmd solution files and someone pointed out that they don&rsquo;t get the solution precisely because of this! Quite a lot of people (especially beginners) don&rsquo;t know they can check &ldquo;Raw&rdquo; to see the raw content so it&rsquo;s quite a pity it&rsquo;s treated as a Markdown file now :(</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I have many Rmd examples in the <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr-examples\">knitr-examples</a> repo, and I have always been worried that users don&rsquo;t know how to read the source of these Rmd files. The chunk options in these examples are vital in teaching them <strong>knitr</strong> features.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"linking-to-specific-lines-of-rmd-files\">Linking to specific lines of Rmd files</h2>\n\n<p>The other thing that deeply bothered me was that I could not link to specific lines in an Rmd file. For those who are familiar with Github, you know when you click the line numbers in a source file on Github, you can get a permanent link to a line or multiple lines like <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/blob/44895dd/R/utils.R#L269\">https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/blob/44895dd/R/utils.R#L269</a>. This feature is great because it saves you time to say something like &ldquo;Hey, open the file <code>R/utils.R</code>, take a look at the line #269, and you&rsquo;ll learn a great thing&rdquo;. This is extremely useful for many purposes, such as teaching others a specific trick or reporting bugs. A simple link takes your audience to the specific destination. They don&rsquo;t need to clone or download your repo or follow the multiple-step instructions.</p>\n\n<p>When Rmd is rendered as Markdown, I have to use a hack to obtain such links. That is, use the &ldquo;Blame view&rdquo; of the Rmd file. For example: <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/blame/master/inst/examples/lucy-demo.Rmd#L20\">https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/blame/master/inst/examples/lucy-demo.Rmd#L20</a>. This view looks odd, because it also presents all commit messages in the history of this file in the left column. I don&rsquo;t know if beginners understand what &ldquo;blame&rdquo; means in GIT, or are aware of the &ldquo;Blame&rdquo; button.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"apologies-and-a-solution-to-a-new-problem\">Apologies, and a solution to a new problem</h2>\n\n<p>Before requesting Github to undo this feature, I reached out to a few people who were originally happy with the feature, including Karthik. <del>I threatened them that we would no longer be friends if they would not allow me to contact Github to revert the change. Just kidding. I didn&rsquo;t do that.</del> They all agreed that displaying Rmd files verbatim rather than rendering them to HTML was a better idea. However, I understand that whenever something has an advantage, there will be a number of users feeling annoyed if it is gone. Please accept my sincere apologies you are one of these users.</p>\n\n<p>Displaying Rmd verbatim on Github does bring one annoying disadvantage: (at least for now) Github does not soft-wrap lines in source files, so you&rsquo;ll have to use the horizontal scrollbar when the lines are too long. Yes, horizontal scrollbars on web pages are terrible! However, this problem is much easier to solve. We don&rsquo;t have to contact Github (<a href=\"https://github.com/github/markup/pull/1235\">actually I have contacted them</a>). If you hate anything related to CSS styles on the web, I&rsquo;d recommend that you install a plugin or extension in your web browser and change the CSS by yourself. For example, I have installed the extension <a href=\"https://chrome.google.com/webstore/detail/stylebot/oiaejidbmkiecgbjeifoejpgmdaleoha\">Stylebot</a> in Google Chrome long time ago (there may be better and newer extensions now), and I have applied the following CSS to github.com:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-css\">.type-rmarkdown .blob-code-inner {\n  white-space: pre-wrap;\n}\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>If you have installed Stylebot, you can click its icon (CSS) after opening any Github web pages, hit the &ldquo;Edit CSS&rdquo; button, and paste the above CSS code in it.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/47231527-bcbd2b00-d392-11e8-8a77-193dc74a1d58.png#border\" alt=\"Before applying the CSS in Stylebot\" /></p>\n\n<p>Then you&rsquo;ll see the long lines nicely wrapped in Rmd files:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/47231528-bcbd2b00-d392-11e8-82df-a72f11dc1c29.png#border\" alt=\"After applying the CSS in Stylebot\" /></p>\n\n<p>I hope I didn&rsquo;t hurt you too much by asking for this change on Github. If I have brought any unexpected annoyance, please feel free to let me know, and I&rsquo;ll try my best to make it up. Thanks for your understanding!</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/10/rmd-github/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 19 Oct 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/47231527-bcbd2b00-d392-11e8-8a77-193dc74a1d58.png#border","linkMd5":"746c3dbea910035bfca16e77510dd46c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn11@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-36-342_e7e7c50202f2972a.webp","destWidth":1069,"destHeight":698,"sourceBytes":58289,"destBytes":61678,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/47231527-bcbd2b00-d392-11e8-8a77-193dc74a1d58.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn11@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-36-342_e7e7c50202f2972a.webp","https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/47231528-bcbd2b00-d392-11e8-82df-a72f11dc1c29.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn27@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-20-00-704_35d9515a5bd81a6d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973444},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Slowly but Steadily, They Started to Help Me Answer Questions","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/help-answer-questions/","description":"\n        <p>Earlier this year, I said &ldquo;<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/thanks-marcel-schilling/\">nothing feels better than someone else answering questions for me</a>&rdquo;. I&rsquo;m pretty bad at remembering people&rsquo;s names).</p>\n\n<p>So if/when you find my work useful, please remember this nice crowd of helpers who liberated my productivity.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/cats-celebrate.gif\" alt=\"Two kittens doing the job so one kitten can rest\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/help-answer-questions/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 24 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/cats-celebrate.gif","linkMd5":"e8704c96de33e8b95160b49ada9e9ca7","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn40@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-461_5e36a88c71b06060.webp","destWidth":500,"destHeight":255,"sourceBytes":399132,"destBytes":200038,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/cats-celebrate.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn40@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-461_5e36a88c71b06060.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973458},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"One (Perhaps Surprising) Reason Why I may Silently Ignore a Github Issue","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/github-issue-format/","description":"<p>Sam Tyner <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/issues/142\">posted an issue</a> to the <strong>xaringan</strong> repo on Github this afternoon. It was a great issue, and I appreciated that she took the time to write such a detailed issue, so I tried to reply the issue as quickly as I could. However, if this issue were posted from someone I don’t know, I would be likely to delay the reply significantly (if a reproducible example were not provided, I’d just ignore the issue). I know this sounds unfair to many people, but with only two hands and 24 hours a day, I have no choice but to skip certain issues.</p> \n<p>What made me hesitate when looking at this issue was the incorrect format of the reproducible example. Here is what it looks like:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-markdown\">---\ntitle: \"Presentation Ninja Headache\"\nauthor: \"Sam Tyner\"\n....\n---\n\n\\```{r setup, include=FALSE}\noptions(htmltools.dir.version = FALSE)\n\\```\n\n....\n\n---\nclass: primary \n# Slide with bg image and  \"too much\" code\n\nHere is some code \n\n\\```{r}\nx &lt;- rnorm(100)\nsummary(x)\ny &lt;- rgamma(100,shape = 1)\nsummary(y)\nsummary(x+y)\n\\```\n\n....\n</code></pre> \n<p>Did you notice the backslashes before three backticks? The reason that Sam put in these backslashes was because she forgot <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/05/four-backticks-github/\">the four-backtick rule</a>, which I had blogged about before, and frequently referenced in my Github replies. The rule is quite simple but many people don’t know it:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>If you want to show N verbatim backticks in the Markdown output, you have to enclose them in at least N+1 backticks.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Without knowing this rule, users will find it hard to post a reproducible example in R Markdown that contains code chunks, because R Markdown’s code chunks use three backticks. Sam put the backslashes in front of the three backticks to prevent the text below from being rendered as code blocks in the output. However, what this means to me, as someone who plans to investigate the issue, is that I will have to copy this example, remove the backslashes, and run the example.</p> \n<p>Note the additional step of removing the backslashes. Sometimes that could be <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Straw_that_broke_the_camel%27s_back\">the last straw to break the camel’s back</a>. If I cannot simply copy an example and run it, I may ignore the issue and move on to the next 30 unread issues.</p> \n<p>You may call me inconsiderate, but before I wrote this blog post, I have edited hundreds of Github issues as well as Stack Overflow posts over the years to correct their formatting problems. This three-backtick problem is only one of them. Other common problems include:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Do not use verbatim code blocks, e.g., R comments are displayed as section headers because of the <code>#</code> character, or YAML metadata looks funny because <code>---</code> is interpreted as the second-level section header.</p></li> \n <li><p>Use the blockquote environment for a verbatim code block, i.e., <code>&gt;</code> instead of <code>```</code>. That is wrong. The Markdown content in the “blockquote” may look funny when it contains special characters, e.g., a line starting with <code>+</code> could become a list item. Unless you really intend to quote some words, always use the syntax for verbatim code blocks. If you are not sure if it should be a quote or a code block, use a code block.</p></li> \n <li><p>Do not indent the content nested within a list item. I guess many people don’t know the four-space rule, either. I wish my eyes were not picky of such formatting issues…</p></li> \n <li><p>Do not put HTML tags in a pair of backticks. Because Github will ignore some HTML tags if you do not show them verbatim, the issue can look highly confusing to me. I remember once someone was talking about the <code>&lt;meta&gt;</code> tag, and I had no idea what the issue meant until I hit the edit button to edit his issue.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Now I have reached to a point where I feel tired of editing issues to correct the formats. I guess we could blame it on the surprising Markdown syntax, but anyway, now if I see an incorrectly formatted issue, I tend to<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Only-tend-to-Exc\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/github-issue-format/#fn:Only-tend-to-Exc\">1</a></sup> keep calm and quietly move on.</p> \n<p>BTW, if your reproducible example really requires external files, I strongly recommend you to compress all files into a zip archive, and upload it to the Github issue. I have also seen issues in which all files are provided as verbatim text blocks in the issues, with filenames indicated above the blocks. In such cases, what I will have to do is to copy these code blocks and save them as the associated filenames to my disk, then run the example. That is also too much work compared to downloading a zip archive and decompressing it. In short, if it is a single file, show the content in the issue; if the example consists of multiple files, use a zip archive.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Only-tend-to-Exc\">Only “tend to”. Exceptions usually include: 1) I’m familiar with you; 2) It is clearly a serious bug of my software; 3) You have made substantial effort in other aspects, such as providing a reproducible example and other information. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/github-issue-format/#fnref:Only-tend-to-Exc\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/github-issue-format/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 25 May 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"24018719956861c4c8f59ad7d7eda33c","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973461},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"makeActiveBinding(): The Most Magical Hidden Gem in Base R","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/make-active-binding/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>Of course, this is a follow-up post on my previous follow-up post on <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/lazy-evaluation/\">lazy evaluation</a>. One commenter (Michael&mdash;I believe I know this Michael) of that post pointed out the function <code>makeActiveBinding()</code> in base R. I have almost forgotten it. It was once the most magical function in my eyes, and was the foundation of one of my only two academic papers, &ldquo;<a href=\"https://projecteuclid.org/euclid.ss/1408368571\">Reactive Programming for Interactive Graphics</a>&rdquo; (Xie <em>et al</em> 2014).</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/43879425-a5818b36-9b69-11e8-98d8-51db61c66a2f.png#border\" alt=\"Figure 2 in the paper\" /></p>\n\n<p>Basically <code>makeActiveBinding()</code> binds a function to a symbol in an environment. The magic is that, when you try to <em>get</em> the value of this symbol, the function is executed without arguments, and when you try to <em>set</em> the value of the symbol, the function is executed with the value as its argument.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"a-toy-example\">A toy example</h2>\n\n<p>That may be difficult to understand if you have just heard of this function for the first time. Let me give an example by rewriting the &ldquo;sword&rdquo; sound effect I used in <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr-talks/blob/master/useR2014/2014-knitr-ninja-Yihui-Xie.R\">my useR! 2014 talk</a> (a few of you might still remember <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2014/07/a-few-notes-on-user2014/\">that</a>):</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">xfun::pkg_load2('beepr')  # install beepr if necessary\n\nmakeActiveBinding('sword', function(i) {\n  if (missing(i)) i = sample(1:11, 1)\n  beepr::beep(i)\n  i\n}, globalenv())\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Now if you type <code>sword</code> in the R console and hit Return, you should hear a random beep. If you assign a number to <code>sword</code>, you should hear the beep corresponding to that number, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">sword = 11  # sword\nsword = 7   # shotgun\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>When you try to <em>get</em> the value of <code>sword</code>, the argument <code>i</code> of the function will be missing. Basically the test <code>if (missing(i))</code> inside the function tells you whether you are trying to get or set the value of <code>sword</code>.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"a-toy-implementation-of-shiny\">A toy implementation of Shiny</h2>\n\n<p>Active bindings can be a lot of fun to play with, simply because a function is executed no matter if you try to obtain or set the value of an object. When you have a function, you have the full control of the world. I have shown an example of brushing a scatterplot in the paper I mentioned in the beginning of this post. Next I&rsquo;m going to show a super naive implementation of <a href=\"https://shiny.rstudio.com\">Shiny</a> based on active bindings.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">input  = new.env()\noutput = list()\n# a dependency table, e.g. list(input1 = c('output1', 'output2'))\ndeps = list()\n# name of the current output being rendered\noutput_cur = NA\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>A new input in the system is an active binding in the <code>input</code> environment. When we need to get the value of the input element, we try to connect it with the output element that actually uses this input value, and store this information in the dependency table <code>deps</code>, so that we know which output elements to render when the input element is changed later.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">add_input = function(ID, init = 0) {\n  makeActiveBinding(ID, local({\n    val = init\n    function(new) {\n      if (missing(new)) {\n        # figure out which outputs use my value\n        if (!is.na(output_cur)) {\n          deps[[ID]] &lt;&lt;- unique(c(deps[[ID]], output_cur))\n          output_cur &lt;&lt;- NA\n        }\n        return(val)\n      }\n      # if value not changed, do nothing\n      if (identical(val, new)) return()\n      # trigger changes in relevant outputs\n      val &lt;&lt;- new\n      for (i in deps[[ID]]) output[[i]]()\n    }\n  }), input)\n}\n\nadd_input('slider', 0)\nadd_input('toggle', TRUE)  # not used this example\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Then we provide a couple of output elements. One uses the value <code>input$slider</code>, and one does not.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">output$render_value = function() {\n  message('The slider value is ', input$slider)\n}\noutput$render_random = function() {\n  message('A random value is ', rnorm(1))\n}\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Finally, we generate all outputs to build the dependency table.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\"># figure out which output depends on which input\nlocal(for (i in names(output)) {\n  output_cur &lt;&lt;- i\n  output[[i]]()\n  output_cur &lt;&lt;- NA\n})\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Now our system should know that when <code>input$slider</code> is modified, <code>output$render_value()</code> should be called (but not <code>output$render_random()</code> since it does not depend on <code>input$slider</code>).</p>\n\n<p>Imagine you are dragging a &ldquo;slider&rdquo; to modify its value (we just directly assign values to the &ldquo;slider&rdquo; below):</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">input$slider = 5\n#&gt; The slider value is 5\ninput$slider = 5  # nothing happens\ninput$slider = 100\n#&gt; The slider value is 100\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>So the above &ldquo;Shiny&rdquo; system written in 40 lines of code actually works. The real Shiny framework is certainly far more complicated than that (thousands of lines of R and JS code), but the toy implementation has shown the possibility of automatically executing a function (mostly for side-effects) when you modify an object.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"r-objects-as-command-aliases\">R objects as &ldquo;command aliases&rdquo;</h2>\n\n<p>The reason that Michael pointed out <code>makeActiveBinding()</code> was because Baptiste Auguie wanted a shortcut for <code>system('open .')</code>. Here is how you could do it:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">makeActiveBinding('open', function() {\n  system('open .')\n  invisible(getwd())\n}, globalenv())\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Then when you type <code>open</code> in the R console, your current working directory should be opened in your file viewer. This example only works on macOS. For other OSes, you need other commands (perhaps <code>xdg-open</code> for Linux, and <code>shell.exec()</code> for Windows).</p>\n\n<p>The object can be viewed as an alias of a function, in which you can run arbitrary commands. The only advantage of using active bindings in this case is that it saves you a pair of parentheses, because typically you would define a function instead: <code>open = function() system('open .')</code>, and call <code>open()</code>. Perhaps being cool is also an advantage.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"appendix-choose-a-type-of-pizza\">Appendix: choose a type of pizza</h2>\n\n<p>Colin Fay wrote a blog post on <a href=\"https://colinfay.me/ractivebinfing/\">active bindings (and pizza)</a> last year, which I believe is a much better tutorial than my post here. I recommend you to read that post, too.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/make-active-binding/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 09 Aug 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/43879425-a5818b36-9b69-11e8-98d8-51db61c66a2f.png#border","linkMd5":"9621045ac717060f5b65f04ea9f904e2","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn31@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-34-730_9459fb875d57fc57.webp","destWidth":848,"destHeight":580,"sourceBytes":74403,"destBytes":25780,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/43879425-a5818b36-9b69-11e8-98d8-51db61c66a2f.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn31@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-34-730_9459fb875d57fc57.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973447},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Miao YU is Looking for a Postdoc Position in the US","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/miao-yu-postdoc/","description":"\n        <p>My friend Miao is going to finish his postdoctoral research in Waterloo this summer. Currently he is looking for a new postdoc position in the US (no particular preference over the location in US). I&rsquo;m trying to help him here because usually I love helping talents who are not widely known (of course, famous people won&rsquo;t need my help, either).</p>\n\n<p>Miao&rsquo;s background is environmental science, about which I know nothing, but we do have one strong interest in common: reproducible research. His R skills are pretty impressive to me, and he is also a creative R programmer. He has some interesting thoughts on open and reproducible journals, which is also something I have been interested in for years.</p>\n\n<p>You can learn more about him from his vitae at <a href=\"https://yufree.cn/en/vitae/\">https://yufree.cn/en/vitae/</a>. Thanks!</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/miao-yu-postdoc/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 12 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"a3f2a727d184d363c5fb670c8b49d75b","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973463},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Write a Book with bookdown and Publish with Chapman & Hall","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/bookdown-crc/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>My editor John Kimmel told me that many authors showed interest in <strong>bookdown</strong> at JSM 2018 in Vancouver last week. I was very glad to know that. In this post, I want to briefly explain how to publish a book with Chapman &amp; Hall/CRC. Actually I <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2013/06/tips-for-writing-an-r-book/\">blogged about it</a> five years ago after I published my first book, but at that time, I was still in the LaTeX (LyX) world, and this time I want to talk about R Markdown instead. I have also written about my experience with <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/09/the-bookdown-book-in-retrospect/\">the <strong>bookdown</strong> book</a> and <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/12/blogdown-book/\">the <strong>blogdown</strong> book</a> previously.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-minimal-template-bookdown-crc\">The minimal template <code>bookdown-crc</code></h2>\n\n<p>While I was working on <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/bookdown\">the <strong>bookdown</strong> book</a> in 2016, I created a minimal book example on Github in the repository <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/bookdown-crc\">https://github.com/yihui/bookdown-crc</a>. This repository contains the LaTeX class file <code>krantz.cls</code> as well as many LaTeX tricks that I copied and pasted from Stack Overflow (e.g., how to print the page numbers in the correct format&mdash;you&rsquo;ll need <code>\\frontmatter</code>, <code>\\mainmatter</code>, and <code>\\backmatter</code>). Hopefully they will save you a lot of time. Basically you can just start writing the content of your book after you clone or download this repository.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/43736420-218f56ec-9983-11e8-8740-ec0a1fce660f.png#border\" alt=\"The front page of the bookdown-crc example\" /></p>\n\n<h2 id=\"get-started\">Get started</h2>\n\n<p>The first file you may edit is <code>index.Rmd</code>, in which you should change at least the title and author in the YAML metadata. You should update the <code>github-repo</code> field if your book source is available on Github, otherwise just delete this field.</p>\n\n<p>The <code>index.Rmd</code> is the preface of your book. It usually contains unnumbered sections (the syntax is <code>{-}</code> after a section header), such as &ldquo;Why read this book&rdquo; and &ldquo;Acknowledgements&rdquo;.</p>\n\n<p>You can edit/rename existing chapters (e.g., <code>01-introduction.Rmd</code>), or add new chapters. When you need an appendix, you need a special header <code># (APPENDIX) Appendix {-}</code>, which you can find in <code>90-more.Rmd</code> in the example. Any chapters after this chapter will be in the appendix.</p>\n\n<p>If you need an index at the end of your book, currently you have to use the raw LaTeX command <code>\\index{}</code> in Markdown. When building your book to PDF, an index page will be generated automatically. Please note that you have to escape special LaTeX characters in <code>\\index{}</code>, e.g., <code>\\index{bookdown::pdf\\_book}</code> (the underscore should be escaped).</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"customization\">Customization</h2>\n\n<p>I have talked about the possible options to customize the PDF in <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/bookdown/publishers.html\">Section 6.3</a> of the <strong>bookdown</strong> book. You are free to use any typeface you like. I have used Alegreya, Palatino, and Source Code Pro myself. When choosing fonts for your code blocks, I suggest you use either a relatively narrow font or reduce the font size (e.g., I use 70%), because your code can easily exceed the page margin when printed on a book (the paper size is roughly B5, which can hold about 55 characters on a line of code).</p>\n\n<p>I think the typeface should be the only thing you may want to customize. Other things are trivialities and not worth too much time. Don&rsquo;t be preoccupied with customizing the appearance of your PDF (at least don&rsquo;t do this too early). I guess the No.1 reason that authors fail to publish their books is not because the PDF does not look good, but they just cannot get the book done. Think about what is essential, and what are cosmetic issues.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"need-more-examples\">Need more examples?</h2>\n\n<p>The above example is a minimal one. If you want full examples, you can find a few more from <a href=\"https://bookdown.org\">https://bookdown.org</a> (e.g., the three of my books, and Hadley&rsquo;s &ldquo;Advanced R&rdquo;). They will be more complicated, but may be useful if you want to learn a particular trick, such as how to place two figures side by side.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"open-source-and-public-access-are-totally-possible\">Open source and public access are totally possible</h2>\n\n<p>To me, the most exciting change brought by <strong>bookdown</strong> regarding publishing books with Chapman &amp; Hall is that there has been a trend for authors to make the source of books and the web versions of the full books freely available online. Now Chapman &amp; Hall only asks for the right for printing and selling the books. Authors can keep the free online versions. I consider one of my life dreams fulfilled.</p>\n\n<p>I have believed there are multiple ways of tearing down the paywall. Personally I want to choose a way that does not really hurt the publishers but benefits the readers nonetheless. I feel it is really unfortunate that some people think authors/readers and publishers must be enemies of each other, and we should either kill the publishers (by pirating) or (literally) kill ourselves. Sometimes we really need a little more patience and understanding.</p>\n\n<p>Anyway, I&rsquo;m looking forward to more exciting books! You don&rsquo;t have to publish with Chapman &amp; Hall. I just happened to choose them after hearing good words about John Kimmel from a random friend. You can certainly use <strong>bookdown</strong> to write books with Springer or O&rsquo;Reilly (there are examples on bookdown.org).</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/bookdown-crc/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 06 Aug 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/43736420-218f56ec-9983-11e8-8740-ec0a1fce660f.png#border","linkMd5":"1ffe5486d3b2e19aa31cc40ac595e042","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn6@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-209_864c7dc5cee09bf7.webp","destWidth":633,"destHeight":295,"sourceBytes":24078,"destBytes":13062,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/43736420-218f56ec-9983-11e8-8740-ec0a1fce660f.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn6@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-209_864c7dc5cee09bf7.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973458},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"In HTML and the Web I Trust","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/in-html-i-trust/","description":"<p>One <strong>bookdown</strong> user seemed to be <a href=\"https://twitter.com/conjja/status/1023621901602770944\">very unhappy</a><a href=\"https://i.imgur.com/5RqREyv.png\"> with</a> the HTML output format.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:And-two-months-l\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/in-html-i-trust/#fn:And-two-months-l\">1</a></sup> Although I don’t quite understand how difficult it is to generate a PDF from <strong>bookdown</strong> (sounds like two mouse clicks to me in RStudio), this tweet did remind me of a broader topic that I wanted to write about: PDF/LaTeX vs HTML.</p> \n<p>In short, I believe <a href=\"https://www.authorea.com/users/5713/articles/19359\">LaTeX is dead (long live LaTeX)</a>. It will definitely survive for a few more years (maybe twenty or thirty years), but I believe HTML has much more potential to prosper. The fittest will survive, and HTML is the fittest. If you choose LaTeX, you are pretty much tied to PDF. If you choose HTML, you will have much more freedom and fun (e.g., interactivity). In terms of typesetting, HTML cannot beat LaTeX, but it all depends on how good you want the typesetting to be. On one extreme, you could spend an infinite amount of time on improving the appearance of your work, but I’m sure most people won’t do that. Getting things done is often more important than making things extremely beautiful or fancy. There has to be a point where you stop investing time in styling and typesetting. The question is when to stop. I tend to stop a little earlier myself, and I understand some people want to stop later.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/flip-table.jpg\" alt=\"Forget about formatting\" /></p> \n<p>One project that made me believe even more in HTML is <a href=\"https://github.com/RelaxedJS/ReLaXed\">ReLaXed</a>. The typesetting quality of web pages will surely catch up with LaTeX, but on the other hand, LaTeX will probably never be able to catch up with HTML in other aspects.</p> \n<p>On a related note, last week <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1022873179532996609\">I tweeted about my dream</a> of liberating authors from the typesetting tasks. I was envisioning that in the (far) future, it would be the readers instead of authors or journal editors who would decide how an article or a publication should look like. The authors should only take care of the content, and the editors only take care of the review. There could be a recommended style, but is not enforced. If an article is published in HTML, styling it with CSS will be much easier than LaTeX. Readers can even choose to do so in their own web browsers. Web designers could also contribute and share themes and styles. Choosing the style that makes you, <em>the reader</em>, the most comfortable when reading the document. Changing the style on the fly. Think about it. Why should we spend time on inventing a style and then blindly hope all readers will like it? People can argue to death on things like serif vs sans-serif fonts, line-heights, colors, white vs dark themes, and so on. What introduced these <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_triviality\">bikeshedding</a> wars? I think it is our belief that what looks good in our own eyes must look good in other people’s eyes, too. Look! I’m using this beautiful font! How can it possibly look ugly in your eyes?!</p> \n<p>My blog post is relatively short, and I strongly recommend that you read the full article “LaTeX is dead” above.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:And-two-months-l\">And two months later, I heard <a href=\"https://twitter.com/dan_p_simpson/status/1040689040419368960\">another similar complaint</a>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/in-html-i-trust/#fnref:And-two-months-l\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/in-html-i-trust/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 30 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/flip-table.jpg","linkMd5":"6eeab7fea0072c0edf3480989aeaaad9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn95@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-33-744_1a90f01f5f8dfb3d.webp","destWidth":480,"destHeight":252,"sourceBytes":28125,"destBytes":21196,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/flip-table.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn95@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-33-744_1a90f01f5f8dfb3d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973458},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Quietly Struggling (with Software)","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/quietly-struggling/","description":"<p>Recently I was kind of amused by two instances of quiet yet long-time struggles with software. I don’t know. I know nothing about Java. I don’t use <strong>rJava</strong> myself. I just tried to install it because I need to check the reverse dependencies of some of my R packages, which happened to use <strong>rJava</strong>.</p> \n<p>Anyway, if a software package seems to try to turn an average user into a sysadmin, that is probably not a good sign. Ummm…</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p>R CMD javareconf?</p></li> \n <li><p>clang: error: unsupported option ‘-fopenmp’? <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/a/43943631/559676\">What does <code>-fopenmp</code> mean?</a></p></li> \n <li><p>Library not loaded: /Library/Java/JavaVirtualMachines/jdk-9.jdk/Contents/Home/lib/server/libjvm.dylib?</p></li> \n <li><p>Java 8? 9? Seriously, it is awesome. But somehow it seems to have something in common with LaTeX: if you look at the output, it is often amazingly beautiful and elegant, but when you start to learn the source code, it starts to hurt (I have to say I’m much more comfortable with JavaScript than LaTeX, though).</p> \n<p>I’m sure if I learn it seriously and step by step, I wouldn’t struggle much. I kind of know JavaScript and SVG, but my brain has been poisoned by R graphics. D3 is even more low-level than base R graphics. I don’t want to spend too much time on learning it, so to some extent, I just deserve my failures.</p> \n<p>So I wanted to create a simple bubble chart after seeing <a href=\"https://bl.ocks.org/mbostock/4063269\">the beautiful official example</a>. It turned out that <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1022231125970903041\">it took me about six hours</a>, which made <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JiaShenTracy/status/1022463247440601091\">Tracy Shen</a> and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/GoldbergData/status/1022318653306548224\">Joshua Goldberg</a> feel relieved. The last time when I touched D3 was six years ago, and I still remember the examples of three dots.</p> \n<h2 id=\"conclusion\">Conclusion?</h2> \n<p>Is there anything we can learn from these two examples? One obvious lesson is that it can be very normal for one to struggle with something, even if one is an expert in other areas. Another lesson is that you may soothe other users if you share your secret struggles.</p> \n<p>While I was writing this post, an idea came to my mind: How about editing a contributed book on all our failed attempts or struggles in installing/using software packages? I think it can be valuable because it will show the average users’ perspectives. From the perspective of software authors, everything may be obvious and easy, and they may not even be able to imagine the possible ways in which users can fail. One challenge for this book, however, is to avoid turning it into a book of complaints.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Not-sure-if-ther\">Not sure if there is an equivalent version in the western culture, but we sometimes joke in Chinese: “What made you so unhappy? He actually <a href=\"https://discuss.ropensci.org/t/1078\">inserted an HTML widget</a> (via <strong>DT</strong>) to an R help page!</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/slow-applaud.gif\" alt=\"Oh my... You hackers are so awesome\" /></p>\n\n<p><strong>Update on 2018-08-01</strong>: I discovered <a href=\"https://twitter.com/yoniceedee/status/1024740274495528960\">yoni sidi&rsquo;s <code>&lt;details&gt;</code> hack</a>.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/hacking-rd/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 06 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DTWLBfhW4AACZGd.jpg:large","linkMd5":"37ecf1dac120ef1ef87ce39f85968509","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn52@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-35-816_e1d0b1f29af452b5.webp","destWidth":726,"destHeight":679,"sourceBytes":52934,"destBytes":33634,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DTWLBfhW4AACZGd.jpg:large":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn52@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-35-816_e1d0b1f29af452b5.webp","https://slides.yihui.org/gif/slow-applaud.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn55@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-20-01-727_e02f2890cfd335c9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973463},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"\"Bite-sized\" Pull Requests","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/bite-sized-pull-requests/","description":"\n        <p>A couple of days ago, the great hacker Leonardo Collado-Torres submitted <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/pull/263\">a pull request to <strong>blogdown</strong></a> after a very detailed <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/issues/261\">bug report</a>. It was definitely one of the best bug reports I had seen over the years. I was busy with something else when I saw the pull request, so I only briefly looked at it. I found it did three things: (1) Fixing the original bug; (2) Adding a new feature; (3) Fixing another &ldquo;bug&rdquo; that turned out not to be a bug. These things were related to each other, but the changes did not depend on each other. For example, you could fix the bug in a PR without adding the new feature. So I suggested him submit &ldquo;bite-sized&rdquo; PRs if possible next time. That way, I can quickly accept one change without considering the other. For example, I may like the new feature (I do in this case), but am not satisfied with the bug fix. The new feature won&rsquo;t be delayed by the bug fix.</p>\n\n<p>The cognitive load of PR reviewers will also be lighter when PRs are bite-sized. Each small PR may take one minute to review and merge, but if three small changes are placed in a moderate PR, it may take more than three minutes to review and merge (1 + 1 &gt; 2).</p>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/_ColinFay/status/1026831038587326464\"><img src=\"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DkAJYk6XgAAEVt2.jpg:large\" alt=\"Hey I've got a pull request for you\" /></a></p>\n\n<p>Because I understand how busy developers are, I&rsquo;ll try to submit small PRs when possible, and avoid bringing too many changes in a single PR. For example, I have submitted <a href=\"https://github.com/gohugoio/hugo/pulls?q=is%3Apr+author%3Ayihui+is%3Aclosed\">three PRs</a> to Hugo so far. In <a href=\"https://github.com/gohugoio/hugo/pull/3639\">#3639</a>, I focused on the essential changes (making Hugo work with Disqus out of the box). I was not satisfied with the JavaScript code and wanted to make some cosmetic changes, but I refrained from doing it in this PR to make it really minimal and highlight the essential changes. After it was accepted, I submitted a separate PR <a href=\"https://github.com/gohugoio/hugo/pull/3655\">#3655</a> for the cosmetic changes. I didn&rsquo;t care if the second PR would be accepted or not.</p>\n\n<p>In terms of cosmetic changes, Leonardo actually paid close attention to my coding style, and tried to use <code>=</code> instead of <code>&lt;-</code> for assignments. That was very considerate of him. However, he can only win the second-place award for being considerate of Yihui&rsquo;s coding style this week, because Ian Lyttle will definitely win the first-place award for his PR <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/pull/265\">rstudio/blogdown#265</a>: I didn&rsquo;t ask him to use the style</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">foo(\n  arg1, arg2, ...\n)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>but apparently he noticed my style, respected it, and started the long list of arguments <em>below</em> the left parenthesis <code>(</code>. Of course, I&rsquo;m not saying my style makes sense or advocating my style (it is a weird style!), but it will be such a smooth experience to review PRs like this, since there is zero mental friction (as if the PR were created by myself). He also meticulously corrected <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/pull/265#discussion_r170489377\">a funny typo</a> of mine in <code>NEWS.md</code> &mdash; apparently my brain was poisoned by the Rd syntax <code>\\code{}</code>. That was a great extra thing to do in a PR (no questions asked).</p>\n\n<p>P.S. Alison also said:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>I have never once used this feature on purpose.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The first time I saw this feature in the JS library remark.js (on which <strong>xaringan</strong> was built) in 2016, I imagined a possible scenario in which it might be useful. That is, when you let your students work on an exercise, you can freely show them the solution on the screen, but&hellip; with the solution slide in this mirrored mode. Students would struggle to decide if they should just work on the exercise or twist their neck in vain for the next few minutes.</p>\n\n<p>I admit I&rsquo;m evil.</p>\n\n<p>Anyway, this reminded me of something in my childhood in the 90&rsquo;s. At that time, films were rare in my village. Films were typically played once or twice in the summer evenings every year. They were projected to a screen (which was pretty much a large piece of white cloth tied to two bamboo poles) in a large open place, so <a href=\"https://www.sohu.com/a/220821759_720273\">lots of people could watch together</a>. Sometimes it was so crowded in the front of the screen that I could barely see the screen because I was buried in the crowd as a little child. One day I figured out a solution, which was to go to the back side of the screen.</p>\n\n<p>I didn&rsquo;t need to read any characters on the screen, and usually there weren&rsquo;t many characters to read in the films. It doesn&rsquo;t really matter when pictures are mirrored&mdash;it was equally awesome no matter if Bruce Lee was kicking to the left or right.</p>\n\n<p>The really nice thing on the other side of the screen was that I could enjoy a huge dedicated theater. I have never had such luxury again later in my life.</p>\n\n<p>Nowadays there is no way that you could see anything from the back side of a screen. For most screens, perhaps you cannot even go to the back side because you&rsquo;d just hit a wall. So I think it should be safe to present the mirrored solution slide to your students.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/look-hard.gif\" alt=\"No matter how hard you look at the screen, you won't be able to read it\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2020/04/xaringan-mirror/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 15 Apr 2020 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/EVhHV5QU4AA_DVu?format=png&name=900x900","linkMd5":"63d720ab6b14dfc513c71e3f1d3d0665","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn83@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-35-216_a2beb8bd5a4861c0.webp","destWidth":895,"destHeight":423,"sourceBytes":29143,"destBytes":24364,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/EVhHV5QU4AA_DVu?format=png&name=900x900":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn83@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-35-216_a2beb8bd5a4861c0.webp","https://slides.yihui.org/gif/look-hard.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn68@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-20-01-558_3373bf108dfd7d9a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973435},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Pour Positive Energy into Github Issues","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/positive-energy-github/","description":"\n        <p>Github issues often make me nervous. I&rsquo;m excited when other people feel the excitement as I did when I worked on a project.</p>\n\n<p>A few days ago, I heard about the <a href=\"https://github.com/datalorax/slidex\"><strong>slidex</strong></a> package on Twitter (to convert PowerPoint to <strong>xaringan</strong> slides), but didn&rsquo;t have time to look into it until today. Oh&hellip; Okay. A Mickey mouse it was indeed.</p>\n\n<p>I&rsquo;m not as good as my little kid at recognizing shapes. Although it seems emojis are extremely popular nowadays, I often fail to figure out what they mean. They are as hard to decipher as <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oracle_bone_script\">oracle bone scripts</a>. Sometimes I also don&rsquo;t understand why they have to be used at all, since the alternative way seems to be much clearer, i.e., writing in text instead of pictographs. Are we so bored by our current writing system? Why are we so interested in going back to a writing system in <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hieroglyph\">3000 BCE</a>? I was able to figure out what these emojis meant in the context. In, the, <em>context</em>. Then why is it worth the trouble?</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>For authors, they have to take the time to find the appropriate emojis. They translate the text to symbols.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>For readers, they have to decipher the symbols, i.e., they translate the symbols back to text.</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>This reminds me of <a href=\"http://www.quotationspage.com/quote/40151.html\">a quote</a>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Writing well means never having to say, &lsquo;I guess you had to be there.&rsquo;</p>\n\n<p>&mdash; Jef Mallett, Frazz, 07-29-07</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>In some cases, using emojis means &lsquo;I guess you have to read my mind.&rsquo;</p>\n\n<p>Of course, I&rsquo;m not that extreme. I rarely work when my little ones are crawling or running or yelling in my home office, but I did merge Github pull requests a couple of times with my right hand while holding a baby in my left hand. Those who send the pull requests won&rsquo;t see him, but that is one practical constraint I might have occasionally.</p>\n\n<p>Hopefully you will understand better why I prefer &ldquo;<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/bite-sized-pull-requests/\">bite-sized pull requests</a>&rdquo; now. After reading my colleague Jim Hester’s feedback and doing some research on the differences between <code>brew install r</code> and <code>brew cask install r</code>, I decided to switch to the latter.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:One-reason-for-t\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/cranextra-macos/#fn:One-reason-for-t\">1</a></sup> Consequently, I changed the scope of the repository <code>macos.rbind.io</code> to only building the missing binary packages on CRAN. Windows users may have heard of the so-called “CRANextra” repository, which contains binary R packages that are not available on CRAN. The repository <code>macos.rbind.io</code> is also a CRANextra repository, but created for macOS users.</p> \n<p>The full documentation is on the repository homepage <a href=\"https://macos.rbind.io\">https://macos.rbind.io</a>. In short, you just add an extra CRAN repository in your global option <code>repos</code>, e.g.,</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">options(repos = c(\n  CRANextra = 'https://macos.rbind.io',\n  CRAN = 'https://cran.rstudio.com'\n))\n</code></pre> \n<p>Then you will be able to install some missing binary R packages, such as <strong>RGtk2</strong>, provided that your R was installed via <code>brew cask install r</code>.</p> \n<p>While working on this CRANextra repository, I briefly looked at some users’ work on <a href=\"https://rattle.togaware.com/rattle-install-mac.html\">installing the <strong>rattle</strong> package</a>, which depends on <strong>RGtk2</strong> and <strong>cairoDevice</strong>. It was such a big mess! There were people still using MacPorts, and some were trying to edit the <code>gtk+</code> formula in Homebrew and building <code>gtk+</code> from source. With the above CRANextra repository, all you need is:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">system('brew install gtk+')\ninstall.packages('rattle')\n</code></pre> \n<p>There will be no more Step 1, 2, …, and 12.</p> \n<p>To be clear, eventually I hope this repository can be retired and CRAN can provide the missing binaries. From what I understand, the major obstacle at the moment is the missing support for OpenMP (<code>-fopenmp</code>) in the system Clang on macOS, so CRAN provided a homemade version of Clang. I don’t know why that is so critical, but I wish one day packages could just build out of the box (or at least make it possible to automate the process). Personally I always frown upon software that tries to turn average users into sysadmins: What does <code>-fopenmp</code> even mean? What is <code>Makevars</code>? And <code>SHLIB_OPENMP_CFLAGS</code>? And <code>R CMD javareconf</code>? Sigh. We all love software.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:One-reason-for-t\">One reason for this was that <code>brew install r</code> does not support <code>cairo</code>, so you cannot use graphical devices such as <code>svg()</code> or <code>cairo_pdf()</code>. Anyway, <code>brew cask install r</code> installs the official version of R from CRAN, which supports features like <code>install.packages(..., type = 'both')</code>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/cranextra-macos/#fnref:One-reason-for-t\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/cranextra-macos/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sun, 01 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"7f54b5ab26bc3298cf6f987c9cf6e4eb","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The Ultimate Infinite Moon Reader for xaringan Slides","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2019/02/ultimate-inf-mr/","description":"<p>Recently I have been playing with WebSocket, partly due to the <code>chrome_print()</code> function in <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown\">the <strong>pagedown</strong> package</a>. Last Friday, it suddenly occurred to me that there could be a very interesting way to improve the user experience of the “Infinite Moon Reader” in the <strong>xaringan</strong> package (i.e., <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code>). After three days’ work, I have finally become happy with it:</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/53144527-35f7a500-3562-11e9-862e-892d3fd7036d.gif\" alt=\"The ultimate infinite moon reader for xaringan slides: when navigating in the source, the corresponding slide in the output will be automatically displayed; when typing in the source, you can instantly preview the output.\" /></p> \n<h2 id=\"two-unpleasant-issues-about-authoring-documents-with-r-markdown\">Two unpleasant issues about authoring documents with R Markdown</h2> \n<p>I have been slightly unhappy with the user experience of authoring documents with R Markdown. The two particular issues are:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>It is hard to hold the desire to preview the document. I feel like I want to click the <code>Knit</code> button after I write every single sentence. With <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code>, this problem is a little less unpleasant: you just save the document, and the output will be automatically recompiled and refreshed. However, this often takes a couple of seconds. Yes, even a couple of seconds bothers me.</p></li> \n <li><p>The source document and the output page are completely independent. When the documents are long, it can be time-consuming for the user to navigate to the R Markdown source corresponding to a certain part in the output, or go from a certain part of the output page to the corresponding source.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Fortunately, these general issues are solvable in the case of HTML5 slides, because slides are usually short and regular pages. It is relatively easy to establish the map between the slide source (Markdown) and the slide output (an HTML element).</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-new-xaringan-inf-mr\">The new <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code></h2> \n<p>If you install the development version of <strong>xaringan</strong> (I recommend that Windows users also install <a href=\"https://www.rstudio.com/products/rstudio/download/preview/\">the latest preview version of RStudio</a> if you cannot preview <strong>xaringan</strong> slides in RStudio Viewer):</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">remotes::install_github('yihui/xaringan', upgrade = TRUE)\n</code></pre> \n<p>and run the “Infinite Moon Reader” addin in RStudio, or <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code> on an R Markdown document that has the output format <code>xaringan::moon_reader</code>, you should be able to observe these new features:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>When you navigate through the R Markdown source document in RStudio, the slide output will be automatically navigated to the page corresponding to the current line in the R Markdown source.</p></li> \n <li><p>When you modify the R Markdown source, you can preview the output immediately (by default, the delay is one second, but this is configurable via the global option <code>options(servr.interval = N)</code> before you run <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code>; you can set <code>N</code> to an arbitrary time interval, e.g., <code>0.5</code>). LaTeX math expressions can also be previewed. Code blocks will be syntax highlighted if necessary.</p></li> \n <li><p>When you click on the slides and navigate through them, the cursor in the RStudio editor will also be automatically moved to the first source line corresponding to the current page in the slides.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Please note that these features only exist for <strong>xaringan</strong> slides. General HTML output formats are not supported: no automatic navigation or instant preview, but the feature of automatically recompiling Rmd and refreshing output as soon as Rmd is modified and saved still works like before.</p> \n<h2 id=\"when-in-doubt-save-the-source-document\">When in doubt, save the source document</h2> \n<p>The above features work when the document is modified but not saved. The navigation won’t work if the numbers of pages of slides calculated from the source and the output are not equal (e.g., after you add a new slide in the source). The preview doesn’t run R code chunks, respect slide attributes (such as classes or background images), or understand any <strong>knitr</strong> code chunk options.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:One-exception-is\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/02/ultimate-inf-mr/#fn:One-exception-is\">1</a></sup> As soon as the source document is saved, the whole document will be recompiled, and things should be normal again.</p> \n<p>However, if you dynamically generate slides via R code, these features won’t work (<code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code> won’t be that smart), e.g.,</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, results='asis'}\ncat('\\n---\\n# A new slide\\n\\n')\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>In this case, it is hard for me to know how to map a line in the source to a page in the output.</p> \n<h2 id=\"please-help-me-test-it\">Please help me test it</h2> \n<p><a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/commit/97922037b38a\">The implementation</a> was not super complicated. At least the mechanism was simple enough: create a websocket in the browser, keep sending the page information to R, let R send the source of the current slide back to the websocket when necessary, and render the Markdown source to HTML. I may have been too excited about the new <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code> and failed to consider certain edge cases, so please help me test it and let me know what you discover or think.</p> \n<p>P.S. Hi, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/EngelhardtCR/status/1096914316702433281\">Chris Engelhardt</a>, you probably should not let your 5-year-old son know these new features, or I’ll worry about your keyboard…</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:One-exception-is\">One exception is that when a code chunk uses the option <code>eval=FALSE</code> or <code>include=FALSE</code>, the code block will be hidden in the preview, but no code chunks are really executed through <strong>knitr</strong> until you save the document. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/02/ultimate-inf-mr/#fnref:One-exception-is\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2019/02/ultimate-inf-mr/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 20 Feb 2019 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/53144527-35f7a500-3562-11e9-862e-892d3fd7036d.gif","linkMd5":"49691e82b3654602658b1f18ace38f93","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn87@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-50-403_75d2b5933de6c0eb.webp","destWidth":1212,"destHeight":720,"sourceBytes":1082087,"destBytes":2363904,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/53144527-35f7a500-3562-11e9-862e-892d3fd7036d.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn87@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-50-403_75d2b5933de6c0eb.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973439},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The Video of My Talk on blogdown at rstudio::conf 2018","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/blogdown-video-rstudio-conf/","description":"\n        <p>After I gave the talk on blogdown at rstudio::conf 2018, some people really thought I was a fast typist, because I &ldquo;typed&rdquo; a relatively long post at the podium in just 20 seconds. On one hand, I was happy to learn that, because my trick worked (too) well on some people. On the other, I was a little sorry that they trusted me so well &mdash; I thought it was obvious that I was cheating.</p>\n\n<p>You can <a href=\"https://www.rstudio.com/resources/videos/create-and-maintain-websites-with-r-markdown-and-blogdown/\">watch the full video here</a> on the RStudio website. I started to type the post at 18:20, if you don&rsquo;t want to watch the full video. In fact, I had explained how I made it <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/typing-fast/\">in that post</a>, but apparently the audience wouldn&rsquo;t notice it because the editor was scrolling fast. Note that when I typed the commit message in GIT, I was so very slow&hellip;</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/programming-movie-actual.gif\" alt=\"Typing fast\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/blogdown-video-rstudio-conf/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 05 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/programming-movie-actual.gif","linkMd5":"f0260e6889d877e60501345247373b0d","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn59@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-37-079_d76eebcf930c36ee.webp","destWidth":390,"destHeight":293,"sourceBytes":1924398,"destBytes":435424,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/programming-movie-actual.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn59@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-37-079_d76eebcf930c36ee.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973464},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"My Trick of Deciding if a Link Should Open a New Browser Tab","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/target-blank/","description":"<p>Albert Kim <a href=\"https://twitter.com/rudeboybert/status/1042418261596545027\">just discovered one way</a> to make links open in new browser tabs for R Markdown users: you can add an attribute <code>target=\"_blank\"</code> after a link, e.g., <code>[example website](https://example.com){target=\"_blank\"}</code>.</p> \n<h2 id=\"you-should-thoroughly-learn-pandoc-s-markdown\">You should thoroughly learn Pandoc’s Markdown</h2> \n<p><a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/11/thanks-tj-mahr/\">No matter how many times</a> I have recommended R Markdown users to read the <em>full</em> Pandoc manual at least once, I still want to recommend it again. You won’t really appreciate how powerful Pandoc’s Markdown is until you read the full manual once. The <a href=\"https://pandoc.org/MANUAL.html#extension-link_attributes\"><code>link_attributes</code> extension</a> is just one of the many, many, many useful features of Pandoc’s Markdown.</p> \n<h2 id=\"what-s-target-blank\">What’s <code>target=\"blank\"</code>?</h2> \n<p>In Albert’s case, however, you still won’t know how to make a link open in a new browser tab unless you understand <a href=\"https://www.w3schools.com/tags/att_a_target.asp\">what <code>&lt;a target=\"_blank\"&gt;</code> means in HTML</a>. The <em>two</em> prerequisites, reading Pandoc’s manual <em>and</em> knowing HTML, is probably why many users couldn’t figure out how to write a URL in Markdown to be opened in a new tab.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-real-tricky-issue\">The real tricky issue</h2> \n<p>Pure technical issues are often relatively easy to resolve. Things will become complicated whenever humans are involved.</p> \n<p>As I expected, there will be people who hate links that always open in new tabs, such as <a href=\"https://twitter.com/Bootvis/status/1042448271350943749\">Bob</a> in the Twitter thread:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>Please don’t decide for the user. If I want to open a link in a new tab, my browser makes it easy to do so, and if I want to go back to your document, I use the back button.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Without <code>target=\"_blank\"</code>, readers can actually make the decision by themselves. If they do want the link to be opened in a new tab, they can right-click on the link and use the context menu “Open Link in New Tab”, or hold a key while (left-)clicking the link (e.g., on macOS, hold <code>Command</code>). This is no big deal<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:It-might-be-tric\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/target-blank/#fn:It-might-be-tric\">1</a></sup>. If you force a link to be opened in a new tab, you may offend readers who would rather hit the Back button instead of closing a new tab when they want to leave the new tab.</p> \n<p>The benefit of opening a link in a new tab is that <em>the state of the current page can be preserved</em> while you read the page in the new tab. This is not something that the Back button can absolutely guarantee. Many of you may have been warned when you make payments online that you should not use the Back button (because you may end up paying twice). What happens when you hit the Back button is not necessarily just to bring you back to the previous page.</p> \n<p>With that in mind, opening a link in a new tab may sound like a good idea (at least it never hurts technically), but as I mentioned in <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/\">a previous post</a>, it is easy to overdo good ideas. Users may hate it when too many new tabs/windows are opened from your web page.</p> \n<h2 id=\"what-on-earth-do-new-tabs-indicate\">What on earth do new tabs indicate?</h2> \n<p>Let’s back up a little from the technical stuff and think more about the core problem: what do new tabs mean, and when are you most likely to open a link in a new tab?</p> \n<p>There may not be a universal answer that satisfies everyone, but to me, a new tab means <em>a new context</em>. Then the question becomes, which links are more likely to take the readers to new contexts? I tend to think that an <em>external</em> URL indicates a new context. By “external URL”, I mean a URL that takes you to a different website. Roughly speaking, two websites are different if their domain names are different.</p> \n<h2 id=\"open-external-links-in-new-tabs-automatically\">Open external links in new tabs automatically</h2> \n<p>Finally we are back to the technical world again. On my personal website, I use a few lines of JavaScript to automatically add <code>target=\"_blank\"</code> to external links:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-js\">(function() {\n  var links = document.getElementsByTagName('a');\n  for (var i = 0; i &lt; links.length; i++) {\n    if (/^(https?:)?\\/\\//.test(links[i].getAttribute('href'))) {\n      links[i].target = '_blank';\n    }\n  }\n})();\n</code></pre> \n<p>To fully understand the code above, you have to understand URLs. You may think URLs are simple, but they may not be. People <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/valid-url/\">make mistakes</a> all the time. People <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1040441928087683072\">get confused</a> over and over again. Relative URLs? Absolute URLs? Absolute relative URLs? Protocol-free URLs? You may read my least favorite part of the <strong>blogdown</strong> book to know more: <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/blogdown/html.html\">Appendix B.1</a>.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/shoot-myself.gif\" alt=\"I'm just going to shoot myself\" /></p> \n<p>Long story short, when I add links to my web pages, I omit the protocol and domain in the URLs<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:This-makes-the-U\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/target-blank/#fn:This-makes-the-U\">2</a></sup> if they are internal links to pages that are still on my website (under <code>https://yihui.org/</code>), e.g.,</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">[the knitr website](/knitr/)\n</code></pre> \n<p>and I use full URLs for links to other websites, e.g.,</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">[the rmarkdown website](https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com)\n</code></pre> \n<p>This way, I know that URLs starting with <code>https://</code> are supposed to be external links, hence I can detect them using the JS regular expression <code>/^(https?:)?\\/\\//</code>, which can match <code>https://</code>, <code>http://</code>, and <code>//</code>. Once I detect a link of which the <code>href</code> attribute points to an external URL, I add the attribute <code>target=\"_blank\"</code> to it. So whenever you see a new tab after clicking a link on my website, it means you are being navigated to a different website (be prepared for the new context).</p> \n<h2 id=\"summary\">Summary</h2> \n<p>I presented a way to make links open in new tabs without requiring the author of the web page to hard-code the <code>target=\"_blank\"</code> attribute in links, and at the same time, without annoying readers too much (hopefully). The fundamental assumption of this approach is: a different tab == a different context == a different website. Is this a reasonable assumption for everyone? Of course, I’m not 100% sure.</p> \n<p>Good software is never about coding alone. It is about coding plus psychology (thinking one step further to minimize the annoyance). Even trivially small problems are worth thinking.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:It-might-be-tric\">It might be tricky or impossible on mobile devices where you don’t have a physical keyboard, though. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/target-blank/#fnref:It-might-be-tric\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:This-makes-the-U\">This makes the URLs more portable: when I change my domain name or protocol, I don’t need to update these URLs. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/target-blank/#fnref:This-makes-the-U\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"xfun::pkg_attach(): A Vectorized Version of library()","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/xfun-pkg-attach/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>Chances are you often need to use more than one package in R, so it is common to see that an R script starts with multiple calls to <code>library()</code>, the function that attaches a package to an R session, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">library(ggplot2)\nlibrary(dplyr)\nlibrary(shiny)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/repeat-smoke.gif\" alt=\"Repeat the same function call over and over again\" /></p>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-vectorized-library\">The vectorized <code>library()</code></h2>\n\n<p>With the function <code>xfun::pkg_attach()</code>, you can attach them through a character vector:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">xfun::pkg_attach(c('ggplot2', 'dplyr', 'shiny'))\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>As I said in a post on &ldquo;<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2014/07/library-vs-require/\"><code>library()</code> vs <code>require()</code></a>&rdquo; in 2014, I believe it was a design mistake to use NSE (non-standard evaluation) in <code>library()</code>. It saves a little bit typing effort since you don&rsquo;t need to type the quotes, but it also opens a can of worms, and introduces more non-standard things due to this non-standard thing (e.g., when you do code analysis, you have to make a special rule for the <code>library()</code> calls to make sure those &ldquo;variables&rdquo; in parentheses are not real variables but character strings of package names). The above <code>xfun::pkg_attach()</code> call is essentially a for-loop:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">for (i in c('ggplot2', 'dplyr', 'shiny')) {\n  library(i, character.only = TRUE)\n}\n</code></pre>\n\n<h2 id=\"automatically-install-missing-packages\">Automatically install missing packages</h2>\n\n<p>It is also common to see code like this in R scripts (install a package if not installed or loadable):</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">if (!require('shiny')) install.packages('shiny')\nlibrary(shiny)\nif (!require('ggplot2')) install.packages('ggplot2')\nlibrary(ggplot2)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>The argument <code>install = TRUE</code> in <code>xfun::pkg_attach()</code> allows you to do the above task, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">xfun::pkg_attach(c('ggplot2', 'dplyr', 'shiny'), install = TRUE)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Or use the shorthand <code>xfun::pkg_attach2()</code>, which is essentially <code>pkg_attach(..., install = TRUE)</code>:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">xfun::pkg_attach2(c('ggplot2', 'dplyr', 'shiny'))\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Some people may argue that you should never install software packages without the user&rsquo;s permission. That is a good point. If your R script is meant to be run by other users, you need to be a little conservative, but some users may actually prefer convenience (i.e., don&rsquo;t bother me with error messages about missing packages). Of course, if you are writing a script for yourself, you can always use <code>pkg_attach2()</code>. If you want to ask for permission, you certainly can, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">xfun::pkg_attach(\n  c('ggplot2', 'dplyr', 'shiny'),\n  install = readline('Install missing packages (y/n)? ') == 'y'\n)\n</code></pre>\n\n<h2 id=\"suppress-package-startup-messages\">Suppress package startup messages</h2>\n\n<p>Some people don&rsquo;t like package startup messages. I think those messages are fine if they only appear once or at most a couple of times to a new user. When you see the same messages over and over again, you will be bored, but you cannot shout at your screen that &ldquo;I KNOW <code>dplyr::filter()</code> MASKS <code>stats::filter()</code>! PLEASE DON&rsquo;T TELL ME THIS AGAIN!&rdquo; when you <code>library(tidyverse)</code>.</p>\n\n<p>How to suppress package startup messages? As you could have guessed, the function is named <code>suppressPackageStartupMessages()</code>. Do you like it? I don&rsquo;t, because it is too long and often calls for disproportional attention when you read the code (worse if you have to call it multiple times). Do I have a better name? No (so I&rsquo;ll shut up on naming).</p>\n\n<p>If you are attaching a package in <strong>knitr</strong>, you can use the chunk option <code>message = FALSE</code> to suppress package startup messages, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-markdown\">```{r, message=FALSE}\nlibrary(tidyverse)\n```\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Note that this will suppress <em>any types of messages</em>, including package startup messages. It might be a problem, but I often load packages in a separate code chunk in the beginning of a <strong>knitr</strong> document, so it is unlikely to suppress messages that I didn&rsquo;t mean to suppress.</p>\n\n<p>Since my colleague Winston <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/issues/1583\">was not satisfied</a> by this solution, I added a <code>message</code> argument to <code>xfun::pkg_attach()</code> to suppress startup messages (and he didn&rsquo;t like it, either, which was understandable).</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">xfun::pkg_attach('tidyverse', message = FALSE)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>The default value of <code>message</code> in this function comes from the global option <code>xfun.pkg_attach.message</code>, so you may set it in your <code>.Rprofile</code> to always suppress startup messages, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">options(xfun.pkg_attach.message = FALSE)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>I wish the <code>library()</code> function has a similar argument, so I don&rsquo;t have to hack at it this way.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"will-xfun-pkg-attach-become-the-new-library\">Will <code>xfun::pkg_attach()</code> become the new <code>library()</code>?</h2>\n\n<p>No, I don&rsquo;t believe so, and I don&rsquo;t mean so, either. I wrote this post just to illustrate one type of pain <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/influence-depth-or-breadth/\">I mentioned a few days ago</a>: the pain that is tiny yet comes frequently.
