To sum it up, the tiny pain here is:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>You cannot <code>library(c('ggplot2', 'dplyr', 'shiny'))</code>.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>You have to test the availability of a package explicitly before deciding to install it, i.e., <code>if (!require()) install.packages()</code>.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>You have to explicitly suppress the package startup messages every single time if you don&rsquo;t want to see them, either through a long-named function or by a <strong>knitr</strong> chunk option.</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/xfun-pkg-attach/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 04 Sep 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/repeat-smoke.gif","linkMd5":"c65979c907449538f2b1c7ff437e29ff","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn28@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-36-799_c2431c2f3104ab29.webp","destWidth":480,"destHeight":360,"sourceBytes":1003566,"destBytes":302316,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/repeat-smoke.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn28@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-36-799_c2431c2f3104ab29.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973446},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"No Slap in the Face to Those Who are Smiling","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/no-slap-to-smiles/","description":"\n        <p>There is an idiom in Chinese that I can roughly translate as &ldquo;You shall not slap a person in the face when he is smiling at you&rdquo;. This means if a person is showing a positive attitude, you&rsquo;d better forgive him even if he has made a mistake.</p>\n\n<p>A couple of days ago, a Stack Overflow user named &ldquo;Caddisfly&rdquo; wrote his/her (hard to guess the gender from this nickname) first post on Stack Overflow titled <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/50164548/559676\">&ldquo;Short caption in kable&rdquo;</a>. What happened to this post partially confirmed my thought in <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/right-easier-than-wrong/\">my previous blog post</a>: some people tend to do easier things that are not very helpful, and avoid doing things that are harder but tremendously more helpful.</p>\n\n<p>In Caddisfly&rsquo;s first post, (s)he showed good intentions: (s)he found a solution to a problem in a hard way (my bad), and wanted to share this solution with the community. Unfortunately, the answer was mixed right in the question. When I first saw the post, Caddisfly&rsquo;s reputation point was only one, meaning no experience in posting anything at all on Stack Overflow. Then the post was downvoted once, and a user voted to close the post. To me, that is <em>technically</em> understandable, but also very, very bad in terms of building a welcoming community.</p>\n\n<p>Downvoting or voting to close a question only requires clicking your mouse once or twice, which is extremely easy. Educating the beginner that an answer should be separate from the question requires you to type more words, which could take a minute or two, and therefore is much harder to do, but also much more helpful and meaningful (thanks, @gparyani, for doing that in the second comment).</p>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/50164548/559676\"><img src=\"https://db.yihui.org/images/so-good-intention.png#border\" alt=\"A Stack Overflow post that shows good intentions but was voted to be closed\" /></a></p>\n\n<p>Perhaps my words &ldquo;slap in the face&rdquo; are too strong. My point is that if a user is showing good intentions, we should try to welcome and encourage him/her. Formality problems such as splitting a post into a question and answer are fairly easy to fix, but kind users who are willing to share solutions are relatively difficult to find (some are busy, and some don&rsquo;t bother). Between the two choices, a Stack Overflow post correct in the form and a generous Stack Overflow user, which would you value more? I think the answer should be obvious.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/no-slap-to-smiles/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 07 May 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://db.yihui.org/images/so-good-intention.png#border","linkMd5":"95bc171b92056820aeab7c0a915f9981","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn23@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-138_c65c1e4992559618.webp","destWidth":759,"destHeight":835,"sourceBytes":53937,"destBytes":79064,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://db.yihui.org/images/so-good-intention.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn23@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-138_c65c1e4992559618.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973462},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The Best Way to Support LaTeX Math in Markdown with MathJax","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/latex-math-markdown/","description":"<p>Recle Etino Vibal <a href=\"https://twitter.com/recleev/status/1020134832519565312\">discovered a MathJax trick</a> I used early last year in the hugo-lithium theme to render LaTeX math in Markdown. However, it was not an elegant trick in my eyes, and I came up with a much better way later. I mentioned before that <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/ian-lyttle/\">Ian Lyttle asked me a question</a> during rstudio::conf 2017, which was exactly the problem that Recle had. I believe a lot of Markdown users must have the same question, too, so I’ll share my solution in this post.</p> \n<p>If you use Pandoc or R Markdown, there is no need to read this post, because Pandoc supports LaTeX math very well. This post is only for plain Markdown users. If you understand Chinese, you may read <a href=\"https://yihui.org/cn/2017/04/mathjax-markdown/\">this post</a> I wrote last year instead.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-problem-of-latex-math-expressions-in-markdown\">The problem of LaTeX math expressions in Markdown</h2> \n<p>The biggest problem of Markdown is that it has way too many flavors that are not completely compatible with each other. One common problem, however, is that most of them do not properly support LaTeX math expressions (inline <code>$ $</code> or display style <code>$$ $$</code>). Even if the support exists, it is often awkward (e.g., in a flavor called <a href=\"https://github.com/miekg/mmark\">Mmark</a>, you have to use double dollars to write inline math expressions, which is wrong LaTeX syntax).</p> \n<p>There are two difficulties when you try to write LaTeX math with plain Markdown, as I mentioned in the <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/blogdown/javascript.html\">Appendix B.3 of the <strong>blogdown</strong> book</a>.</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>You want to make sure the Markdown render will not touch your special characters, e.g., <code>$x_{i} + y_{i}$</code> should not be rendered as <code>$x&lt;em&gt;{i} + y&lt;/em&gt;{i}$</code> (i.e., a pair of underscores should not mean italics).</p></li> \n <li><p>MathJax does not treat a pair of single dollars as an inline math expression (only <code>\\(</code> and <code>\\)</code> work). You may force it to, but you may have false positives. For example, in a phrase “I have a $10 and $5 bill”, the text between the two dollar signs is not math.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>It is relatively easy to solve the first problem: you can put your math content in a pair of backticks to protect it as <code>&lt;code&gt;&lt;/code&gt;</code>. Most Markdown renders will not touch the characters in this case, but this will bring a new problem: MathJax does not parse LaTeX math in <code>&lt;code&gt;</code> tags. Again, you may force it to, but your math expressions will inherit CSS styles from the <code>code</code> tag. For example, if you defined</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-css\">code {\n  background: lightgray;\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>your math expressions will have a light-gray background, too. It is possible to remove the background color, but it is relatively complicated.</p> \n<p>Even if you use a pair of backticks, you still have the second problem above.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-solution-pre-process-code-tags\">The solution: pre-process <code>code</code> tags</h2> \n<p>I have mentioned and briefly explained the solution in the <strong>blogdown</strong> book, too. The basic idea is:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>You write math expressions in pairs of backticks.</p></li> \n <li><p>Use JavaScript to examine all <code>&lt;code&gt;</code> tags in HTML, and identify possible math expressions.</p></li> \n <li><p>Remove the outside <code>&lt;code&gt;&lt;/code&gt;</code> tags if they contain math expressions, e.g., convert <code>&lt;code&gt;$$x + y$$&lt;/code&gt;</code> to <code>$$x + y$$</code>.</p></li> \n <li><p>Additionally, convert a pair of single dollars <code>$ $</code> to <code>\\( \\)</code>, so that MathJax can understand it without any special configurations.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>My JavaScript solution is available at <a href=\"https://yihui.org/js/math-code.js\">https://yihui.org/js/math-code.js</a>. It is not very long (about 20 lines). After loading this script, you can include MathJax (even <em>asynchronously</em>):</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-html\">&lt;script src=\"//yihui.org/js/math-code.js\"&gt;&lt;/script&gt;\n&lt;!-- Just one possible MathJax CDN below. You may use others. --&gt;\n&lt;script async\n  src=\"//mathjax.rstudio.com/latest/MathJax.js?config=TeX-MML-AM_CHTML\"&gt;\n&lt;/script&gt;\n</code></pre> \n<p>Then in Markdown, you can write math expressions as usual, e.g.,</p> \n<pre><code>Hi `$z = x + y$`.\n\n`$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$`\n\n`$$\\begin{vmatrix}a &amp; b\\\\\nc &amp; d\n\\end{vmatrix}=ad-bc$$`\n</code></pre> \n<p>Output (you can see the output on this page because I used the JavaScript code above):</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>Hi <code>$z = x + y$</code>.</p> \n <p><code>$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$</code></p> \n <p><code>$$\\begin{vmatrix}a &amp; b\\\\ c &amp; d \\end{vmatrix}=ad-bc$$</code></p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>This is the best solution I could come up with to support LaTeX math expressions in Markdown if the Markdown renderer does not have native support for them like Pandoc.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:I-used-this-tric\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/latex-math-markdown/#fn:I-used-this-tric\">1</a></sup> If you are able to use Pandoc, I still suggest that you use Pandoc instead of my hack, because it is targeted only at HTML output.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:I-used-this-tric\">I used this trick in the <strong>xaringan</strong> package to support LaTeX math, because the Markdown renderer <strong>remark.js</strong> does not support LaTeX math. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/latex-math-markdown/#fnref:I-used-this-tric\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/latex-math-markdown/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sun, 22 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"377d41f9f3dabf1fdab8108cc7cf97cd","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Using TinyTeX from a Flash Drive","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/tinytex-flash-drive/","description":"\n        <p>One nice fact about <a href=\"https://yihui.org/tinytex/\">TinyTeX</a>, the custom LaTeX distribution I created last year, is that once you install it, it is just a self-contained folder that can be moved elsewhere, even across computers of the same operating system. That is what &ldquo;portable&rdquo; means.</p>\n\n<p>Yesterday I pushed a new version (v0.8) of the R package <strong>tinytex</strong> <a href=\"https://cran.rstudio.com/package=tinytex\">to CRAN</a> mainly to address the issue of using TinyTeX from a portable device such as a flash drive. I added two new functions in this version:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p><code>tinytex::copy_tinytex()</code> copies your existing TinyTeX installation to another location, such as your flash drive or a Dropbox folder (this could take a minute or two depending on the I/O speed of your devices). Of course, this means you have installed TinyTeX before (e.g., via <code>tinytex::install_tinytex()</code>). By default, it will pop up a dialog box asking you to choose the destination folder to copy to, if you don&rsquo;t want to explicitly provide a path like:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">tinytex::copy_tinytex('E:/Software/')\n</code></pre></li>\n\n<li><p><code>tinytex::use_tinytex()</code> asks you the location of TinyTeX, so that <strong>tinytex</strong> can find it later when compiling LaTeX documents. By default, it also pops up a dialog asking you to choose the TinyTeX folder, which can be on your flash drive.</p>\n\n<p>Under the hood, it runs the command <code>tlmgr path add</code> to add TinyTeX to the environment variable <code>PATH</code> of your system (or create symlinks of TinyTeX&rsquo;s executables that can be found via <code>PATH</code>). If this fails, you still have a fallback plan. That is, set the global option <code>tinytex.tlmgr.path</code> and point it to the executable <code>tlmgr</code> in your system, e.g. (remember to replace the path in the example with the actual path to your TinyTeX),</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\"># a Windows example\noptions(tinytex.tlmgr.path = 'E:/Software/TinyTeX/bin/win32/tlmgr.bat')\n    \n# a macOS example\noptions(tinytex.tlmgr.path = '~/Dropbox/TinyTeX/bin/x86_64-darwin/tlmgr')\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>You can do this in your <code>~/.Rprofile</code> (recommended) or a code chunk of an R Markdown document that generates PDF output. Don&rsquo;t set this option in your R console unless you are trying to generate PDF in the R console via functions like <code>rmarkdown::render()</code> or <code>knitr::knit2pdf()</code>.</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>One folder to rule them all. No dependency hell. No waste of disk space. No IT support. Just help yourself.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/car-transform.gif\" alt=\"Help yourself\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/tinytex-flash-drive/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 31 Aug 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/car-transform.gif","linkMd5":"fa88df7f84fc963eeaf10898bd569860","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn40@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-35-699_5c3d06c0249be70c.webp","destWidth":150,"destHeight":150,"sourceBytes":843124,"destBytes":311152,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/car-transform.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn40@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-35-699_5c3d06c0249be70c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973446},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Write / Don't Write the Whole Book in bookdown","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/whole-bookdown/","description":"\n        <p>For any software package, there may be users who love it or hate it, which never surprises me. In the past two days, I noticed two tweets that said opposite things about <strong>bookdown</strong>. The other day, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tjmahr/status/1016504012802322434\">tj mahr said</a>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>don&rsquo;t write your book from start to finish in bookdown. it&rsquo;s too easy to hit a bug, and it&rsquo;s impossible to interactively debug. also, today my figure captions stopped working.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The next day, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/MattCrump_/status/1016727443716149250\">Matt Crump said</a> (independently, I assume):</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>write the whole thing in #bookdown compiles seamlessly to webook, pdf epub. Easy editing compared to latex. All source code in a github repo</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Isn&rsquo;t that amusing?</p>\n\n<p>Half an hour after tj mahr complained, he realized it was because <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tjmahr/status/1016512045284577280\">he didn&rsquo;t follow the documentation</a>. So I&rsquo;d like to <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/11/thanks-tj-mahr/\">thank him again</a> for reading the documentation.</p>\n\n<p>Should you write the whole book in <strong>bookdown</strong>? Well, if you ask me, the answer is of course yes, and I&rsquo;m certainly biased as the author of this package. I have written three whole books in <strong>bookdown</strong>. The PDFs I generated from <strong>bookdown</strong> were directly ready for the publisher&rsquo;s printer (at least I didn&rsquo;t need to do anything else).</p>\n\n<p>I guess the moral is, if you want to use Feature X of a software package, you may ignore its documentation for the first time. If everything goes well, great. But when you run into problems, it is probably a good idea to read the relevant documentation.</p>\n\n<p>And don&rsquo;t panic.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/you-can-do-it.gif\" alt=\"You can do it\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/whole-bookdown/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/you-can-do-it.gif","linkMd5":"d831d9fcd09f13bcdbb2c4d1032ee949","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn71@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-209_f24dd66a489554ff.webp","destWidth":400,"destHeight":225,"sourceBytes":1031711,"destBytes":598588,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/you-can-do-it.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn71@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-209_f24dd66a489554ff.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Build Binary R Packages for the Homebrew Version of R?","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/homebrew-binary-r-packages/","description":"\n        \n\n<blockquote>\n<p><strong>Update on 2018-07-01</strong>: I have changed the scope of the repository <a href=\"https://macos.rbind.io\">https://macos.rbind.io</a>. Please ignore the post below, and see <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/cranextra-macos/\">this post</a> instead.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>One of the open source projects is not like the others. The project is called <a href=\"https://brew.sh\">Homebrew</a>. In my eyes, it is <em>the</em> most successful open source project in history in several aspects. I saw an interesting graph <a href=\"https://twitter.com/nayafia/status/1004526578175361024\">on Twitter</a> a few days ago, which perfectly characterized the amazing community nature of Homebrew:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DfDI76AUwAAHE8m.jpg\" alt=\"Magnet or Sticky?\" /></p>\n\n<p>Personally I don&rsquo;t really care about if a project is &ldquo;sticky&rdquo;, and I believe being magnetic is a lot more difficult and valuable than being sticky for an open source project. Okay, that is a little bit digression for this blog post.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"why-using-homebrew\">Why using Homebrew?</h2>\n\n<p>Do I need to repeat the merits of Homebrew? The key is automation. In most cases, you can install a system software package with one command, and it does not require <code>sudo</code>. Compare this with the traditional way of installing software on macOS: how many mouse clicks does it take for you to find, download, open, and install a package? No matter how inconvenient it was the last time you installed a package, the next time you will have to go through the painful process again if you want to upgrade a package. Worse, if you want to <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/25925752/559676\">uninstall a package</a> installed from a <code>*.pkg</code> installer, sometimes you may want to kill yourself, and Windows users will be able to laugh out loud at you, since it is so awkward to cleanly uninstall a package on macOS.</p>\n\n<p>Isn&rsquo;t Homebrew just <code>apt(-get)</code> on Debian/Ubuntu or <code>yum</code> on CentOS? Well, I believe Homebrew did one thing absolutely correctly: it does not require <code>sudo</code> (and everything is written to <code>/usr/local</code>). This is important and a perfect decision for personal computer users. Yes, <code>sudo</code> makes perfect sense for a system with multiple users (e.g., a Linux server), but I really don&rsquo;t like it in the case of a single user installing software on his/her own computer. Requiring <code>sudo</code> also makes me nervous: What exactly will the installer do? Does it write to <code>/usr</code> or <code>/opt</code> or elsewhere? I have no idea. I&rsquo;m just a dumb user of my own computer. I don&rsquo;t want to be a sysadmin, and I don&rsquo;t want to touch the directories to which I don&rsquo;t have write permission without inputting my password. If I touch them, I&rsquo;m likely to forget what I did someday.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"homebrew-and-r\">Homebrew and R</h2>\n\n<p>Thanks to many contributors to <a href=\"https://github.com/Homebrew/homebrew-core/blob/master/Formula/r.rb\">the R formula</a> in Homebrew, it is easy to install a pre-built binary package of R:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-sh\">brew install r\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>For developers, you may also want TeXinfo (for <code>R CMD check</code>) and XQuartz (for R packages like <strong>rgl</strong>):</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">brew install texinfo\nbrew tap homebrew/cask\nbrew cask install xquartz\n</code></pre>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-missing-cran-repository\">The missing CRAN repository</h2>\n\n<p>If you use the Homebrew version of R, it will be super easy to upgrade or remove R in the future. However, the biggest problem is the add-on R packages from CRAN. Currently you can only install those packages from source with the Homebrew version of R (because it is not officially supported by CRAN), i.e.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">install.packages(..., type = 'source')\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Actually, installing R packages from source may not be as bad as you thought. About 75% packages on CRAN do not need compilation, which means they are pure R packages, without C/C++/Fortran code. These packages can be easy and fast to install from source.</p>\n\n<p>For the rest of 25%, most don&rsquo;t require external system dependencies, so your Hombrew version of R should be able to compile them (you have Clang, and also <code>gfortran</code> from Homebrew&rsquo;s <code>gcc</code> formula). The only problem is some packages take long time to compile (e.g., <strong>stringi</strong>), so we may want to prebuild the binaries for them.</p>\n\n<p>The trickiest packages are those with (heavy) system dependencies, such as <strong>RGtk2</strong>, <strong>cairoDevice</strong>, and <strong>rJava</strong>. They are not too bad, either, because their dependencies typically exist as Homebrew formulas, e.g., you can <code>brew install gtk+</code> for <strong>RGtk2</strong>. The problems are: 1) you need to know the names of the formulas (you may look it up in the <a href=\"https://github.com/r-hub/sysreqsdb\">r-hub/sysreqsdb</a> repo), and 2) the R packages may also take a long time to compile.</p>\n\n<p>With the above background information, the problem of &ldquo;the missing CRAN repository of binary R packages for the Homebrew R&rdquo; is actually not too bad. We may not really need to build 12,000 binary R packages. Perhaps building only the top 30 trickiest and also frequently used R packages is enough for most R users on macOS.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"an-experiment-macos-rbind-io\">An experiment: macos.rbind.io</h2>\n\n<p>I have been hoping to experiment with this for a couple of years. Finally I finished <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown-book\">the R Markdown book</a>, which pretty much swallowed the first half of my 2018. Then I got a short break mainly because I started to run the reverse dependency check for the <strong>rmarkdown</strong> package. To those who are not R package developers, this basically means I have to run <code>R CMD check</code> on more than 2000 reverse dependencies of <strong>rmarkdown</strong>. This usually takes a couple of days. Every time I do this task, I need to figure out how to install a few tricky R packages (same pain comes when I check the <strong>knitr</strong> package).</p>\n\n<p>This time I just gave up and decided to take a stab at building some binary R packages against the Homebrew version of R, so that I won&rsquo;t need to worry about installing them again in the future. I used the <code>osx</code> environment for the first time on Travis, built some packages on Travis, and automatically deployed to <a href=\"https://macos.rbind.io\">https://macos.rbind.io</a>. So far so good, although I have to admit I&rsquo;m definitely not an expert in compiling packages from source. I just have some bare minimum knowledge.</p>\n\n<p>Currently the scripts for building the packages are in the Github repo <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/homebrew-r-packages\">yihui/homebrew-r-packages</a> (I may move it to a certain organization in the future if a wider community shows interest in it). The main script is <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/homebrew-r-packages/blob/master/build.R\">build.R</a>, which I believe is not too complicated.</p>\n\n<p>See <a href=\"https://macos.rbind.io\">https://macos.rbind.io</a> for detailed instructions on how to install these pre-built binary packages, as well as things that I need help with. You have been warned that this is only a quick experiment of mine over two days. Trust me: I&rsquo;m not an expert, I&rsquo;m not an expert, and I&rsquo;m not an expert (e.g., OpenMP? What&rsquo;s that?)! I&rsquo;ll be waiting for experts&rsquo; feedback and help!</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"appendix-hello-rgtk2-finally\">Appendix: Hello, RGtk2, Finally!</h2>\n\n<p>In January this year, I wrote down some notes <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/install-rgtk2-macos/\">on how to install <strong>RGtk2</strong> on macOS</a>. I&rsquo;m delighted to say that the post is obsolete now. All you need is:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">system('brew install gtk+')\ninstall.packages(\n  'RGtk2', type = 'mac.binary.el-capitan',\n  repos = 'https://macos.rbind.io'\n)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>No more hacking at the <code>gtk+</code> formula in Homebrew!</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/homebrew-binary-r-packages/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 14 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DfDI76AUwAAHE8m.jpg","linkMd5":"3149bcc9c7a8ba5bc7d871a3e89b9f36","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn83@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-34-168_82d84f4e43e19dfd.webp","destWidth":452,"destHeight":327,"sourceBytes":14638,"destBytes":7578,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DfDI76AUwAAHE8m.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn83@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-34-168_82d84f4e43e19dfd.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973509},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Ideally, I Hope to Simply Copy and Run Your Example","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/copy-and-run/","description":"<p>When you ask questions or file bug reports, you want to get answers and solutions as quickly as possible, right? If that is the case, I’d like to talk about a very common situation in questions and bug reports in this post. Basically, if I’m unable to answer the question due to lack of information or reproducible examples the first time I see it, I’m likely to skip it and won’t come back for the second time, unless I happen to be free and happy enough to <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/10/501st-reminder/\">remind a user for the 502nd time</a> about reproducible examples, or <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/11/12-questions/\">ask 12 questions</a> for complete information. I believe most users understand this, but every now and then, I receive “reproducible examples” in the form of verbal descriptions when a full example can speak for itself. Had a full example been provided, I could be able to simply copy and run it, and hopefully see the problem immediately, instead of following a number of steps from the verbal description to prepare the example by myself.</p> \n<p><strong>Words can be ambiguous. Code is precise.</strong></p> \n<p>That is why you should provide a fully reproducible example whenever possible, instead of describing all the steps to create such an example.</p> \n<p>I have seen this kind of issues many times in the past, and finally decided to write this post after I saw <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/issues/1562\">yihui/knitr#1562</a> yesterday.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:And-rstudio-blog\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/copy-and-run/#fn:And-rstudio-blog\">1</a></sup> Think about the work I had to do for this issue:</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p>For step 1, I had to copy it to an Rmd document in my RStudio session.</p></li> \n <li><p>For step 2, I had to copy the code and put it in an R code chunk.</p></li> \n <li><p>For step 3, I had to add more headers, and it was unclear how many “more headers” or what kind of headers I would need. I added two, but didn’t realize the problem for some reason.</p></li> \n <li><p>For step 4, I tried, but since step 3 was unclear to me, I didn’t know what I should be looking at.</p></li> \n</ul> \n<p>In terms of the structure, this is actually a pretty good bug report. It stated the steps to reproduce the problem, and the expected behavior. But I can’t simply copy and run an example. I just can’t.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/images/cant-do-it.jpg\" alt=\"I just can't\" /></p> \n<p>I asked for a full reproducible example, and finally realized that I should be looking at the two elements “title” and “title2” in the TOC. Within five seconds, I was able to reproducible the problem after I copied and ran the full example.</p> \n<p>BTW, when you discover bugs about something that you can <em>see</em> on your screen, screenshots can be highly useful. They can be strong evidence of bugs. Although it is not really what I think in my head, but when you file a bug report, you may imagine that the developer would deny it if you don’t show enough evidence. If you provide a relatively simple reproducible example and attach screenshots, there will be no way for me to escape but acknowledge the problem, which means a much higher chance to fix it.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:And-rstudio-blog\">And <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/issues/337\">rstudio/blogdown#337</a> is another example a couple of months later. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/copy-and-run/#fnref:And-rstudio-blog\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/copy-and-run/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 22 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/images/cant-do-it.jpg","linkMd5":"5015e3b52446e0501953454f1b0c185f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn97@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-34-033_a2c0ec3486007871.webp","destWidth":577,"destHeight":770,"sourceBytes":63747,"destBytes":41226,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/images/cant-do-it.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn97@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-34-033_a2c0ec3486007871.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On a Clear Night","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/on-a-clear-night/","description":"\n        <p>When I finished writing <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/bookdown/\">the <strong>bookdown</strong> book</a>, I started to think about what I should put on the dedication page. In the spirit of Markdown, the theme of these words should be &ldquo;simplicity&rdquo;. Eventually I selected an ancient Chinese poem and a Buddhist&rsquo;s chant. You won&rsquo;t see them in the HTML version of the book. They only appeared in <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/bookdown/bookdown.pdf\">the PDF</a> and printed versions.</p>\n\n<p>The reason that they came to my mind again is that I met <a href=\"https://www.linkedin.com/in/kylemonahan/\">Kyle Monahan</a> and his partner at rstudio::conf(2020). To my surprise, Kyle was also interested in Chinese poems. When he asked me for recommendations on (simple and beautiful) Chinese poems, I thought of Shao Yong&rsquo;s <a href=\"https://yihui.org/cn/2014/06/on-writing/\">清夜吟</a> (On a Clear Night):</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>月到天⼼处，风来⽔面时。<br />\n⼀般清意味，料得少⼈知。</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>which I tried to translate to English as:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>The moon rises to the center of the night,<br />\nAnd the breeze touches the surface of the pond.<br />\nThis feeling, unremarkable yet clear and quiet,<br />\nFew of us have found.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>There isn&rsquo;t anything remarkable about the moon, night, breeze, or pond. However, if you are a careful observer, you will definitely feel the joy of standing in the middle of a quiet night and appreciate the beauty of these simple scenes. You don&rsquo;t have to travel five hundred miles to a scenic spot to enjoy the beauty of nature. There may well be enough beauty right in front of your eyes, which you&rsquo;d easily miss if you cannot control your desire.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/on-a-clear-night/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 11 Feb 2020 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"fcc7d18f1c3ac73de25d38edf99c5200","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973438},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Let MiKTeX Install Missing LaTeX Packages Automatically","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/miktex-auto-install/","description":"\n        <p>While debugging the <strong>rmarkdown</strong> issue <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/1285\">#1285</a>, I discovered that MiKTeX might fail to install missing LaTeX packages in certain cases. The default behavior of MiKTeX is to ask you whether you want to install missing LaTeX packages, but it may fail to pop up the dialog box to ask you in the first place. I&rsquo;m still not sure why it could fail (I feel when MiKTeX is installed by the system admin, it may fail to pop up the dialog), but you can change the default setting and let MiKTeX install the missing LaTeX packages without asking. In the MiKTeX Console (you can find it from the Start menu on Windows), choose &ldquo;Always install missing packages on-the-fly&rdquo;:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/37617602-9e2ffe38-2b81-11e8-9e98-0853a08a4413.png\" alt=\"Always install missing packages on-the-fly\" /></p>\n\n<p>From the viewpoint of the developer, it is absolutely the right thing to do to ask users before installing the missing LaTeX packages. However, from the viewpoint of users, I guess 99.99% of users will agree to install the missing packages. What would you do otherwise in this case? Just stare at the screen, let LaTeX fail, and feel the miserable life? I don&rsquo;t think you want to do that. Convenience outweighs the potential risk of letting certain software install (random) packages automatically in this case.</p>\n\n<p>The <strong>rmarkdown</strong> bug #1285 once again confirmed that <a href=\"https://yihui.org/tinytex/\">TinyTeX</a> could be a better option than MiKTeX on Windows. Although it was not MiKTeX&rsquo;s fault in this case, MiKTeX&rsquo;s <code>pdflatex</code> fails when called via <code>system2('pdflatex', 'test.tex', stdout = FALSE)</code> in the R console in RStudio, even though <code>system2('pdflatex', 'test.tex')</code> succeeds, which is <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rstudio/issues/2446\">a really weird bug</a> that took me a long time to realize. By comparison, TinyTeX&rsquo;s <code>pdflatex</code> works just fine.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/miktex-auto-install/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 20 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/37617602-9e2ffe38-2b81-11e8-9e98-0853a08a4413.png","linkMd5":"ee756ada0bf81ecedb9e8473616aee5b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn64@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-33-782_b628896254cfd12d.webp","destWidth":848,"destHeight":722,"sourceBytes":153631,"destBytes":32340,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/37617602-9e2ffe38-2b81-11e8-9e98-0853a08a4413.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn64@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-33-782_b628896254cfd12d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973463},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"English is Still Hard for Me, and Thoughts on (Computer) Language Wars","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/","description":"<p>My native language is Chinese (Mandarin). Although I started learning English in middle school 22 years ago, and even have been in the US for more than 9 years, I still struggle with English as a foreign language on a daily basis. I’m only slightly content with my English writing. For reading, speaking, and listening, they are all miserable. In particular, my listening sucks. For example, when someone tells a joke in a conversation or talk and everyone laughs, I often have to smile awkwardly like an idiot, not knowing what exactly the joke was.</p> \n<p>Those who know me may think I’m being modest: “Come on, Yihui, you have written four books and given numerous talks in English! How could you possibly struggle with English?”</p> \n<h2 id=\"some-of-my-struggles-with-english\">Some of my struggles with English</h2> \n<p>You see my four books, but you don’t know that I used the dictionary for thousands of times. I use the dictionary when writing anything in English, including books, papers, software documentation, blog posts, Github replies, and emails.</p> \n<p>I read books almost every day, but I rarely read books in English. If I hear a good book in English, I’ll find its translation in Chinese (e.g., “Poor Charlie’s Almanack”, “Deep Work”, and “Principles”). My English reading speed is too slow, not to mention the trouble of constant pause to look up words in the dictionary. I guess my vocabulary is no larger than a 3rd grader of native English speakers. Even when I read books for toddlers, I often find words that I don’t know. In the adult’s world, some English words are just so confusing and I can never remember what they mean. Today I just saw <a href=\"https://twitter.com/Julie_B92/status/1058330883927797761\">the example of “bi-weekly” vs “biannual”</a>. There are too many hills for me to die on…</p> \n<p>For speaking, my biggest headaches are the pronunciation and my limited vocabulary. Let me give you four (funny) examples of my pronunciation problems:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>During my PhD defense in 2013, I said “paradigm” multiple times and pronounced it as “para-dig-m”. Yes, I said “dig”. I guess it didn’t matter, though, since I passed the defense anyway. It was quite some time later that I heard someone else said “paradigm” and realized my pronunciation had been wrong for years. I hate those silent letters in English. Tom<em>b</em>. I<em>s</em>land. Gra<em>h</em>am. Dam<em>n</em>. BTW, there is a Durham building at Iowa State, and most new Chinese students pronounce it incorrectly.</p></li> \n <li><p>I went to LA in 2015 and <a href=\"http://datascience.la/yihui-xie-presents-html-widgets/\">gave a talk</a> there on HTML widgets after <a href=\"http://datascience.la/joe-cheng-presents-shiny/\">Joe Cheng’s talk</a> on Shiny. It was in Joe’s talk that I learned for the first time in my life that I had pronounced the word “verbatim” incorrectly for years (I learned the word from LaTeX by reading). I thought “ba” in “verbatim” should obviously be as in “bat”, but Joe’s pronunciation clearly told me it should be as in “bake”. I could hear my inner world collapsed at that moment.</p></li> \n <li><p>One evening in September this year, as I watched my 3-year old playing in a park, I listened to Nick Tierney and Saskia Freytag’s podcast on <a href=\"https://soundcloud.com/crediblycurious/sweave-knitr-and-rmarkdown\">Sweave, knitr, and rmarkdown</a>. It struck me when Nick mentioned my book “R Markdown: The Definitive Guide”. Again, for the first time of my life, I realized I had pronounced “definitive” incorrectly for years. I thought it was similar to “definite”. I had used the wrong pronunciation in the promotion video of my upcoming <a href=\"https://www.rstudio.com/conference/\">R Markdown workshop</a> before I listened to Nick’s podcast. Damn it. They might doubt if I’m really the author of the book.</p></li> \n <li><p>Last weekend my neighbor’s son came to my backyard to play with my son. His parents followed him over. I was frying some sesame balls outside.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Cooked-sweet-pot\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fn:Cooked-sweet-pot\">1</a></sup> I invited them to taste the balls and told them these were “see-same” balls. I’m not sure if they really heard me, but after I returned to my pot to fry the rest of balls, I overheard the wife told her husband that those were “/ˈsɛsəmiː/” balls. Wait? What is “se-sa-me”?!… What was worse was that when I came inside, looked it up in the dictionary, and realized that I had not only screwed up the pronunciation for ten years, but also misspelled it as “seesame” (which explains my wrong pronunciation).</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>I don’t really worry about the problem of pronunciation or even accent. I’m sure people can understand me in most cases anyway. The other problem, the limited vocabulary, is a substantial trouble in speaking. When I give a talk, I have to start a background process in my brain to find the proper words, and this parallel process may or may not succeed. During a talk, you cannot pause in the middle of a sentence, ask your audience to wait for a moment, and use a dictionary. In writing, it will be much easier since there is no requirement of immediacy.</p> \n<p>For listening, I have to blame myself for being too shy to talk to native speakers more often or ask them to repeat when I don’t get it. I can only deal with technical conversations. For conversations on other topics, sometimes I can understand 30% to 50%, and sometimes I’m basically deaf, especially when the environment is noisy, such as in a bar or when there is live music around. I feel most American restaurants are just too loud for me, so I rarely feel comfortable dining in those restaurants.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:My-listening-is\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fn:My-listening-is\">2</a></sup> In a group conversation, I also feel awkward to interrupt others when I don’t understand certain things due to language problems (like, “Excuse me, could you repeat your joke and say it more slowly?”).</p> \n<p>By sharing my experience above, I mean it can be surprisingly hard to master a foreign language (at least for me).</p> \n<h2 id=\"why-wars-on-computer-languages-or-tools-are-a-waste-of-time\">Why wars on computer languages or tools are a waste of time</h2> \n<p>First things first: Don’t <a href=\"https://news.ycombinator.com/item?id=18319717\">ever</a>, <a href=\"https://news.ycombinator.com/item?id=18336202\">ever</a>, <a href=\"https://news.ycombinator.com/item?id=18342415\">ever</a> read comments about computer languages or tools on Hacker News. Leave them alone, and let them waste their own time there. Or actually, just don’t read any comments on any topics on Hacker News.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:There-is-certain\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fn:There-is-certain\">3</a></sup></p> \n<p>Why are some people so obsessed with computer language wars? Why don’t people also argue to death on human languages? My theories are:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Most people only have one native language. They don’t know enough about foreign languages. When they learn a foreign language, they know attacking this language will make no sense even if they see all kinds of unexpected rules compared to their native languages. For example, from the point of view of Chinese speakers, none of plural nouns (hence countable/uncountable nouns), the subject-verb agreement, or tenses of verbs in English make practical sense. Surprises to one language speaker may feel fairly natural to another language speaker who has learned all these “surprises” since he/she was one year old.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:For-example-just\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fn:For-example-just\">4</a></sup> When talking about computer languages, people seem to forget this. They become snarky about surprises in other languages, even if they haven’t really spent any substantial time on learning the foreign computer languages.</p> <p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/wrong-usage.gif\" alt=\"Some people don't bother learning a language or tool seriously\" /></p> <p>I don’t mean you should spend 22 years on a computer language before you are qualified to comment on its disadvantages, but I won’t listen to arguments from someone who hasn’t learned a language for more than half a year. I guess if someone hates a language, he won’t even bother learning it seriously, so language wars tend to be shallow.</p></li> \n <li><p>Native languages of humans are learned when humans are still little babies and don’t have a choice. Computer languages are learned much later, and often consciously chosen by the learners. To some degree, you may feel insulted when someone criticizes or insults your computer language, because it implies you made a stupid choice of an inferior language, which often urges you to defend your wisdom. By comparison, if someone insults a human language, I believe everyone will agree that it is sheer discrimination, because we were pretty much born with our native languages.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>To sum it up, people are reluctant to learn a different language seriously, so surprises in other languages will stay in their mind and be denied forever. Computer languages also tend to become a part of people’s identities, which can be hard to deprive.</p> \n<p>There may also be people who lack the sense of inner security, and they seek confidence by looking down upon other people’s choices of languages.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Mathematicians-s\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fn:Mathematicians-s\">5</a></sup> This is apparently the wrong way to go, and unfortunately, it triggers endless wars, too.</p> \n<h2 id=\"keep-your-identity-small\">Keep your identity small</h2> \n<p>Paul Graham wrote an essay “<a href=\"http://www.paulgraham.com/identity.html\">Keep your identity small</a>” in 2009. I found it to be a piece of good advice.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Well-I-confess-t\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fn:Well-I-confess-t\">6</a></sup> If you love a language, don’t turn it into a religion, but just show your love by good work with the language. I love my native language, so I blog extensively in Chinese, even though I feel Chinese people have been losing confidence in the Chinese language mainly due to the dominance of English (and endless English exams!). English makes me struggle, but I write frequently in English anyway. Of course, speaking of computer languages, I like R, and also find JavaScript a lot of fun. In early years, I used to scorn and make fun of SAS and MS Office (<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2009/09/how-to-import-ms-excel-data-into-r/\">example</a>), but I have stopped it in recent years because it is absolutely not wise,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:If-you-read-one\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fn:If-you-read-one\">7</a></sup> and I have gained enough confidence in R (as well as Pandoc’s Markdown).</p> \n<p>Sometimes I’m surprised that even <a href=\"https://twitter.com/ivanov/status/243244721034637312\">leaders and experts</a> of extremely popular languages still need <a href=\"https://twitter.com/wesmckinn/status/326771315170230273\">endorsements about languages</a> from higher-level experts. That probably explains why ordinary users fight even harder for the languages of their own choice. Few people seem to have enough inner peace to let them ignore comments or comparisons on the languages they use and love. \n <!--\nOnly ugly languages become popular. Python is the one exception. -- Donald Knuth\n--></p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>If we were to wake up some morning and find that everyone was the same race, creed and color, we would find some other cause for prejudice by noon.</p> \n <p>— George Aiken</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>In the end, to those who are still interested in language wars and believe languages and software are an essential part of your identities, I’d highly recommend a novel, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/And_Quiet_Flows_the_Don\"><em>And Quiet Flows the Don</em></a>, to help you get rid of your identities. It is an epic novel and took me quite a few months to finish in 2014. Among all the novels I have read, this one has had the biggest impact on my view of the world. Who are your real enemies? What have you been fighting for? A dream, or a label?</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Cooked-sweet-pot\">Cooked sweet potato mixed with rice flour, and coated with sesame—in case you are interested in the recipe. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fnref:Cooked-sweet-pot\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:My-listening-is\">My listening is one problem. The other problem is that I feel I have to keep shouting when talking to other people, which is quite a stress to my throat. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fnref:My-listening-is\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:There-is-certain\">There is certainly useful information, but overall I feel it is even worse than Twitter (disorganized and rage-driven). <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fnref:There-is-certain\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:For-example-just\">For example, just tell me why the plural of “cow” is “cows”, but the plural of “sheep” is still “sheep” instead of “sheeps”. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fnref:For-example-just\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Mathematicians-s\">Mathematicians stand on each others’ shoulders and computer scientists stand on each others’ toes. — Richard Hamming <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fnref:Mathematicians-s\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Well-I-confess-t\">Well, I confess that I learned this article from the Hacker News mailing list, but I’ve been considering unsubscribing the list recently, because I feel even the quality of the weekly newsletters has been dropping while the length is going up . <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fnref:Well-I-confess-t\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:If-you-read-one\">If you read <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/\">one of my previous posts</a> carefully enough, you may have seen that I actually praised Excel in Footnote #2. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/#fnref:If-you-read-one\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 02 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/wrong-usage.gif","linkMd5":"bb513b9a522e3df5a0afd84ba4053145","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn64@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-36-798_16433f7ad8a1436e.webp","destWidth":500,"destHeight":281,"sourceBytes":546509,"destBytes":379050,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/wrong-usage.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn64@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-36-798_16433f7ad8a1436e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973445},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The First Notebook War","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/","description":"<p>Last week I learned about an interesting JupyterCon talk given by Joel Grus titled “<a href=\"https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1n2RlMdmv1p25Xy5thJUhkKGvjtV-dkAIsUXP-AL4ffI/preview#slide=id.g362da58057_0_1\">I Don’t Like Notebooks</a>”. I’d applaud the open-mindedness of the conference committee to invite Joel to give such a talk to (presumably so many) notebook lovers. I agree with Joel that criticizing a popular tool is not “an unhelpful way to spend time” (see slide #4). I wish there were more criticism to popular tools in the R world, and personally I’d welcome criticism and complaints about my own work.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:It-doesn-t-mean\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:It-doesn-t-mean\">1</a></sup></p> \n<p>Joel’s talk triggered a lot of discussions on Twitter (come on, people, let’s get off Twitter whenever there is something worth a deep discussion). For example, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hspter/status/1034511489632067584\">Hilary Parker doesn’t like notebooks</a>, either. Philip Guo <a href=\"https://twitter.com/pgbovine/status/1034626381735317504\">declared</a> that the summer 2018 ended with “the first great notebook war”. “A war between whom and whom?” You may ask. I guess it was a war between notebooks and editors/IDEs.</p> \n<p>Before I start this post, I want to mention that I know little about Python (I have written one short Python script in total in my life), and I don’t use Jupyter myself. In fact, I also rarely use any notebooks, including R Markdown notebooks. That is mainly because I’m a software developer (engineer). For this reason, I think I can understand Joel’s complaints about notebooks pretty well. My main point is that if you use notebooks for <em>software engineering</em>, you are probably using the wrong tool, no matter how popular it is. I’m not sure if Joel would agree with me, but if I were to give this talk, that would be my main message to the audience.</p> \n<p>While reading Joel’s critiques on Jupyter notebooks, I couldn’t help thinking whether they apply to R Markdown notebooks, or R Markdown documents in general, so I’ll mention how some of the problems have been addressed in the R Markdown ecosystem in this post, too.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-two-cultures-the-r-vs-python-culture-or-data-analysis-vs-software-engineering-culture\">The two cultures: the R vs Python culture, or data analysis vs software engineering culture</h2> \n<p>I feel a major difference between the R culture and Python culture is that Python users seem to <em>create</em> code more often, whereas R users often <em>use</em> code. There seems to be a strong atmosphere of software engineering in the Python world: in the beginning was the custom <em>class</em> (with methods). For R users, in the beginning was the <em>data</em>.</p> \n<p>I think notebooks are more suitable for a world where the data analysis culture is stronger than the engineering culture. Joel insists that even if you are only experimenting or prototyping, you should follow good software engineering rules (slides #46-49). I tend to disagree, because prototyping is prototyping, and engineering is engineering. Good software engineering is important, but I don’t think it is necessary to write unit tests or factor out code at the prototyping stage. It is fine to do these things later, but again, I agree with Joel that if you are going to develop software seriously later, you’d better leave the notebook and use a real editor or IDE instead (e.g., to write reusable modules or packages).</p> \n<p>How about doing data analysis in notebooks? Is “good software engineering” relevant? I’d argue that it is not highly relevant, and it is fine to use notebooks. Analyzing data and developing software are different in several aspects. The latter is meant to create generally useful and reusable products, but the former is often not generalizable—you only analyze a specific dataset, you want to draw conclusions from the specific data, and you may not be interested in or have the time to make your code reusable by other people (or it may not be possible). When the analysis is done, it is done (pretty much). Of course, if you happen to discover any parts that could be potentially reusable and useful, you could factor them out into a package, but I think this should be relatively rare—how many packages like <a href=\"https://dplyr.tidyverse.org\"><strong>dplyr</strong></a> have been abstracted or distilled from the process of data analysis? Not many.</p> \n<p>How would you write unit tests for data analysis? I feel it will be both tricky and unnecessary. For a function/method, if you defined it, you know what its expected output should be. For data, you often don’t know what exactly to expect in the output. For example, when you subset a dataset, how do you know the result is correct?</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">mtcars2 = dplyr::filter(mtcars, hp &gt; 100)\n</code></pre> \n<p>That is probably not something you, as a data analyst, need to worry about. It is the responsibility of the package author (the software engineer) to write enough unit tests in the package that you are using.</p> \n<p>On the other hand, data analysts often do tests in an informal way, too. As they explore the data, they may draw plots or create summary tables, in which they may be able to discover problems (e.g., wrong categories, outliers, and so on). Notebooks are great for these inline output elements, from which you can make quick discoveries.</p> \n<h2 id=\"what-do-notebooks-and-spreadsheets-have-in-common\">What do notebooks and spreadsheets have in common?</h2> \n<p>Or ask it another way, why are notebooks so popular? My answer is that they make the (typically boring and abstract) program code much more <em>tangible</em>. You can see the output of each snippet of source code right below the snippet of source code. Although Joel’s #1 complaint about notebooks is that it is hard to reason about the output due to the possible out-of-order execution of cells, I think being able to see the output inside the source document can actually help you reason about the code better. Yes, you can use the traditional way of running code line by line, and see the output in the console, but the distance between source and output is much longer. Imagine you create ten plots in ten snippets of code, it will be quite messy to have ten separate plot windows floating around on your screen, and it is hard to know which piece of code created which plot. It will be worse if a plot doesn’t have a title, in which case you may not even remember what story a particular plot is supposed to tell.</p> \n<p>I think notebooks are popular for the same reason that explains the popularity of spreadsheets such as Excel. I haven’t met a single software engineer who loves Excel. <a href=\"https://twitter.com/brookLYNevery1/status/1037462991015432195\">Everyone hates it and makes fun of it</a>, but why do so many users still use it? Again, Excel makes things tangible. You can touch the data (although it is usually a very bad idea), and draw graphics in a sheet that contains the source data (bad idea again). It makes you feel everything is well under your control: oh here is my data, and here is a graph next to it; oh I should use that column to draw the graph instead, so let me change it and I can see the updated graph immediately.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:I-have-to-say-th\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:I-have-to-say-th\">2</a></sup> You can do everything in a single place, and the short distance between the source (data) and the output is ace.</p> \n<p>Excel makes things tangible at the price of making things messy (e.g., it may contain manually edited data that is hard to keep track of, or merged cells or graphs that make it hard for other software to read the data). By comparison, although notebooks can mess up the state, but that is only an intermediate problem. At its core, it is still relatively clean and encourages the reproducibility principle, i.e., you shall use code to generate results automatically instead of manually copying and pasting results in your report. If you are concerned about the state, you can restart the session and recompile the whole notebook from scratch. Spreadsheets are often hopeless here—you cannot easily restart your brain and redo exactly the same things.</p> \n<h2 id=\"things-joel-likes-about-notebooks\">Things Joel likes about notebooks</h2> \n<p>Joel mentioned two things he liked about notebooks: well-documented code (and the idea of mixing Markdown and code) and inline plots. I want to expand the first thing a bit. The idea should be attributed to literate programming, a <em>programming</em> paradigm invented by Donald Knuth. Knuth probably didn’t expect its popularity in tools for data analysis decades later, but I absolutely love the idea of writing narratives in a specialized authoring language (such as Markdown or LaTeX) instead of the traditional code comments. Using an authoring language makes the narratives easier to read. By comparison, code comments are always boring plain text with no structure or rich-text elements. Literate programming is more suitable in the data analysis culture.</p> \n<h2 id=\"joel-s-complaints-about-notebooks\">Joel’s complaints about notebooks</h2> \n<p>If I have read Joel’s slides carefully enough and counted it correctly, he mentioned the following 11 problems about Jupyter notebooks.</p> \n<h3 id=\"1-hidden-state-and-out-of-order-execution\">1. Hidden state and out-of-order execution</h3> \n<p>I agree when this happens, the output can be highly confusing, but I think the biggest issue brought forward from his arguments against notebooks is the lack of mentioning the serious problem of hidden state in all those “notable tutorials”, “definitive guide”, and “comprehensive beginner’s guide”. I care a lot about reproducibility, so does RStudio, and we have made a decision on Day One that R Markdown documents should be compiled in new R sessions, which will (mostly) get rid of the problems of hidden state and out-of-order execution. Although many users have complained about it and wanted to run R Markdown documents in the current R session instead, we have never changed the decision. After we created R Markdown notebooks, we have also been constantly alerting users about the potential problems of notebooks. For example, in our book “R Markdown: The Definitive Guide”, <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/compile.html\">we emphasized this issue</a> over and over again:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>[…] Again, for the sake of reproducibility, you will need to compile the whole document eventually in a clean environment.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Joel admitted that “the ipython console has plenty of hidden state, too” (slide #26) but he “can scroll back and see the command history to reason about the state” (slide #27). Jupyter provided the <code>%history</code> magic to print the command history, and Joel thought it was too much hassle and counterintuitive. I want to mention that this isn’t a problem for R Markdown notebooks. When you run cells (we call them “code chunks” in the R world), you can always see the history in the “History” pane in RStudio, e.g., I ran the second cell before the first one, and you can tell it from the right pane below:</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/45166263-379ffd00-b1bc-11e8-9ade-ceeaeef7b594.png\" alt=\"Command history of R Markdown notebooks in RStudio\" /></p> \n<p>Anyway, I think as long as Jupyter users are educated well enough to develop the habit of recompiling the whole notebook from scratch in the end instead of just leaving the results from out-of-order execution as the final product, Jupyter is probably fine.</p> \n<h3 id=\"2-notebooks-are-difficult-for-beginners\">2. Notebooks are difficult for beginners</h3> \n<p>This (slides #33-43) is essentially a corollary of the above hidden state issue. Joel suggested that the (beginner’s) tutorials scream out loud “DON’T RUN YOUR CELLS OUT OF ORDER YOU FOOL”. I can see the point, but honestly this is not strictly true. Some cells may be independent and do not introduce (hidden) state, and they could be run in arbitrary order, which is sometimes a benefit (you can work on a small part of the notebook a time). Allowing users to run cells in an arbitrary order doesn’t necessarily mean giving them enough rope to hang themselves. Even when you script in a text editor or IDE, I don’t believe you always run the code in the linear order from beginning to end—you may still need to focus on or iterate certain parts from time to time.</p> \n<h3 id=\"3-notebooks-encourage-bad-habits\">3. Notebooks encourage bad habits</h3> \n<p>Joel mentioned three bad habits encouraged by notebooks:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Unnamed notebooks like <code>Untitled24.ipynb</code>, <code>Untitled25.ipynb</code>, etc. I don’t believe this problem is specific to Jupyter notebooks. Everyone must have a bunch of these files (<code>Untitled.docx</code>, <code>Untitled.pptx</code>, <code>Untitled23.R</code>, <code>Untitled5.Rmd</code>, …). Sometimes it is just that the file is not worth saving, and sometimes the user is just <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hadleywickham/status/959806329693941760\">too busy</a> or lazy to name a file, which is completely understandable.</p></li> \n <li><p>Notebooks don’t encourage users to follow good software engineering rules. I tend to agree with the person (whose name was blacked out on slide #46) that data science is not about creating software. However, Joel’s “data science” might involve more “creating software” than others. After all, who knows what “data science” really means…<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:I-only-know-that\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:I-only-know-that\">3</a></sup> Of course, if your data science is about creating software, you should definitely follow software engineering rules.</p></li> \n <li><p>Notebooks encourage users to import notebooks instead of writing modules or libraries. Joel’s complaint here (slides #50-53) makes perfect sense to me.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Joel-mainly-crit\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:Joel-mainly-crit\">4</a></sup> If you want to import code, import code (instead of a notebook). That’s it.</p> <p>Importing a notebook in another is not necessarily a totally bad idea, if you actually import the whole thing (not only the code but also text). This feature is known as “<a href=\"https://yihui.org/knitr/demo/child/\">child documents</a>” in the R Markdown world (actually in the broader <strong>knitr</strong> world). I think this is a very useful feature, because you can break a giant notebook into smaller ones, on which you can work individually. In the end, you can compile the master notebook, which will run the child notebooks.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<h3 id=\"4-notebooks-discourage-modularity-and-testing\">4. Notebooks discourage modularity and testing</h3> \n<p>As I said earlier, if you use notebooks to develop software, you are probably using the wrong tool.</p> \n<h3 id=\"5-jupyter-s-autocomplete-linting-and-way-of-looking-up-the-help-are-awkward\">5. Jupyter’s autocomplete, linting, and way of looking up the help are awkward</h3> \n<p>Again, don’t use notebooks to develop software. However, autocomplete and linting are also very helpful even when you are not developing software. I cannot say anything for Jupyter, but for R Markdown notebooks, you get autocomplete and linting “for free” from the RStudio IDE, e.g.,</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/45172761-e7c93200-b1cb-11e8-83ad-b19be396526b.png\" alt=\"Variable defined but not used\" /></p> \n<p>For the help info of a function, you will see the hint as you type the function name. And when you type the parentheses, you will see hints about arguments, too. To see the full help page, you can press F1.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/45173275-4fcc4800-b1cd-11e8-8f83-124ce7fac529.png\" alt=\"Looking up the help info of a function in RStudio\" /></p> \n<p>RStudio doesn’t care whether you are writing a pure R script or an R Markdown document. Most tools for helping you write code work anywhere. I know it is unfair to compare Jupyter with an fully functional IDE, but using an IDE gives you access to many features that a notebook alone cannot easily provide.</p> \n<h3 id=\"6-notebooks-encourage-bad-processes\">6. Notebooks encourage bad processes</h3> \n<p>Joel thinks it is an extremely bad idea for two notebooks to share state. I agree that sharing state can make things complicated (and introduce potential security risks), but Joel didn’t answer the question on slide #75: if the data is huge, reading it once in one notebook and letting another notebook have access to it may be a good idea (or perhaps the only way to go, given the memory limit or time constraint). I think this is fine as long as the user knows clearly what he/she is doing.</p> \n<p>Note that when we run scripts in an editor environment, we usually also use a shared process when different scripts are executed interactively in the same console. This is not necessarily bad when doing exploratory data analysis.</p> \n<h3 id=\"7-notebooks-hinder-reproducible-extensible-science\">7. Notebooks hinder reproducible + extensible science</h3> \n<p>Joel quoted another person on slide #78 that “poorly factored code is bad science”. Again, it sounds like he was talking more on the software engineering side. I have no doubt that notebooks are not a great medium to distribute reusable and extensible code.</p> \n<p>But… the particular example (the Github repo using neural network to generate music) he used is not very convincing to me. Some problems in the example (slide #84) are completely irrelevant to using notebooks, such as the hard-coded paths, hard-coded parameters, and lack of comments. These problems are very common outside the notebook world, too. As you gain more experience in software engineering, you will surely know that hard-coded paths and parameters are very bad, and you shall comment your code, but these things really take time to learn. The mindset of writing code that can be easily used by others is even harder to obtain. Practically, the authors may not just have the time and only want to get things done for themselves. I don’t believe it is the notebook that <em>encouraged</em> these poor practices. On a side note, I actually think notebooks encourage commenting, but it cannot force the user to write enough narratives about the code.</p> \n<p>In my opinion, the valid points in his arguments are:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>If you use notebooks as the only medium to distribute software, you will force other people (who want to reuse your code) to use notebooks, too. This is pretty bad.</p></li> \n <li><p>Jupyter doesn’t offer a way to manage requirements (<code>requirements.txt</code>).</p> <p>I have a question, though. Should it be the responsibility of the notebook tool (such as Jupyter) to manage requirements? Not necessarily. There may be third-party solutions. I don’t know enough about Python (maybe <code>virtualenv</code> or conda environments?). In R, there are multiple solutions, one of which is <a href=\"https://rstudio.github.io/packrat/\"><strong>packrat</strong></a>. As you might have guessed, RStudio has integration with <strong>packrat</strong>, which works for any RStudio projects (the project doesn’t have to contain notebooks). Again, this is something an IDE can give you for free but may be too much for a notebook tool to offer.</p> <p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/45180674-af345300-b1e1-11e8-8199-9c81fe0ec64d.png\" alt=\"Enable packrat in an RStudio project\" /></p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Except for these two points, I wouldn’t attribute the problems Joel mentioned to notebooks. If there is anything to blame, I guess we have to blame the popularity of notebooks—so popular that people even use them to write software (perhaps they are just victims of the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_the_instrument\">law of the hammer?</a>).</p> \n<h3 id=\"8-notebooks-make-it-hard-to-copy-and-paste-into-slack-github-issues\">8. Notebooks make it hard to copy and paste into Slack/Github issues</h3> \n<p>The main lesson I learned from this part of Joel’s slides (#91-102) is that users often don’t know how to create <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/09/the-minimal-reprex-paradox/\"><em>minimal</em> reproducible examples</a>. If you know how to minimize an example, you can probably completely get rid of the notebook and provide pure code when asking questions (unless your problem is actually caused by the notebook itself). Debugging code in a notebook is certainly less pleasant than debugging pure code.</p> \n<p>On slide #102, I’m curious since Joel doesn’t like notebooks, why did he use the notebook format himself to file <a href=\"https://github.com/chimpler/pyhocon/issues/171\">the Github issue</a>? Did he just file the issue for someone else who passed a notebook to him? I didn’t get the point of this slide (maybe I should watch the video recording if it exists somewhere).</p> \n<p>Anyway, Jupyter notebooks are not easy to copy and paste because they are not plain text. Well, they are, but they are essentially JSON and feel more like Word (essentially XML) instead of the plain text in the sense of <code>.txt</code>. By comparison, R Markdown documents are true plain text (in the sense of <code>.txt</code>), so you can easily <code>Ctrl + C</code> and <code>Ctrl + V</code> (or <code>Command + C/V</code>).</p> \n<h3 id=\"9-errors-will-always-halt-execution\">9. Errors will always halt execution</h3> \n<p>…unless you install a certain notebook extension. The installation seems to be too much work for Joel and users with whom he wants to share the notebook.</p> \n<p>In R Markdown, you just set the chunk option <code>error = TRUE</code>, and errors will be shown in the output without halting the process. This is very convenient and useful for people who write tutorials to demonstrate errors on purpose. For example:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, error=TRUE}\n1 + \"a\"\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>Then in the output document you will see the error message:</p> \n<pre><code>Error in 1 + \"a\" : non-numeric argument to binary operator\n</code></pre> \n<p>By default (<code>error = FALSE</code>), errors will halt the R process.</p> \n<h3 id=\"10-notebooks-make-it-easy-to-teach-poorly\">10. Notebooks make it easy to teach poorly</h3> \n<p>I feel the problem is more about “the notable Jupyter tutorials are bad” than “notebooks are bad for tutorials”. Joel is strongly against the “Ctrl-Enter” type of tutorials. He didn’t mention what type of tutorials were good in the slides, but I guess besides the interactive way of running code in notebooks, there are pretty much only two other types of tutorials:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Code that you can only stare at. A typical example is hardcopies of books. You cannot easily copy code from paper.</p></li> \n <li><p>Code that you could copy and run by yourself, but you are strongly discouraged to do so. Instead, you are recommended to type out the code by yourself. That is what is advocated by the “<a href=\"https://learnpythonthehardway.org/python3/intro.html\">Learn X (Language) The Hard Way</a>” series of books.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Yes, notebooks can make the learner lazy (code is given and ready to run), and other types of tutorials force the learner to be diligent. My question is, is it definitely a good thing for everyone to type out the code line by line when learning a programming language? Honestly I have learned a great deal from “copying and pasting from Stack Overflow” (I don’t mean <a href=\"https://www.quora.com/Does-reading-Copying-and-Pasting-from-Stack-Overflow-make-you-a-better-developer\">that book</a>; I mean I do copy and paste code from Stack Overflow from time to time). Of course, I don’t blindly copy code. I only copy code after I read and fully understand it.</p> \n<p>In my opinion, notebooks can save learners time (which may or may not affect the quality of learning, depending on whether the code is worth typing). One unique advantage of notebooks for learning is that learners can modify the code and run it again. This is a great way of learning—<a href=\"https://twitter.com/ThePracticalDev/status/720257210161311744/\">changing stuff and seeing what happens</a>.</p> \n<h3 id=\"11-notebooks-make-it-hard-to-teach-well\">11. Notebooks make it hard to teach well</h3> \n<p>By “hard to teach well”, Joel meant Jupyter notebooks make it hard for him to write two types of code:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Code that is split across multiple cells, and each cell contains an incomplete snippet (slides #114-123).</p></li> \n <li><p>Code that is not meant to be actually executed (slide #124).</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>For the second problem, if you use R Markdown, the chunk option <code>eval = FALSE</code> is all you need, e.g.,</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, eval=FALSE}\nstopwords_list = c(\"a\", \"an\", \"at\", hundreds_of_other_words, \"yet\", \"you\")\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>The three existing solutions to the first problem in Jupyter are all awkward: <code>%%add_to dog</code>, <code>class MyClass(MyClass)</code>, and <code>Table.update = update</code>. Again, this is not a problem in the <strong>knitr</strong>/R Markdown world. You can write arbitrary incomplete code in code chunks, and compose code chunks freely. <em>This</em>, I believe, is the true spirit of literate programming, but is often overlooked by software authors who implement software products using the idea of literate programming. Literate programming is a <em>programming</em> technique.
