It does not simply mean mixing code and text in a linear order.</p> \n<p>Below is a toy example of R Markdown, in which we create a function in three parts in three code chunks respectively:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">We define a function to add one to a number:\n\n```{r, func-def, eval=FALSE}\nadd_one = function(x) {\n```\n\nIt is a very simple function: it just returns `x + 1`.\n\n```{r, func-body, eval=FALSE}\n  return(x + 1)\n```\n\nAnd we are done!\n\n```{r, func-end, eval=FALSE}\n}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>None of the above code chunks are meant to be executed individually, so I used the chunk option <code>eval=FALSE</code>. I also labeled the code chunks, so I can reference them later in a code chunk that is to be actually executed:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, func-full, ref.label=c('func-def', 'func-body', 'func-end')}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>which will be automatically parsed (by <strong>knitr</strong>) to a complete code chunk like this:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, func-full}\nadd_one = function(x) {\n  return(x + 1)\n}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>If you don’t want to display the R source code of the chunk <code>func-full</code> in the output document (because you have already displayed the three parts before), you can add the chunk option <code>echo=FALSE</code>, e.g.,</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, func-full, echo=FALSE, ref.label=c('func-def', 'func-body', 'func-end')}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>The code chunk <code>func-full</code> can appear <em>before</em> the three code chunks in the document if you want. For example, in a tutorial, you may want to run the function and show your readers the results before you explain each part of the function.</p> \n<p>You can even reference other code chunks in the body of a code chunk. For example, if you decide to check if <code>x</code> is numeric before computing <code>x + 1</code>, you can do:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, func-full-more}\n&lt;&lt;func-def&gt;&gt;\n  if (!is.numeric(x)) stop(\"The argument 'x' must be numeric\")\n&lt;&lt;func-body&gt;&gt;\n&lt;&lt;func-end&gt;&gt;\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>I guess although R Markdown is very popular in the R world, not many users are aware of these ways of composing code chunks, which is understandable to me, because not many users actually need to explain a long piece of code by parts. Joel is a book author, and happens to want this feature badly.</p> \n<p>Joel ended up with copying and pasting code (and explaining each part) in Markdown to his book, as most people do traditionally. I’d argue that this traditional way may actually hinder reproducibility, because it is hard to verify if your code always works after it is pasted into the book. There might be human errors introduced in the process of copy-and-paste. When writing tutorials or books, I believe literate programming is the safest way to go. The problem is just that Jupyter’s implementation of literate programming seems to rather limited in Joel’s case.</p> \n<h2 id=\"how-you-could-win-joel-over\">How you could win Joel over</h2> \n<p>Jake VanderPlas proposed a “reproducibility mode” for Jupyter notebooks (slide #130), in which</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Code cells are read-only once executed;</p></li> \n <li><p>New code cells cannot be inserted above previously executed cells;</p></li> \n <li><p>No cell can be executed until all previous cells are executed.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Joel said this would alleviate a lot of his complaints, but as far as I can see, this mode will only address the first problem in the above 11 problems he mentioned in the talk. I’m not sure if this means the rest 10 problems do not matter much to Joel.</p> \n<p>Anyway, personally I feel this mode will be a very, very bad idea. It reminds me of a paradox mentioned by Charlie Munger in the book “Poor Charlie’s Almanack”:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>It’s not the bad ideas that do you in, it’s the good ideas. And you may say, “That can’t be so. That’s paradoxical.” What he [Graham] meant was that if a thing is a bad idea, it’s hard to overdo. But where there is a good idea with a core of essential and important truth, you can’t ignore it. And then it’s so easy to overdo it. So the good ideas are a wonderful way to suffer terribly if you overdo them.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Out of question, reproducibility is a good idea, but you shouldn’t push it so far as to let users suffer.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:The-best-strateg\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:The-best-strateg\">5</a></sup> Here are my specific comments to the proposal:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Code cells are read-only once executed;</p> <p><em>Who can write the correct code in a single pass? What should users do if they make mistakes in a cell? What if they change their mind?</em></p></li> \n <li><p>New code cells cannot be inserted above previously executed cells;</p> <p><em>Who can plan everything in advance in a data analysis? What should users do if they want to add another model to analyze the data but show it earlier? What shoudl they do if they want to introduce another dataset to be merged into the current dataset?</em></p></li> \n <li><p>No cell can be executed until all previous cells are executed.</p> <p><em>What if a later cell doesn’t depend on previous cells, but previous cells are very time-consuming to run? Should the user just sit there and wait?</em></p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>The ideal solution in my opinion is very simple, and I have mentioned it earlier in this post when talking about the out-of-order execution problem: just let users do whatever they want in the notebook, but alert them loudly that they shall not fully trust the results until they restart the session and recompile the whole notebook in the linear order. Even when you do serious software engineering, I believe this may be what you do routinely—you may execute different parts of your scripts in the console not in the strictly linear order as you develop the software, you may modify the code after you have executed it, you may insert new code, and so on. In the end, when you build the library, everything will be executed in the linear order in a new session. So why must we make the life of notebook users so different and hard?</p> \n<p>Likewise, software engineering rules are certainly good, but I feel we will be overdoing the good idea if we force data analysts to follow these rules. For example, for a data analyst who doesn’t write a single function but only uses functions in an existing package to analyze the data, how should she write unit tests? What should she test? Different people have different priorities and constraints.</p> \n<p>Joel even wished Jupyter to force people to name new notebooks (slide #131). Naming a file is a good idea, but to force naming is bad. I don’t know if Visual Studio Code (the IDE that Joel uses) forces users to give meaningful names to new files.</p> \n<p>In conclusion, I don’t think we will be able to win Joel over to Jupyter, because he seems to be looking for an IDE to develop software, but Jupyter is not an IDE.</p> \n<p>BTW, the last slide (#138) is my favorite. I find vertical slides in reveal.js ridiculous, too. That is a good example of “people are often preoccupied with what they <em>can</em> do and don’t think about whether they <em>should</em> do it”.</p> \n<h2 id=\"how-about-r-markdown-notebooks\">How about R Markdown notebooks?</h2> \n<p>Jupyter notebooks are notebooks. Jupyter is the product. R Markdown notebooks are just plain text R Markdown documents. The “notebook” is only <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JennyBryan/status/1034629338275016704\">a special execution mode</a> of R Markdown documents (as we mentioned in the book “R Markdown: The Definitive Guide”). Or you may treat it as a by-product instead of a product. If you like it, use it; if you don’t, use the original R Markdown mode, i.e., the batch mode—click the <code>Knit</code> button to compile the whole document in a new R session every time. We don’t lock you in the notebook (you can check out anytime and you can <em>always</em> leave).</p> \n<p>The other unique thing about R Markdown notebooks is the RStudio IDE support. Autocomplete, linting, and documentation tooltips—they all come from the IDE. When you stand on the shoulders of an IDE, your life with notebooks can be much easier. However, please note that we don’t lock you in the RStudio IDE, either. You can compile notebooks through command line or other editors, too (the underlying R packages and Pandoc are all open-source).</p> \n<p>Among the 11 problems above, #1 is real for pretty much all notebooks including R Markdown notebooks, but in general I don’t think it is a big problem (remember: restart your session and recompile the whole notebook). The RStudio IDE has even explicitly provided a menu for you to restart R and run all code chunks:</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/45199046-66a38680-b22f-11e8-80de-ea639ea32f82.png\" alt=\"Restart R and Run All Chunks\" /></p> \n<p>Joel seems to have helped Jupyter users debug many painful problems due to the hidden state and out-of-order execution issues. For some reason, I have never heard such problems from an R Markdown notebook user. Perhaps R users are just too shy to ask or they quietly give up.</p> \n<p>For problems #3, #4 and #7, I have argued that notebooks are not suitable for software engineering, and you should consider using a true IDE. For R Markdown users, there is an obvious IDE for you to use. Problem #5 is also solved by the IDE.</p> \n<p>Problem #6 is not necessarily bad in my eyes, although it also exists in R Markdown notebooks.</p> \n<p>Problem #8 doesn’t exist in R Markdown. Problem #9 is trivially easy to solve in R Markdown.</p> \n<p>I have no conclusion for problem #10. I don’t really know being able to run cells by <code>Ctrl + Enter</code> is good or bad for learning. I feel it is not necessarily a bad thing, but I don’t have data to support it.</p> \n<p>Problem #11 doesn’t exist in R Markdown.</p> \n<p>Overall I guess Joel’s life would be a little easier if he were an R user. Well, actually <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1035727684524040194\">R Markdown does support Python</a> through the <a href=\"https://rstudio.github.io/reticulate/\"><strong>reticulate</strong></a> package. In my (biased, of course) opinion, the support is reasonably good, although I guess the RStudio IDE is unlikely to satisfy hardcore Python users looking for features like autosuggest based on the static analysis of type annotations (slide #65). Besides, if you are interested in writing books, you can check out <a href=\"https://bookdown.org\">the <strong>bookdown</strong> package</a>, which is an R Markdown extension. Your life as a book author could be even easier.</p> \n<h2 id=\"should-we-rename-notebook-to-scratch-pad\">Should we rename “Notebook” to “Scratch Pad”?</h2> \n<p>I read through some comments on Twitter (e.g., threads started by <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hspter/status/1034511489632067584\">Hilary Parker</a>, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/rdpeng/status/1034606358237245440\">Roger Peng</a>, and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JennyBryan/status/1034626933961572352\">Jenny Bryan</a>). My major impression is that those who don’t like notebooks feel notebooks are messy, and they are messy in two aspects:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>The state can be messy, which is exactly the Problem #1 above. Interestingly enough, I found that a fair amount of R Markdown users <a href=\"https://twitter.com/kierisi/status/1034633407299559424\">didn’t seem to know</a> what R Markdown notebooks really are (repeat: a notebook is only <em>one possible execution mode</em> for R Markdown). As a result, they have never suffered from this problem, because they have always been using the batch mode instead of the notebook mode. For those who know what notebooks are, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/raphg/status/1034642407034314752\">they just switch to</a> the traditional batch mode, which is exactly what we expect them to do.</p> <p>Note that we (R Markdown developers) released R Markdown with the batch mode first, and introduced the notebook mode about four years later. That probably explains why the state problem seems to be rare in the R Markdown world.</p></li> \n <li><p>The content can be messy. This point was brought forward by Hilary, and I tend to agree. A notebook encourages you to dump experimental and even random things in it. To quote Hilary:</p> \n  <blockquote> \n   <p>[…] I think they [notebooks] encourage people to write crummy analyses. They’re usually not much more than a stream-of-consciousness narrative, like raw model output, then some insufficient comment on it, then a plot, then insufficient comments… etc.</p> \n  </blockquote> <p>To quote Roger Peng:</p> \n  <blockquote> \n   <p>I think notebooks encourage putting everything into a single document, but that goes against clear communication and developing a coherent narrative of an analysis.</p> \n  </blockquote> <p>Do these words remind you of something I mentioned earlier?…</p> <p>Yes, Microsoft Excel. In Excel, you put raw data, raw output, and everything in a single sheet, with insufficient comments (actually often no comments at all).</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Notebooks do encourage experimenting and prototyping. That is why the content can be messy. With that in mind, I feel “scratch pad” would be a more appropriate name practically than “notebook” (and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/deronaucoin/status/1034513653452746752\">some people</a> do use notebooks as scratch pads).</p> \n<p>Can notebooks be used to write a well-structured, thoughtful, and reproducible (data analysis) report? I believe they surely can. Nothing stops you from extracting the useful parts from the scratch pads to write a formal document.</p> \n<p>What was the real problem above? I think it is that the expectation of going from a notebook to a sophisticated analysis in a single pass is wrong. In most cases, you cannot skip experimental or exploratory analysis. You don’t have to use notebooks for exploratory data analysis, but they are just one possible way to do it. You can, for example, use pure R scripts to do it, too, but you probably will never publish the raw output of all these R scripts as is.</p> \n<p>In short, chances are your first notebook will/should not be your final product (e.g., a customer report).</p> \n<h2 id=\"xaringan-inf-mr-another-lesser-known-unpopular-solution-to-the-out-of-order-execution-problem\"><code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code>, another (lesser-known/unpopular) solution to the out-of-order execution problem</h2> \n<p>I mentioned four ways to compile an R Markdown document <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/compile.html\">in the Definitive Guide book</a>:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>click the <code>Knit</code> button in RStudio;</p></li> \n <li><p>call the function <code>rmarkdown::render()</code>;</p></li> \n <li><p>use the notebook mode;</p></li> \n <li><p>or run <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code> (here <code>inf_mr</code> stands for <a href=\"http://naruto.wikia.com/wiki/Infinite_Tsukuyomi\">Infinite Moon Reader</a>).</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>Only the notebook mode can suffer from the out-of-order execution problem. For the other three ways, the first two have a tiny problem, which may or may not bother you: they require a manual operation each time you recompile the document (click a button, or type a function call).</p> \n<p>The fourth way may sound odd but it is also pretty cool. At least <a href=\"https://twitter.com/cantabile/status/1034216497634131971\">one person has been excited about it</a> for quite a while. It doesn’t have the out-of-order execution problem, yet it gives you a pseudo-notebook experience. Essentially it is similar to the first two ways, i.e., it compiles the whole R Markdown document, but it features LiveReload. That is, when you modify the R Markdown source and save the file, the output page will be automatically refreshed after the R Markdown document is recompiled. More importantly, the previous scroll position is preserved after the page is refreshed. This means if you were looking at a code chunk (in the RStudio editor) and its output (in RStudio Viewer) side by side previously, they will still be side by side after you make changes to the source document.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:If-you-are-editi\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:If-you-are-editi\">6</a></sup> However, please note that there is no mechanism of synchronizing the scroll positions of the source and output file. They are scrolled independently. Anyway, I think it is helpful that the document can be recompiled automatically on save.</p> \n<p>This way can suffer from the state problem, because it compiles R Markdown in the current R session, which means anything in your workspaces is available to the R Markdown document, and anything created by the R Markdown document will be exposed in your workspace. They can pollute each other. This is not necessarily a bad thing (repeat: when in doubt, restart and recompile), and sometimes can be beneficial. For example, if you have a huge data object that takes time to read, you only need to read it once in the current R session, and you can keep using it until the session is ended or you intentionally delete it.</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\"># read the data only if it has not been read\nif (!exists('MY_HUGE_DATA')) MY_HUGE_DATA = read_the_data(...)\n</code></pre> \n<p>Besides, you can freely examine any objects created in your R Markdown document in the R console. For example, you can draw different plots using the object <code>MY_HUGE_DATA</code> in the console before you decide which plot you want, and write the code in R Markdown. This can save you a lot of effort and time, since you don’t have to recompile the whole document in a new R session after every single change to see the output. The reproducibility principle is important, but you can leave it to a later stage. Worrying about it in every single minute in your data analysis is costly (in terms of your mental power and time).</p> \n<h2 id=\"literate-programming-for-real-for-software-development\">Literate programming, for real (for software development)</h2> \n<p>As I mentioned in Problem #11 above, you can use the literate programming technique for programming. I have prepared <a href=\"https://yihui.org/rlp/\">a concrete example</a> a couple of years ago. You can see how you could write code in small (incomplete) pieces in different chunks and compose them to a complete package. When reading that example, I strongly recommend that you also read its <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/rlp/edit/master/vignettes/LP-demo1.Rmd\">R Markdown source document</a> on Github to know how it actually works.</p> \n<p>So yes, you <em>can</em> develop software using literate programming and R Markdown (notebooks). The natural question is whether you <em>should</em>. To be honest, I will probably never use this technique to develop software. “Haha, Yihui, so you are only kidding us?” You may ask. No, I’m not. I still believe this technique has its unique advantage that cannot be found in traditional programming methods. That is, the rich documentation (narratives) to explain the code. The narratives can be infinitely <em>rich</em> and <em>granular</em>: rich because you can write anything that the authoring language (e.g., Markdown) allows you to write (images, videos, and math equations, etc.), and granular because your can break your long code into any number of smaller units and explain each unit.</p> \n<p>Literate programming is unbeatable when you need to explain your code very clearly. Note that Knuth’s original implementation was base on TeX (the authoring language) and Pascal (the computing language). To my knowledge, only a few people in the TeX community have adopted this technique—they use literate programming to explain TeX code.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Is-that-because\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:Is-that-because\">7</a></sup> The vast majority of programmers just use comments to explain code in software.</p> \n<h2 id=\"what-if-i-m-standing-at-the-intersection-of-software-engineering-and-data-analysis\">What if I’m standing at the intersection of software engineering and data analysis?</h2> \n<p>I’ll only answer this question for R users since I’m not familiar with the Python ecosystem. There isn’t a clear borderline between software engineers and data analysts. It is likely that sometimes data analysts do need to factor out code and write functions. In this case, should you use the IDE or notebook?</p> \n<p>My answer is, if your project is small enough, it is fine to write a few external R scripts and <code>source()</code> them in the notebook, otherwise you may consider using the IDE to write an R package, and include your notebooks as <em>package vignettes</em>. To quote <a href=\"https://twitter.com/cboettig/status/1034665522904723457\">Carl Boettiger</a>:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>I think a good .Rmd notebook acts like an #rstats vignette: complex code belongs in functions in a package, (with docs and tests if appropriate); the notebook part merely ensures code underlying the narrative &amp; results are reproducible.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Yes, if you have complex code, considering putting them in a package, instead of throwing a thousand lines of code in a notebook.</p> \n<p>Although I’m a statistics PhD, I didn’t read many papers during my training, but I did read “Statistical Analyses and Reproducible Research” by <a href=\"https://biostats.bepress.com/bioconductor/paper2/\">Gentleman and Temple Lang (2004)</a>. This 14-year old<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:The-only-thing-t\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:The-only-thing-t\">8</a></sup> paper basically proposed that we use the R package structure to manage the data analysis of research projects, because this structure has pretty much everything you need: you can have a <code>data/</code> folder, the <code>R/</code> and <code>src/</code> source folders, the <code>tests/</code> folder, and the <code>vignettes/</code> folder, etc. You can write good software (yes, please do write unit tests now) and do good data analysis in different folders.</p> \n<p>Then after <code>R CMD build</code>, you get both a reusable R package that can be distributed to others, as well as data analysis reports that are reproducible.</p> \n<h2 id=\"reasons-why-you-don-t-like-r-markdown-notebooks\">Reasons why you don’t like R Markdown (notebooks)?</h2> \n<p>Okay, I’m looking for the Joel Grus in the R Markdown world, and I’d like to know what you don’t like about R Markdown and R Markdown notebooks. I saw a few comments on Twitter, like <a href=\"https://twitter.com/romunov/status/1034659703173914626\">Roman Luštrik’s</a>:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>I stopped using notebooks because in RStudio, they don’t allow a <code>browser()</code> call, but otherwise very helpful.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>which doesn’t seem to be true, though. Perhaps it is because I’m using <a href=\"https://www.rstudio.com/products/rstudio/download/preview/\">a newer version of RStudio</a>. I tried to run the code chunk in the following minimal example, and <code>browser()</code> works as expected.</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">---\ntitle: \"R Notebook\"\noutput: html_notebook\n---\n\n```{r}\nf = function() {\n  x = 1:10\n  browser()\n  x + 1\n}\nf()\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>What else? Please feel free to pour out your thoughts (also feel free to email me if you prefer a private conversation).</p> \n<h2 id=\"summary\">Summary</h2> \n<p>This is a long post. It took me four full days to write. I like writing and I hope writing can help me think deeply on things of importance. In this case, R Markdown is important to me, so I used Joel Grus as my “mirror”. Although he was criticizing Jupyter, I wondered if the criticism could also apply to R Markdown. My overall conclusion is that R Markdown has a relatively solid base and enough deep roots in literate programming to defend itself. That said, I’ll be excited if someone plans to give a talk “I don’t like R Markdown” at the next <a href=\"https://www.rstudio.com/conference/\">RStudio Confernce</a>.</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p>In this post, I first talked about the two cultures, namely the data analysis culture, in which people often use code, and the software engineering culture, in which people often create code. I believe notebooks are great for exploratory data analysis, but bad for software engineering.</p></li> \n <li><p>Then I tried to explain why notebooks become so popular by an analogy to Excel: people love seeing immediate output that is close to its source, and love putting everything in a single place, so they don’t have to constantly switch from one tool or one window to another. Reproducibility introduced by notebooks is more or less merely a coincidence, and users are not attracted by the reproducibility principle but convenience instead. Notebooks cannot guarantee reproducibility, especially when you run cells in a weird order, but they are much better than Excel in terms of reproducibility due to the fact that you always use scripts to do tasks.</p></li> \n <li><p>Joel’s #1 complaint (hidden state and out-of-order execution) is real but not the end of the world in my eyes. Users have to be better educated on this problem. Problems related to software engineering are better to be solved by an IDE instead of the notebook itself. Jupyter is not an IDE, and that is probably why Joel struggled so much. The rest of problems seem to be the real limits of Jupyter itself. Fortunately, in the R world, the “IDE + Notebook” combo has made a lot of things easier.</p></li> \n <li><p>Notebooks encourage experimenting and prototyping, which can lead to crummy analyses, because users simply dump a lot of raw content (such as raw model output) to a notebook. Such a notebook is unlikely to be a proper final data analysis product or report. Don’t send this to your boss or customer.</p></li> \n <li><p>If you want the notebook feel but absolutely hate the possible out-of-order execution, you may try <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code> (or equivalently, the RStudio Addin “Infinite Moon Reader”) on your R Markdown document.</p></li> \n <li><p>If you want to use the literate programming technique to develop software, you can, but chances are you don’t want to.</p></li> \n <li><p>If you do both software engineering and data analysis heavily in a project, consider using an IDE to develop an R package, instead of stuffing your poor notebooks with thousands of lines of code.</p></li> \n</ul> \n<p>Okay, I’m done. If you have made it to the end not through a quick scroll, congratulations! We now live in an age in which <a href=\"https://www.theglobeandmail.com/opinion/i-have-forgotten-how-toread/article37921379/\">we have forgotten how to read</a>, and in fact, also generally lost the ability to do <a href=\"http://calnewport.com/books/deep-work/\">deep work</a>. You can actually forget about those wars in technologies,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Facepalm-Sorry-I\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fn:Facepalm-Sorry-I\">9</a></sup> if you still have the ability to focus on reading one thing for even an hour. That ability is much more important and precious than technology.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:It-doesn-t-mean\">It doesn’t mean you have to be harsh. For example, this post “<a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/8108\">What is hard about blogdown?</a>” by Alison is a good one. I’m always interested in knowing the possible pain you have with tools that I created. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:It-doesn-t-mean\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:I-have-to-say-th\">I have to say that Excel is one of the best examples of reactive programming in my eyes. No matter how hard you laugh at Excel, the reactivity through formulas and cell references is genius. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:I-have-to-say-th\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:I-only-know-that\">I only know that real sciences don’t have the word “science” in the names, such as chemistry, physics, and biology. They don’t call themselves “chemical science”, “physical science”, or “biological science”. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:I-only-know-that\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Joel-mainly-crit\">Joel mainly criticized a blog post by Adrien Lina, originally titled “<a href=\"https://blog.sicara.com/present-data-science-results-jupyter-notebook-import-into-another-108433bc8505\">Present Your Data Science Results in a Jupyter Notebook, the Right Way</a>”. Oh, this post title was… was so… so… Forget it. You know what I was going to say. After Joel’s talk, the author removed “the Right Way” in the title (what does that mean?), but it is still such a general and broad title, which sounds like the title of a 300-page book. Why not using an explicit and clear title “How to import one Jupyter notebook into another” (which is currently the subtitle)? To quote Andrew Gelman, “<a href=\"https://andrewgelman.com/2018/05/13/say-less-clearly-please-general-purpose-data-garbler-applications-requiring-confidentiality/\">Could you say that again less clearly, please?</a>” The enthusiasm of writing tutorials is great, precious, and will be appreciated, but if you decide to use an attractive title, you should take the responsibility of providing relevant, correct, and solid content, which eyeballs and page views will naturally follow, if you care about this sort of things. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:Joel-mainly-crit\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:The-best-strateg\">The best strategy is to let people “fall into the pit of success” if possible. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:The-best-strateg\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:If-you-are-editi\">If you are editing an R Markdown source document of which the output format is <code>xaringan::moon_reader</code>, the slide page number will be preserved after refreshing. For example, if you were looking at page #25, the slides will still be on page #25 after they are recompiled. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:If-you-are-editi\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Is-that-because\">Is that because TeX code is particularly difficult to understand? I don’t know. I don’t understand TeX well. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:Is-that-because\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:The-only-thing-t\">The only thing that I’d strongly disagree with the authors is the suggested use of XML. XML would be a terrible authoring language in my opinion. You can see that 14 years later, Markdown has pretty much dominated all plain-text authoring languages. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:The-only-thing-t\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Facepalm-Sorry-I\">Facepalm! Sorry, I wrote a lengthy post on a technological war and wasted your time. The war itself is not important, but I hope some of my thoughts can be helpful to you. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/#fnref:Facepalm-Sorry-I\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"August 12, 1991 Review of S-PLUS Statistical Software","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2019/05/review-of-s-plus/","description":"<p>Note: This is a guest post and not written by Yihui. Its author desires to remain anonymous. If anyone would like to contact the author, please contact me (Yihui). And if any statistical sleuths out there determine the author’s identity, please refrain from revealing it. Thank you.</p> \n<hr /> \n<p><strong>INTEROFFICE MEMORANDUM</strong></p> \n<p><strong>DATE:</strong> August 12, 1991</p> \n<p><strong>TO:</strong> □□□□□□□□□□□□□</p> \n<p><strong>FROM:</strong> □□□□□□□□□</p> \n<p><strong>SUBJECT:</strong> REVIEW OF S-PLUS STATISTICAL SOFTWARE</p> \n<p>S-PLUS, from StatSci Corp., is a new type of statistical programming language. It was originally called “S” and developed by AT&amp;T Bell Labs for UNIX. StatSci has expanded upon its capabilities and converted it for PCs. It differs from other statistical programming languages in many ways.</p> \n<p>I consider it to be a cross between Minitab and object-oriented C. It is like Minitab in that it is interactive and fully integrates graphics and matrix operations with the statistical routines. Unlike Minitab, it also provides programming capabilities like object-oriented C. This makes the language very powerful, because one can easily and quickly develop nonstandard or repetitive routines.</p> \n<p>This package has several advantages over more common statistical language packages such as SAS. SAS has DATA steps to prepare your data and PROC steps to analyze your data. In S-PLUS, there is no distinction. You can manipulate your data at any time, even within routines. This saves programming time and frees you from many of SAS’s constraints. Another major difference is that SAS comes in modules: one for basic functions, one for statistical analysis routines, one for graphics routines, one for matrix algebra, plus others. It even has a separate macro language for repetitive operations. This means you must buy - actually, rent - and install several products, and, more importantly, figure out how to program them together. This is not an easy task. With S-PLUS, these capabilities are integrated, which results in Exploratory Data Analysis (EDA) being very easy to perform, unlike SAS.</p> \n<p>S-PLUS does have its disadvantages, mainly due to its data structure organization. The inherent matrix algebra-like organization requires a higher level of thinking than other packages do. Also, creating a data structure is a nontrivial task. However, once these concepts are mastered and a proper data set is created, analyzing the data is much easier and faster than other packages, because there are very few constraints to program around.</p> \n<p>Another aspect that some people might consider a drawback is that S-PLUS does not have an exhaustive supply of routines to do everything under the sun. It doesn’t try to please everyone all the time. What it does try to do is provide a tool for scientist to analyze their data in a meaningful way. And if there is something else you want it to do, you can program it.</p> \n<p>To give you a better idea of what S-PLUS can do, I’m enclosing an index of S-PLUS’s functions and examples of some of its capabilities. The first example is a matrix or “draftsman’s” plot of some automotive data. It is a multivariate technique to show the relationships between several variables. The second example is a partial listing of star plots of the same data set. The spikes on the stars represent a metric and the length of the spikes represents the value of the metric. This type of display makes it easy to see how items are similar or different. Both complex examples required only a few commands once the data structure was set up. The last examples are the plots and S-PLUS code for a reliability growth plan I did for □□□ □□□□□□□□ □□□□□□□ program. As you can see from the code, not much work was required to generate the plots, and if the parameters change, I can easily generate new plots by changing the arguments for the function.</p> \n<p>I recommend that we buy two copies: one for the □□□□□□□ office and one for the □□□□□□□ office. The cost of one copy is $1195. But if you buy between two to five copies, the cost is $995 per copy. If you have any specific questions, either call me or call StatSci at (206) 283-8802.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:A-footnote-by-Yi\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/05/review-of-s-plus/#fn:A-footnote-by-Yi\">1</a></sup></p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">library(corrgram)\npairs(\n  auto[c(\"MPG\", \"Weight\", \"Length\", \"Turn\", \"Displa\", \"Gratio\")],\n  main = \"1979 Consumers Reports Automotive Data\"\n)\n</code></pre> \n<p><img class=\"language-r\">stars(\n  auto[1:16, ], \n  main = \"Star Plot of 1979 Consumers Reports Automotive Data - 1st 16 models\",\n  labels = as.character(auto[1:16, \"Model\"])\n)\n</code></pre> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985262-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-9f81-b74f2026477a.png\" alt=\"Star Plot of 1979 Consumers Reports Automotive Data - 1st 16 models\" /></p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">ideal &lt;- function(alpha, Ti, Mi, Mf) {\n  # S function to plot idealized growth curve from alpha, Ti, Mi, &amp; Mf.\n  \n  Ti &lt;- Ti/1000\n  Mi &lt;- Mi/1000\n  Mf &lt;- Mf/1000\n  MMTC &lt;- c(0:(10 * Mf))/10\n  mile &lt;- exp(log(Ti) + (log(MMTC/Mi) + log(1 - alpha))/alpha)\n  Tf &lt;- max(mile)\n  MMTC[MMTC &lt; Mi] &lt;- Mi\n  header &lt;- paste(\"Idealized Growth Curve - Alpha =\", alpha)\n  par(pty = \"s\")\n  plot(mile, MMTC, type = \"l\", main = header, xlab = \"Test Miles (x1000)\", ylab = \"MMTC (x1000)\", xaxs = \"i\")\n  abline(h = Mf, lty = 2)\n  abline(h = Mi, lty = 2)\n  text(Tf, Mi, \" Start\", adj = 0, xpd = NA)\n  text(Tf, Mf, \" Target\", adj = 0, xpd = NA)\n}\n\nideal(0.35, 5500, 4750, 30000)\n</code></pre> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985264-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-9334-264e41a3883c.png\" alt=\"Idealized Growth Curve\" /></p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">plan &lt;- function(alpha, Ti, Mi, Mf) {\n  # S function to plot planned growth curve from alpha, Ti, Mi, &amp; Mf.\n  # Assumes 3 Test Phases after initial test phase.\n  \n  T1 &lt;- Ti/1000\n  M1 &lt;- Mi/1000\n  M4 &lt;- Mf/1000\n  T4 &lt;- exp(log(T1) + (log(M4/M1) + log(1 - alpha))/alpha)\n  \n  # Calculate beginning and end of Test Phases.\n  T2 &lt;- (2 * T1)/3 + T4/3\n  T3 &lt;- T1/3 + (2 * T4)/3\n  test.phase &lt;- c(T1, T2, T3, T4)\n  test.phase.1 &lt;- test.phase[c(1, 2, 3)]\n  test.phase.2 &lt;- test.phase[c(2, 3, 4)]\n  test.diff &lt;- test.phase.2 - test.phase.1\n  \n  # Calculate MMTC in between Test Phases.\n  N &lt;- (T1/M1) * (test.phase/T1)^(1 - alpha)\n  N.1 &lt;- N[c(1, 2, 3)]\n  N.2 &lt;- N[c(2, 3, 4)]\n  N.diff &lt;- N.2 - N.1\n  MMTC &lt;- test.diff/N.diff\n  \n  # Make data vectors to plot.\n  test.data &lt;- c(0, T1, T1, T2, T2, T3, T3, T4)\n  MMTC.data &lt;- c(M1, M1, MMTC[1], MMTC[1], MMTC[2], MMTC[2], MMTC[3], MMTC[3])\n  \n  # Plot data.\n  header &lt;- paste(\"Planned Growth Curve - Alpha =\", alpha)\n  par(pty = \"s\")\n  plot(\n    test.data, \n    MMTC.data, \n    type = \"l\", \n    main = header, \n    xlab = \"Test Miles (x1000)\", \n    ylab = \"MMTC (x1000)\", \n    xaxs = \"i\", \n    ylim = c(0, M4)\n  )\n  abline(h = M1, lty = 2)\n  abline(h = M4, lty = 2)\n  abline(v = T2, lty = 2)\n  abline(v = T3, lty = 2)\n  text(T4, M1, \" Start\", adj = 0, xpd = NA)\n  text(T4, M4, \" Target\", adj = 0, xpd = NA)\n  text((T1 + T2)/2, MMTC[1] - 2, \"Phase I\")\n  text((T2 + T3)/2, MMTC[2] - 2, \"Phase II\")\n  text((T3 + T4)/2, MMTC[3] - 2, \"Phase IIII\")\n}\n\nplan(0.35, 5500, 4750, 30000)\n</code></pre> \n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985266-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-93df-4a9257ceb3df.png\" \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2019/05/review-of-s-plus/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 01 May 2019 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985261-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-9e64-048e08a95f64.png","linkMd5":"1b9f1a076e7a8f235d09800b2bb5885f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn68@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-34-941_7cce64260d6fd372.webp","destWidth":672,"destHeight":528,"sourceBytes":53083,"destBytes":53952,"author":"Anonymous","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985261-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-9e64-048e08a95f64.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn68@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-34-941_7cce64260d6fd372.webp","https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985262-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-9f81-b74f2026477a.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn52@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-20-00-721_eaca45a309b55472.webp","https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985264-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-9334-264e41a3883c.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn47@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-20-00-633_1cb33a3ed5e972de.webp","https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/56985266-9819eb00-6b4d-11e9-93df-4a9257ceb3df.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn23@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-20-01-062_3b2f383ad339751f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973439},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The Implementation of `yolo = TRUE` in xaringan via yolofy()","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2019/03/yolo-karl/","description":"\n        <p>It has been more than two years since <a href=\"https://twitter.com/_inundata/status/798970002992873472\">Karthik requested</a> the (most famous?) <code>yolo = TRUE</code> feature in <strong>xaringan</strong>, and I feel amused to see <a href=\"https://twitter.com/Chucheria/status/1104422687345180673\">people still</a> having <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tam07pb915/status/1101434299369185280\">great fun with it</a>.</p>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/tam07pb915/status/1101434299369185280\"><img src=\"https://pbs.twimg.com/ext_tw_video_thumb/1101434265231798272/pu/img/BLOrnmcx_AARqWyH.jpg\" alt=\"@tmrkn's tweet about the funny yolo option in xaringan\" /></a></p>\n\n<p>The implementation was actually not too complicated. The idea is that we just randomly insert new slides with background images (the default is <a href=\"https://kbroman.org/blog/2014/08/28/the-mustache-photo/\">Karl&rsquo;s mustache photo</a>) into existing slides. We know that slide pages are separated by three dashes <code>---</code>, so what we need to do is to find out the three dashes, and replace a random set of them with</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-markdown\">---\nbackground-image: url(path/to/the/image)\n\n---\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>In fact, there is a function <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/blob/2543fa1/R/utils.R#L51-L65\"><code>xaringan:::yolofy()</code></a> to implement this. It has two arguments: <code>x</code>, a character vector of the Markdown source of the original slides, and <code>config</code>, a list of configurations that specify how many times (or probability) you want the image to appear in the slides, and which image you want to use.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">x2 = xaringan:::yolofy(\n  x = c('---', 'First', '---', 'Second', '---', 'Third'),\n  config = list(times = 2, img = 'karl-mustache.jpg')\n)\ncat(x2, sep = '\\n')\n</code></pre>\n\n<pre><code>---\nbackground-image: url(karl-mustache.jpg)\n---\nFirst\n---\nSecond\n---\nbackground-image: url(karl-mustache.jpg)\n---\nThird\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>If you have read the source code of <code>xaringan::yolofy()</code>, you will realize that the <code>yolo</code> option of <code>xaringan::moon_reader</code> is flexible. Below are all valid usage:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">output:\n  xaringan::moon_reader:\n    yolo: true  # make Karl appear once randomly\n</code></pre>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">output:\n  xaringan::moon_reader:\n    yolo: false\n</code></pre>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">output:\n  xaringan::moon_reader:\n    yolo: 3  # let Karl appear at most 3 times\n</code></pre>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">output:\n  xaringan::moon_reader:\n    yolo: 0.2  # appear in 20% of original slides\n</code></pre>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">output:\n  xaringan::moon_reader:\n    yolo:\n      times: 5\n      img: kangaroo.jpg  # use a different image 5 times\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>This has been one of the most representative &ldquo;low effort, high return&rdquo; examples in my packages. I think the actual implementation contributed the least to its influence. The key was Karthik&rsquo;s idea and Karl Bromona-Lisa&rsquo;s picture. I was just lucky enough to have seen both.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2019/03/yolo-karl/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 06 Mar 2019 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://pbs.twimg.com/ext_tw_video_thumb/1101434265231798272/pu/img/BLOrnmcx_AARqWyH.jpg","linkMd5":"fee2d59b38f5c49d470e76d2152d95df","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn27@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-022_14aeb72bf1a9de97.webp","destWidth":1200,"destHeight":675,"sourceBytes":49478,"destBytes":30828,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://pbs.twimg.com/ext_tw_video_thumb/1101434265231798272/pu/img/BLOrnmcx_AARqWyH.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn27@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-34-022_14aeb72bf1a9de97.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973439},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"HTML Widgets for Non-HTML Output Formats","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/html-widgets-non-html/","description":"\n        <p>Not surprisingly, <a href=\"http://www.htmlwidgets.org\">HTML widgets</a> were designed for HTML output formats (e.g., <code>rmarkdown::html_document</code> and <code>rmarkdown::ioslides_presentation</code>). Their interactivity relies on JavaScript. In general, you should not expect JavaScript to work in LaTeX/PDF or Word or PowerPoint.</p>\n\n<p>In January 2015, <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=dV4gtARPvu8\">I gave an introductory talk</a> on HTML widgets in the LA R User Group. I joked in the talk that &ldquo;If you ask me how to use HTML widgets in Word or LaTeX, we cannot be friends .&rdquo; (I meant, how could you possibly ask such a question?) A year later, this became only a half joke.</p>\n\n<p>While developing <strong>bookdown</strong> in 2016, I added a feature in <strong>knitr</strong> to &ldquo;support&rdquo; HTML widgets for non-HTML output, which basically means if the output is not HTML, <strong>knitr</strong> will try to use the <strong>webshot</strong> package to automatically take a screenshot of the widget, and embed the static screenshot in the output. Of course, you won&rsquo;t be able to interact with this static image, but it may be better than nothing (or throwing an error).</p>\n\n<p>The detailed documentation about this feature can be found in <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/bookdown/html-widgets.html\">Section 2.10 of the <strong>bookdown</strong> book</a>.</p>\n\n<p>Similarly, Shiny apps won&rsquo;t work in non-HTML output, either. If you want to include a screenshot of a Shiny app in a <strong>knitr</strong> document, you may explicitly use the <code>knitr::include_app()</code> function after you publish the app to a server. More generally, you can include a screenshot of any web page via <code>knitr::include_url()</code> when the output format is not HTML.</p>\n\n<p>Screenshotting HTML widgets, Shiny apps, and arbitrary URLs works in any non-HTML output formats: PDF, Word, EPUB, RTF, and PowerPoint, etc. If you want, you may even force a screenshot in HTML output, too (see documentation).</p>\n\n<p>I&rsquo;d like to thank Winston for the heavy-lifting in <a href=\"https://github.com/wch/webshot\">the <strong>webshot</strong> package</a>. It is a very neat package.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/html-widgets-non-html/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 19 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"3a9f7d44868ed7b91cca201486dfe96d","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The \"Talk-Driven Development\" Model","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/talk-driven-development/","description":"<p>Last year at rstudio::conf 2017, I said I used the “conference-driven development model”. As I said, Christophe is a super kind person, and that user certainly misunderstood him. I felt some gun powder in the air.</p>\n\n<p>The lengthy <code>tlmgr</code> manual was one of the two motivations of TinyTeX: I wished the TinyTeX documentation would be short enough, but&hellip; facepalm! This 300-page book was finished by the end of May. I <a href=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2018/07/13/announcing-the-r-markdown-book/\">just announced</a> the book in the RStudio official blog, but want to write a personal blog post to reflect some personal thoughts, too.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/images/cover.png\" alt=\"R Markdown: The Definitive Guide\" /></p>\n\n<h2 id=\"happily-eating-my-own-dog-food-for-the-third-time\">Happily eating my own dog food (for the third time)</h2>\n\n<p>I don&rsquo;t know other people&rsquo;s secrets on how to create successful software, but my experience is that if you create a software package that you like to use by yourself on a daily basis, it is likely to succeed and will be used by many other people, too. In other words, if even you don&rsquo;t enjoy your own dog food, how would you expect other people to eat it?</p>\n\n<p>Before creating the <strong>bookdown</strong> package, I had written two long-form documents. One was my PhD thesis, and one was the <strong>knitr</strong> book. They were written in LaTeX (using LyX). You may think &ldquo;two&rdquo; is a small number, but it turned out to be big enough to me. I didn&rsquo;t want to bury myself in the LaTeX world again if I were to write anything longer than ten pages. LaTeX is great. The only problem is that I don&rsquo;t need so much power (i.e., it is too good to me).</p>\n\n<p>I still love writing long documents. I just don&rsquo;t want to feel like a typesetter all the time.</p>\n\n<p>This is the third book I have published with <strong>bookdown</strong>. Had I not worked on <strong>bookdown</strong> first, I don&rsquo;t know if I would be able to keep the momentum of &ldquo;one book a year&rdquo; (well, only since 2016 &ndash; it is hard to tell how many more years I can keep going).</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"simple-steps-to-writing-a-book\">Simple steps to writing a book</h2>\n\n<p>If you ask me if there are simple steps to write a book. The answer is &ldquo;yes&rdquo;. The simple steps are:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>Take a deep breath. Write Chapter 1.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Write Chapter 2.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>&hellip;</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Write Chapter 19.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Write the Acknowledgements. Done. Lie on the couch, stare at the ceiling, and speculate about the meaning of life for a day.</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>That was pretty much what happened (except that I didn&rsquo;t lie on the couch or stare at the ceiling) if you <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown-book/commits/master?after=9868887b843de2e30596727fddba1a5902c7e6de+69\">take a look at some of my GIT commit messages</a> (like &ldquo;add chapter 1&rdquo;, &ldquo;add section 2.1 - 2.6&rdquo;, &ldquo;add section 2.7&rdquo;, &hellip;).</p>\n\n<p>Of course you know I&rsquo;m kidding. If you look for &ldquo;simple steps&rdquo; to do any nontrivial tasks, you are already losing. More often than not, you have no choice but bite the bullet, over and over again. You need to be prepared for that.</p>\n\n<p>That said, I have to say that Step 1 is important, i.e., it is important to at least get started. A dreadful task is less dreadful after you actually start working on it, even if it is only 1% less dreadful.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"managing-distractions\">Managing distractions</h2>\n\n<p>I have said enough about managing distractions in <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/acknowledgments.html\">the acknowledgements section</a> of the book. It is absolutely not fun. I ruthlessly turned down the invites to some meetings on my company calendar when I felt the meetings wouldn&rsquo;t be very productive (I preferred emails because emails could be async). I set my Slack status to &ldquo;Writing a book: may not respond to messages&rdquo; to inform my colleagues who may message me. Fortunately, my role in the company is relatively independent, and not many colleagues really rely on me heavily.</p>\n\n<p>Managing distractions from others is one thing. Managing myself is another, and actually harder. I&rsquo;m constantly drowning in Github issues, emails, and Stack Overflow questions, although I have been trying to ignore some of them. The problem is, these things are often more enjoyable than writing a book, mainly because each of them, individually, looks significantly easier than writing the book. It is tempting to put aside the book, and voluntarily jump into these small tasks. The fact is that even if I spend all my time on them, I still won&rsquo;t ever be able to finish them. Good news is that several people have started answering questions on Github and Stack Overflow before I see them.</p>\n\n<p>During the four months, I had yet another source of distraction that I absolutely could (and should) not get rid of &ndash; small kids. My younger boy was 6-mo old when I started writing this book. I almost thanked him in the book (like &ldquo;<a href=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2016/12/02/announcing-bookdown/\">big thanks to my younger boy</a>, without whom I should have finished this book two months earlier&rdquo;). Fortunately, he didn&rsquo;t deprive too much of my sleep, and quickly &ldquo;agreed&rdquo; not to take the bottle at night.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"this-is-a-reference-book\">This is a reference book</h2>\n\n<p>Just to set your expectation, I have to clarify that this is primarily a book for your reference. It is not a text book. If you read it from the beginning to end, you may feel exhausted or overwhelmed. It is meant to be a guide, and some chapters are not comprehensive (but should be good enough to get you started). You may need to read more about the specific R Markdown extension in its own full documentation (e.g., <strong>blogdown</strong>, <strong>pkgdown</strong>, and <strong>learnr</strong>).</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"once-again-thanks-to-the-chapman-hall-team\">Once again, thanks to the Chapman &amp; Hall team</h2>\n\n<p>Both our publisher Chapman &amp; Hall and us have tried to make this book a little more affordable to readers. We have waived the royalties. We are allowed to keep the online version of this book, so readers can read it for free anytime.</p>\n\n<p>Our editor John Kimmel and copy-editor Suzanne Lassandro were super fast, partly because we are familiar with each other now, I guess. When sending me the comments for a revision, Suzanne said &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not even going to set a deadline for you.&rdquo; She knew I&rsquo;d return the revised manuscript the next day.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"what-s-next\">What&rsquo;s next?</h2>\n\n<p>So what&rsquo;s next? I don&rsquo;t really know at the moment. As I said <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/12/busy-vs-productive/\">in a previous post</a>, I always have interesting ideas that I want to work on. It is hard to decide. On the other hand, as I maintain more and more R packages, perhaps it will be harder and harder for me to focus on larger projects. For example, <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/issue-zero/\">I just spent two good weeks</a> on the <strong>rticles</strong> package.</p>\n\n<p>Another book? An alternative to LaTeX? A fancy JS visualization library? Recording more video tutorials? There are just too many interesting projects. I&rsquo;ll need a quiet environment for such projects anyway, so first of all, I have to walk through all the chaos accumulated and left behind while I wrote the book, which I think can easily take me a month. Oh, by the way, I just noticed the other day that some rabbits managed to get into my garden and ate all my cilantro. I probably need to fix my garden fence, too. My hardware skills are extremely poor. Darn it.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/r-markdown-book/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 13 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/images/cover.png","linkMd5":"5aac18573f342a9d62521af19800e113","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn75@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-33-673_1863269ca43e6bf6.webp","destWidth":441,"destHeight":677,"sourceBytes":19468,"destBytes":19528,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/images/cover.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn75@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-33-673_1863269ca43e6bf6.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Winners Take All in the Dependency World (or Hell)","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/","description":"<p>Yesterday I read <a href=\"https://twitter.com/sarah_edo/status/1059616001937960960\">a tweet on the marketing of software</a>, which encourages software engineers to promote their good software packages. It seems to be reasonable at the first glance. The problem, in my opinion, is that we are now living in this miserable age in which we are deeply manipulated by the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attention_economy\">attention economy</a>.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:So-deeply-manipu\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fn:So-deeply-manipu\">1</a></sup> At the same time, there are just so many great software packages. As a result, marketing more or less depends on <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tslumley/status/950806599232192512\">how loud you can shout out</a> and whether you are able to keep raising your voice in a room that has already been fairly loud (or <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JJ_Emerson/status/945830711503634432\">using more emojis</a>).</p> \n<p>That was one of the main reasons I have pretty much stopped marketing since a few years ago, at least on social media. The other reason is that I consider myself to be famous <a href=\"https://m.signalvnoise.com/enough-1d48019c7335\">enough</a>, and people can hear me even if I’m just murmuring to myself in my own world (like what I’m doing right now). There is a problem (or side-effect) of being famous and influential: people tend to trust and use everything you do based on your past good track record. That is natural and understandable, because our brain tends to simplify the process of evaluation and judgement and avoid the effort of collecting more information for strict objectivity.</p> \n<p>All software developers know the problem of the dependency hell: your package may be broken if any of your upstream packages is broken. The more packages you depend on, the higher the risk of breakage is.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Speaking-of-the\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fn:Speaking-of-the\">2</a></sup> However, this definitely does not mean you should absolutely avoid dependencies. Not many people can live the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walden\">life in the woods</a> like Thoreau. The meaningful question is, what are the cost and gain of introducing a dependency?</p> \n<p>Actually that is not what I want to talk about in this post. The dependency hell problem has been discussed many times by many people. The observation that I want to share here is that I discovered a phenomenon of “winners take all” in the world of dependencies. That is, if a package (or its author) is extremely famous, other users tend to depend on it unconditionally. I have seen such examples several times, and will give one below (with my apologies to Patrick Schratz and Miles McBain in advance—I don’t mean to judge your code, but show how tempting it is to introduce dependencies).</p> \n<p>Recently the cool hacker Patrick Schratz sent a pull request to <strong>xaringan</strong> to create PDFs from <strong>xaringan</strong> slides, in which he <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/pull/177#discussion_r227974726\">initially introduced two dependencies</a>, <strong>glue</strong> and <strong>PBSmodelling</strong>. I could definitely see the advantages of <strong>glue</strong> (including the fact that it has <a href=\"https://github.com/jimhester/presentations/blob/26056248/2018_07_13-Glue_strings_to_data_with_glue/2018_03_28-Glue_string_to_data_with_glue.Rmd#L133\">zero depedencies</a>), but in this case, the feature introduced in the PR didn’t seem to be essential to me (i.e., it is a “nice-to-have” feature). On the other hand, there is no essential gain by using <code>glue::glue()</code> instead of <code>sprintf()</code> or <code>paste()</code> in base R:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">function(p1, p2) {\n  glue::glue(\"docker run ... {p1} {p2}\"\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>It could be well written with <code>sprintf()</code>:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">function(p1, p2) {\n  sprintf(\"docker run ... %s %s\", p1, p2)\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>or <code>paste()</code>:<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:I-hope-nobody-wi\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fn:I-hope-nobody-wi\">3</a></sup></p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">function(p1, p2) {\n  paste(\"docker run ...\", p1, p2)\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>The dependency on the <strong>PBSmodelling</strong> is even heavier with a marginal gain, which was not worth it in my eyes.</p> \n<p>Yesterday Romain Lesur<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:At-some-point-th\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fn:At-some-point-th\">4</a></sup> told me a truly amazing package developed by Miles McBain. By “truly amazing”, I mean it is the kind of packages that can make me sleepless at night (together with my one-year old). When I started looking at its source code, I saw it used <code>glue::glue()</code> <a href=\"https://github.com/MilesMcBain/chradle/blob/295705f12d3423010f5a454a03e064e19d0b7db2/R/chradle.R#L25\">multiple times</a>. This case is actually better than the previous case, since at least the dependency has been used multiple times, and in my opinion, it demonstrated one benefit of <code>glue::glue()</code>, which is the human-readability of the string template, e.g., the readability of</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">glue('The total is {total}.')\n</code></pre> \n<p>is better than</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">sprintf('The total is %f.', total)\n</code></pre> \n<p>or</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">paste0('The total is ', total, '.')\n</code></pre> \n<p>These examples are by no means to imply that <strong>glue</strong> should never be a dependency of any packages (apologies to my colleague and the author of <strong>glue</strong>, Jim Hester, if readers are misled by my post). Remember, I’m <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/10/base-r-broman/\">a desiccated baseR-er</a>, so the force on the other side must be strong enough to pull me over, and popularity is not a strong force to me.</p> \n<p>So is “winners take all” truly bad? Actually I don’t think so. Winners should be awarded (by more people depending on them). I don’t think there is a solution to eliminate the side-effect of popularity.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Yes-there-exist\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fn:Yes-there-exist\">5</a></sup> Overall I think the main effect is beneficial enough to most people, so that we can ignore the relatively minor side-effects. If you could stop and weigh the cost against the gain of every dependency you introduce, that will certainly be cool. If you don’t bother, I think that’s fine, too.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:So-deeply-manipu\">So deeply manipulated that <a href=\"https://twitter.com/Hellchick/status/942863353403150336\">you don’t even know it when one of your friends died</a>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fnref:So-deeply-manipu\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Speaking-of-the\">Speaking of the number of dependencies, I suddenly recalled the first time when I was surprised by the number. It was in 2010 when Barret Schloerke, who was an undergraduate at Iowa State at that time, presented an amazing package named <a href=\"https://github.com/hadley/helpr\"><strong>helpr</strong></a>. It was totally magic to me. When I tried to install it by myself, I was kind of terrified because R kept downloading other packages for a while and I thought something went wrong. That was the first time I had seen an R package could depend on more than ten other packages. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fnref:Speaking-of-the\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:I-hope-nobody-wi\">I hope nobody will notice this footnote, but I actually wrote a function named <a href=\"https://cran.rstudio.com/web/packages/knitr/vignettes/knit_expand.html\"><code>knitr::knit_expand()</code></a> in 2012, which has some similarities with <code>glue::glue()</code>, but using <code>knit_expand()</code> here is definitely an overkill, too. BTW, <strong>knitr</strong>’s dependencies are too heavy, which is one of the things I don’t like about it, but some dependencies are <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/pull/1552\">really hard to get rid of</a>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fnref:I-hope-nobody-wi\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:At-some-point-th\">At some point this year or early next year, I will write a post about this amazing hacker. I don’t usually collaborate substantially with other people, and made his acquaintance only recently, but he has been <em>the</em> most wonderful collaborator with whom I have ever worked in my career on software projects. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fnref:At-some-point-th\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Yes-there-exist\">Yes, there exist problems that can be solved by simple linear regressions but some people always (ab)use deep learning or AI techniques anyway. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#fnref:Yes-there-exist\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 07 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"425f135fa810a9d0472a010ab9a9ff62","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973444},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Thanks, Eduardo Junior, for Giving Up your Pull Request","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/thanks-eduardo-junior/","description":"\n        <p>Last week I received <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/pull/1520\">a pull request</a> from Eduardo Junior in the <strong>knitr</strong> repo to add classes to odd/even rows of HTML tables generated from the function <code>knitr::kable()</code>. Eventually he closed the PR, because I believed there wouldn&rsquo;t be any use cases. The most typical use case would be making zebra striped tables. Perhaps ten years ago, we would need different classes for odd and even rows of a table, but now the <code>:nth-child</code> CSS selector has been widely supported, and it is extremely simple to make striped tables with only <a href=\"https://css-tricks.com/snippets/css/css3-zebra-striping-a-table/\">a single line of CSS</a> like:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-css\">tbody tr:nth-child(odd) { background-color: #ccc; }\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>In general, I try not to close PRs. I appreciate Eduardo&rsquo;s effort in this PR, and especially appreciate his understanding of my rejection. Through his <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/pull/113\">pull request</a>, I learned a lovely new word, &ldquo;hygge&rdquo;. For those who don&rsquo;t know it, the flavor of Markdown in remark.js, on which <strong>xaringan</strong> is based, supports the syntax <code>.foo[text]</code> (translated to <code>&lt;span class=&quot;foo&quot;&gt;text&lt;/span&gt;</code>, or <code>&lt;div&gt;...</code>), which is very neat. This is the first time a user has contributed some CSS making use of this syntax. I loved his design.</p>\n\n<p>This &ldquo;theme&rdquo; (not really a theme) is available with the name &ldquo;hygge&rdquo; in the <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan\">development version</a> of <strong>xaringan</strong>, which should be on CRAN soon (v0.6). To enable it, add <code>hygge</code> to the <code>css</code> option, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">output:\n  xaringan::moon_reader:\n    css: [&quot;default&quot;, &quot;default-fonts&quot;, &quot;hygge&quot;]\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>A few screenshots provided by Claus (and <a href=\"http://biostatistics.dk/presentations/xaringan/ghoul.html\">a full example</a>):</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/6160801/36378678-ffd093f8-157b-11e8-9718-7e4a9faef007.png#border\" alt=\"colored content boxes\" /></p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/6160801/36378683-03114d14-157c-11e8-9646-fe778db21a99.png#border\" alt=\"fancy picture effects\" /></p>\n\n<p>I called for help with new CSS themes in <strong>xaringan</strong> <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/10/xaringan-themes/\">last time</a>, and have received a few interesting contributions.
