It is the first time I have felt completely satisfied by the video conference equipment. The audio quality was amazing: I was able to clearly hear everyone in the room (they use a wireless microphone), and I feel it is even better than when I physically stand on a podium (in which case I sometimes cannot hear the audience clearly). There was a camera presumably on the ceiling in the front of the room, so I was able to see everyone in the room, too. The visual feedback is important when giving a remote talk. In the past, I often felt I was talking to a brick wall by myself in my room, and half an hour later, I had no idea whether the audience was still listening or even if the Internet connection was still good. Then their video conference system also showed me on a separate screen, so <a href=\"https://twitter.com/quiestrho/status/1006535865538146306\">we could actually see each other</a> during the talk (it was a little weird to see myself on an entire screen, though).</p>\n\n<p>For someone who usually does not prefer traveling (and cannot travel too far in a couple of years), I have to say this system is just perfect. I&rsquo;d like to thank Marcin Kierczak for the invite, and also thank their wonderful tech help desk (in particular, Svante Agnestig).</p>\n\n<p>BTW, my talk was titled &ldquo;Some Lesser Known Features of knitr&rdquo;, and <a href=\"https://bit.ly/2018-raukr\">slides are available here</a>.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/best-remote-talk/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 12 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"1aa5484ebac6c1546552bdd726bee269","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973461},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On Disagreement","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/on-disagreement/","description":"<p>There is a problem with being famous. When you become famous, you may lose some opportunities of hearing other people’s disagreements with you. There are a few possible reasons:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>They may not be confident enough with their own opinions, and think you must be correct when there is a disagreement.</p></li> \n <li><p>They may think you are wrong, but choose not to speak up anyway, because they are worried that everyone else would agree with you, which could make them unpopular.</p></li> \n <li><p>Out of courtesy, they don’t want to disagree. They know you often feel good about yourself, and they don’t want to destroy your fantasy.</p></li> \n <li><p>They just don’t bother arguing with you. For example, they may think you are so obviously wrong, or they just don’t care about you.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>So the actual number of disagreements should usually be higher than the number you have seen. From my experience of reading comments and feedback on my blog posts over the years, I feel the vast majority of readers wouldn’t express their disagreement. There is no way for me to tell if they really agree with me, or just choose not to speak up. This has always been a puzzle to me.</p> \n<p>Anyway, I always welcome different opinions. We all have blind spots, which is why different perspectives can be extremely helpful. My words, written or spoken, are not meant to be carved into stone. I may change my opinions, especially after I hear different thoughts or see different perspectives from a discussion with other people. Below are two examples:</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p><a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/closed-so-question/\">I ranted about some Stack Overflow users</a> and criticized them for closing posts too quickly. I said I didn’t understand why posts had to be closed. Colin Gillespie kindly explained it to me and defended the Stack Overflow community. I appreciated the explanation very much.</p></li> \n <li><p>Originally I linked to Aaron Swartz when <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/bookdown-crc/\">I was talking about open-access publications</a>. Maëlle Salmon felt uncomfortable with that and thought I was judging Aaron. I admit that some links in my posts are very subtle and deserve explanations, but sometimes I don’t have enough time or the topics are too sensitive.</p></li> \n</ul> \n<p>I wish more readers could be like Colin and Maëlle. It is totally fine that you don’t agree with me, or dislike what I said—please feel free to let me know in these cases. I think there is a good chance that we can reach an agreement after exchanging more thoughts. Even if we don’t, that is also fine.</p> \n<p>I’m totally open to critiques, too. There are things I hate in my own work and products (and actually, my self). If nobody mentions them, I may not be motivated to fix or improve them. If you criticize me, you will actually be helping me. Here are two examples:</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p>I’m not really satisfied by <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/\">the R Markdown book</a>. Before I started writing it, I explicitly aimed at publishing it as quickly as possible,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:On-one-hand-I-m\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/on-disagreement/#fn:On-one-hand-I-m\">1</a></sup> so I sacrificed a bit quality. I feel most chapters are fairly dry (especially those about output formats), and some chapters are too brief (e.g., Chapter 17). If people can point out the parts they particularly dislike, I will have a better idea on what to improve in a next edition. For example, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/bearloga/status/1012422401534058501\">Mikhail Popov mentioned</a> his struggles with customizing templates, and that was great feedback to me.</p></li> \n <li><p>Chester Ismay <a href=\"https://twitter.com/old_man_chester/status/994690785982345216\">published a review of the <strong>bookdown</strong> book</a> in The American Statistician this year, and pointed out several places that are confusing or too challenging in the book. I agree with his critiques. If I publish a second edition of this book in the future, I’ll make sure to take this review into consideration.</p></li> \n</ul> \n<p>In fact, occasionally I want to speak up my disagreement with certain people but I’d hesitate because I don’t know what their attitude is and I don’t want to offend or hurt them. I hope this post will remove the hesitation of readers who disagree with me. Going forward, I’m always looking forward to more of your corrections of my biases and mistakes.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/daaaad.gif\" alt=\"Feel free to correct me when you feel anything is wrong. Don't hesitate.\" /></p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:On-one-hand-I-m\">On one hand, I’m usually busy enough and could not afford too much time on writing the book. On the other hand, R Markdown had been invented for five years, and it should be a good time to have an official guide. I could aim at quality instead of publication speed, but I doubt if I would ever be able to finish the book. BTW, let’s keep our fingers crossed for “the Shiny book”, or the seven volumes of “<a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/plans-for-a-shiny-book/1080/15?u=yihui\">The Art of Reactive Programming</a>”! :) <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/on-disagreement/#fnref:On-one-hand-I-m\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/on-disagreement/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 24 Dec 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/daaaad.gif","linkMd5":"196b2db6a1406f4acd6b5505bb39d97d","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn83@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-34-225_3e5958584ba70e00.webp","destWidth":200,"destHeight":200,"sourceBytes":541126,"destBytes":488628,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/daaaad.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn83@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-34-225_3e5958584ba70e00.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973440},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On Adjectives","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/on-adjectives/","description":"<p>Among all types of words, I have always found adjectives the most challenging to use in communication (either writing or talking). They are challenging because (1) it is so hard to refrain from using them, and (2) it is hard to be precise.</p> \n<p>In my opinion, it is hard to refrain from using adjectives because usually we want to draw attention, and adjectives are the most effective type of words to draw attention. If we want to draw attention, we often tend to use adjectives that are biased and fail to represent facts. For example, we may use “supreme” when we only mean “good”, because we are afraid that other people won’t notice the good quality. Every time I go to grocery stores, I find labels like “<em>extra large</em> eggs” funny – as if the eggs would be a lot larger because of the label.</p> \n<p>The single piece of advice on writing that influenced me the most was this one:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>In writing. Don’t use adjectives which merely tell us how you want us to feel about the thing you are describing. I mean, instead of telling us a thing was “terrible,” describe it so that we’ll be terrified. Don’t say it was “delightful”; make us say “delightful” when we’ve read the description. You see, all those words (horrifying, wonderful, hideous, exquisite) are only like saying to your readers, “Please will you do my job for me.”</p> \n <p>— C.S. Lewis, Letter to Joan Lancaster, 26 June 1956</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Although I have reminded myself several times of this advice, I have to admit that I have failed numerous times to actually follow it. I often exaggerate, and don’t consider how readers would feel. Once a <strong>blogdown</strong><sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Perhaps-bookdown\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/on-adjectives/#fn:Perhaps-bookdown\">1</a></sup> user pointed out to me that I often use “just” and “simply” in my writing (e.g., “just do this” / “simply do that”), just like mathematicians often use “obviously” in proofs. These words are not necessary, and could potentially hurt readers: “You said ‘<em>simply</em> do these twelve things and I’ll be there?!’ How??” Sam Tyner’s <a href=\"https://twitter.com/sctyner/status/978821060220121088\">recent twitter thread</a> is an excellent example of how you could unconsciously hurt people by using words like “just” or “should”.</p> \n<p>The reason I use “just” or “simply” is that I tend to fool myself into thinking my tools are simple to use, and then hopefully fool readers, too, but the fact is that using the word “simply” alone won’t making tools simple. Only solid efforts in developing the tools will.</p> \n<p>In the real world, I have only found <a href=\"https://twitter.com/CMastication/status/959856196369793024\">a single person</a> so far who only states facts without using unnecessary adjectives or any tricks to draw attention in writing or talking. He is such a different person, and I admire him for his inner peace.</p> \n<p>I have hoped to write this post for long, because I see examples every now and then (including my own). After I saw the adjective <a href=\"http://blog.revolutionanalytics.com/2018/03/the-most-prolific-package-maintainers-on-cran.html\">“prolific”</a> today, I thought it might be a good time to write this post (I almost did it the last time when I saw <a href=\"http://blog.revolutionanalytics.com/2016/11/most-popular-r-packages.html\">“popular”</a>). To clarify, the purpose of this post is not to criticize anyone, but remind myself of either using adjectives appropriately, or just avoiding adjectives when possible.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Perhaps-bookdown\">Perhaps <strong>bookdown</strong>? I don’t remember. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/on-adjectives/#fnref:Perhaps-bookdown\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/on-adjectives/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 23 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"5049884620fea261c4634c69a1cd5809","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973462},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The R Markdown Source Documents of My Presentations","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/pres-rmd-source/","description":"<p>Since I have been asked several times how I created my slides, I’m going to share a little secret about my slides in this short post: most of my slides in the recent years were generated from R Markdown,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:The-more-recent\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/pres-rmd-source/#fn:The-more-recent\">1</a></sup> and you can easily obtain the <code>.Rmd</code> source documents if you replace the extension <code>.html</code> with <code>.Rmd</code> in the address bar of your web browser. That’s it. For example, for the slides at</p> \n<pre><code>https://slides.yihui.org/2018-blogdown-rstudio-conf-Yihui-Xie.html\n</code></pre> \n<p>The Rmd source file can be obtained from:</p> \n<pre><code>https://slides.yihui.org/2018-blogdown-rstudio-conf-Yihui-Xie.Rmd\n</code></pre> \n<p>I host most of my slides in my Dropbox folder and serve them through Updog,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:It-is-a-little-e\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/pres-rmd-source/#fn:It-is-a-little-e\">2</a></sup> so I don’t need to explicitly upload them to a server, and you have access to any files in this folder.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:The-more-recent\">The more recent ones were created via the <strong>xaringan</strong> package. Previously I had used Slidy, revealjs, and ioslides in R Markdown. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/pres-rmd-source/#fnref:The-more-recent\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:It-is-a-little-e\">It is a little expensive now, but I was fortunate enough to catch the train early when the lifetime membership only cost me five dollars. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/pres-rmd-source/#fnref:It-is-a-little-e\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/pres-rmd-source/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 06 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"c4454f0e4be3f435eddbdfd2db3ba44b","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973463},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The xaringan Math Limerick","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/xaringan-math-limerick/","description":"\n        <p>When my colleague Kevin Kuo asked a question about a problematic LaTeX math expression in a <strong>xaringan</strong> presentation, Claus Ekstrøm <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/issues/146\">replied with an amusing limerick</a>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>When dollars appear it&rsquo;s a sign<br />\nthat your code does not quite align<br />\nEnsure that your math<br />\nin xaringan hath<br />\nbeen placed on a single long line</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I laughed out loud. That was much better than my <a href=\"https://slides.yihui.org/xaringan/#16\">&ldquo;official&rdquo; documentation</a>!</p>\n\n<p><strong>Update on 2018-07-02</strong>: Another limerick by Claus <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/issues/151#issuecomment-401827615\">on three dashes</a>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Three dashes are used for a split<br />\nBut careful when using your wit<br />\n&lsquo;Cause you must erase<br />\nany sign of a space<br />\nor else it will just look like &hellip;</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/xaringan-math-limerick/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 12 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"d896b8b6548580df964a7719a9dd7a4b","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973461},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"My First Uncoast Unconference in 2019","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/uncoast-unconf/","description":"<p>When Sam emailed me about this idea of “uncoast unconference” in November 2018, I replied her email within five minutes with “Yes! And Yes!” I don’t usually reply emails so quickly, but I had wished for a Midwest R conference for years.</p> \n<p>Born in a poor family, I have always been a frugal person, even though I don’t consider myself poor any more. On the other hand, I usually don’t like traveling, either (especially by air, which is often time-consuming and costly). Sam explained the motivations of the uncoast unconference <a href=\"https://sctyner.github.io/uncoast-unconf.html\">in her blog post</a>, and the travel cost is one of them.</p> \n<p>I drove from Omaha to Des Moines for a little over two hours, and attended my first unconference in April 2019. I should have written this post after I came back, but haven’t had a chance. In the past few days, I saw the announcement of the <a href=\"https://twitter.com/UUnconf/status/1229796050392829953\">2020 Uncoast Unconference</a>, and thought I should write down some reflections before I completely forget this event. I’m sure I have forgotten many things, and this post is my attempt to recall the rest of them from memory:</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p>I liked this unconference a lot. One of its striking differences with all other conferences was the food—nothing fancy about the meals or snacks. As Sam mentioned in the post “<a href=\"https://www.aaaspolicyfellowships.org/blog/five-ps-make-most-professional-conferences\">The Five P’s: Make the Most of Professional Conferences</a>“:<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:1\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/uncoast-unconf/#fn:1\">1</a></sup></p> \n  <blockquote> \n   <p>Conferences tend to provide a lot of snacks, but they are often things like sweets and muffins.</p> \n  </blockquote> <p>To be honest, personally I wish all conferences (including rstudio::conf) <em>not</em> to provide snacks during breaks. The extra calories are absolutely unnecessary for me. The breakfasts and lunches are often already too good to me. Normally I’m able to control my desire for snacks, but I hate that I have to control it. I’d rather they don’t exist in the first place.</p></li> \n <li><p>I remember Sam mentioned at the last day’s dinner that she wanted the attendees to “have the full Iowa experience”. I don’t take pictures very often, but I took this boring one from my hotel room:</p> <p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/75079989-496c8180-54d0-11ea-993e-fc57a37c2928.jpg\" alt=\"The hotel in which I stayed during the unconfernce\" /></p> <p>I love this feeling of living in the woods. There is no doubt that I enjoyed the “Iowa experience”. During the icebreaker session before the unconfernce, Sam asked us if we loved the Midwest. Craig (Van Pay), a student at Iowa State, told me the criterion to check if someone truely loves the Midwest was whether he/she loves Ames. I absolutely do. Even Omaha is too big to me. I love small villages.</p> <p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/CKVanPay/status/1234839299696119809\"><img src=\"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/ESMGdH-WAAQby6s?format=jpg&amp;name=large\" alt=\"Craig and me, chatting about Ames at the door\" /></a></p></li> \n <li><p>I gave a crash course on HTML/CSS/JS on Day 0. I didn’t prepare the slides and just talked leisurely, since I was busy preparing the next three days’ meals for my family in the morning before I left (and they ordered pizza instead when I was away…). I thought I taught the tutorial quite poorly, but later I found <a href=\"https://uncoast-unconf.github.io/uu-2019-day-zero/04-html-css-js/Amelias-notes.html\">Amelia’s notes</a>, and I was astonished by how well she recorded what I mumbled at the podium.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:2\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/uncoast-unconf/#fn:2\">2</a></sup></p></li> \n <li><p>I discovered there was a ping pong table outside the conference room, and remembered someone said in her self-introduction that she liked playing ping pong. Eventually I found it was <a href=\"https://ranae.github.io\">Ranae Dietzel</a>. We played for about half an hour until other people started presenting their projects. Ranae’s ping pong skills were quite impressive! BTW, Laura Ellis just discovered <a href=\"https://twitter.com/RanaeDietzel/status/1228877735663194112\">Ranae’s tweet about <strong>patchwork</strong></a> last week, and I think <a href=\"https://twitter.com/LittleMissData/status/1229176433123168256\">Laura’s tweet</a> has become the single most viral #rstats tweet in history!</p></li> \n <li><p>I promised <a href=\"https://twitter.com/taraskaduk/status/1118591910384476160\">Taras Kaduk</a> that I’d write a blog post about this unconference, and finally here I am! I wrote the prototype of the <strong>rolldown</strong> package in a couple of hours, which surprised a few people because the productivity seemed to be too high. For this package, the prototype was easy for me because (1) I was familiar with the <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/html-widgets.html\">HTML Widgets</a> framework, and (2) the <a href=\"https://github.com/russellgoldenberg/scrollama\">scrollama library</a>’s API was relatively simple. Prototyping is often the easiest stage of developing a package. There will often be mountains of work in later stages. I was unable to afford more time on developing this package, so it should still be considered a prototype even if I have released an initial version to CRAN.</p> <p><img src=\"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/D4TaN5BWkAEr6Yk?format=jpg&amp;name=medium\" alt=\"Presenting rolldown at the 2019 uncoast unconference\" /></p></li> \n <li><p>In terms of productivity, I think the key is always to focus. Or in other words, to manage distractions. The unconference gives you a unique chance to hack on something for two days. When you are working with a group of peers side by side, it will be embarrassing if you continuously check email or social media. We do not need a huge amount of time to be productive—we do need an uninterrupted chunk time (it could be only two hours). During the unconference, I was only distracted by <a href=\"https://juliasilge.com/blog/datacamp-misconduct/\">one thing</a> on the first evening, and lost a couple of hours on it. Other than that, I was pretty much focused on the <strong>rolldown</strong> package.</p></li> \n <li><p>Sam asked us several questions in the icebreaker session and we stood by different sides of the room according to our answers. One question was “do you say coke or soda?” I decided that I would form my own group and not go with anyone else in the room because Chinese say neither coke nor soda. We actually say “Cola”. I thought I’d be unique, but to my great surprise, my PhD advisor Heike also joined my group. “Of course it is Cola!” The German said.</p></li> \n <li><p>It was great to catch up with some old friends such as my classmate Karsten Maurer and some RStudio colleagues at this unconference. I was also acquainted with a new friend there, Siqi Zhang. He was an interesting person, also helpful and enthusiastic. On our way back to the hotel, he basically wrote the <a href=\"https://cran.r-project.org/package=R6\"><strong>R6</strong></a> package with his tongue while sitting on the passenger seat in my car, without knowing the existence of <strong>R6</strong>. Five minutes later, I congratulated him on reinventing <strong>R6</strong>.</p></li> \n <li><p>Sam felt so excited when delivering the closing remarks on the last day that she couldn’t help crying in full joy. Yes, it was absolutely wonderful for these great people to get together and hack on interesting projects for two days. I really want to thank all the organizers, especially the other Sam (Helmich).</p></li> \n</ul> \n<p>I don’t plan to attend this unconference this year, but I recommend that you do. I don’t think you have a lot of chances to isolate yourself from the rest of the world for a couple of days and focus on coding with a group of people. Meanwhile, this year’s Day 0 workshop will be led by Amelia McNamara, the most amazing note-taker I mentioned above, and Ian Lyttle, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/AmeliaMN/status/1222990245991313408\">the nicest person on the Internet</a>. The application deadline is approaching, so I encourage that you apply soon: <a href=\"https://uuconf2020.rbind.io\">https://uuconf2020.rbind.io</a>.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:1\">In the picture in this post, you can see me in the jacket that I have worn for several years. When I appear in pictures with a jacket, I’m almost always in this jacket. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/uncoast-unconf/#fnref:1\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:2\">On a weakly related note, I was <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1230158493614837763\">also surprised</a> by the pronunciation of “sew” in <a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/lightning-talk-dr-amelia-mcnamara\">her rstudio::conf talk</a> this year. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/uncoast-unconf/#fnref:2\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Looking for a New Maintainer of the tikzDevice Package","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/tikzdevice-maintainer/","description":"\n        <blockquote>\n<p><strong>Update on 2018-06-19</strong>: We have found a new maintainer (Ralf Stubner), and the Github repo has been transferred to <a href=\"https://github.com/daqana/tikzDevice\">https://github.com/daqana/tikzDevice</a>.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Today the <strong>tikzDevice</strong> package was orphaned <a href=\"https://cran.rstudio.com/package=tikzDevice\">on CRAN</a>, mainly because neither Kirill Müller nor I have time to maintain it (both of us have other priorities). I <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2011/04/produce-authentic-math-formulas-in-r-graphics/\">still love</a> the fact that it makes it possible to write native LaTeX expressions in R plots and generate high-quality PDF. I still think it has the most appealing PDF vignette among all R packages I have seen.</p>\n\n<p>If anyone has time, understands C, and loves TikZ, please feel free to take over the maintainership. Since the package has been orphaned, you won&rsquo;t need to ask for our permission to take it over. I&rsquo;ll be happy to give you write access to the Github repo <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/tikzDevice\">yihui/tikzDevice</a>, or simply transfer this repo to you.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://db.yihui.org/images/tikzDevice-vignette.png#border\" alt=\"The tikzDevice vignette\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/tikzdevice-maintainer/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 14 Feb 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://db.yihui.org/images/tikzDevice-vignette.png#border","linkMd5":"4f3b1ed081e4794c30cfb4fa9d409f37","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn31@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-34-087_951667662c8c9049.webp","destWidth":1586,"destHeight":818,"sourceBytes":27847,"destBytes":53102,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://db.yihui.org/images/tikzDevice-vignette.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn31@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-34-087_951667662c8c9049.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973465},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"How to Put All Your Code in the Appendix in R Markdown","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/code-appendix/","description":"<p>My previous long post on <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/notebook-war/\">the notebook war</a> scratched the surface of literate programming in <strong>knitr</strong> and R Markdown. I don’t often talk about the “literate programming” aspect of <strong>knitr</strong>, but it is very interesting. I guess people mostly use <strong>knitr</strong> for the purpose of report generation from a linear combination of text and code. The power of “<em>programming</em> code chunks” is probably seriously underestimated. Note that here “program” is a verb.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/git-push-force.gif\" alt=\"The force is strong!\" /></p> \n<h2 id=\"why-code-in-the-appendix\">Why code in the appendix</h2> \n<p>Last week <a href=\"https://twitter.com/alexpghayes/status/1039170310560464897\">Alex Hayes showed an interesting trick</a> on how to put all your code in the appendix of a document. This can be very useful when you don’t want to distract your readers with program code in the body of your report, but want to give those who care about the computational details a chance to know the details. An appendix will be the natural place to go for these code (especially when your output format is PDF or other formats to be printed).</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-magical-ninja-alex-s-method\">The magical ninja Alex’s method</h2> \n<p>Alex combined the chunk option <code>code</code> with <code>knitr::purl()</code>:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\"># Code Appendix\n\n```{r code = readLines(knitr::purl(\"path/to/file.Rmd\", documentation = 1)), echo = T, eval = F}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>For those who don’t know the <code>code</code> option well, it is one way to provide the content of the code chunk. For example, the code chunk</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r code=c('1 + 1', 'print(\"Hello woRld\")')}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>is equivalent to</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r}\n1 + 1\nprint(\"Hello woRld\")\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>So the <code>code</code> option can be highly useful when the code of a chunk is <em>dynamic</em>. For example, you can read the code dynamically from an external R script.</p> \n<p>What <code>knitr::purl()</code> does is to extract all code chunks from a document and write them to an external file (an R script).</p> \n<p>Then with chunk options <code>eval = F</code> (don’t evaluate the code in this chunk again because the individual previous code chunks have been executed) and <code>echo = T</code> (display code of this chunk in the output document), you will have a code appendix.</p> \n<p>In Alex’s method, <code>path/to/file.Rmd</code> doesn’t have to be hard-coded, and can actually be replaced by <code>knitr::current_input()</code>, which gives you the path to the current input file.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-cool-hacker-lucy-s-method\">The cool hacker Lucy’s method</h2> \n<p>Although Alex’s method definitely works, it is not very efficient, because every time you compile the document, <strong>knitr</strong> has to parse the document twice (once for <code>knitr::knit()</code> and once for <code>knitr::purl()</code>).</p> \n<p>Among the replies to Alex’s original tweet, the very cool hacker<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:I-wish-I-had-tim\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/code-appendix/#fn:I-wish-I-had-tim\">1</a></sup>, Lucy D’Agostino McGowan, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/LucyStats/status/1039178545715662848\">pointed out the efficient way</a>:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r ref.label=knitr::all_labels(), echo = T, eval = F}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>After <strong>knitr</strong> parses a document, all code chunks and their labels are stored internally. You have access to these labels via <code>knitr::all_labels()</code>. With the <code>ref.label</code> option, you can pull all code chunks into this code chunk.</p> \n<h2 id=\"another-cool-hacker-in-a-parallel-universe\">Another cool hacker in a parallel universe</h2> \n<p>(Ten months later.)</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/aosmith16/status/1144669579630669829\">Ariel Muldoon</a> independently discovered <code>all_labels()</code>, and also tried to filter the chunk labels programmatically:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r getlabels, echo = FALSE}\nlabs = knitr::all_labels()\nlabs = labs[!labs %in% c(\"setup\", \"toc\", \"getlabels\", \"allcode\")]\n```\n\n```{r allcode, ref.label = labs, eval = FALSE}\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>Clever! You got it.</p> \n<h2 id=\"but-calling-a-single-function-doesn-t-sound-like-programming\">But calling a single function doesn’t sound like <em>programming</em>…</h2> \n<p>Some people may say that the taste of programming in “literate programming” seems to be too light. Don’t judge code by its length. Simplicity is definitely a merit. However, if your task is complicated enough, you can certainly program a <strong>knitr</strong> document in any way you like. The sky is your limit when you can program something.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Practically-your\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/code-appendix/#fn:Practically-your\">2</a></sup></p> \n<p><a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/40735727/559676\">Here is a slightly advanced example</a>, in which you can extract certain attributes of code chunks, and print them in the end of a document. For example, you can create an appendix with the names of all theorems in your document.</p> \n<p>The fact that <strong>knitr</strong>’s code chunk options are evaluated like function arguments (meaning that you can write arbitrary R code in chunk options) and <strong>knitr</strong> gives you a hook system (<code>knitr::knit_hooks</code>) to manipulate pretty much everything means infinite possibilities to those who want to customize and program <strong>knitr</strong> documents. There was only one thing upon which I hesitated when deciding whether I should give users the access. That is <code>knitr:::knit_code</code>. Here the triple-colon is obviously a danger sign. When you can even modify the content of a code chunk, I have no idea what can happen. Evil or creative? I’ll leave it to you to think about.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:I-wish-I-had-tim\">I wish I had time to write the “cool hacker” series of posts about those awesome R users. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/code-appendix/#fnref:I-wish-I-had-tim\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Practically-your\">Practically, your toddler’s mood is your limit. The sky doesn’t matter at all. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/code-appendix/#fnref:Practically-your\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/code-appendix/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 18 Sep 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/git-push-force.gif","linkMd5":"dd9d1b382b30daaaf3b30b458c4d0d3a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn11@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-113_4c5b194b42c32d6c.webp","destWidth":320,"destHeight":240,"sourceBytes":1769997,"destBytes":192082,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/git-push-force.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn11@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-113_4c5b194b42c32d6c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973445},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Serving a Website or Shiny App at 0.0.0.0","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/localhost-0-0-0-0/","description":"\n        <p>Last week I came across <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/52312957/559676\">a question on Stack Overflow</a>, in which the OP asked how to serve a <strong>blogdown</strong> website locally and let folks at home see it through the local network. That sounded like a fun thing to me, so I posted an answer.</p>\n\n<p>Basically all you need to do is serve the website at the IP address <code>0.0.0.0</code> instead of the default <code>127.0.0.1</code>, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">blogdown::serve_site(host = '0.0.0.0')\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Then all people with access to the local network should be able to see the website via your IP address (which typically looks like <code>192.168.0.x</code>) and the port number (<strong>blogdown</strong>&rsquo;s default is <code>4321</code>). If you are not sure about the IP address, take a look at your network preferences (or run the command <code>ifconfig</code> on macOS/Linux). The URL of your website should be of the form <code>http://192.168.0.x:4321</code>. You will see this address when you start serving the website on your computer.</p>\n\n<p>Similarly, you can serve Shiny apps at <code>0.0.0.0</code>, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">shiny::runApp(host = '0.0.0.0')\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>This is a convenient way to show websites or apps to people in the same local network. All kinds of circles emerging in my mind&hellip;</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/how-to-tie-a-tie.jpg\" alt=\"How to tie a tie\" /></p>\n\n<p>I have wished many times the function name were <code>stopif()</code> instead. Reasoning about negations is often trickier than affirmatives. Compare these examples:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">stopifnot(x == 1)\nstopifnot(x != 1)\n\nstopif(x == 1)\nstopif(x != 1)\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>To me, the <code>stopif()</code> versions are much clearer (<code>stopif(x)</code> means <code>if (x) stop()</code>, and <code>stopifnot(x)</code> means <code>if (!x) stop()</code>). That was one of the minor reasons why I created <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/testit\">the <strong>testit</strong> package</a> and wrote the <code>assert()</code> function. When you <code>assert()</code>, you must assert something is true.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">library(testit)\n\nassert(1.0 == 1)\n\nassert('1.0 is equal to 1', {\n  (1.0 == 1)\n})\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>This morning I opened the help page <code>?installed.packages</code>, and saw the argument <code>noCache = FALSE</code>. It was not the first time I had read this help page, but&hellip;</p>\n\n<p><code>noCache = FALSE</code>? Excuse me. I&rsquo;m slow. Just give me five seconds to decipher it again.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/lost-key.gif\" alt=\"How to find the key\" /></p>\n\n<p>Strunk and White actually recommended in <em>The Elements of Style</em> that &ldquo;we put statements in positive form&rdquo;, which I believe also applies to program code.</p>\n\n<p>Well, if you look at <code>?download.file</code>, there is an argument <code>cacheOK = TRUE</code>. Sigh&hellip; Why the name <code>cacheOK</code>? A very typical example is a Github repo that has no description, website URL, or topics, which looks like this:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/42730615-8b35a0be-87be-11e8-8095-f891230134be.png#border\" alt=\"No Description, Website, or Topics Provided\" /></p>\n\n<p>I sigh a sigh every time I see it. I don&rsquo;t know what the repo owner is thinking. If one just prefers hiding oneself in public, the lack of more information makes sense, otherwise I think a description and a README are the most basic elements you should provide in a Github repo. You probably don&rsquo;t expect everyone to read your source code to know what this repo actually does.</p>\n\n<p>Better, you should provide a URL to a website associated with this repo. It could be a documentation website, or other types of websites. Even if the repo is the special and conventional <code>*.github.io</code> (meaning Github Pages), you should provide a link explicitly so that visitors can simply click it. I have tried to copy the text <code>*.github.io</code> and paste it in my browser&rsquo;s address bar many times to open such websites when a repo does not provide an explicit and clickable link (yes, I&rsquo;m this curious to read other people&rsquo;s websites). Sometimes I even have to open the <code>CNAME</code> file in the repo to figure out the website URL. I don&rsquo;t think many people are as curious as me in these cases, so unless you want to hide yourself intentionally, please consider providing a description and a website in your Github repo (click the Edit button and fill out the two text fields).</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/bad-marketing/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 14 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/42730615-8b35a0be-87be-11e8-8095-f891230134be.png#border","linkMd5":"ca086368968cb56775a96de1758cdc8f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn80@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-35-100_3ceeca49c8750785.webp","destWidth":716,"destHeight":216,"sourceBytes":28700,"destBytes":12938,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/42730615-8b35a0be-87be-11e8-8095-f891230134be.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn80@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-35-100_3ceeca49c8750785.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Thanks, Alicia Schep, for Digging into knitr Engines","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/alicia-knitr-engine/","description":"\n        <p>Alicia Schep recently discovered an interesting fact about <strong>knitr</strong>: you can not only use other languages in R Markdown (Python, Julia, C++, Bash, SQL, and so on), but also actually have full control of <em>how</em> to run such code chunks. While thinking about improving the <strong>gganimate</strong> support in <strong>knitr</strong> (per <a href=\"https://twitter.com/thomasp85/status/1021456429855780865\">Thomas&rsquo;s request</a>), I learned a little more about <strong>gifski</strong>. The usage seems to be quite straightforward to me, so I wrote a hook function in <strong>knitr</strong> today to support this package, which means you can easily create a GIF animation from a code chunk now if you have installed <strong>gifski</strong>, and at least version 1.20.14 of <strong>knitr</strong>:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">install.packages('gifski')\nif (packageVersion('knitr') &lt; '1.20.14') {\n  remotes::install_github('yihui/knitr')\n}\n</code></pre>\n\n<h2 id=\"basic-usage\">Basic usage</h2>\n\n<p>To make use of <strong>gifski</strong>, you may set the chunk option <code>animation.hook='gifski'</code>. Below is an R Markdown example:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, animation.hook='gifski'}\nfor (i in 1:2) {\n  pie(c(i %% 2, 6), col = c('red', 'yellow'), labels = NA)\n}\n```\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>This will produce the &ldquo;Pac man&rdquo; below (assuming the output format is HTML):</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/44246516-30c93000-a1a4-11e8-8aa5-8876e51a227f.gif\" alt=\"A gif animation of Pac man\" /></p>\n\n<h2 id=\"customization\">Customization</h2>\n\n<p>The main things you may want to tweak for the GIF animation are perhaps the dimension (controlled by <code>fig.width</code> and <code>fig.height</code>, or <code>fig.dim</code>), and <code>interval</code> (the delay between frames), e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, animation.hook='gifski', interval=0.2, fig.width=4}\nfor (i in 1:2) {\n  pie(c(i %% 2, 6), col = c('red', 'yellow'), labels = NA)\n}\n```\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>There are other options that you can customize, but I guess you probably don&rsquo;t care (e.g., <code>dpi</code>). Note that the graphical device must produce PNG images since <strong>gifski</strong> only supports PNG at the moment, which means the chunk option <code>dev</code> must be a PNG device (e.g., <code>'png'</code> or <code>'CairoPNG'</code>).</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"ffmpeg-also-works\">FFmpeg also works</h2>\n\n<p>You can also use <a href=\"https://ffmpeg.org\">FFmpeg</a> to create a GIF animation, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r, animation.hook='ffmpeg', ffmpeg.format='gif', dev='jpeg'}\nfor (i in 1:2) {\n  pie(c(i %% 2, 6), col = c('red', 'yellow'), labels = NA)\n}\n```\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Without setting <code>ffmpeg.format='gif'</code>, you will get a video instead (the default format is WebM). Gifski claims to be &ldquo;the fastest GIF encoder in the universe&rdquo;, and the package <strong>gifski</strong> does not have system dependencies (you don&rsquo;t need to install external software like FFmpeg), so if you want to create a GIF, it is probably the best way to go.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"please-test-it-yourself\">Please test it yourself</h2>\n\n<p>Please help me test the development version of <strong>knitr</strong>, and I&rsquo;ll appreciate your feedback. Many thanks to the R ninja Jeroen Ooms for the <strong>gifski</strong> package! For <strong>gganimate</strong> users, I guess a tighter integration between <strong>knitr</strong> and <strong>gganimate</strong> should be coming soon. <a href=\"https://github.com/thomasp85/gganimate/issues/134\">Please stay tuned</a>.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/gifski-knitr/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 16 Aug 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/44246516-30c93000-a1a4-11e8-8aa5-8876e51a227f.gif","linkMd5":"e9b9b33dc278143810987874de4af924","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn95@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-33-781_7d758e03064be7a1.webp","destWidth":672,"destHeight":480,"sourceBytes":12717,"destBytes":10552,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/44246516-30c93000-a1a4-11e8-8aa5-8876e51a227f.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn95@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-33-781_7d758e03064be7a1.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973447},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On source(\"https://bioconductor.org/biocLite.R\")","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/source-bioclite/","description":"\n        <p>About six years ago when <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2012/10/visiting/\">I visited Bioconductor</a> for the first time in Seattle, I asked them a question that I had for long: Why is <code>source(&quot;https://bioconductor.org/biocLite.R&quot;)</code> the recommended way to install Bioconductor? Unfortunately, I don&rsquo;t remember their answer now, but I guess I was not convinced at that time anyway. Sourcing an R script directly from the web was an obviously very bad idea in my eyes. If you open this script, you will see that it further sources another script from bioconductor.org (and even tries to fall back to the insecure HTTP URL if HTTPS fails), which in turn sources yet another script. If the web server is compromised and someone injects something like <code>system('rm -rf /')</code> in these scripts, users will be in deep deep trouble <em>immediately</em>.</p>\n\n<p>Yesterday I saw <a href=\"https://twitter.com/strnr/status/1022451016736927745\">Stephen Turner&rsquo;s tweet</a> about the new <strong>BiocManager</strong> package, and he recommended that we stop using the <code>biocLite()</code> function. While we should definitely thank the security team at Bioconductor for keeping the server secure for several years, I feel the <code>source()</code> way of installing Bioconductor should be retired as soon as possible.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/source-bioclite/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 27 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"c8cdf1f1fbe66a15118c6bc6c4fe79cc","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973458},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The First Bookdown Contest","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/first-bookdown-contest/","description":"\n        <p>This morning I announced <a href=\"https://twitter.com/rstudio/status/1022865820869976065\">the first <strong>bookdown</strong> contest</a>. I wished to launch this contest last year but hadn&rsquo;t had a chance. Basically I was hoping to encourage <strong>bookdown</strong> users to share their templates and examples, so that other people can reuse them. One motivation was writing academic dissertations. I really wish graduate students won&rsquo;t need to waste too much time on formatting their dissertations like I (and presumably many other former students) did. To quote <a href=\"https://twitter.com/juliesquid/status/1022941425036664833\">JuliaStewart Lowndes</a>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>[&hellip;] I wish I could go back in time and write my dissertation in bookdown&hellip;</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/43284090-651365f8-90e0-11e8-8092-a9b10775fda0.png\" alt=\"The first bookdown contest\" /></p>\n\n<p>Details about this contest can be found in <a href=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2018/07/27/first-bookdown-contest/\">this blog post</a>. Instead, you may want a character string <code>&quot;a, b, and c&quot;</code> (Oxford comma FTW!). In 2014, I gave a guest lecture in a course at Iowa State. I prepared an example in which I wanted to output the names of some genes dynamically with an inline R expression in a <strong>knitr</strong> document. In the beginning, I didn&rsquo;t want to spend too much time on this task, so I just used <code>`r paste(x, collapse = ', ')`</code>, which would output a character string like <code>&quot;a, b, c&quot;</code>. This did not feel natural in the context (e.g., &ldquo;We identified genes a, b, c.&rdquo;).</p>\n\n<p>Driven by perfectionism, I decided to write a function, which ended up in the <strong>knitr</strong> package as <code>knitr::combine_words()</code>. The implementation was actually quite simple. Basically, for a single word, it will just return this word; for two words A and B, it return <code>&quot;A and B&quot;</code>; for three or more words, it returns <code>&quot;A, B, C, ..., Y, and Z&quot;</code>. The function also has a few arguments that can customize the output. For example, if you want to output the words in pairs of backticks, you may use <code>knitr::combine_words(x, before = '`')</code>:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">knitr::combine_words(LETTERS[1:5], before = '`')\n# output: `A`, `B`, `C`, `D`, and `E`\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Since then, I have used this function by myself several times. Today I saw <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/50899805/559676\">a Stack Overflow question</a> that asked about the same thing, so I thought it might be a good idea to reveal this little function in <strong>knitr</strong>, although I&rsquo;m relatively sure someone else must have also implemented it independently in other packages.</p>\n\n<p>P.S. On an only marginally related note, here is a cat trying to demonstrate the importance of the &ldquo;Oxford comma&rdquo; (the Oxford cat?):</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/camouflage-3.jpg\" alt=\"Meerkat, meerkat, the Oxford cat, and meerkat\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/knitr-combine-words/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 18 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/camouflage-3.jpg","linkMd5":"c3cac0a7cd3ddc730ebf0aa17731a4b4","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn35@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-36-267_d0f06dc9e594a400.webp","destWidth":500,"destHeight":375,"sourceBytes":41535,"destBytes":36450,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/camouflage-3.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn35@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-36-267_d0f06dc9e594a400.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Only One Person Can Help You with That","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/single-driver/","description":"\n        <p>There is only one thing that really concerns me in open source software development. It is never about how cool the technology is, or how smart a developer is. It is the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bus_factor\">bus factor</a>.</p>\n\n<p>A couple of days ago I saw <a href=\"http://r.789695.n4.nabble.com/CRAN-Update-protocol-buffers-on-macOS-for-RProtoBuf-td4750815.html\">an interesting reply</a> from Prof Ripley (I have to take a deep breath right now) re: building the macOS binary of an R package, in which he said:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Only one person can help you with that, and it is most effective to contact him directly.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I think you all know whom he meant. The whole R world on macOS is pretty much on his shoulders. Help us, Simon Wan Kenobi, you are our <a href=\"http://scifiquotes.net/quotes/152_Youre-My-Only-Hope\">only hope</a>. We all macOS R users should be extremely grateful to him. But&hellip;</p>\n\n<p>That is also what I&rsquo;m deeply worried about. When users run into certain problems, only one person can help. One person managing more than 12,000 binary packages (although most should be easy to build).</p>\n\n<p>I have been waiting for CRAN to fully embrace Homebrew &ndash; not only the Homebrew packages, but the Homebrew development model: <em>let the most motivated people work on the particular problems</em>, instead of having one person to manage them all. You care about a certain binary package? Then you bring a pull request to build it yourself, and make sure you pass the automated tests. The repo maintainer does not really have to know well about every single package.</p>\n\n<p>To some degree, building an open source project is like growing cucumbers. When the project is large enough, it is much more important and sustainable to build a trellis for cucumbers to grow by themselves than taking care of individual cucumbers.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/single-driver/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 10 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"d099ab1093b3d5f07b33c6808ada9d45","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Do You Have to Use FontAwesome or Other Libraries for Web Symbols?","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/character-entity/","description":"\n        <p>As a software developer, I prefer lightweight stuff and avoid dependencies when possible. Over the years, I have been leaning more and more towards &ldquo;frugality&rdquo;. I used to be big fan of libraries like jQuery and FontAwesome, but now I rarely use them any more. I started to write vanilla JavaScript after I suddently discovered <code>document.querySelector()</code> one day (before that, I thought <code>$()</code> was the only way to select a node in the world).</p>\n\n<p>Similarly, I stopped using special libraries such as FontAwesome for icons and symbols after I looked through the full list of <a href=\"https://dev.w3.org/html5/html-author/charref\">HTML character entities</a>. Yes, this list of symbols is much shorter than that in FontAwesome, but I often refrain from using fancy symbols, so this list is rich enough for me.</p>\n\n<p>Most of the time, I only need a couple of symbols, so I really don&rsquo;t want to import a full external JS/CSS library. For example, yesterday I wanted to add icons after those external links on <a href=\"https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com\">the <strong>rmarkdown</strong> website</a>, to indicate that they will be opened in new windows. Eventually <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/commit/02a87d69bf\">I found and added the entity <code>&amp;boxbox;</code></a> (double boxes &boxbox;), which was a perfect symbol for my purpose.</p>\n\n<p>Last year, when I was designing <a href=\"https://xmag.yihui.org\">the hugo-xmag theme</a> for Hugo, I also got an inspiration from the list of HTML entities: I realized I could easy turn a letter into a Blackletter, e.g., <code>&amp;Afr;</code> is &Afr;, and <code>&amp;Bfr;</code> is &Bfr;, etc. So the default site name is generated in Blackletter in the hugo-xmag theme. It looked cool to me, and didn&rsquo;t need any third-party dependencies.</p>\n\n<p>Certain HTML entities may not work in all web browsers, but I don&rsquo;t really care. For my personal websites, I have no intention to support legacy browsers.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/character-entity/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"87e4d279196a47042d0782f34b9ab93d","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Yue Jiang: A Ninja with Sharingan","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/yue-jiang/","description":"\n        <p>So you all know that the package name <strong>xaringan</strong> was from <a href=\"http://naruto.wikia.com/wiki/Sharingan\">Sharingan in Naruto</a>. I&rsquo;m definitely looking forward to more wonderful themes.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/36743657-654b0b48-1bb0-11e8-8ada-3fcffeea957f.png\" alt=\"The TAMU theme in xaringan\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/tamu-theme-xaringan/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 27 Feb 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/36743657-654b0b48-1bb0-11e8-8ada-3fcffeea957f.png","linkMd5":"f8deb6fd3915b2838099b8b18f0a3e6b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn43@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-34-723_00e6c42b7207c34d.webp","destWidth":678,"destHeight":510,"sourceBytes":51139,"destBytes":11740,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/36743657-654b0b48-1bb0-11e8-8ada-3fcffeea957f.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn43@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-34-723_00e6c42b7207c34d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973464},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Two Roads Diverged in a Wood; Take One without Blocking the Other","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/","description":"<p>When two roads diverge in a yellow wood, you often have to take one as a traveler. In software development, we often face such difficult decisions as developers. Sometimes one road may seem to be “obviously” better than the other, so you make the decision for your potential users, and only support that road.</p> \n<p>The only problem is “obviously”. Obviousness is in the eye of the beholder.</p> \n<p>When designing the <strong>knitr</strong> package, I thought it was obvious that the working directory when running a code chunk should be the parent directory of the input file, instead of whatever the current <code>getwd()</code> is. This decision turned out to be the one that users hated most. I think I can still defend my decision, but I can also see their point and cannot deny the confusion caused by this decision. Anyway, <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/limit-of-bureaucracy/\">I didn’t block the other way</a>.</p> \n<h2 id=\"to-docker-or-not-to-docker\">To Docker or not to Docker</h2> \n<p>Recently, Patrick, one of the contributors of <strong>xaringan</strong>, wrote a function <code>decktape()</code> to export slides to PDF via DeckTape, which looked like this originally:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">decktape = function(file, output) {\n  args = shQuote(c(file, output))\n  system2('docker', c(\n    'run', '--rm', '-t', '-v', '`pwd`:/slides', '-v',\n    '$HOME:$HOME', 'astefanutti/decktape', args\n  ))\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>He liked Docker, and I could definitely see the benefits of Docker. DeckTape can run with or without Docker, like any other software packages, but DeckTape is a NodeJS package, so you know… The lovely dependency hell there…</p> \n<p>When I saw Patrick’s function, I decided to make it work without Docker, too. The reason was that the alternative way was trivially easy to implement. For conciseness, the four dots <code>....</code> below indicates identical code in the above function so that you can easily see the extra code I added:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">decktape = function(...., docker = Sys.which('decktape') == '') {\n  ....\n  if (docker) system2(....) else system2('decktape', args)\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>That is low cost in implementation, but high return in usability. There might be users who’d rather deal with the Node dependency hell instead of pulling a Docker image of several hundred megabytes.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Well-if-you-npm\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/#fn:Well-if-you-npm\">1</a></sup> It is not necessary to block their way or convince them why Docker is the ultimate best solution.</p> \n<p>BTW, note that the default <code>docker = Sys.which('decktape') == ''</code> means if the user has chosen to install <code>decktape</code> locally and it can be found via the <code>PATH</code> variable, <code>xaringan::decktape()</code> will not use Docker (i.e., <code>docker = FALSE</code>). The default should reflect the user’s intention reasonably well. If the guess is incorrect, they can manually specify <code>docker = TRUE</code> or <code>FALSE</code>.</p> \n<h2 id=\"from-file-or-not-from-file\">From File or not from File</h2> \n<p>Here is another example of two roads in a wood: <a href=\"https://github.com/jeroen/jsonlite/issues/265\">jeroen/jsonlite#265</a>. I don’t mean to criticize the bestest ninja in the R community (i.e., Jeroen), but this example is very representative in my eyes. I want to talk about it so others can learn from it. Basically, when there is a function that is supposed to deal with text data, it is common that the first argument of the function can be either the text or a file path (or a URL, etc.). A common trick is like this:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">process_text = function(x) {\n  if (file.exists(x)) x = readLines(x)\n  paste(x, collapse = '\\n')\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>So both <code>process_text('foo/bar.txt')</code> and <code>process_text(c('a', 'b'))</code> will work. This looks very neat, but the problem is, what if the input <code>x</code> is literally a character string that only happens to be a real file path?<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:The-function-sho\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/#fn:The-function-sho\">2</a></sup> And what if the user entered a wrong file path by accident?<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:The-function-sho\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/#fn:The-function-sho\">3</a></sup> The cleverness will bite us in these cases. What I often do is this:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">process_text = function(x, content = readLines(x)) {\n  paste(content, collapse = '\\n')\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>It provides both ways to users. They can process either a text file, or a character string. They know more about their data, and we don’t have to guess for them.</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">process_text('foo/bar.txt')\n# if foo/bar.txt is not meant to be a file path\nprocess_text(content = 'foo/bar.txt')\n# signal an error if the file path is wrong\nprocess_text('foo/baz.txt')\n</code></pre> \n<p>Actually <code>jsonlite::fromJSON()</code> is cleverer than that in guessing. <a href=\"https://github.com/jeroen/jsonlite/blob/a68e1d46e2ae6450cd46c12be0e32fd66d12a90c/R/fromJSON.R#L86\">It has multiple rules</a> to decide whether the input is text or a file path, but no matter how clever it is, it still can backfire if the user doesn’t have a way to tell it if the input is text or a file path, because guessing, is guessing, after all.</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\"># what if there is really a file named [1]?\nxfun::in_dir(tempdir(), {\n  f = '[1]'\n  writeLines('[1, 2, 3]', f)\n  on.exit(file.remove(f))\n  jsonlite::fromJSON(f)  # expecting c(1, 2, 3) but returns c(1)\n})\n</code></pre> \n<p>FWIW, <a href=\"https://github.com/jeroen/jsonlite/commit/e384ddd\">Jeroen has added a new function</a> <code>jsonlite::parse_json()</code> that only deals with text input, which is great!</p> \n<p>In short, we could absolutely love one road, or be clever on one road. Just make sure you have thought about whether it makes sense to block the other road.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/latex-ignore.gif\" alt=\"This way or that way? Whichever you like.\" /></p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Well-if-you-npm\">Well, if you <code>npm install -g decktape</code>, you may also end up with using a lot of your disk space because of the way <code>npm</code> manages packages. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/#fnref:Well-if-you-npm\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:The-function-sho\">The function should return the character string, instead of reading the file. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/#fnref:The-function-sho\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:The-function-sho\">The function should signal an error instead of returning the character string of the wrong file path. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/#fnref:The-function-sho\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/two-roads/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 29 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/latex-ignore.gif","linkMd5":"150eeab57baee338cd9c7efbd252bfec","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn15@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-475_e580940589d113b8.webp","destWidth":399,"destHeight":215,"sourceBytes":1048126,"destBytes":892206,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/latex-ignore.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn15@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-475_e580940589d113b8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973440},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"How Will an Animated GIF Work in a Textbook?","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/animated-gif-textbook/","description":"\n        <p>When I gave my <a href=\"http://slides.yihui.org/2016-ASAChicago-bookdown-Yihui-Xie.html\">first public talk on <strong>bookdown</strong></a> in Chicago in 2016, I mentioned a few motivations behind this project. One of them was that I believed books should/could have richer content. I meant there could be videos, dynamic or interactive content in a book. For example, readers could be able to fit a model right inside the book if you embed a Shiny app.</p>\n\n<p>Yesterday (i.e. two years later) I finally saw a real example from <a href=\"https://twitter.com/MattCrump_/status/1018897122635239424\">a tweet by Matt Crump</a>, where he showed an animated GIF that demonstrates how small sample sizes could fool you when you look for a correlation between two variables.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://crumplab.github.io/statistics/gifs/corUnifFourNs-1.gif\" alt=\"how correlation behaves when there is no correlation\" /></p>\n\n<p>Nick Brown <a href=\"https://twitter.com/sTeamTraen/status/1018875230805258240\">asked</a> in a reply &ldquo;How will an animated GIF work in a textbook?&rdquo; I was excited to learn that Matt was working on a book written with <strong>bookdown</strong>, so the answer was &ldquo;Read the web version of the book.&rdquo; You can find the GIF in their text book &ldquo;<a href=\"https://crumplab.github.io/statistics/Correlation.html#some-more-movies\">Answering Questions with Data</a>&rdquo;. That is exactly the type of books I was looking forward to.</p>\n\n<p>BTW, actually I used a similar idea in <a href=\"https://yihui.org/animation/example/sim-qqnorm/\">the function <code>animation::sim.qqnorm()</code></a> to show that even when the actual distribution is Normal, the points in the QQ plot do not necessarily line up nicely on the diagonal. When the sample size is small, the uncertainty could be so big to fool you into believing that you have discovered patterns which are essentially artifacts.</p>\n\n<video controls loop autoplay><source src=\"https://assets.yihui.org/figures/animation/example/sim-qqnorm/demo-a.mp4?dl=1\" /><p>Simulated QQ plots</p></video>\n\n<p>I should also mention <a href=\"https://www.stat.auckland.ac.nz/~wild/VIT/\">the VIT project</a> (Visual Inference Tools) started by Chris Wild, in which you can find more interesting examples related to this topic.</p>\n\n<p>Oh, I should also mention one of <a href=\"https://twitter.com/_ColinFay/status/1012964820004548609\">Colin Fay&rsquo;s favorite tech-related memes</a>:</p>\n\n<p><img
