I rudely refused the help, and stubbornly went my own way, vanishing into the darkness.</p> \n<p>Now I feel the fundamental reason why I could not stop procrastination was because procrastination didn’t really hurt (enough). I procrastinated on <strong>cranvas</strong> because it didn’t hurt me. I was still able to survive and complete my PhD. Had my advisors said “I wouldn’t let you graduate unless you resume the work on <strong>cranvas</strong>”, I’d definitely give up <strong>knitr</strong> and COS (at least temporarily).</p> \n<p>Simply put, you just won’t procrastinate when you realize you are going to starve soon.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-fundamental-cure\">The fundamental cure</h2> \n<p>That was the direct cure, and it was not the best or fundamental cure. Again, I’m a self-motivated person and an introvert. Changing my personality in a short time will be hard. I do have my strengths, and the question is how to better take advantage of them.</p> \n<p>The answer turned out to be fairly simple. I just tell the company what I want to do, and they will think if the interest of the company is aligned with mine. Guess what? They have rarely said “No” to me. They value and respect my personal interests very much.</p> \n<p>Problem solved — if only I could talk to them.</p> \n<p>Before that, I was very afraid of telling them my thoughts, because I was afraid of rejections. I’d imagine them saying “Hey Yihui, you shall work on Shiny since Joe is so busy”, and “There is no point of working on R Markdown since JJ is on it.” These imaginary conversations may sound funny, but they show how poor I was at communication.</p> \n<p>My deep depression continued until I started working on <strong>bookdown</strong> in late 2015. It was a project that I wished to work on for at least two years. It was not only a project that I loved, but also a project in which Tareef patiently coached me to become a professional software engineer (that was why I thanked him in both the <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/bookdown/acknowledgments.html\"><strong>bookdown</strong> book</a> and the <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/blogdown/acknowledgments.html\"><strong>blogdown</strong> book</a>). The most important lesson I learned was: take some serious time to plan ahead, and try your best to stick with the plan in case of all kinds of distractions. This is drastically different with developing “selfish open-source software”. With the latter, you just do whatever you want, and whenever you like. No commitment, no responsibilities, no guarantee. While the freedom could make the developer fairly efficient sometimes (no pressure), it is harmful in the long run. You will be at best successful with a handful of things in a rather narrow field. Once you move outside of your comfort zone, your life will be utterly miserable.</p> \n<p>Another benefit of making a plan in advance is that you will be better at estimating the time to finish a project. Besides all weaknesses I mentioned above, I have yet another one: I’m bad at estimating time. More often than not, I need twice as much time as I originally imagined. For example, when I tell Tareef I’ll finish something in two weeks, it often ends up taking me four weeks or even more.</p> \n<p>To be honest, I’m still not comfortable with making plans and writing them down. I feel I’m busy enough every day, and don’t want to think what I want to do tomorrow, next week, or next year. But I’m still trying to think about it even only for one or two minutes every night before I go to bed. It is very helpful. In particular, it helps a lot with pushing away things that are not important but look urgent. You can always compare your plan with those “urgent” things, and decide the priorities.</p> \n<p>Among the many emails that Tareef sent to help me in early 2015, he once quoted Chris Hadfield (the astronaut):</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>“Decide in your heart of hearts what really excites you and challenges you, and start moving your life in that direction. Every decision you make, from what you eat to what you do with your time tonight, turns you into who you are tomorrow, and the day after that. Look at who you want to be, and start sculpting yourself into that person. You may not get exactly where you thought you’d be, but you will be doing things that suit you in a profession you believe in. Don’t let life randomly kick you into the adult you don’t want to become.”</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Don’t let life randomly kick you into the adult you don’t want to become.</p> \n<p>I think Tareef’s diagnostics of me were very accurate. I was basically letting life randomly kick me around. I had my strong passion (for software), and I had my life goals, but I constantly drowned in the random world.</p> \n<p>2016, <strong>bookdown</strong>, made a plan, and done. 2017, <strong>blogdown</strong>, made a plan, and done.</p> \n<p>It seems I have gained better control over my life now, but I clearly know that there is no cure that works once and for all. I still have to reflect on my failures and mistakes from time to time, because it is just so very easy and tempting to start drifting again.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-tumor\">The tumor</h2> \n<p>I’m going to digress a bit again here, to describe an incident in Oct 2015. Remember, “Yihui has always got issues.” One day I suddenly had a pain in the back neck. Initially I thought it was due to an injury when I lifted a heavy object upstairs a few days ago. In the following days, the pain became severer and severer. I had never had so much pain in my body. I went to see a doctor (for the first time in my five years in the US — I had been very healthy). A couple of weeks later, the MRI showed a tumor in my neck.</p> \n<p>Long story short, I had a surgery in December (first time in my life) and the tumor turned out to be benign. Thankfully, everything seemed to be fine. I wasn’t too scared when the tumor was first discovered, but I did think about life and death.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:It-was-also-refl\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/#fn:It-was-also-refl\">4</a></sup> It would be a shame if I died when I was still a terrible person (on my tombstone they would write “here lies a guy, irresponsible, and a failure”). Although it sounds like a cliche, you will be better at sorting out priorities today if you are going to die tomorrow. You wouldn’t care if “<a href=\"https://xkcd.com/386/\">someone is WRONG on the Internet</a>”, or someone is not convinced by your work, or someone wants a figure caption to be bold.</p> \n<p>But I still forget this from time to time. For example, why should I care about someone who didn’t buy the convenience of <strong>roxygen2</strong> a few weeks ago? I really shouldn’t have replied to that email. It was completely a mistake.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-future\">The future</h2> \n<p>I want to become a professional and responsible software engineer, and more importantly, I wish I won’t have to struggle much to work on my less favorite projects (such as <strong>DT</strong>). I’ll make fewer promises to the random world, because my past records show that I often failed to keep them, and the failures made me feel very guilty. I hate that feeling.</p> \n<p>I have been working 100% on open source packages since I joined RStudio. Although I’m definitely biased from the inside view, I have to say RStudio’s commitment to open source is amazing, considering that RStudio is a relatively small company. Both RStudio and I believe firmly in open source. I feel extremely proud of working for such a company. At RStudio, I can be rest assured that I don’t need to worry about other issues as long as I can focus on my work. There is zero bureaucracy in the company. I only need to spend about 10 minutes on a weekly meeting, and no time on traffic (since I work from home). Work hard, deliver results as planned, and I’ll be paid well. It is just that simple. Isn’t it a dream company? It is hard to imagine that I was so blind back in the early days. I need to value the opportunities.</p> \n<p>Starting an open source project is easy, but building it into a mature product is much harder. I think many people have discovered this fact, such as the very creative Romain Francois (he said this in <a href=\"https://r-podcast.org/episode/023-interviews-francois-pedersen/\">an R podcast</a>). As a matter of fact, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wei_Zheng\">Wei Zheng</a> in the Tang dynasty of China also said it well 1500 years ago: “善始者实繁，克终者盖寡” (many people can start but few can finish).<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:From-https-zh-wi\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/#fn:From-https-zh-wi\">5</a></sup> I wish I could develop a more solid habit of consistently carrying a product all the way to its destination.</p> \n<p>I want to quote Ray Dalio again to finalize this post. I went through a long period of pain and made bad mistakes, but the wise thing to do is embrace pain and mistakes:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>“Instead of feeling frustrated or overwhelmed, I saw pain as nature’s reminder that there is something important for me to learn.”</p> \n <p>“The most valuable habit I’ve acquired is using pain to trigger quality reflections.”</p> \n <p>“Everyone makes mistakes. The main difference is that successful people learn from them and unsuccessful people don’t.”</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Thanks, Tareef, JJ, and Joe.</p> \n<hr /> \n<p><strong>Update on 2018-02-17</strong>: After I published this post, I received a lot of feedback in a short period of time, and I truly appreciate all your thoughts, encouragements, and care. I will see if I could summarize them in the future. For now, I just want to clarify it to those who were concerned about my mental health issues: I’m no longer depressed. The years 2016 and 2017 have been fantastic, and I have mostly recovered. Thanks again!</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Stephanie-was-th\">Stephanie was the happiest graduate student in the department. I still miss her laughter as well as Prof Dan Nordman’s. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/#fnref:Stephanie-was-th\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:As-Prof-Tian-Zhe\">As Prof Tian Zheng <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tz33cu/status/964950098223525888\">pointed out</a>, it should be more of a look of concern instead of disappointment, which was later <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tz33cu/status/964950098223525888\">confirmed</a> by Di. She wanted to make sure I’d have a thesis product that could pass the final examination. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/#fnref:As-Prof-Tian-Zhe\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Tareef-often-ask\">Tareef often asks me this by the end of our one-to-one meetings. In the beginning, I felt awkward to say “I don’t have any questions”, so I often answered “Um mm… I probably don’t have questions”. After I had said it so many times, he couldn’t help asking me “Why <em>probably</em>? I’ve never heard anyone say ‘<em>probably</em> no questions’“. He didn’t know my awkwardness of giving the straight answer “No, I don’t have questions.” <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/#fnref:Tareef-often-ask\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:It-was-also-refl\">It was also reflected on <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/bookdown/bookdown.pdf\">the dedication page</a> of the <strong>bookdown</strong> book. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/#fnref:It-was-also-refl\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:From-https-zh-wi\">From <a href=\"https://zh.wikisource.org/zh-hans/%E8%AB%AB%E5%A4%AA%E5%AE%97%E5%8D%81%E6%80%9D%E7%96%8F\">谏太宗十思疏</a>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/#fnref:From-https-zh-wi\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 16 Feb 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"0c411eec911f5ee3a879cd1f35a3aaad","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973510},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The Two Surprisingly Hard Things about the Otherwise Simple Markdown","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/hard-markdown/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>Markdown is simple in general. Over the years, however, I have observed two things that are surprisingly hard and many people have stumbled over them, including experts in my eyes.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"1-how-to-write-verbatim-content-especially-text-that-contains-three-backticks\">1. How to write verbatim content, especially text that contains three backticks</h2>\n\n<p>This is the thing that tortures me weekly, if not daily, because without knowing how to write verbatim text, the Markdown output will be poorly formatted. It is not rare for me to see Github issues like this:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48208438-665f5e80-e338-11e8-9e9c-22e770a7e27e.png\" alt=\"Users don't know how to write verbatim content\" /></p>\n\n<p>Despite of the fact that I have explicitly mentioned in the Github issue template how to write verbatim text and I have also given an example, some users still fail to do this correctly. With dozens of other Github issues to deal with, I hope they could forgive me skipping issues like this. I don&rsquo;t mean to blame users like this one. Obviously they have read the issue template&mdash;the above user said in the Github issue:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Hoping I have all the backticks right in this example.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>It is just that this Markdown syntax is too hard to understand:</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>To write text verbatim, use three backticks to wrap the text.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```\nverbatim text here\n    \nmore verbatim text\n```\n</code></pre></li>\n\n<li><p>To write verbatim text that contains three backticks, you have to use four backticks to wrap the text.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">````\na normal paragraph\n    \n```\nverbatim text here\n    \nmore verbatim text\n```\n````\n</code></pre></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>In general, you need at least N+1 backticks to write N backticks verbatim. As an exercise, now you may ask yourself what you should do if your text contains four backticks, or five, or six. Note that instead of using N+1 backticks, you could also <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/05/four-backticks-github/\">indent the text</a>, but it can be tricky to determine how many spaces you need in some cases.</p>\n\n<p>I didn&rsquo;t fully realize how hard this &ldquo;simple&rdquo; rule was until I saw R experts like <a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/13250\">JD Long failed to provide a reprex in plain text</a> a few weeks ago because it contained three backticks. He chose to post a screenshot of the text. I almost jumped off my chair and killed him <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/screenshots-vs-text/\">when I saw the screenshot</a>. At that moment, I realized I should give it up, and <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/10/501st-reminder/\">accept the fact</a> that this is just surprisingly hard for most Markdown users. Okay, I&rsquo;ll just bite the bullet and edit all your poorly formatted Github issues if I have time.</p>\n\n<p>Below is <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xaringan/issues/180\">one more common example</a> of what users do when they don&rsquo;t know how to format text containing three backticks. I could give many, many <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/github-issue-format/\">more examples</a> like this. Again, the point is not to complain about these users, but show how hard it is for users to know the right syntax.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48207715-dbca2f80-e336-11e8-942e-2232cf02585f.png\" alt=\"Users don't know how to write verbatim text containing three backticks and chose to comment out the text instead\" /></p>\n\n<p>BTW, I want to <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/thanks-marcel-schilling/\">thank Marcel Schilling</a> one more time for <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/help-answer-questions/\">helping me reply Github issues</a> (it is so great to have someone in an earlier timezone to help). You have made indirect yet important contributions to my other open source projects.</p>\n\n<p>The reason for this issue to occur frequently in the R Markdown world is that R Markdown documents often contain three backticks (for code chunks). They also often contain three dashes intended for the YAML frontmatter, which happens to be the syntax for the second-level section headers, and you certainly don&rsquo;t want this to be displayed as a section header:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">---\noutput: powerpoint_presentation\n---\n</code></pre>\n\n<h2 id=\"2-nest-child-elements-properly-in-a-list\">2. Nest child elements properly in a list</h2>\n\n<p>The second hard thing about Markdown doesn&rsquo;t appear frequently, but from the examples I have seen, few people really got it. That is, how to write elements other than bullets within a bullet list. In most cases, the answer is simple: you need to indent these elements. For example, if a bullet item has three paragraphs, the second and third paragraphs have to be indented, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">- One bullet.\n\n  With one more paragraph.\n  \n  And another paragraph.\n\n- Another bullet.\n\n  &gt; A quote.\n  \n  1. Or a numbered list.\n  \n  2. Number two.\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>The actual rules are actually fairly complicated. <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/11/thanks-tj-mahr/\">Again</a>, you have to read the Pandoc manual for <a href=\"https://pandoc.org/MANUAL.html#lists\">the precise syntax</a>. Note that I&rsquo;m talking about <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/time-for-pandoc-2-x/\">Pandoc 2.x</a> here. The rules were different in Pandoc 1.x.</p>\n\n<p>BTW, there is one lesser-known rule for numbered lists. That is, you don&rsquo;t have to use the incremental numbers <code>1.</code>, <code>2.</code>, &hellip;, <code>N.</code>. None of the numbers except the first one matter. Any numbers will work, which can be surprising. For example, the following examples all produce the same list:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">1. One\n2. Two\n3. Three\n</code></pre>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">1. One\n3. Two\n2. Three\n</code></pre>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">1. One\n9. Two\n5. Three\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Only the first number determines the starting number (it doesn&rsquo;t have to be <code>1</code>). The rest of numbers will be ignored. For people who care about the cleanness of their GIT history (like me), you can always use <code>1.</code> as the prefix, so your GIT history can be absolutely clean when you reorder items in a numbered list or add/delete items.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">1. One\n1. Two\n1. Three\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>If you have child elements in a numbered list, you also have to indent them, otherwise the output can be unexpected. As an exercise, guess what the following Markdown source produce:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">1. One\n\nMore on one.\n\n1. Two\n\nExplaining two.\n\n1. Three\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Now you may call me liar since I have been telling you that Markdown is simple.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chi%C8%99in%C4%83u_%2819726522385%29.jpg/399px-Chi%C8%99in%C4%83u_%2819726522385%29.jpg\" alt=\"Liar Liar Pants On Fire! Image from Wikipedia, originally authored by Tony Bowden\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/hard-markdown/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 08 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48208438-665f5e80-e338-11e8-9e9c-22e770a7e27e.png","linkMd5":"1fee7fdb25f8948672bbd90f3928c0fa","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn47@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-33-800_7372d6a5dacbce0b.webp","destWidth":712,"destHeight":1097,"sourceBytes":100557,"destBytes":67446,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48208438-665f5e80-e338-11e8-9e9c-22e770a7e27e.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn47@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-33-800_7372d6a5dacbce0b.webp","https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48207715-dbca2f80-e336-11e8-942e-2232cf02585f.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn80@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-20-01-207_7fd636659ed5bdd3.webp","https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chi%C8%99in%C4%83u_%2819726522385%29.jpg/399px-Chi%C8%99in%C4%83u_%2819726522385%29.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn3@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-20-00-951_1659e13b1d1076c8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973444},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Two of My Use Cases of Lazy Evaluation","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/lazy-evaluation/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>This is a short follow-up post on Colin Fay&rsquo;s <a href=\"https://colinfay.me/lazyeval/\">follow-up post</a> on Thomas Lumley <a href=\"https://notstatschat.rbind.io/2018/07/30/quoting-and-macros-in-r/\">follow-up post</a> on Miles McBain <a href=\"https://milesmcbain.xyz/the-roots-of-quotation/\">post about quotation</a>. Yeah, I just cannot help it when I see this streak.</p>\n\n<p>I&rsquo;m not an expert of quotation or lazy evaluation. I just happen to have used them occasionally. I&rsquo;m going to talk about two use cases of lazy evaluation.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"executing-r-code-the-ninja-way\">Executing R code &mdash; the ninja way</h2>\n\n<p>In two of my talks, I used delayed assignments to execute R code for no good reasons except that I just wanted to confuse the audience. After making a delayed assignment like this in advance:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">delayedAssign('command', {\n  # some R code\n})\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>I can simply type <code>command</code> in the R console to actually execute the code. Of course, you could go with the normal way:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">command = function() {\n  # some R code\n}\ncommand()\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>But that will be boring, right?</p>\n\n<p>I used this trick in my talks at <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr-talks/blob/master/useR2014/2014-knitr-ninja-Yihui-Xie.R\">useR! 2014</a> and <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/typing-fast/\">rstudio::conf 2018</a>.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"lazy-loading-the-cache-database-in-knitr\">Lazy-loading the cache database in <strong>knitr</strong></h2>\n\n<p>The caching system in <strong>knitr</strong> <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/blob/89b34a661/R/cache.R#L59\">uses lazy-loading</a> by default (the chunk option <code>cache.lazy = TRUE</code>). Lazy-loading means the results you cached will not be loaded in memory unless they are actually used later. This is very similar to the LazyData mentioned in Colin&rsquo;s post. To those hackers, you may make a lazy-load database using the <em>internal</em> function <code>tools:::makeLazyLoadDB()</code> (again, it is an internal function, so no guarantee in the future), and load the database using <code>lazyLoad()</code>.</p>\n\n<p>I don&rsquo;t quite remember how I discovered this. Chances are I found it by reading the source code of Roger Peng&rsquo;s <strong>cacheSweave</strong> package in 2012.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/lazy-evaluation/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 31 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"315a864b6fba31973387cf50efc7a347","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973457},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On \"Quick Questions\"","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/quick-questions/","description":"\n        <p>&ldquo;Hey Yihui, quick question for you&hellip;&rdquo; For many times I have been asked &ldquo;quick questions&rdquo; on Twitter, on Slack, or in emails. I do love questions that are quick, because the net gain can be significant: you won&rsquo;t need to spend hours on questions of which the answers are obvious to me.</p>\n\n<p>Most of time I reply to such questions within seconds after I see them, and some people have been surprised by how quickly I respond. The only problem is, those questions are not always quick. This can be particularly problematic when I&rsquo;m reading messages in IM (instant messaging) tools such as Twitter and Slack, because I usually don&rsquo;t intend to stay in IM tools for long. I often open Slack only once every morning to check messages from yesterday, and quit it for the rest of the day. I have installed a Chrome extension to limit my Twitter time to 10 minutes per day. When quick questions do not really seem to be quick, I feel I&rsquo;ll be stuck there. Should I continue to ask for more information (which can lead to several rounds of asking), or simply ignore the question? I don&rsquo;t know.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/give-up.gif\" alt=\"Should I give up?\" /></p>\n\n<p>In general, I&rsquo;m not big fan of IM tools, although I&rsquo;m not truly against them. They are convenient indeed. For example, you can ask a friend for dinner together via a text message. That is totally cool. However, if you have something important to say, it is probably not a good idea to bury it in the endless and chaotic stream of messages. For example, I never believe it is a good idea to debate on anything on Twitter.</p>\n\n<p>The possibility of your questions being unintentionally overlooked is one problem. The other problem is, by asking a quick question, you probably won&rsquo;t have a chance to think hard enough about the question or try a little bit to find the answer first. As a result, the question could be unclear, or due to a simple mistake. If you make a little bit more effort, you might just discover the answer by yourself.</p>\n\n<p>With apologies to Carl Boettiger, I want to use <a href=\"https://twitter.com/cboettig/status/1014210705736851457\">a quick question he asked me on Twitter</a> today as an example to show the possibility of answering your own quick question:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>@xieyihui stupid question for you: how can I put literal TeX into Rmd these days? I thought that just worked but pandoc (v2.1.1) seems to be auto-escaping stuff like <code>\\begin{tcolorbox}</code></p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I was surprised by this issue because that did not sound like what Pandoc would do. I mean Pandoc has supported raw LaTeX content in Markdown for years. I had a guess about how this could happen (special LaTeX characters that are not properly escaped in <code>tcolorbox</code>), but without seeing a reproducible example, it was hard for me to know if my guess was correct, which means I had to ask for more information. Then this wouldn&rsquo;t be a &ldquo;quick question&rdquo;. Instead of guessing and asking back and forth, I recommended him to file an issue with a reproducible example to the <strong>rmarkdown</strong> repo on Github. A Github issue would certainly be better than a tweet in this case: it is hard to provide a self-contained reproducible example in a tweet due to the arbitrary 280-character limit.</p>\n\n<p>Guess what? Then he just quickly realized what was wrong by himself. Had he tried to make a small reproducible example, he would not need to ask the question at all. Had I seen a reproducible example, I&rsquo;d probably notice the problem within seconds, too. The problem was just that the quick question contained too little information. When one has to start asking for more information or clarification, the quick question is no longer quick.</p>\n\n<p>I don&rsquo;t mean to discourage people from asking me quick questions. I just want to set your expectation correctly: when I don&rsquo;t answer a question on Twitter, it probably means it is not clear to me, and you may try to ask a slow version of the question on <a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com\">RStudio Community</a> or Stack Overflow or other places that don&rsquo;t have the length limit, providing as much information as you can. If you need convincing examples, you may take a look at <a href=\"https://twitter.com/datalorax_/status/1048199896132595713\">Daniel Anderson&rsquo;s experience</a> (he almost wanted to ask on Twitter but decided to go with Stack Overflow instead, and succeeded), or <a href=\"https://twitter.com/richarddmorey/status/1202261694149472256\">Richard D. Morey&rsquo;s</a>, or <a href=\"https://twitter.com/itsmevidhya_k/status/1206198154774761472\">Vidhya Kalaichelvan&rsquo;s</a>.</p>\n\n<p>Remember, the quick question might be like the two circles in the guide below on how to draw an owl, and the real question might be the owl on the right. Are you absolutely sure I can answer your question after seeing the two cirles?</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/draw-an-owl.jpg\" alt=\"How to draw an owl\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/quick-questions/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 03 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/give-up.gif","linkMd5":"5e219675ca1cd306dd9b0df31ed3467f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn36@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-36-684_c9616a7195c5b12a.webp","destWidth":333,"destHeight":250,"sourceBytes":1435586,"destBytes":360140,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/give-up.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn36@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-36-684_c9616a7195c5b12a.webp","https://slides.yihui.org/gif/draw-an-owl.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn76@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-20-01-393_230861207ea17c1f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Impact: Depth or Breadth?","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/influence-depth-or-breadth/","description":"\n        <p>Last year someone invited me to give a talk to early career researchers at <a href=\"https://iscbasc2018.com/early-career-researchers-day/\">an ISCB conference</a> in Australia. I didn&rsquo;t go since it wouldn&rsquo;t be practical for me to travel that far in the next couple of years, but there was one thing I hoped to talk about if I were to talk to those early career researchers on choosing research topics (disclaimer: <em>I&rsquo;m not a researcher</em> so you probably shouldn&rsquo;t listen to me): whether you should pursue depth or breadth of the impact of your research.</p>\n\n<p>My principle is depth first. The broad impact may be a natural by-product.</p>\n\n<p>Actually <a href=\"https://slides.yihui.org/2017-rstudio-conf-rmarkdown-Yihui-Xie.html#34\">I briefly mentioned this</a> in an R Markdown tutorial I gave at rstudio::conf 2017. I said (as a software developer):</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Make one person extremely happy first. Do not aim at making everybody (even mildly) happy.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Of course, I&rsquo;d love to see people excited about my software packages, but I prefer a small number of people (could even be only one person) feeling extremely excited over a large number of people only slightly nodding. A couple of recent examples:</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>A while ago, Charles T. Gray discovered that <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code> works on any Rmd documents (as I documented <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown/compile.html\">in the R Markdown book</a>). And <a href=\"https://twitter.com/cantabile/status/1034216497634131971\">her excitement was still not over yesterday</a> (which started <a href=\"https://twitter.com/cantabile/status/947622766857150467\">in December 2017</a>). I created the <strong>xaringan</strong> package in late 2016, but didn&rsquo;t mention the secret of <code>xaringan::inf_mr()</code> in public <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/08/why-xaringan-remark-js/\">until the August of 2017</a>. I knew it was a cool feature, and I use it all the time. I just want users to discover it slowly by themselves. I always feel highly motivated by the occasional ecstasy from individual users like Charles. This has been a sustainable source of power for me.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Yesterday, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/ZKamvar/status/1034494397126197249\">Zhian N. Kamvar discovered</a> that the <strong>tinytex</strong> package can automatically install missing LaTeX packages when compiling LaTeX documents. Yeah, <a href=\"https://yihui.org/tinytex/pain/\">I feel all your pain with LaTeX</a>. Sometimes people are just comfortably numb with the pain (why do you have to waste time in installing LaTeX packages when this could largely be automated?). I love seeing the pain going away, little by little, and from <a href=\"https://twitter.com/nj_tierney/status/941126710602301441\">one user</a> after <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/tinytex/issues/7\">another</a>.</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>If you can impact a few people deeply, they will just shout from the rooftops for you. The breadth of the impact will be a matter of time, i.e., it will come eventually. You won&rsquo;t need to invest your own precious time in marketing. &ldquo;No marketing&rdquo; has been my own marketing strategy for years (I mean I usually avoid active marketing by myself, and I thank those who have been shouting out for me). That was a deliberate choice of mine. I trust the organic growth of impact, although I know it is slow, and sometimes unbearably slow. The impact two years later is worth waiting for. Looking ahead, two years sounds long, but looking back, it is nothing.</p>\n\n<p>Needless to say, impact should not be your goal. Impact itself is also a by-product. It should be based on usefulness or helpfulness, which in turn depends on the &ldquo;pain level&rdquo; of an existing problem. The more painful a problem is, the more useful the solution will be. Note that such problems are not necessarily difficult to solve. Some problems are not terribly painful on their own, but if you run into them every day, they become super annoying, and will be worth fixing. For early career researchers, problems that are not too technically challenging yet frequently and mildly annoying may be good starting points of your career. It will be even better if there is such a problem that <a href=\"https://twitter.com/mxcl/status/1006363200277942275\">deeply annoys yourself</a>, because your product, provided that you can work it out, will deeply impact yourself (e.g., significantly improving your own efficiency).</p>\n\n<p>How many times have I mentioned that the <strong>knitr</strong> package was first created for myself to write my own homework at Iowa State more easily? And reproducible research&hellip; was not my goal back in 2011. To some extent, I was quite lucky.</p>\n\n<p>Don&rsquo;t try to win the whole world in the beginning. Win one person (even yourself), and win him/her deeply. The world may follow later.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/influence-depth-or-breadth/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 29 Aug 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"15b732a4dbaecfd576a6423c97cfd694","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973446},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"One Little Thing: Touch a Source File in a blogdown Website","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/touch-file-blogdown/","description":"\n        <p>Motivated by <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/issues/294\">a <strong>blogdown</strong> issue</a> raised by Liang Zhang, I added an RStudio addin named &ldquo;Touch File&rdquo; in <strong>blogdown</strong> last month to update the modification time of the current file in RStudio.</p>\n\n<p>Most <strong>blogdown</strong> users should already know <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/blogdown/livereload.html\">the LiveReload feature</a>, which means if you edit a source file and save it, your website will be automatically rebuilt and refreshed in the RStudio viewer once you have used the &ldquo;Serve Site&rdquo; addin or the corresponding function <code>blogdown::serve_site()</code>.</p>\n\n<p>This feature is great, but on some (relatively rare) occasions, you may want to force rebuilding an Rmd post. For example, when you read an external data file, and the data file has been updated, you want to rebuild the Rmd file even if you don&rsquo;t need to update the content of the Rmd file. In the past, I used to add a space in the Rmd file, delete it, and save the file, which would update the modification time of this file. This is a little awkward. Unix users know there is a <code>touch</code> command, which can update the modification time of a file. <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/commit/d1934655c\">I emulated it</a> in the RStudio addin &ldquo;Touch File&rdquo; in <strong>blogdown</strong>. The basic idea is to call <code>touch</code> if the command is available. If not, do what I used to do: add a space, delete it, and save the file.</p>\n\n<p>Four keystrokes (<code>Space</code>, <code>Delete</code>, <code>Ctrl/Command + S</code>) were &ldquo;simplified&rdquo; to two mouse clicks (<code>Addins -&gt; Touch File</code>). Is it worth it?</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/missed-shot.gif\" alt=\"Should I really celebrate it?\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/touch-file-blogdown/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 16 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/missed-shot.gif","linkMd5":"29ff3b84ad1d57f802c04c5ac8e40e80","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn7@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-33-922_913450d23c7b246e.webp","destWidth":240,"destHeight":172,"sourceBytes":322027,"destBytes":140710,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/missed-shot.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn7@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-33-922_913450d23c7b246e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"One Little Thing: Embed a File in the HTML Output of R Markdown","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/embed-file/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>Sometimes it is even hard for myself to believe how long it would take for me to implement a simple feature. Last year, when I saw Steven Fazzio&rsquo;s <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/issues/106\"><strong>blogdown</strong> feature request</a> on Github (to embed the Rmd source file in the HTML output so readers can download it), I had an idea how to implement it. I knew it wouldn&rsquo;t be hard.</p>\n\n<p>Actually two years ago, Noam Ross sent <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/pull/708\">a pull request</a> to the <strong>rmarkdown</strong> repo, which was related to Steven&rsquo;s request. I felt the implementation could be easier.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"xfun-embed-file\">xfun::embed_file()</h2>\n\n<p>While deciding the issues/features for <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/projects/1\">the next release</a> of <strong>rmarkdown</strong>, I revisited the <strong>rmarkdown</strong> PR and the <strong>blogdown</strong> issue. At one late night, I finally <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/xfun/commit/d8199f86dbf06\">typed out the two lines of code</a> to implement it in the <strong>xfun</strong> package, so now you can embed an arbitrary file, or multiple files, or a whole directory, in the HTML output of an R Markdown document. For now you need the development version of <strong>xfun</strong>:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">devtools::install_github('yihui/xfun')\n# embed_file() requires a few more packages\nxfun::pkg_load2(c('base64enc', 'htmltools', 'mime'))\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Basically, you can use one of the functions <code>xfun::embed_file()</code>, <code>xfun::embed_dir()</code>, or <code>xfun::embed_files()</code> in an R code chunk to embed one or multiple files or an entire directory in the HTML output, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r echo=FALSE}\n# a single file\nxfun::embed_file('source.Rmd')\n\n# multiple files\nxfun::embed_files(c('source.Rmd', 'data.csv'))\n\n# a directory\nxfun::embed_dir('data/', text = 'Download full data')\n```\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>You can also provide the list of files programmatically, e.g.,</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-r\"># embed all Rmd and csv files\nxfun::embed_files(list.files('.', '[.](Rmd|csv)$'))\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>For multiple files, they are first compressed to a zip file, and the zip file will be embedded. These functions return a link, which a reader can click on the HTML page to download the embedded file.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-trick-a-href-base64-data-download-filename\">The trick <code>&lt;a href=&quot;base64 data&quot; download=&quot;filename&quot;&gt;</code></h2>\n\n<p>Under the hood, <code>xfun::embed_file()</code> simply generates a link of the form <code>&lt;a href=&quot;base64 data&quot; download=&quot;filename&quot;&gt;text&lt;/a&gt;</code>, where <code>&quot;base64 data&quot;</code> is the file encoded as a base64 string. If you are using a modern web browser (you are fine if it is not IE), it should be able to decode the base64 string into the file and download it when you click the link.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"when-to-use-these-functions\">When to use these functions?</h2>\n\n<p>If your project is public, for example, on Github, there is no need to embed files in the HTML output: just give other people the address of the repo. If you are using <strong>blogdown</strong>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/issues/106\">it is also possible</a> to automatically generate a link on each page pointing to the corresponding source file on Github (e.g., on my website, you can click the link &ldquo;Edit this page&rdquo; to edit the source files of most pages).</p>\n\n<p>If you project is completely private, you may compress the whole directory as a zip file and send it via email. That is common and fine, but you may not want to manually compress the directory every time you update any files in it. These <code>embed_*()</code> functions can automatically read and embed files in your HTML output, so all you need to do is knit the Rmd source document again, and you will only need to email an HTML file.</p>\n\n<p>If the source project is private, but the output is expected to be public, that is when you may also consider embedding certain files on the output page, e.g., your Rmd source file, and/or data files. Readers who are interested in reproducing your results or analyzing the data differently can download the necessary files. This way, you only need to publish a single HTML file.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/embed-file/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"b1c04c454d49b0bcc4000cf6a949cdcb","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"One Little Thing: Quote a Poem with blogdown","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/quote-poem-blogdown/","description":"\n        <p>One of the most frequent topics on which I blog in recent years is Chinese literature (of course, only in my Chinese blog). In particular, I often quote poems in my posts. To quote a poem in Markdown, you have to add two trailing spaces after every line of the poem. Two trailing spaces means a hard line break in Markdown. A line break in the Markdown source does not mean a line break in the output (usually white spaces are not meaningful in Markdown).</p>\n\n<p>I have been tired of manually adding the trailing spaces (also <code>&gt;</code> to the beginning of every paragraph) . Finally <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/commit/ad8be3ffb5\">I wrote a tiny function <code>quote_poem()</code></a> in <strong>blogdown</strong> to do this. The function was not exported, but that should be fine, because I also provided a corresponding RStudio addin named &ldquo;Quote Poem&rdquo;. Currently this feature is only available in the <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/blogdown/\">development version of <strong>blogdown</strong></a>. The usage of the addin is simple: just select the poem in the RStudio source editor, and click the addin. For example, if you paste a poem in RStudio:</p>\n\n<pre><code>Like Barley Bending\n　by Sara Teasdale\n\nLike barley bending\n　In low fields by the sea,\nSinging in hard wind\n　Ceaselessly;\n\nLike barley bending\n　And rising again,\nSo would I, unbroken,\n　Rise from pain;\n\nSo would I softly,\n　Day long, night long,\nChange my sorrow\n　Into song.\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>You can select it, and click the addin, which will generate the expected output below:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Like Barley Bending<br />\n　by Sara Teasdale</p>\n\n<p>Like barley bending<br />\n　In low fields by the sea,<br />\nSinging in hard wind<br />\n　Ceaselessly;</p>\n\n<p>Like barley bending<br />\n　And rising again,<br />\nSo would I, unbroken,<br />\n　Rise from pain;</p>\n\n<p>So would I softly,<br />\n　Day long, night long,<br />\nChange my sorrow<br />\n　Into song.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I guess <strong>blogdown</strong> users might be surprised by this function/addin the first time they see it. Poems&hellip; and <strong>blogdown</strong>?  How can <em>an R package</em> be possibly related with <em>poems</em>?!</p>\n\n<p>Yes, I just care that much about blogging, and want to improve at least my own writing experience by one little function at a time.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/quote-poem-blogdown/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 16 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"8bac33520c951bb74ca4c436df5e1889","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Back from rstudio::conf(2020)","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/","description":"<p>Wait a minute. I thought I just wrote the blog post “<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/01/rstudio-conf/\">Back from rstudio::conf 2019</a>” not long ago. A whole year has passed?! I cannot believe it has been so long since I wrote a blog post last time. In this post, I’ll share some experiences as well as the materials of my presentation at the conference.</p> \n<h2 id=\"my-talk-one-r-markdown-document-fourteen-demos\">My talk: One R Markdown document, fourteen demos</h2> \n<p><strong>TL;DR</strong> You can download the R script I used in the talk here: <a href=\"https://slides.yihui.org/2020-rstudio-conf-rmarkdown-demo.R\">2020-rstudio-conf-rmarkdown-demo.R</a>. Note that it will try to automatically install the required R packages and download files required for the demos. If you want to run the script by yourself, I suggest you run it in a new directory to avoid cluttering your current working directory. You can find the video of this talk on <a href=\"https://rstudio.com/resources/rstudioconf-2020/one-r-markdown-document-fourteen-demos/\">resources.rstudio.com</a>.</p> \n<p>I presented fourteen demos based on the same R Markdown document in my talk. The goal was to show that a relatively simple R Markdown document can generate a large variety of output document formats, some of which can look quite sophisticated.</p> \n<p>The idea for this talk originally came from my remote talk for the 2019 RaukR summer school in Sweden, in which I presented a series of R Markdown demos to show the versatility of R Markdown, but I used different R Markdown documents in that talk. BTW, I also met the organizers Marcin Kierczak and Sebastian Dilorenzo at rstudio::conf this time, and we “complained” together about how little kids made it so difficult for their parents to travel. They also brought a T-shirt of the summer school to me all the way from Sweden, to which I was very grateful.</p> \n<p>At JSM 2019, I gave another talk on the R Markdown ecosystem, and decided to use the same R Markdown document. I put all output formats in the YAML metadata, and clicked the <code>Knit</code> button 14 times during the talk, which was a little awkward, because I had to look closely at the output formats and think which one would be the next one to present. It was not easy to do this in front of the audience when the time for the talk is short (20 minutes).</p> \n<p>This time I decided to automate it as much as possible, so I wouldn’t have to spend time on thinking about which output format to present next. With <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/typing-fast/\">my experience at rstudio::conf(2018)</a>, I wrote an R script to automatically insert the <code>output</code> field into the R Markdown document in RStudio using <code>xfun::rstudio_type()</code>. With a <code>for</code>-loop iterating over all output formats, and a call to <code>readline()</code> at each step of the loop, I managed to present these output formats one by one. All I had to do was to keep pressing <code>Enter</code>, and the audience would see something like this being dynamically “typed” into my RStudio IDE:<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:1\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/#fn:1\">1</a></sup></p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">output:\n  html_document:\n    number_sections: true\n    theme: journal\n    toc: true\n</code></pre> \n<script> (function() { var el = document.querySelector('pre &gt; code'); el.style.height = el.offsetHeight + 'px'; var tx = el.innerText; setInterval(function() { var n = el.innerText.length; el.innerText = n &lt; tx.length ? tx.slice(0, n + 1) : ''; }, 50); })(); </script> \n<p>Again, you can find my R script at the beginning of this section. Keep in mind that it has to be executed in the RStudio IDE.</p> \n<p>In retrospect, my tone in the talk was perhaps too disparaging on LaTeX, Word, PowerPoint, and LaTeX beamer. Here I want to apologize to whomever felt hurt by my silly comments (sometimes I have no idea what I’m talking about on the podium). I always say I’m tired of beamer slides, but beamer is actually extremely powerful. The only issue is that too many people use more or less the same beamer theme. I’d be equally tired of <strong>xaringan</strong> slides if I see the same theme over and over again.</p> \n<h2 id=\"q-a-s-on-my-talk\">Q&amp;A’s on my talk</h2> \n<p>Due to the time limit, I wasn’t able to answer all questions on sli.do. Now I’ll answer them below, in case the askers are still interested.</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p><strong>Q: What’s the updog app?</strong></p> <p>A: I had no clue how this person noticed Updog, because I didn’t mention Updog in my talk at all. When I heard this question, I was totally confused. A whole week later, I suddenly realized that my demo files were in my Dropbox folder, and one folder name was “updog”! What sharp eyes! Later <a href=\"https://twitter.com/taraskaduk/status/1222974644833017856\">Taras Kaduk told me on Twitter</a> that I missed a great opportunity to honor the traditional “Nothing much, what’s up with you?” Anyway, <a href=\"https://updog.co\">Updog</a> used to be a great service to host static websites in Dropbox, but unfortunately it is down now (again, not my fault!).</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: How do you get the search box for a data frame in rmarkdown?</strong></p> <p>A: I used <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/DT\">the <strong>DT</strong> package</a>.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: How did you get the pacman animation to work in PowerPoint?</strong></p> <p>A: The pacman was a GIF created <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/gifski-knitr/\">with <strong>gifski</strong></a>. GIFs work in the presentation mode of PowerPoint.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Did you lose a bet so you had to do your talk the same time as Hadley?</strong></p> <p>A: I didn’t realize that Hadley’s talk was at the same time as mine until about an hour before my talk. <a href=\"https://twitter.com/sharon000/status/1222983977541046273\">It was a bummer</a> to some people, but at least the two rooms <a href=\"https://twitter.com/jcheng/status/1223327608219131904\">wouldn’t be too crowded</a>. Well, I was especially grateful that my room was not empty after I knew Hadley was speaking in the next room.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Is there any way to add a footer in PowerPoint when knitting with R Markdown?</strong></p> <p>A: Not to my knowledge. I don’t use Office products myself, and whenever I get questions about Word/PowerPoint that I cannot answer, I’ll recommend looking at <a href=\"https://github.com/davidgohel/officer\"><strong>officer</strong></a>. David Gohel has far more expertise than I do.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: The direction of the pie chart changes depending on the output format?</strong></p> <p>A: Good eyes! The direction of pacman is actually random to each output format.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Do you know or are working in integrating audience interactions in xaringan?</strong> Something related to <a href=\"https://sli.do\">sli.do</a> / <a href=\"https://www.mentimeter.com\">mentimeter</a>.</p> <p>A: I have a few ideas, but I don’t know when I’ll be able to implement them. Before I do, the easiest way I could think of is to embed an <code>&lt;iframe&gt;</code> of a web page on a slide, and all interactions occur on that web page.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: How much time do you find it takes getting the formatting to look good for HTML outputs?</strong> I find I can get the page looking OK but not great.</p> <p>A: A lot of time, if I really want the HTML pages to look good. However, I usually refrain from spending too much time on CSS. I’m a minimalist in both my daily life and my technical world. That said, I can only be a minimalist when I work for myself (e.g., design a theme for my own website). If I design products for others, I will certainly invest more time in the appearance.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: How do you integrate GIS visualization from ArcGis software into R Markdown document?</strong></p> <p>A: Sorry, but I know nothing about ArcGis. I used the <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/leaflet\"><strong>leaflet</strong></a> package in my demos to render the map.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: What is the difference between bookdown vs pagedown?</strong></p> <p>A: The goal of the <strong>pagedown</strong> package is to typeset HTML documents with CSS. HTML is its only output format. PDF is a by-product in this package (printed from HTML). The <strong>bookdown</strong> package supports a variety of output formats, including HTML, LaTeX, Word, and EPUB, etc.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Have we solved the issue of overhanging code in code chunks when rendering to PDF?</strong></p> <p>A: I know verbatim content could be wrapped by the LaTeX package <strong>listings</strong>, and Pandoc supports <strong>listings</strong>, too. However, I have never tried by myself. If anyone has tried and succeeded, please let me know and I’ll be happy to share a link to your solution. Usually I just hard-wrap the lines by myself.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Have you considered calendar based release cycle?</strong></p> <p>A: I have an R Markdown document that runs every two weeks on RStudio Connect to remind me of the packages that I may need to update on CRAN. I may or may not actually update these packages after I see the reminder email. I don’t plan to switch to a strictly calendar-based release cycle. On the other hand, I think the base <strong>rmarkdown</strong> package and <strong>knitr</strong> are relatively mature now.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Is the markdown document available to download? Can we get your code for your 14 demos?</strong></p> <p>A: Yes, please find the link to the R script in the beginning of the previous section in this blog post.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Is there such thing as a googleslide”down”?</strong></p> <p>A: Not to my knowledge, but I’m very interested. I know many people are interested in a product that connects R Markdown with Google Docs/Slides, or an online platform like Overleaf where multiple authors can collaborate. I cannot comment much on this at the moment, but personally I’m definitely highly interested in such products/platforms!</p></li> \n</ul> \n<h2 id=\"rob-s-talk-on-how-r-markdown-changed-his-life\">Rob’s talk on how R Markdown changed his life</h2> \n<p>When I first saw the title of Rob’s talk last year, I felt the pressure for my own talk. If someone highly praised R Markdown in a previous talk, what should I talk about so I wouldn’t let the audience down?</p> \n<p>I could also talk about how R Markdown changed my own life—it has made me incredibly busy day and night for years, and I could not eat or sleep. Just kidding. It did change my life. For example, it brought me a dream job at RStudio, and to some extent, its simplicity turned me to a minimalist.</p> \n<p>You can find materials of Rob’s talk at <a href=\"http://robjhyndman.com/rmd2020\">http://robjhyndman.com/rmd2020</a>. He reviewed the authoring tools that he had used since he was in kindergarten,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:2\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/#fn:2\">2</a></sup> which was a lot of fun because we could hear more and more people in the audience started to recognize those tools as he moved forward in his life path. He told me the night before in the California Academy of Sciences that he was going to show an old Twitter conversation between him and me in his talk, but I had no idea which one it was. Had I not seen his slide the next day, I’d have totally forgotten <a href=\"https://twitter.com/robjhyndman/status/271112533929238529\">that tweet from 2012</a>!</p> \n<p>On his last slide, he showed a picture of me and thanked me. Of course, I felt touched. The funny fact is that the picture was from <a href=\"https://vimeo.com/94181521\">my very first talk about R Markdown</a> in Seattle in 2014 (at Fred Hutch). If you search for my name with Google Images, this is the first image you get.</p> \n<p>Many thanks to Rob for this very inspiring talk! I’ll think about the “maildown” package.</p> \n<h2 id=\"book-signing\">Book signing</h2> \n<p>There was a book signing event at the reception on Tuesday evening. It was the first time I had attended such an event. There was also one at rstudio::conf(2019), but somehow I didn’t find the place (apologies to those who might have waited for me there).</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/maxheld/status/1221985368657952769\">Max Held</a> took a picture of the event, which happened to have me on it:</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/EPVdNgAUwAAwi17?format=jpg&amp;name=medium\" alt=\"Signing books at rstudio::conf(2020)\" /></p> \n<p>Max said it was not a comic conference, yet there were so many people lined up for the books. I felt the same excitement. I really loved the conference, so much that I didn’t even care to go to see the Golden Gate Bridge during my stay in SF, although I had never really seen it.</p> \n<p>I was in a hurry to drink my beer (kidding), so I became the first one to finish signing the 100 copies of books. <a href=\"https://twitter.com/greg_dubrow/status/1222235939537215489\">Greg Dubrow</a> was the 100th person coming to my table, and he got the last copy. Behind him was the poor <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/yue-jiang/\">Yue Jiang</a>.</p> \n<p>Sitting on my left hand side was JJ, the CEO and founder of RStudio. While I was signing the books, I overheard someone who apparently didn’t know him and asked him “Do you work for RStudio?”</p> \n<p>Heh, heh, heh.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/awesome-me.gif\" alt=\"The CEO of RStudio\" /></p> \n<h2 id=\"some-people-i-met\">Some people I met</h2> \n<p>On my plane from Omaha to SFO, my neighbor glanced at my laptop screen and recognized the RStudio IDE. He told me he didn’t know much about R, but his wife was an R user. I didn’t tell him that I was going to rstudio::conf. However, two days later, my colleague said on Slack that someone asked him if there was an RStudio employee based in Omaha and met her husband on the plane. Huh! I didn’t know his wife was also attending this conference! In case any other R users in Omaha are reading this post, here is the link to <a href=\"https://www.meetup.com/Omaha-R-Users-Group/\">the Omaha R User Group</a>.</p> \n<p>At the entrance of the California Academy of Sciences, I ran into <a href=\"https://github.com/HenrikBengtsson\">Henrik Bengtsson</a>, whom I had never met before (hence I didn’t recognize his face). Henrik has made many valuable contributions to the R community, and is also the unsung hero behind <a href=\"https://yihui.org/knitr/demo/vignette/\">non-Sweave package vignettes</a> in my eyes. If it were not for him, there wouldn’t be thousands of package vignettes on CRAN.</p> \n<p>Inside the academy, I met many friends whom I hadn’t seen for a long while, such as Roger Peng, Hilary Parker, Jeff Leek, and Emily Robinson, etc. I didn’t know Jeff started to grow his beard! I forgot to take a picture, otherwise I’d consider adding it to the <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/03/yolo-karl/\"><code>yolo = TRUE</code></a> collection in <strong>xaringan</strong>. Emily was still such a kind, lovely, and <a href=\"http://lcolladotor.github.io/2020/02/03/conference-feelings-from-newbie-to-sponsor/\">inspiring</a> soul.</p> \n<p>When I returned to the hotel, I was caught by Theresa Henle in the lobby. She attended our R Markdown workshop, and is a very warm-hearted person. Soon we were joined by Carolina Johnson, Ellis Hughes, Jonathan Carroll, and Joshua Goldberg. Ellis was wearing his Halloween costume—a T-shirt with the default R Markdown example printed on it, which I had previously seen <a href=\"https://twitter.com/ellis_hughes/status/1220006359292235781\">on Twitter</a>, but it was fantastic to see it for real. Jonathan just put together <a href=\"https://rweekly.org/2020-04.html\">the latest issue of R Weekly</a>, which was still open in my browser because I hadn’t had a chance to read it at that time. We chatted in the lobby for quite a while, and I started to debate to myself whether I should go back to my room to prepare my talk for the next morning, since it was fairly late in the night (past 11:30pm, meaning 1:30am central time for me). I had finished writing that R script, but I hadn’t thought about the most important part of the talk—the jokes. Finally I covered Josh’s mouth to stop him from talking further, and ran off. Sorry, Josh! BTW, last year Josh <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/01/rstudio-conf/#comment-4307358256\">encouraged me to write a non-technical book</a>, and I definitely still remember it. Still no promises, but I think I’ll get there someday since I’m truly interested in writing such a book.</p> \n<p>At the conference reception, I met three Japanese: <a href=\"https://twitter.com/kazutan\">kazutan</a> (前田和寛), <a href=\"https://twitter.com/u_ribo\">Uryu Shinya</a>, and <a href=\"https://orcid.org/0000-0003-4967-3831\">Kenta Yoshida</a>. Kenta was in my R Markdown workshop. I think I have known kazutan for a number of years, but it was the first time I had met him in person. I feel Japanese are born comics. Even when they are in an English environment, they still demonstrate a sense of humor naturally through their face and gestures. I told them that sometimes I’d thumb through the slides of the Tokyo R User Group, and I felt the content was often deeply technical, which was surprising to me. For this conference, apparently they also had <a href=\"https://twitter.com/kazutan/status/1222491746052915201\">problems with English</a>, just like Hiroaki Yutani did <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/01/rstudio-conf/\">last year</a>.</p> \n<p>Ian Lyttle told me at the reception that he discovered a bug in <strong>rmarkdown</strong> but decided not to bother me and figured it out by himself. In this case, I wouldn’t mind a quick message, since that bug was <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/1756\">simple enough to fix</a>. Anyway, it was so very kind of him!</p> \n<p>I still had a few emails that I didn’t manage to reply. As a result, I greeted some people with “Hey I’m sorry I haven’t replied your email!” That was what I did to Malcolm Barrett when I met him for the first time in front of his poster. I owe the same email debt to Brooke Anderson, and our Mine Çetinkaya-Rundel and Alison Hill.</p> \n<p>I had struggled with <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/1733\">an <strong>rmarkdown</strong> issue</a> related to LaTeX that I was unable to reproduce for a long time. Fortunately, in the week before the conference, Jared Lander reported that he had the same problem, and he would attend the conference, too. Finally I could see the problem on someone’s computer for real. <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/1733#issuecomment-582165621\">It turned out</a> that we only had to update LaTeX packages in MiKTeX. Thanks, Jared, for letting me use your computer for three hours!!<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:3\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/#fn:3\">3</a></sup></p> \n<p>Not surprisingly, I met a couple of more conference attendees at the SFO airport on Friday morning. I could easily tell from their conference T-shirts. Actually one woman was right in front of me as we went through the security, and she recognized the “Markdown guy”. On my flight back to Omaha, I saw at least two other attendees on the same plane.</p> \n<h2 id=\"naras-and-literate-programming\">Naras and Literate Programming</h2> \n<p>I met <a href=\"https://web.stanford.edu/~naras/\">Naras</a> on the last day of the conference. He told me R Markdown also changed his life, as well as several other interesting things. One particularly interesting thing was that he actually published <a href=\"https://www.jstatsoft.org/article/view/v004i03\">a paper in JSS</a> in 1999(!) based on literate programming techniques. He deeply appreciated Knuth’s literate programming paradigm, and I absolutely agreed with him that (dynamically) composing a big chunk of code with smaller pieces of chunks was an undervalued idea. I also mentioned the advantages of literate programming in my article “<a href=\"https://yihui.org/rlp/\">Write An R Package Using Literate Programming Techniques</a>”. I’m sure there are other papers written with this technique, but Naras’s paper is the earliest one to my current knowledge. I haven’t met many true fans of Knuth’s literate programming. Naras and Terry (Therneau) are the only two that I know.</p> \n<h2 id=\"some-frequently-asked-questions-to-me\">Some frequently asked questions to me</h2> \n<p>In case anyone else is interested, I’m listing some FAQ’s below according to my experiences at the conference.</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Where are you based?</strong></p> <p>A: I’m in Omaha. (People often continue to ask why since it seems to be unbelievable that anyone would like to live in Nebraska, and the reason is that my family is here.)</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: What’s the headquarter of RStudio?</strong></p> <p>A: I don’t think we have an official headquarter, but I’d say Boston anyway, since we have our biggest office there. Many of us work from home, like myself.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Can we invite you to give a talk in our department/company?</strong></p> <p>A: I’d love to, but it is not very practical for me to travel in recent years because my little ones are still too little. Some people have invited me multiple times, e.g., both Marcin (RaukR summer school) and Jared (New York R) told me I could go whenever I want, but I still haven’t visited them. That said, I’ll be happy to give a remote talk if that’s okay to you. This will save you the cost (no need to reimburse or pay me), and also save me the long trips. If your institution is within three hours of drive from Omaha, I can probably drive there to give the talk and return home afterwards.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Can I have you sign my books?</strong></p> <p>A: My pleasure! I can sign on anything you like (books, stickers, T-shirts, etc.), except a fake debit note that says I owe you a million dollars.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Can I take a picture with you?</strong></p> <p>A: Of course! I can also pretend to smile as hard as you want me to.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Can I sit at your table or by your side at the meal time?</strong></p> <p>A: Absolutely! Feel free to take any seat you like and chat. I may not chat too much, but I’d love to listen. Even if you see I have a friend with me, feel free to join us. Most of time I’m not having a formal meeting with someone, so please don’t worry. If I need to leave, I’ll keep looking at the watch on my wrist. BTW, I don’t have a watch on my wrist.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: Why did you stop blogging for the past half year?</strong></p> <p>A: One participant at the conference asked me what happened to my blog. I was very grateful to his attention. Yes, I was super busy last year. I planned to finish writing a book, but ended up being interrupted for a few times for software development work (e.g., when Pandoc 2.8 was released, and Bootstrap 3 was officially dead). With about two dozens of R packages on my shoulders, I have found it more and more difficult to focus on any new projects. In 2019, I released a single new package in total (<a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/rolldown\"><strong>rolldown</strong></a>). To be honest, I was not very satisfied with its quality, and I only spent less than a week’s time on developing this package. I often felt overwhelmed by the number of Github issues and Stack Overflow questions every day. Eventually I decided that I could not afford the whole R Markdown ecosystem and should hire someone to help me, although I had already got <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/help-answer-questions/\">several big faithful helpers</a>. Apologies in advance to those who are interested in this new position—it has been there for a month but I didn’t promote it because I have already had a few perfect candidates, and it is going to be really tough for me to make the decision.</p></li> \n <li><p><strong>Q: What’s your next project? In other words, what’s going to be “down” next?</strong></p> <p>A: I don’t have a plan yet. I need to finish writing the current book first. If you have any cool ideas, I’d always love to listen.</p></li> \n</ul> \n<h2 id=\"final-words\">Final words</h2> \n<p>Emil Hvitfeldt has kindly put together <a href=\"https://github.com/EmilHvitfeldt/RStudioConf2020Slides\">a Github repo</a> to collect all materials related to this conference. RStudio also has an official repo to do this, but it seems we forgot to promote it: <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rstudio-conf\">https://github.com/rstudio/rstudio-conf</a>.</p> \n<p>I’d like to thank the TAs of our <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio-conf-2020/rmarkdown-dashboard\">R Markdown workshop</a> for their help over the two days, including Hadrien (who also wrote <a href=\"https://medium.com/@HadrienD/f5adff36e2cc\">a useful post on customizing CSS</a> later), <a href=\"https://www.linkedin.com/in/melanie-mayer-69554214a/\">Melanie</a>, <a href=\"https://adisarid.github.io\">Adi</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.jienamclellan.com\">Jiena</a>. I also want to thank my co-instructor Carl, who did most of the work at the workshop and made my (teaching) job much easier. Many thanks to my RStudio colleagues, who have made a lot of effort to make this conference a success. Every time I go to this conference, I feel very much inspired by the attendees. Thank you all for your encouragement and kind words! I’m especially glad to see that people like <a href=\"http://lcolladotor.github.io/2020/02/03/conference-feelings-from-newbie-to-sponsor/\">Leonardo Collado-Torres</a> started to follow Emily’s call to become sponsors. At the same time, I also feel proud that <a href=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/01/29/rstudio-pbc/\">RStudio has become a Public Benefit Corporation</a>.</p> \n<p>Lastly, if anyone is going to the useR! conference this year, I’ll be happy to meet you in St. Louis in July! I’ll do <a href=\"https://user2020.r-project.org/program/tutorials/\">a tutorial</a> there, and hopefully also a talk. If things (I mean, kids, of course) go well, I’ll also go to rstudio::conf(2021) in Orlando FL.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:1\">If you are curious about how the “typing effect” was implemented in this blog post, view its HTML source and look for the JS code below the <code>&lt;pre&gt;</code> tag. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/#fnref:1\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:2\">Well, I don’t know if he started using computers in kindergarten, but he did have a cute and handsome picture of himself at that time. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/#fnref:2\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:3\">It didn’t take me three hours to figure out the solution. I was mainly chatting with passersby as I was updating the packages, and the WiFi was fairly slow. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/#fnref:3\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 06 Feb 2020 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/EPVdNgAUwAAwi17?format=jpg&name=medium","linkMd5":"d47342c392edfd47e5bd6b2421b5fc28","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn39@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-36-236_2fc444a9dd36e878.webp","destWidth":1200,"destHeight":900,"sourceBytes":230574,"destBytes":151790,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/EPVdNgAUwAAwi17?format=jpg&name=medium":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn39@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-36-236_2fc444a9dd36e878.webp","https://slides.yihui.org/gif/awesome-me.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn48@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-20-01-938_197f30067dc51edf.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973439},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Romain Lesur: The Most Wonderful Collaborator I Have Ever Worked With","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2019/07/romain-lesur/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>In the footnote #4 of <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/\">a blog post</a> from last year, I said that Romain Lesur was <em>the</em> most wonderful collaborator with whom I had ever worked with in my software career. <a href=\"https://twitter.com/jozefhajnala/status/1089607705289990144\">I meant to write a blog post about it</a> but it was so hard to find time this year. Finally I have uploaded <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/rolldown\">the <strong>rolldown</strong> package</a> to CRAN, and can take a brief break now.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-birth-of-pagedown\">The birth of pagedown</h2>\n\n<p>Romain first came to my attention in September last year when <a href=\"https://twitter.com/RLesur/status/1037126641846312967\">he tweeted about Paged.js</a>. Now I don&rsquo;t remember how I noticed this tweet. Perhaps it was because Christophe Dervieux, Heather Turner, or Nicolas Roelandt liked it, and Twitter figured out I liked these people (I do), so Twitter recommended it to me. Perhaps it was because it contained the <code>#bookdown</code> hashtag by chance.</p>\n\n<p>Anyway, I happened to be thinking of something similar around that time, because I had always <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/in-html-i-trust/\">believed in HTML and web technologies</a> in terms of typesetting. I saw <a href=\"https://github.com/RelaxedJS/ReLaXed\">ReLaXed</a> earlier and was excited about it (especially the incredible examples made out of CSS and HTML). Everything looked perfect and beautiful, but there were two problems: 1) One couldn&rsquo;t write the full content in Markdown naturally (there was Markdown support, but the full document had to be HTML or Pug); 2) ReLaXed was a Node.js package. Of course, I was looking for something that could work best with R and R Markdown without an external system dependency. Then Romain&rsquo;s tweet opened a door for me just in time. Since Paged.js is a JavaScript library, we can easily integrate it into R through the HTML Widgets framework. Problems solved.</p>\n\n<p>One week later, I emailed Romain:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>On Thu, Sep 13, 2018 at 8:34 AM Yihui Xie &lt;???@gmail.com&gt; wrote:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Hi Romain,</p>\n\n<p>I have been looking at <a href=\"https://github.com/RLesur/weasydoc\">your <strong>weasydoc</strong> package</a> and thanks again for bringing Paged.js to my attention. I have been very excited, and am still learning more about it. I have to say you opened a new door through which I feel I&rsquo;ll be able to fulfill <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/in-html-i-trust/\">one of my life dreams</a>.</p>\n\n<p>I&rsquo;m going to devote some serious time in this, and I hope we can collaborate on it (unless you have finished all the work :).</p>\n\n<p>Regards,<br />\nYihui</p>\n</blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n\n<h2 id=\"what-made-romain-a-great-collaborator-of-mine\">What made Romain a great collaborator of mine</h2>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/\">I have admitted that</a> I&rsquo;m a terrible person to collaborate with. Usually I tend to do most of the work on my own, and do not collaborate with other people substantially. I have reviewed and merged many pull requests on Github, but these projects typically have only one absolute owner (me). I think <strong>pagedown</strong> is the first project in which I have communicated with a co-author frequently and haven&rsquo;t made all the decisions only by myself. There are a few reasons why I regarded Romain as a great collaborator to me:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>His skill set complements mine perfectly. I know R Markdown and HTML Widgets well, and he knows Paged.js and Pandoc Lua filters. Since he joined me in the work of <strong>pagedown</strong>, I lost the motivation to learn more about Paged.js and Pandoc filters. Besides, he read the Chrome Developer Protocol and <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown/pull/30\">single-handedly wrote</a> the wonderful <code>pagedown::chrome_print()</code> function. I confess that this is still a mystery to me, and I&rsquo;m unable to reach the depth in these technologies as Romain did.
