He has submitted <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown/pulls?q=is%3Apr+author%3ARLesur\">many other pull requests</a> to <strong>pagedown</strong>, and I often merged them with awe (e.g., <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown/pull/16\">#16</a>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown/pull/21\">#21</a>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown/pull/43\">#43</a>, and <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown/pull/48\">#48</a>). In particular, my jaw dropped when I saw he reinvented the Chapman &amp; Hall/CRC book style and the Journal of Statistical Software style with CSS. Epic magic! That was perfect timing for <a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2019/pagedown-creating-beautiful-pdfs-with-r-markdown-and-css\">my rstudio::conf(2019) talk</a>.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>His debugging skills are amazing. Debugging requires extreme attention to details, which Romain has clearly manifested. I&rsquo;m often surprised by his simple and elegant fixes to bugs that looked complicated in my eyes.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>He is a very creative hacker and knows how to take advantage of existing tools to solve a hard problem in an novel way. <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/50946083/559676\">This Stack Overflow question</a> asked by Colin Fay (also an excellent hacker) and answered by Romain is a great example to show what an expert of WebSockets Romain is. I, for one, also love hacking the RStudio Viewer. The fact that the RStudio IDE is essentially a web browser can bring forward many fun possibilities.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>He is exceptionally humble&mdash;so humble that I have to remind him a few times that his role is a co-author and I&rsquo;m not qualified to lead him as the &ldquo;<a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1110978126618771458\">leading author</a>&rdquo;. Anyway, in the first few months of <strong>pagedown</strong>, he had always submitted his work via pull requests instead of pushing to the master branch directly (although I had granted him write access), to make sure he wouldn&rsquo;t break anything and had respected my coding style. That was both careful and considerate.</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>Somehow he also established a personal connection with the Paged.js developers, which turned out to be very important for the <strong>pagedown</strong> package, because sometimes bugs came from the Paged.js library and we could talk to Paged.js developers to get them fixed quickly. Apparently, both the Paged.js developers and the Shuttleworth Foundation noticed his talent soon. Later he was awarded a grant from the foundation.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"romain-s-other-contributions\">Romain&rsquo;s other contributions</h2>\n\n<p>Besides <strong>pagedown</strong>, I have seen Romain help me in several other Github repositories that I maintain, although I didn&rsquo;t ask him to. To be clear, I do need help, and always do. Below is an incomplete list of Github issues with which Romain has helped recently:</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p><a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/bookdown/issues/733\">rstudio/bookdown#733</a> (Margin/div bug when rendering gitbook). Multiple users had confirmed the bug, but I was too busy and lazy to install both Pandoc 2.7.2 and 2.7.3 to test it by myself. Romain did the vast majority of the debugging work, identified the root cause, and made it trivially easy for me to fix the issue.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/1577\">rstudio/rmarkdown#1577</a> (HTML tabsets don&rsquo;t work with shiny runtime). Similarly, this bug was confirmed by multiple users. I suspected it was a really bad bug, but I didn&rsquo;t have a clue off the top of my head. I didn&rsquo;t know how long it would take me to figure it out. Romain carefully examined the problem, identified the cause, and submitted a perfect pull request <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/pull/1587\">rstudio/rmarkdown#1587</a>, in which he fixed not only the original issue but also two additional potential issues. His explanation and minimal examples were so clear.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/bookdown/issues/683\">rstudio/bookdown#683</a> (MathJax info shown in references). This was a really weird bug in my eyes. I said I was able to reproduce it but didn&rsquo;t have time to fix it. Romain saw my reply and took a stab at it. He made a minimal reproducible example <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/1593\">rstudio/rmarkdown#1593</a>, and submitted a simple fix <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/pull/1594\">rstudio/rmarkdown#1594</a>.</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>Romain&rsquo;s first pull request to packages that I maintain was <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/pull/1243\">rstudio/rmarkdown#1243</a>, created on Jan 14, 2018. I didn&rsquo;t know him back then, but the pull request showed his kindness to help and also his humbleness (&ldquo;Sorry, I&rsquo;m not a CSS expert!&rdquo;).</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"summary\">Summary</h2>\n\n<p>To sum it up, Romain has exactly the skills that I don&rsquo;t have but definitely need, has a creative mind as a hacker, shares the same belief in the web, is able to sense my headache in Github issues, and often actively helps me in a way that really minimizes the work on my shoulders. I know he is <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/pagedown/issues/120#issuecomment-511243498\">currently on vacation</a>, and probably won&rsquo;t see this post until three weeks later, but anyway, I want to thank him so much for being such a wonderful collaborator as well as all the help along the way!</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/duiyou-4.gif\" alt=\"A wonderful collaborator\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2019/07/romain-lesur/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 16 Jul 2019 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/duiyou-4.gif","linkMd5":"1e684ef14e33274c3f31ee10554b6d76","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn11@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-35-096_59e8f733c6826080.webp","destWidth":270,"destHeight":180,"sourceBytes":1305566,"destBytes":435734,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/duiyou-4.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn11@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-35-096_59e8f733c6826080.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973438},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Make the Right / Positive Things Easier to Do than Wrong / Negative Things","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/right-easier-than-wrong/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>Last week I saw a fantastic post written by Jay Hanlon from Stack Overflow titled &ldquo;<a href=\"https://stackoverflow.blog/2018/04/26/stack-overflow-isnt-very-welcoming-its-time-for-that-to-change/\">Stack Overflow Isn&rsquo;t Very Welcoming. It&rsquo;s Time for That to Change</a>&rdquo;. I wanted to stand up in front of my computer and applaud for five minutes after reading this post. It is great that Stack Overflow was admitting the problem publicly and showing the intention to fix it.</p>\n\n<p>First of all, I should make it clear that I do love Stack Overflow, use it almost every day, and truly appreciate its value, despite of my previous rants. I ranted just because I care a lot about this site and I wish it continued success for a really long time.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-two-sides\">The two sides</h2>\n\n<p>In this post, I want to share a single thought on how to make Stack Overflow more welcoming, which I feel is ignored in the current design / model of Stack Overflow. That is, so far it has been almost equally easy to make Stack Overflow welcoming and unwelcoming. I think this is problematic.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"upvoting-vs-downvoting-and-voting-to-close-questions\">Upvoting vs downvoting, and voting to close questions</h2>\n\n<p>Take voting for example. Upvoting and downvoting are equally easy, but the levels of love and hurt felt are definitely not the same. It hurts much more after your question/answer is downvoted than how much you feel encouraged after you get an upvote, probably because of the pain of losing something is more overwhelming than the joy of gaining something. A downvote on Stack Overflow is pretty much like someone gives you a thumbs-down in public. It could cause shame or anger. A downvote without an explanation is even worse. I&rsquo;d suggest that downvoting must be accompanied with explanations.</p>\n\n<p>Putting questions on hold has the same problem. It takes the equal number of people (five) to vote to close/hold and open a question. Requiring more people to vote to close a question than re-opening a question may be a better idea. If that does not sound fair, I think it definitely makes sense to at least require evidence of expertise before a user can vote to close a question. For example, if you have never answered a single question related to any of the tags of the current question, you should not be eligible to close a question. Currently anyone can vote to close any questions, which was the main reason of my previous rants (<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/12/so-bounties/\">first one</a>, <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/closed-so-question/\">second one</a>). In these cases, I don&rsquo;t believe they really knew what they were doing when they voted to close a question (they might be really kind people trying to help Stack Overflow, but the outcome was really bad). I even believe some people just blindly follow other people to vote. To prevent this kind of blind voting, Stack Overflow should let users vote <em>independently</em>, i.e., do not show previous votes. I believe if five users independently voted to close a question, there is a much higher chance that it is a truly bad question or simply spam.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"feelings-matter\">Feelings matter</h2>\n\n<p>Building a welcoming online community without losing the quality is definitely not easy, especially for a community of the scale of Stack Overflow. I have been running a Q&amp;A web forum for more than ten years. I have seen many times how moderators unintentionally treat beginners poorly, although these moderators have contributed a lot in the past and are often helpful. Many people just forget what it feels like to be a beginner after they become experts. When they make you feel the hostility, they may not really mean it. However, as Jay wrote in his post:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>[&hellip;] Feelings have no &ldquo;technically correct.&rdquo; They&rsquo;re just what the feeler is telling you. When someone tells you how they feel,  you can pack up your magnifying glass and clue kit, cuz that&rsquo;s the answer. You&rsquo;re done. And a lot of devs feel like Stack Overflow is an intimidating, unwelcoming place. We know because they tell us.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>No matter what you really mean, if users have got the bad feelings, there is a problem. I don&rsquo;t think <a href=\"https://medium.com/dunder-data/10-000-stack-overflow-toxicity-challenge-75951a739993\">a $10,000 challenge to prove the Stack Overflow toxicity</a> makes much sense (but this is really another topic).</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"easy-but-wrong-vs-harder-but-correct\">Easy but wrong, vs harder but correct</h2>\n\n<p>I&rsquo;m going to finish this post with a recent example to illustrate the point of &ldquo;people tend to avoid doing the right things if they are harder, and will do wrong things if they are easier.&rdquo; Five users have voted to close the recent post &ldquo;<a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/50084663/559676\">Graphs don&rsquo;t show when Rmarkdown posted to blogdown on shiny server</a>&rdquo;. Let me show you what it looks like when it was first posted:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://db.yihui.org/images/so-no-edit.png#border\" alt=\"No one bothers to edit the post\" /></p>\n\n<p>The OP, Rain Wei, was apparently a completely beginner on Stack Overflow. His (apologies if I guessed the gender wrong) reputation point was one. What happened to this post in two hours?</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>Someone (not the one who first commented) downvoted it, without an explanation.</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Five people voted to close it, and they succeeded. I encourage you to look at the profile pages of these five people, and will let you judge whether they are experts of Shiny Server or <strong>blogdown</strong>.</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>Actually these are not the main problem I want to mention. The main problem is, one person downvoted the question, five people voted to close the question, but&hellip; but none of them bothered to edit the post to show the images! Why is that? My guess is <em>voting is much easier than editing a post</em>. However, the question would be much clearer, had anyone with the editing privilege edited it, because these screenshots were actually very informative!</p>\n\n<p>For those who are not familiar with Stack Overflow, users with only one reputation point cannot post images. That is why the OP could not show the images. The post had been viewed more than 20 times before I saw it, yet no one wanted to help. I edited the post (twice, because the OP, as a beginner, overrode my first revision) to show the images. How much effort it really is? Adding four exclamation marks (turn <code>[]()</code> to <code>![]()</code>).</p>\n\n<p>I think it is a valid question after the screenshots are displayed (so I upvoted it), but it will require substantial expertise about Shiny Server to answer the question. I doubt it will ever be answered, but I don&rsquo;t think it should be closed.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"an-old-but-relevant-thought-about-reproducible-research\">An old but relevant thought about reproducible research</h2>\n\n<p>Well, an old thought just came to my mind, so I decided to add another section. I think everyone will agree that reproducible research is a great idea and the right thing to do, even twenty years ago. Knuth invented literate programming in 1984, which happened to be a really nice foundation for reproducible research (probably not what he expected). However, I have always believed it is not wise to tie the implementation to TeX/LaTeX when it comes to writing reproducible reports (especially for data analysis). Reproducible research is the correct thing to do, but LaTeX is relatively hard. To make reproducible research easier and more popular, we have to choose a popular authoring format, which should not be LaTeX. I&rsquo;m not saying LaTeX is bad. It can be a great tool for experts. I just don&rsquo;t think it is worth the time when your goal is sharing knowledge instead of typesetting. Obviously you all know I&rsquo;m implying Markdown is a better choice for average users. Markdown is a weak language, but it makes the right thing, reproducible research, easier (and sometimes even enjoyable) to do than the wrong thing (point-and-click and/or cut-and-paste data analysis). That&rsquo;s it.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/05/right-easier-than-wrong/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 02 May 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://db.yihui.org/images/so-no-edit.png#border","linkMd5":"7272b43714257310f4a1c92594de25c1","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn15@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-36-489_8e1365f7bd5c6a39.webp","destWidth":750,"destHeight":738,"sourceBytes":102783,"destBytes":79336,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://db.yihui.org/images/so-no-edit.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn15@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-36-489_8e1365f7bd5c6a39.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973462},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Once in a Blue Moon, I Reached \"Issue Zero\" in a Github Repo","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/issue-zero/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>&ldquo;Inbox Zero&rdquo; (no emails in the inbox) may never be possible to me again, but today I reached a goal of &ldquo;Issue Zero&rdquo; for the Github repo <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles\">rstudio/rticles</a>. I have to say this is so rare. In fact, it is the very first time I have been able to close <em>all</em> issues in a Github repo.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/42387508-1158fcbc-8108-11e8-8e09-7fe979ac1a13.png#border\" alt=\"no more rticles issues\" /></p>\n\n<h2 id=\"answer-all-questions-and-kill-all-bugs\">Answer all questions and kill all bugs</h2>\n\n<p>Last week <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/178\">we were reminded</a> of the fact that the <strong>rticles</strong> package had not been updated on CRAN for more than a year. Its original maintainer (JJ) asked me for help, so I spent the last week and this week on the new CRAN release. Initially I only planned to take a look at existing pull requests, which contained new article formats contributed from other users. After reviewing all pull requests, I also looked at the issues. I knew there was an annoying long-standing bug (you could not use <code>$</code> in JSS or R Journal articles), which I really hoped to fix, because it has affected multiple users (<a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/6\">#6</a>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/10\">#10</a>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/49\">#49</a>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/70\">#70</a>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/132\">#132</a>, <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/149\">#149</a>, and <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/398\">rstudio/rmarkdown#398</a>).</p>\n\n<p>Once I started working on Github issues, I thought it might be good to take advantage of the inertia, which is often hard to obtain. Finally I answered all questions and fixed all bugs reported in these issues. During this process, I realized that <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/71\">we didn&rsquo;t even support bibliography</a> for The R Journal articles. That was such a shame. How could we have provided such a half-baked product? It revealed the fact that <strong>rticles</strong> maintainers at RStudio didn&rsquo;t really write journal articles with the <strong>rticles</strong> package (i.e., we don&rsquo;t eat our own <strong>rticles</strong> dog food), which means we are probably not the most appropriate people to work on these article formats. We absolutely need more contribution from real authors and LaTeX experts. On the other hand, this also means <strong>rticles</strong> users may have better luck when asking questions in other public forums such as Stack Overflow or RStudio Community instead of Github issues, because we maintainers are not experts of these article formats, and I don&rsquo;t want you to wait for four years for me to answer your question.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"no-more-looking-for-missing-latex-packages\">No more looking for missing LaTeX packages</h2>\n\n<p>The best thing I did for this release was <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/pull/130\">enabling Travis CI</a> for this repo (thanks to Zhian N. Kamvar). Yes, it was so late, and such a big shame. We should have enabled Travis CI years ago. Previously JJ was just trying to manually test everything locally on his computer when new pull requests came in.</p>\n\n<p>One nice thing about the Travis setup is that it uses (and caches) <a href=\"https://yihui.org/tinytex/\">TinyTeX</a>, so that any missing standard LaTeX packages required by the article formats can be automatically installed on Travis (and cached). No <code>sudo</code> is required.</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-yaml\">cache:\n  packages: true\n  directories:\n    - $HOME/.TinyTeX\nlatex: false\n\nenv:\n  global:\n    - PATH=$HOME/bin:$PATH\n\nbefore_install:\n  - wget -qO- https://yihui.org/gh/tinytex/tools/download-travis-linux.sh | sh\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>At this moment, the setup for R on Travis adds symlinks of TeX Live executables to <code>/usr/local/bin</code>, which prevents us from installing other executables (such as <code>epstopdf</code>). That is why I disabled the default LaTeX installation <code>latex: false</code>.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"hi-journal-of-statistical-software\">Hi, Journal of Statistical Software</h2>\n\n<p>Early last year, the editor of JSS said the JSS article format in <strong>rticles</strong> <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/102\">was &ldquo;unacceptable&rdquo;</a>. I was sad to hear that. I&rsquo;m <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/12/formatting-papers/\">always sad to hear</a> formatting issues could be real and critical issues. Anyway, after some communication with the editor, we happily and <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/commit/65a66b51\">easily</a> resolved the main issue (bibliography should have been generated via <strong>natbib</strong>). So it was actually not too bad. After fixing the dollar sign issue mentioned before, and the issue of authors on multiple lines (<a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rticles/issues/100\">#100</a>), personally I feel the JSS article format in <strong>rticles</strong> is pretty good now. The new version of <strong>rticles</strong> (v0.5) has been released on CRAN. Let&rsquo;s see if we can survive the journal of the highest LaTeX standard in the world this time.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/awesome-me.gif\" alt=\"Am I awesome enough now?\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/issue-zero/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 06 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/42387508-1158fcbc-8108-11e8-8e09-7fe979ac1a13.png#border","linkMd5":"2ded77b3707c9531e07d41e3ede2cb91","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn56@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-34-846_24e4d29f009e863f.webp","destWidth":1236,"destHeight":550,"sourceBytes":67185,"destBytes":21832,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/42387508-1158fcbc-8108-11e8-8e09-7fe979ac1a13.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn56@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-34-846_24e4d29f009e863f.webp","https://slides.yihui.org/gif/awesome-me.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn48@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-20-01-938_197f30067dc51edf.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Netlify is Hiring Its First Data Scientist","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/netlify-data-scientist/","description":"<p>Netlify was a company that I started to appreciate very much from 2017. I could feel how committed it was to open source, its passion for making it super convenient for people to publish content to the web, and also its amazing customer service.</p> \n<p>Now they are trying to hire their first ever data scientist. The full position <a href=\"https://hire.withgoogle.com/public/jobs/netlifycom/view/P_AAAAAACAAADHCD4tYrNqFs\">can be found here</a>. I’m helping here for two reasons: (1) As I said, I truly appreciate what they have been doing; (2) This position looks like an excellent challenge to data scientists in my eyes. I have never published any posts for a commercial company before in my blog (not even for my own employer), but I don’t really care if it is commercial or not. It looks like a good company doing very meaningful work in my eyes. That’s it. Of course, they didn’t pay me to say good words.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Had-they-really\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/netlify-data-scientist/#fn:Had-they-really\">1</a></sup></p> \n<p>That said, you should definitely judge through your own eyes during the interview if you decide to apply. All I was doing here was to share the job position as a grateful customer.</p> \n<p>BTW, I also want to take this opportunity to praise an amazing customer service person at Netlify named Chris M,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Not-sure-what-hi\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/netlify-data-scientist/#fn:Not-sure-what-hi\">2</a></sup> who has diligently answered a lot of my questions, even when I was not a paying customer. Sometimes I receive replies in Friday evenings, and once he even continued to follow up on a question I asked a year ago! I imagine they must receive a lot of questions every day, so this is amazing to me.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Had-they-really\">Had they really paid me, I wouldn’t publish this post. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/netlify-data-scientist/#fnref:Had-they-really\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Not-sure-what-hi\">Not sure what his last name is; perhaps Mccraw? <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/netlify-data-scientist/#fnref:Not-sure-what-hi\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/netlify-data-scientist/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 23 Feb 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"3617eab1b6a0d0111f4ec3a0daeee0b8","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973465},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Quick Notes on Some rstudio::conf(2020) Talks after I Watched the Videos","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-videos/","description":"\n        <p>Last week I finished watching the videos of talks that I was interested in but missed at rstudio::conf(2020). Now I&rsquo;m writing down some quick notes and random thoughts. The list of talks below has no particular order.</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/rstudio-open-source-software-for-data-science-j-j-allaire\"><strong>Open Source Software for Data Science - J.J. Allaire</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>This talk triggered some of my thoughts. I have always been pround of RStudio for its deep commitment to open source. I feel one obvious thing that people often ignore is that open source developers need to eat and live&mdash;they are also humans. Open source is free as in both speech and beer. &ldquo;Free as in beer&rdquo; can be a substantial challenge to open source developers to make a living, which is perhaps why we often see open source projects are side jobs of developers in their spare time. In 2014 (when I was still very cynical), <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/449309622025740289\">I wrote on Twitter</a>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>&ldquo;THOU SHALL NOT BECOME RICH&rdquo;, as if GPL had said.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I don&rsquo;t remember whose or which tweet motivated me to say that. I guess it was a certain tweet of John Myles White (he also retweeted mine later). I said that because I felt some people were so unreasonable that they thought open source developers had to live a poor life, and were not allowed to earn money out of their work. That was unfair.</p>\n\n<p>I have been a long-time admirer of JJ. It is rare to see someone who is both a visionary entrepreneur and an excellent open source software developer. I&rsquo;m very grateful that I have been given the chance to live a decent life while doing 100% open source. I&rsquo;m excited that RStudio <a href=\"https://blog.rstudio.com/2020/01/29/rstudio-pbc/\">has become a PBC</a>, which is a much stronger guarantee for our commitment to open source in the future.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/styling-shiny-apps-with-sass-and-bootstrap-4-joe-cheng\"><strong>Styling Shiny apps with Sass and Bootstrap 4 - Joe Cheng</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>I watched this talk mainly because Joshua Goldberg said to me later that &ldquo;Some people can try to be funny, while some people are inherently funny, and Joe is inherently funny.&rdquo; Then he repeated one funny conversation in the talk:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>Audience: &hellip;</p>\n\n<p>Joe: Oh, increase the font size?</p>\n\n<p>Audience: Thank you!</p>\n\n<p>Joe: I didn&rsquo;t know if that voice was coming from inside my head. So thanks.</p>\n\n<p>[Laughter]</p>\n\n<p>Joe: Yes, mother?</p>\n\n<p>[Laughter]</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Joe is very capable of making instant fun during a talk. I&rsquo;m not very good at this. I need to prepare the fun. I can often think of a much better and funnier answer after the talk, and regret not having given the better version. For example, next time if someone asks me &ldquo;What&rsquo;s UpDog?&rdquo; again after <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-2020/\">my talk</a>, I&rsquo;d answer &ldquo;Not much, what&rsquo;s up with you? And by the way, I&rsquo;m not a dog.&rdquo;</p>\n\n<p>Anyway, transitioning from Bootstrap 3 to 4 will be the next big thing in R Markdown, too. Joe and Carson have done a huge amount of work behind it. I really appreciate it.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/rebecca-barter\"><strong>Becoming an R blogger - Rebecca Barter</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>I always strongly encourage people to build their personal websites (and write blog posts if possible), and I&rsquo;d also recommend that you watch this lightning talk. I don&rsquo;t think I knew Rebecca before, but after I watched it, it suddenly occurred to me that Bin (Yu) once mentioned her to me. I dug out the email and it was her indeed.</p>\n\n<p>In her blog post &ldquo;<a href=\"http://www.rebeccabarter.com/blog/2020-02-03_blogger/\">Becoming an R blogger</a>&rdquo;, she listed 4 reasons to start a blog, and a bonus reason&mdash;somone might send you a nice email. So I sent her a nice email after I read her post. Of course, she was absolutely correct.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/lightning-talk-dr-amelia-mcnamara\"><strong>Making a tidy dress - Amelia McNamara</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>This lightning talk is an unusual one but also very interesting and inspiring. I wrote the <strong>knitr</strong> package, but I have no real knitting skills. It was refreshing to see how a dress was made. The hex logos is only one obvious highlight of the dress. The other highlight is that the dress has pockets!!</p>\n\n<p>In this talk, I learned the correct pronunciation of &ldquo;sew&rdquo; for the first time. It had <a href=\"https://twitter.com/ma_salmon/status/1229821025342324737\">surprised Maëlle Salmon, too</a>. Then a few other people became interested in watching the talk just because they started to doubt if they knew how to pronounce &ldquo;sew&rdquo;.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/r-for-graphical-clinical-trial-reporting-frank-harrell\"><strong>R for Graphical Clinical Trial Reporting - Frank Harrell</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>I wish I&rsquo;d be as good at embracing new technologies as <a href=\"https://www.fharrell.com\">Frank</a> when I&rsquo;m his age. I know he has been almost a lifetime LaTeX user, but he also quickly adopted HTML output formats from R Markdown. I find it amazing that as the founding Chair of the Department of Biostatistics at Vanderbilt, he still often asks technical questions on Stack Overflow (just a few days ago I saw him ask a <strong>blogdown</strong> question here). I thought people in such high positions would no longer be interested in software problems.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/we-re-hitting-r-a-million-times-a-day-so-we-made-a-talk-about-it-heather-nolis-dr-jacqueline-nolis\"><strong>We&rsquo;re hitting R a million times a day so we made a talk about it - Heather Nolis &amp; Jacqueline Nolis</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>The next time when someone says R can&rsquo;t be used in production again, I&rsquo;ll send them this video.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/flipbooks-evangeline-reynolds\"><strong>Flipbooks - Evangeline Reynolds</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>Simple yet creative idea. Great implementation. The <strong>flipbookr</strong> package inspired me of another idea, and I&rsquo;ll spend more time on thinking about it. It might become another exciting and useful R package.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/using-r-to-create-reproducible-engineering-test-reports-braulio-cuandon-ana-alyeska-santos\"><strong>Using R to Create Reproducible Engineering Test Reports - Braulio Cuandon &amp; Ana Alyeska Santos</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>I watched this talk both in awe and with sympathy&mdash;in awe because they did such an incredible job to make the Word output from R Markdown compliant to their company and FDA regulatory standards (by post-processing Word as XML documents), and with sympathy because the standards sounded incredibly strict. If I heard them correctly, some &ldquo;standards&rdquo; seemed to be purely cosmetic, which reminded me of the paper by LeBlanc et. al: <a href=\"https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0223116\">Scientific sinkhole: The pernicious price of formatting</a>.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/3d-ggplots-with-rayshader-dr-tyler-morgan-wall\"><strong>3D ggplots with rayshader - Tyler Morgan-Wall</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>To me, this is the most visually appealing talk of the conference. Tyler also did <a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2019/3d-mapping-plotting-and-printing-with-rayshader\">an awesome job</a> at the last year&rsquo;s rstudio::conf.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/lightning-talk-colin-rundel\"><strong><code>livecode</code>: broadcast your live coding sessions from and to RStudio - Colin Rundel</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>From the technical perspective, this was the talk that I loved the best. As I mentioned <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/back-to-dt/\">in a post in 2018</a>, I think <strong>httpuv</strong> is one of the most undervalued R packages. It was created mainly because of <strong>shiny</strong>, but its possible applications can be far beyond <strong>shiny</strong>. I was only aware of two substantial applications: <strong>plumber</strong> and <strong>fiery</strong>. Colin&rsquo;s <strong>livecode</strong> package was the fourth most interesting application of <strong>httpuv</strong> that came to my attention. I believe it will be extremely useful in teaching. As I mentioned to Colin <a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1230161301420347394\">on Twitter</a>, I felt some <a href=\"https://github.com/rundel/livecode/blob/master/DESCRIPTION\">hard dependencies</a> could be moved to &ldquo;Suggests&rdquo; to make this package more lightweight. For example, I don&rsquo;t quite believe <strong>crayon</strong> has to be a hard dependency, and am <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/\">still not convinced</a> that <strong>glue</strong> is superior than <code>paste()</code> or <code>sprintf()</code>.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/object-of-type-closure-is-not-subsettable-jenny-bryan\"><strong>Object of type &lsquo;closure&rsquo; is not subsettable - Jenny Bryan</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>A clever title, apparently. This talk resonated very well with me, and I encourage all R users to watch it. It will be tremendously helpful if you learn some basic debugging skills, and more importantly, learn how to report bugs appropriately. I think it resonated well with me because I&rsquo;m a developer, and have gone through the pain of having too little information to answer a user&rsquo;s question or bug report for numerous times.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/lightning-talk-amanda-gadrow\"><strong>Lessons about R I learned from my cat - Amanda Gadrow</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>Adorable, amusing, and useful. Let&rsquo;s thank Amanda&rsquo;s cat.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/cat-hands.gif\" alt=\"Thank you, kitten!\" /></p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/how-to-win-an-ai-hackathon-without-using-ai-colin-gillespie\"><strong>How to win an AI Hackathon, without using AI - Colin Gillespie</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>Before you build the models and jump on AI, ask yourself again if you are solving the right problem. Do you want to predict a future leakage, or reduce the cost of repairing the leakage? They are different problems, and the latter probably doesn&rsquo;t really require AI.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/of-teacups-giraffes-and-r-markdown-desiree-de-leon\"><strong>Of Teacups, Giraffes, &amp; R Markdown - Desiree De Leon</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>Sophisticated yet cute. I watched this talk together with my two-year old. He was not even able to count to 20, not to mention standard deviations or histograms, but he cheered at every giraffe in the talk.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/rmarkdown-driven-development-emily-riederer\"><strong>RMarkdown Driven Development - Emily Riederer</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>If you ever work on projects of moderate sizes that involve R Markdown, I highly recommend this talk. It teaches you a range of skills to manage projects of different sizes by incorporating software development best practices.</p></li>\n\n<li><p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020/the-glamour-of-graphics-william-chase\"><strong>The Glamour of Graphics - William Chase</strong></a></p>\n\n<p>This title is perhaps even cleverer than Jenny&rsquo;s. It is a play on &ldquo;The Grammar of Graphics.&rdquo; If you don&rsquo;t have much experience in (graphics) design like me, you will learn some simple and effective principles from Will. BTW, Will also has an amazing elegantly designed <a href=\"https://www.williamrchase.com\">personal website</a> that I recommend you to check out.</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>If you are interested in other talks from this conference, you may find all of them at <a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020\">https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2020</a>.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/rstudio-conf-videos/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 25 Feb 2020 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/cat-hands.gif","linkMd5":"4b8789181ba49c2e1109d191641cae0c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn100@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-35-309_958a8b65b31bc921.webp","destWidth":360,"destHeight":203,"sourceBytes":1866004,"destBytes":323024,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/cat-hands.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn100@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-35-309_958a8b65b31bc921.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973436},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Isn't Medium an Obviously Terrible Platform for Blogging?","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/terrible-medium/","description":"<p>Last week I read an article on Medium.com <a href=\"https://medium.com/@nikitonsky/medium-is-a-poor-choice-for-blogging-bb0048d19133\">criticizing Medium.com</a> (the fact that it was published on Medium sounded quite amusing to me). I have never truly understood the huge success of Medium. When it was born, it seemed to be “yet another WordPress.com or Blogger.com or MSN Space” in my eyes, with two major differences. One is its fresh-looking style. The other is the tools for user/reader engagement.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-three-css-attributes\">The three CSS attributes</h2> \n<p>I guess the following three CSS attributes can explain half of Medium’s success:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-css\">p {\n  font-family: medium-content-serif-font,Georgia,Cambria,\"Times New Roman\",Times,serif;\n  font-size: 21px;\n  line-height: 1.58;\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>That is, Medium uses a custom typeface <code>medium-content-serif-font</code> (and will fall back to Georgia and other serif fonts), a large font size that is not too commonly seen on the web (21px), and a large line height.</p> \n<ul> \n <li><p>As I have said a few times in my <strong>blogdown</strong> talks, the best font is always the font you have never used or seen. Our eyes have already been deeply bored by Georgia, Cambria, Times New Roman, Arial, Helvetica, Verdana, and all classical fonts. I’m pretty sure that in a year or two, everyone will be bored by this new <code>medium-content-serif-font</code>, too, no matter how fresh it looks right now.</p></li> \n <li><p>The font size of 21px is 30% larger than Chrome’s default (16px). A larger font size can make readers feel comfortable and easier to focus. A side-effect (not sure if it is good or bad) is that an article will look longer. In my eyes, most modern web pages are too crowded with dense information or distracting elements, and it is becoming harder to read the actual body of a page. Is a larger font size an effective antidote? I don’t know. I just feel it is kind of ironic, as if there were a silent yet furious war among different elements on web pages to get our attention.</p></li> \n <li><p>The large line height has the same effect as the large font size. I also use a larger line height myself, to make it easier for my eyes to find the next line after finishing reading the previous line. A smaller body width can also help in this regard (which is why I actually like LaTeX’s default narrow page width).</p></li> \n</ul> \n<h2 id=\"claps\">Claps</h2> \n<p>Claps may explain the other half of Medium’s success. Traditional social media platforms invented the thumb-ups and like buttons. Medium invented claps. I’m waiting for the next platform to invent hat-off claps, bows, hugs, or maybe even blow kisses.</p> \n<p>Think about it. Thumb-ups, likes, favorites, red hearts (Twitter, you won, at least before Medium came), claps. It seems we are making a certain kind of progress to show stronger and stronger emotions. The only problem is, they are all virtual, and require little physical effort—any fingers, fat or not, can do it.</p> \n<p>I’m not saying these reactions are useless, or authors should absolutely get rid of their vanity (I haven’t). What I want to express from my observations are:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>We might be <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zhenniao\">drinking poison to quench our thirst</a>. Social media has made it much easier for people to connect with each other, which is great. At the same time, it has also created the irresistible thirst for attention. Initially, the thirst was easy to quench. For example, you might feel excited when three friends liked your post, because you don’t often hear three friends literally say in real life that they like what you did. Then the bar kept raising, and ten likes might feel like nothing. Then there came the bots, and you have no idea <em>who really</em> liked you. Then there came the claps, and you have no idea <em>how much</em> they liked you—the platform has tried to give you an illusion that your audience is expressing stronger emotions, but who knows? You just sit there, and keep clearing up the blue or red dot that indicates new reactions received on the app or page. Note that <a href=\"https://twitter.com/revodavid/status/1070118237478772737\">their system might cheat</a> for the sole purpose of getting your attention by constantly sending you notifications.</p></li> \n <li><p>There is an obvious way to provide solid feedback: <em>typing</em>, instead of <em>tapping</em>. I’m not sure if it is a real trend or not, but over these years, I have seen fewer and fewer readers write comments on blogs.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:The-only-excepti\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/terrible-medium/#fn:The-only-excepti\">1</a></sup> Maybe it is because social media has trained us well enough to find the like button to hit and then run off. Maybe it is because there is just too much to read and digest on the web, and we don’t have time to write comments when there are dozens of other articles waiting for us ahead.</p> <p>Coincidentally, Disqus recently launched a “Reactions”module in the comment area, which seems to encourage you to tap (the like buttons) instead of typing:</p> <p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/49538527-46687f80-f891-11e8-9733-4fe4dfb1d5a2.png#border\" alt=\"Disqus's new Reactions\" /></p> <p>Personally I don’t like Disqus, but I haven’t found good alternatives yet. This “reactions” module makes me feel even more uncomfortable. Thankfully, it can be turned off, so I immediately turned it off when I saw it on my sites.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>I don’t mean you should stop liking my posts or things I did. I do appreciate the likes, if only they were not addictive. I don’t mean you must write down comments, either, but I do appreciate those who have done so, because those comments constantly give me different perspectives and inspiration, as I mentioned <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/blogdown/personal-experience.html\">in the <strong>blogdown</strong> book</a>.</p> \n<p>The general problem with the web today is that there are too many candies to engage both authors and readers, and too few carrots to protect our teeth and maintain a balance in our nutrition.</p> \n<h2 id=\"where-to-blog-then\">Where to blog, then?</h2> \n<p><a href=\"https://medium.com/@nikitonsky/medium-is-a-poor-choice-for-blogging-bb0048d19133\">The Medium post</a> didn’t give recommendations on the alternative platforms for blogging. I don’t intend to recommend <strong>blogdown</strong> here because it is not the key. The only key is that you should have enough control over your platform. For example, you should be able to decide whether to put ads or banners on your blog, which theme you want to use (if not totally satisfied, you should be able to modify its CSS freely), and how to distribute your content (RSS? Mailing list? Twitter?).</p> \n<p>Basically, I recommend hosting a website by yourself, and I believe static sites are far easier now (at least they are becoming easier). When you use a third-party service, even if it is free, there is a potential problem: they might be selling you and your content indirectly.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://db.yihui.org/images/facebook.jpg\" alt=\"You are not the customer of Facebook. You are their product being sold.\" /></p> \n<h2 id=\"p-s-the-consequence-of-the-death-of-google-reader\">P.S. The Consequence of the Death of Google Reader</h2> \n<p>I cannot help missing Google Reader whenever thinking about this world of social media, in which industry leaders have always been trying to <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2017/09/08/meet-the-tech-company-that-wants-to-make-you-even-more-addicted-to-your-phone/\">manipulate us with means</a> that human beings can never counterattack and even cannot notice. The reason I miss Google Reader is that it was a service with which we could actively choose the content we want to read. After Google Reader died, RSS almost died (I switched to Feedly). The infinite scrolling replaced RSS.</p> \n<p>Infinite, scrolling.</p> \n<p>It is bottomless. You no longer have control over what you want to read. The app or the website will keep pushing new content to you. If you don’t like the content, they are able to make you like it soon (partly because you have submitted your data of personal preferences to them <em>for free</em> by hitting the like buttons). Even when you don’t like a presidential candidate, they may be able to change your mind because they control what you see on the web.</p> \n<p>I guess Google shut down Google Reader not because “the product had a declining following”, but because it gave users full control of what they wanted to read, which means there was no way to push irrelevant content to users. Then Google went to the social media battlefield and bet on Google+. Thank goodness that Google+ eventually died. No one seemed to miss Google+ at all, whereas <a href=\"https://gcemetery.co\">tons of people still miss Google Reader</a> like me.</p> \n<p>By shutting down Google Reader, the “Don’t Be Evil” Google essentially destroyed our good online reading habit. Since then, we have been trained to embrace noise and distractions. Reading is no longer a private process primarily driven by oneself. Someone else decides what you should read, and motivates you to motivate the content generators (whom we used to call “authors”).</p> \n<p>Medium’s RSS is a mess, as indicated by <a href=\"https://medium.com/@nikitonsky/medium-is-a-poor-choice-for-blogging-bb0048d19133\">the aforementioned post</a>. I guess that is because Medium just doesn’t want you to read an article quietly by yourself in your RSS reader: you ought to go to the bazaar, see what other people highlight or quote or tweet, hear what other people say, be engaged in the interactions, and clap your paws.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:The-only-excepti\">The only exception that I know is Andrew Gelman’s blog. That is probably because it is well-known that Andrew Gelman doesn’t use Twitter. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/terrible-medium/#fnref:The-only-excepti\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/terrible-medium/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 21 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/49538527-46687f80-f891-11e8-9733-4fe4dfb1d5a2.png#border","linkMd5":"8e40c7294256fcd38f7aba4e319a7d76","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn95@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-35-697_78a8ac5e176d4ecf.webp","destWidth":1514,"destHeight":730,"sourceBytes":16448,"destBytes":31840,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/49538527-46687f80-f891-11e8-9733-4fe4dfb1d5a2.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn95@2020_1/2020/08/25/02-19-35-697_78a8ac5e176d4ecf.webp","https://db.yihui.org/images/facebook.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn88@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-20-00-663_1254cefd9507b0de.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973440},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On TIOBE Index","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2019/06/tiobe-index/","description":"<p>Python users must be happy to see this month’s <a href=\"https://www.tiobe.com/tiobe-index/\">TIOBE Index</a>, while R users might have felt sad about R’s ranking. <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TIOBE_index\">There has been criticism</a> on the TIOBE Index. I, for one, don’t like this index, but not because R’s ranking dropped this month. Even if it ranks R as the most “popular” programming language, I’d still be skeptical (well, I guess everyone would).</p> \n<p>The main reason I don’t like this index is that the effort behind the index seems to be fairly cheap, because it is based on a few automated queries to search engines and the formula (with somewhat arbitrary weights) to calculate the index is very questionable when you consider what it actually indicates v.s. what this index claims to be (from their web page):</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>It is important to note that the TIOBE index is not about the best programming language or the language in which most lines of code have been written.</p> \n <p>The index can be used to check whether your programming skills are still up to date or to make a strategic decision about what programming language should be adopted when starting to build a new software system.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>Basically it says that you should follow whatever programming languages that most other people have searched for in search engines. No wonder the current US president was elected. Speaking of media and search engine coverage, he was definitely the winner.</p> \n<p>It is not that I dislike the TIOBE Index. It is that I hate all sorts of rankings in general. Rankings are too abstract and vague. They are often relatively easy to calculate and easy to become headlines in the news. The worst of all is, people who calculated these rankings will be the ultimate winners, no matter if the specific objects being ranked become “winners” or “losers” in their definition for the ranking. For example, regardless of Python entering TIOBE’s “Hall of Fame” or R dropping out the “Top 20” or even dying, TIOBE is always the winner in terms of the attention they have gotten. This reminds me of a Chinese idiom: Behind a general’s reputation, the bones of ten thousand corpses of his soldiers have dried (一将功成万骨枯).</p> \n<p>However, my point is not about blaming TIOBE or any ranking systems. If I’m blaming anyone at all, I’m blaming ourselves for our laziness. We are too lazy to evaluate one thing carefully by ourselves, which gives a great chance to ranking systems: Forget about it, and let me just tell you that X is better than Y (be it a programming language, a university, or a restaurant, etc.). “Better” in what sense? That is the hard question, and we tend to avoid answering it.</p> \n<p>Actually I’m not sure if we should blame ourselves for our laziness or just blame this era of decision fatigue. We simply have too many, trivial or not, decisions to make every day. Practically, we don’t have the time to do the research by ourselves, so we have to blindly trust the rankings. This feels like a tragedy or curse of our times. In Daniil Granin’s book “<a href=\"https://www.litmir.me/br/?b=226757&amp;p=1\">This Strange Life</a>” (which depicted the life of Lyubishchev), he mentioned a biologist who researched worms for his whole life and said something like this:<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Oddly-enough-it\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/06/tiobe-index/#fn:Oddly-enough-it\">1</a></sup></p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>Worms are so long, yet my life is so short!</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>I doubt if anyone is still able to focus on one thing like a worm for a whole life today. One day I mentioned on Twitter that I only read Twitter on every Wednesday afternoon, and someone joked that he only read Twitter in the first half of every minute.</p> \n<p>Programming languages are so complicated, yet the index is so simple.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/have-permit.gif\" alt=\"I can do any rankings I want.\" /></p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Oddly-enough-it\">Oddly enough, it is hard to find this book in the English world, so I don’t know the original words. I read the Chinese translation about 8 years ago. It was a shockingly fantastic book. If you are interested in time management, I’d highly recommend this book. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2019/06/tiobe-index/#fnref:Oddly-enough-it\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2019/06/tiobe-index/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 28 Jun 2019 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/have-permit.gif","linkMd5":"76693375bfc6dbf09de45d8b25a1613b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn16@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-35-903_e709ecafb41313a8.webp","destWidth":300,"destHeight":168,"sourceBytes":1045432,"destBytes":784818,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/have-permit.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn16@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-35-903_e709ecafb41313a8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973438},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On Cache Invalidation","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/cache-invalidation/","description":"<p>Many people must have heard this quote (by Phil Karlton) many times: <em>There are only two hard things in Computer Science: cache invalidation and naming thing.</em> Two days ago, Nick Tierney mentioned it again in his post “<a href=\"http://www.njtierney.com/post/2018/06/20/naming-things/\">Naming Things</a>”. Since he said he was not sure what cache invalidation meant, and I have a tiny bit experience here, I want to write this short post to explain why cache invalidation is hard from my experience.</p> \n<h2 id=\"why-caching\">Why caching?</h2> \n<p>First of all, the main purpose of caching is speed. The basic idea is simple: if you know you are going to compute the same thing, you may just load the result saved from the previous run, and skip the computing this time. There are two keywords here: “the same thing”, and “the saved result”. The latter means you are essentially trading (more) storage space for (less) time. That is the price to pay for caching, and also an important fact to be aware of when you use caching (i.e., caching is definitely not free, and sometimes the price can be fairly high).</p> \n<p>The tricky thing is “the same thing”. How do you know that you are computing the same thing? That is all “cache invalidation” is about. When things become different, you have to invalidate the cache, and do the (presumably time-consuming) computing again.</p> \n<h2 id=\"a-toy-example\">A toy example</h2> \n<p>Implementing caching without considering the invalidation is often simple enough. Here is a simple example of turning a normal function to a function that supports caching:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">greet = function(person) {\n  switch(person,\n    Nick = {\n      # fast and smart\n      Sys.sleep(3); 'Hello!'\n    },\n    Yihui = {\n      # slow and shy\n      Sys.sleep(15); '你好！'\n    }\n  )\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>I used <code>Sys.sleep()</code> only to pretend the function was time-consuming. In the <code>greet()</code> function, Nick is fast and will say “Hello”, and Yihui is slow, speaking Chinese. If we have to call this function many times, there is no need to wait if we can save what Nick and Yihui say. We will need a database to store their words, and keys to retrieve the results.</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">greet_cache = local({\n  \n  # a caching database (in-memory only; could be on disk, too)\n  database = list()\n  \n  function(person) {\n    res = database[[person]]\n    # if found in database, just return the cached result\n    if (person %in% names(database)) return(res)\n    # not found? compute and save it\n    res = greet(person)\n    database[[person]] &lt;&lt;- res  # note the double arrow here\n    res\n  }\n\n})\n</code></pre> \n<p>The first time you run this function, it will be slow, but for the second time, it will be instant.</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">greet_cache('Nick')  # 3 seconds\ngreet_cache('Nick')  # immediately returns\n\ngreet_cache('Yihui')  # 15 seconds\ngreet_cache('Yihui')  # no time\n</code></pre> \n<p>The toy example shows the basic idea of implementing caching: turn your input into a key, use this key to retrieve the output in a cache database if it exists, otherwise do the computing and save the output to the database with the key.</p> \n<h2 id=\"when-to-invalidate-the-cache\">When to invalidate the cache?</h2> \n<p>Of course, toy examples often cannot represent the reality. If Nick goes to Japan, he may speak Japanese. When Yihui is in the US, he should speak English. We need to update the cache database in these cases (invalidating the previously saved results).</p> \n<p>Now let me talk about a real example in <strong>knitr</strong>’s caching, which should sound similar to the case of Nick being in Japan (or Yihui in US). For those who care about technical details, <strong>knitr</strong> caches results using <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/blob/2b3e617a700f6d236e22873cfff6cbc3568df568/R/block.R#L60-L81\">these lines of code</a> and invalidate the cache <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/blob/2b3e617a700f6d236e22873cfff6cbc3568df568/R/block.R#L283\">here</a>.</p> \n<p>The basic idea of <strong>knitr</strong>’s idea is that if you did not modify a code chunk (e.g., did not modify chunk options or the code), the result will be loaded from the previous run. The key of a code chunk is pretty much an MD5 hash (via <code>digest::digest()</code>) of the chunk options and the chunk content (code). Whenever you modify chunk options or the code, the hash will change, and the cache will be invalidated.</p> \n<p>That sounds about correct, right?</p> \n<p>I have heard unhappy users curse <strong>knitr</strong>’s caching. Some thought it was too sensitive, and some thought it was dumb. For example, when you add a space in an R comment in your code chunk, should <strong>knitr</strong> invalidate the cache? Modifying a comment certainly won’t affect the computing at all (but the text output may change if you show the code in the output via <code>echo = TRUE</code>), but the MD5 hash will change.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Actually-you-can\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/cache-invalidation/#fn:Actually-you-can\">1</a></sup></p> \n<p>Then an example to explain why people thought <strong>knitr</strong>’s caching was dumb: if you read an external CSV file in a code chunk, <strong>knitr</strong> does not know whether you have modified the data file. If you happen to have updated the data file, <strong>knitr</strong> won’t re-read it by default if you didn’t modify chunk options or the code. The cache key does not depend on the external file. In this case, you have to explicitly associate the cache with the external file, e.g.,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:You-could-also-u\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/cache-invalidation/#fn:You-could-also-u\">2</a></sup></p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">```{r import-data, cache=TRUE, cache.extra=tools::md5sum('my-precious.csv')}\nd = read.csv('my-precious.csv')\n```\n</code></pre> \n<p>Since the chunk option <code>cache.extra</code> is associated with the CSV file, cache will be invalidated when the file is changed (because the cache key will be different).</p> \n<p>Another example is one code chunk using a variable created from a previous code chunk. When the variable is updated in the previous chunk, this chunk’s cache should be invalidated, too. This leads to the topic of the dependency structure of code chunks, which can be complicated, but there are some helper functions such as <code>knitr::dep_prev()</code> and <code>knitr::dep_auto()</code> to make it a little easier.</p> \n<p>When a code chunk is extremely time-consuming, <strong>knitr</strong> should be more conservative (not to invalidate the expensive cache unless there have been critical changes). When a code chunk is only moderately slow (e.g., 10 or 20 seconds), the caching probably should be more sensitive.</p> \n<p>The tricky thing is, it is hard to find the balance. Either direction can offend users.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-hidden-cost-that-is-often-ignored\">The hidden cost that is often ignored</h2> \n<p>I said above that the obvious price to pay for caching is storage (either in memory or on disk). However, to make caching work perfectly for you, there is a hidden cost. That is, the cost to understand caching. This is similar to a situation in our daily life: we may spend a lot of time and energy to save some money. We can only see the money we saved, but ignore the cost of time and emotion. If you don’t analyze the two costs, the money you saved may not really be worthwhile.</p> \n<p>If you don’t fully understand how caching works and the conditions for its invalidation, caching could be too sensitive or dumb, and may not serve you well. Some users may be able to quick understand it, and some may not. If you want to speed, you’d better know the traffic rules first, otherwise you may be pulled over.</p> \n<p>The full documentation of <strong>knitr</strong>’s caching is in the <strong>knitr</strong> book “<em>Dynamic Documents with R and knitr</em> (2nd ed)“. If you don’t have this book, there is <a href=\"https://yihui.org/knitr/demo/cache/\">a page on <strong>knitr</strong>’s website</a> that contains more information.</p> \n<p>I don’t know what was on Phil Karlton’s mind when he said those words, but the above is my experience about caching. The ultimate suggestion I often give to users is that if you feel <strong>knitr</strong>’s caching is too complicated, it is totally fine to use a much simpler caching mechanism like this:</p> \n<pre><code class=\"language-r\">if (file.exists('results.rds')) {\n  res = readRDS('results.rds')\n} else {\n  res = compute_it()  # a time-consuming function\n  saveRDS(res, 'results.rds')\n}\n</code></pre> \n<p>In this case, you clearly understand how your caching works. The one and only way to invalidate the cache is to delete <code>results.rds</code>, which is no longer hard at all. If you prefer this mechanism, <a href=\"https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown-cookbook/cache-rds.html\">you may consider using <code>xfun::cache_rds()</code></a>.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Actually-you-can\">Actually you can use the chunk option <code>cache.comments = FALSE</code> to prevent cache invalidation when only comments were changed in a code chunk. This can be useful when <code>echo = FALSE</code>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/cache-invalidation/#fnref:Actually-you-can\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:You-could-also-u\">You could also use <code>file.mtime()</code> instead of <code>tools::md5sum()</code> if you want the cache to depend on the modification time of the CSV file. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/cache-invalidation/#fnref:You-could-also-u\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/06/cache-invalidation/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 22 Jun 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"38d9d8873c889f851f0285c5931a55a3","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973460},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Back from rstudio::conf 2019","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2019/01/rstudio-conf/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>There was an obvious reason for me being quiet in my blog this month: the rstudio::conf(2019) took place last week. I (co-)taught a workshop and gave a talk there, so I needed a lot of time to make the preparation. In this post, I want to share some observations and experiences. Note that this is not a summary of the conference. Many others have blogged about the conference, and you can find those links in <a href=\"https://github.com/kbroman/RStudioConf2019Slides\">Karl&rsquo;s Github repo</a>.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"some-people-i-met\">Some people I met</h2>\n\n<p>Usually I don&rsquo;t have much interest in meeting anyone in person because the online connection seems to be good enough for me. Sometimes I feel there is no real need to physically meet someone. For example, the total time I have literally talked to Karthik Ram should be no more than five minutes, but I feel we just know each other so well and have been paying attention to each other&rsquo;s work over the years. That said, I definitely don&rsquo;t avoid meeting people deliberately.</p>\n\n<p>At the R Markdown workshop, I met Stas Kolenikov and Josh Goldberg (for the first time, I think). I started to notice Josh since last year because of his several comments in my blog. If all students were of his level in <strong>rmarkdown</strong> and <strong>knitr</strong>, I&rsquo;m afraid I wouldn&rsquo;t have much left to teach in the workshop. Stas seems to be active on Twitter, and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/StatStas/status/1042216743479914496\">he actually suggested</a> that I cover David Gohel&rsquo;s <strong>officer</strong> package in the R Markdown workshop. That was not on my original plan, but after Eric Nantz <a href=\"https://twitter.com/theRcast/status/1073441479257980928\">offered to help</a>, I accepted the <strong>officer</strong> tutorial without hesitation, because I thought it would be great for the students to know the alternatives to R Markdown (and R Markdown is certainly not perfect for everything).</p>\n\n<p>Of course, it was great to meet my co-instructor (and now colleague) <a href=\"https://alison.rbind.io\">Alison</a> for the first time in person. She is such a great and enthusiastic instructor! I admire her capability of learning things quickly and teaching them clearly. BTW, I think she is absolutely the top expert of the hugo-academic theme now. I don&rsquo;t remember if it was also the first time I had met our TA Jennifer Thompson. <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/user-developer/\">I mentioned her</a> last year in a blog post. Apparently Alison did a great job at finding TAs for our workshop. Other awesome TAs include <a href=\"https://www.jienamclellan.com\">Jiena Gu</a>, <a href=\"https://haozhu233.github.io\">Hao Zhu</a>, and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/thomas_mock\">Thomas Mock</a>. I asked Jiena to be TA because she had asked me a lot of questions about R Markdown and Shiny in the past.
