Hao is famous at least for his work on the <strong>kableExtra</strong> package. At JSM 2018, someone just called him &ldquo;<a href=\"https://twitter.com/haozhu233/status/1024346015774597120\">the kable guy</a>&rdquo;.</p>\n\n<p>One random person I happily and surprisingly bumped into was Suthira Owlarn during a lunch. It was a few minutes into our conversation that I realized she was the person who <a href=\"https://twitter.com/S_Owla/status/1062678377528786944\">mentioned my moon cake recipe</a> two months ago! Sitting on my left-hand side at the same lunch table was <a href=\"https://twitter.com/yutannihilat_en\">Hiroaki Yutani</a>. I had never met him before, but I was actively looking for him at the conference. He had a poster but his flight was late, so I didn&rsquo;t find him in front of his poster. I have discovered several cool Japanese hackers on Github in these years, including Hiroaki. Last year I decided to give him write access to the <strong>knitr</strong> repo because I felt he was careful enough and knew well enough about the source code of <strong>knitr</strong>. He was actually the first person who gained write access to the <strong>knitr</strong> repo besides me. Personally I&rsquo;m very interested in the Japanese culture, and I wish I could spend a few months living in Japan someday.</p>\n\n<p>I met <a href=\"https://github.com/cderv\">Christophe Dervieux</a> for the second time at rstudio::conf. I cannot thank him enough for his active help with my Github issues almost on a daily basis. During the 2018 rstudio::conf, I talked to him briefly about automating the generation of the bookdown.org website, and he quickly finished what I planned to do, which has saved me at least one hour every week in maintaining this website.</p>\n\n<p>At last year&rsquo;s rstudio::conf, an American lady talked to me in fluent Mandarin. I was so very impressed, but didn&rsquo;t remember her name, which had been a shame to me. Luckily, she came to this year&rsquo;s conference again. The first thing I did when I found her was to look at her badge and made sure I remembered the name, Amanda Johnson. However, I made the same mistake two days later when another lady came to me and told me she could also speak and read Chinese. It is kind of embarrassing for me to bend my neck to look at other people&rsquo;s badges while talking. Anyway, she asked me for permission to correct other people&rsquo;s pronunciation of my name. I was so touched! People were so kind and humble at this conference.</p>\n\n<p>Ian Lyttle was still carrying <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/01/ian-lyttle/\">his notebook</a> with him this year.</p>\n\n<p>The list of amazing people I met could go on and on, but I&rsquo;m just going to stop here.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"how-do-you-pronounce-it\">How do you pronounce it?</h2>\n\n<p>An extremely common topic I heard from all conversations was how to pronounce the name of a package or a certain term. For example, the most frequently asked question about the <strong>xaringan</strong> package is its pronunciation. Well, I have to make an official announcement that there is no official pronunciation of <strong>xaringan</strong>&mdash;just pronounce it in whatever way you like. If you are really curious about its authentic pronunciation, you can learn it from the video (at 01:04) on <a href=\"https://naruto.fandom.com/wiki/Sharingan\">the Sharingan page</a> of the Naruto wiki, or ask a Japanese friend to teach you.</p>\n\n<p>Similar examples include <strong>GGally</strong> (GG-ally or G-Galy), <strong>reprex</strong> (re-prex as in &ldquo;reproduce&rdquo; or rep-rex as in &ldquo;replicate&rdquo;), and <strong>memor</strong> (like mem-er or memo-R).</p>\n\n<p>Human names can be difficult to pronounce, too (e.g., my last name). The day before I gave my talk on <strong>pagedown</strong>, I asked Christophe, a native French, to record the pronunciation of the name &ldquo;Romain Lesur&rdquo; on my phone, and practiced it quite a few times in my room, so I could hopefully make my pronunciation of my collaborator&rsquo;s name close enough in my talk (I have never met or talked to Romain in person).</p>\n\n<p>At a lunch, one person mentioned the &ldquo;sword&rdquo; sound in the <strong>beepr</strong> package, and I proudly told her that it was largely due to <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr-talks/tree/master/useR2014\">my useR! 2014 talk</a>. Then we talked about the difficulties in the pronunciation of package and human names. I suggested that we record the authentic pronunciations and send pull requests to <strong>beepr</strong>, so that, for example, <code>beepr::beep(12)</code> tells us the pronunciation of &ldquo;xaringan&rdquo;, and <code>beepr::beep(13)</code> is &ldquo;Yihui Xie&rdquo;, etc. Would that be a good idea?</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"some-of-my-experience-in-doing-presentations\">Some of my experience in doing presentations</h2>\n\n<p>I asked Hiroaki if he also had a talk at the conference, and he said he was concerned about his English language. Although <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/english-is-hard/\">I also struggle with English</a>, I&rsquo;m rarely worried about my language in presentations, because I&rsquo;m sure native speakers will pretty much understand it regardless of all grammar or pronunciation problems. The requirement for English is much lower in a presentation than in a conversation, because a presentation is primarily a solo activity: you just keep talking on the stage. Given that rstudio::conf is a super friendly conference, presenters who are not native English speakers could have more confidence in themselves.</p>\n\n<p>With the observation of Hiroaki&rsquo;s concern, I feel that perhaps we should offer some help to non-native speakers with their language if they prefer. I don&rsquo;t know how many people decided not to give a talk because of language problems.</p>\n\n<p>In fact, non-native speakers have a special advantage. That is, idioms and jokes that are only familiar and popular to native speakers will sound particularly funny when said by a non-native speaker, because it is totally unexpected to them that you know such a joke or idiom. For example, someone (I believe it was an American) at the conference told me &ldquo;your packages are very 666&rdquo; (pronounced roughly as &ldquo;leo-leo-leo&rdquo; in Chinese). I guess no one knows what &ldquo;<a href=\"https://baike.baidu.com/item/666/17192634\">666</a>&rdquo; means in today&rsquo;s Chinese cyber culture (awesome/neat/slick) unless you are a young Chinese, so I had a good laugh when 666 was said from an American&rsquo;s mouth. He told me his Chinese friend taught him this, and apparently it was very effective.</p>\n\n<p>Many people feel nervous on the stage. I&rsquo;m rarely nervous there, but I do feel a little nervous in the few minutes before I walk onto the stage. My heartbeat quickens, too. That is very normal. I don&rsquo;t feel nervous while I give the talk because I&rsquo;m usually extremely focused on my talk. I wouldn&rsquo;t notice it even if you pull an elephant into the room and walk it around while I&rsquo;m giving the talk. FWIW, I failed the <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vJG698U2Mvo\">selective attention test</a> the first time I saw it (I completely overlooked that animal).</p>\n\n<p>At the useR! 2014 conference in LA, I told Kevin Ushey that the more people in the room, the more comfortable I would be to give my talk. He was surprised. To some degree, this is similar to &ldquo;one death is a tragedy, and a million deaths is a statistic.&rdquo; When there are only a few people listening to your talk, you may feel the stare. When there are a thousand people in the audience, it really doesn&rsquo;t matter how an individual reacts to your talk, so you should just shift your focus from the audience to your talk. And your talk may not be as important as you thought&hellip;</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>One of the symptoms of an approaching nervous breakdown is the belief that one&rsquo;s work is terribly important.</p>\n\n<p>&mdash; Bertrand Russell</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/renatagerecke/status/1086305574696964098\"><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/51764240-4c612c80-209a-11e9-9c92-ba35c4f0d802.jpg\" alt=\"The room for my pagedown talk. Photo credit: Renata Gerecke on Twitter.\" /></a></p>\n\n<p>There is a special bonus of a large audience: jokes will work much better. Even mild jokes will make a fair number of people laugh. In all of my talks with a large audience, there are almost always a few people who laugh out so hard as if I paid them to laugh.</p>\n\n<p>I&rsquo;m good at creating strange and unexpected associations among seemingly unrelated things (such as <a href=\"https://twitter.com/apreshill/status/1086305302964813827\">rmarkdown and the government shutdown</a>), which often creates unexpected jokes. If anyone has a presentation that needs some flavor, I&rsquo;ll be happy to help. Actually, if you think more about your best jokes coming up in the talk, it will also help you relax on the stage.</p>\n\n<p>Of course, being funny is not the goal. There are <a href=\"https://twitter.com/CMastication/status/959856196369793024\">many excellent talks</a> that don&rsquo;t sound funny at all. It feels satisfactory when the audience laugh out loud, but we need to keep in mind that the means is not the goal. I have heard negative feedback on at least one of my talks, and that person thought the talk didn&rsquo;t have much content, despite of the loud laughter. Perhaps I overdid it. I certainly want people to laugh and remember, instead of laughing and forgetting.</p>\n\n<p>BTW, no matter how much you love the dark theme of your editor, switch to a bright theme (and increase the font size) when you show live code demos.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"where-do-you-get-ideas-for-your-next-r-package\">Where do you get ideas for your next R package?</h2>\n\n<p>I was asked a few times how I found ideas for my new packages. First of all, I only work on one package each year in recent years, which has made the decision a little bit easier. Once I decide what to work on, I can ignore most of the news throughout the year. The amount of work that <em>could</em> be done is always much bigger than what we <em>can</em> actually do. You may need a long-term dream (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/xieyihui/status/1022873179532996609\">like mine</a>) to be able to focus.</p>\n\n<p>I read the Hacker Newsletter regularly, although I feel most of the items in each newsletter are not relevant or useful to me. I learned Gitbook, remark.js, Hugo, and ReLaXed from there, which in turn gave birth to <strong>bookdown</strong>, <strong>xaringan</strong>, <strong>blogdown</strong>, and <strong>pagedown</strong>, respectively. That means I find one thing or two in the newsletter directly relevant to my new R packages every year, which is actually enough.</p>\n\n<p>My other source of inspiration is Twitter. The <strong>pagedown</strong> package was finally born mainly because of <a href=\"https://twitter.com/RLesur/status/1037126641846312967\">one of Romain&rsquo;s tweets about Paged.js</a>. Even though I was excited about ReLaXed, I really didn&rsquo;t want to introduce a Node package as the dependency of an R package. I saw a much brighter future from Romain&rsquo;s tweet. More importantly, I felt <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/in-html-i-trust/\">the shared belief</a> between him and me, so I reached out to him and we started to work on <strong>pagedown</strong>.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"my-personal-favorite-talks\">My personal favorite talks</h2>\n\n<p>There were three parallel sessions, so I only attended 1/3 of the talks. For those I attended, I especially loved these ones:</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>Joe Cheng, Shiny in production</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Kara Woo, Box plots: A case study in debugging and perseverance</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Karl Broman, R/qtl2: Rewrite of a very old R package</p></li>\n\n<li><p>David Robinson, The unreasonable effectiveness of public work</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>Kara&rsquo;s talk showed an inspiring and detailed example of how to fix a bug in a famous (if not the most famous) R package. The R community needs more contributors like her. I love Karl&rsquo;s special humor. Sometimes I cannot even tell if it is humor or he is just nervous at the podium, because he often appears to be speechless (and emotionless at the same time). I asked him later, and it seemed he was just speechless at <a href=\"https://kbroman.org/blog/2011/08/17/the-stupidest-r-code-ever/\">the ridiculous examples</a> in his talk. I thought he needed some encouragement at the podium (come on, Karl, <a href=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/you-can-do-it.gif\">you can do it</a>). Anyway, there was a lady sitting in front me who laughed so loud and often, which made Karl&rsquo;s talk even more amusing. Dave gave the closing keynote, and I agree with everything he said (definitely not because he mentioned me twice in his talk). Yes, publicize your work.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"jared-lander-s-annual-feature-requests\">Jared Lander&rsquo;s annual feature requests</h2>\n\n<p>I meet Jared Lander roughly annually. Each time he would make certain requests to me. For example, the <strong>bookdown</strong> package was an response to his request in 2014 when we met at the Strata conference. This year he wanted self-contained <strong>xaringan</strong> slides. This is a tricky issue because Markdown is not processed through Pandoc, but I&rsquo;ll see what I can do. The next request was a talk at the <a href=\"https://www.rstats.nyc\">New York R conference</a>. I absolutely love this conference, but I&rsquo;m still waiting for my two little kids to grow (I cannot offer them six meals a day to let them grow twice as fast). If any R Markdown ninja volunteers to go, I&rsquo;ll be happy to help create a Yihui-style talk.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"a-random-idea-turn-your-dropbox-folder-into-a-public-website-through-plumber-and-rdrop2\">A random idea: turn your Dropbox folder into a public website through <strong>plumber</strong> and <strong>rdrop2</strong></h2>\n\n<p>On the first day of our R Markdown workshop, Hao told me my slides were no longer accessible, which scared me. I usually serve my slides through <a href=\"https://updog.co\">Updog.co</a>. Unfortunately the service was down right before the workshop, but was recovered later that day. I came up with an idea of an alternative implementation of Updog.co after this incidence, and will leave it as an exercise to those who are interested:</p>\n\n<p>In R, we have the <a href=\"https://github.com/karthik/rdrop2\"><strong>rdrop2</strong></a> package to access a Dropbox folder. Then we also have the <a href=\"https://www.rplumber.io\"><strong>plumber</strong></a> package to build simple web APIs. With these two pieces, it is enough to turn a private Dropbox folder into a public website. What the API does is simply read static files and return their content (i.e., <a href=\"https://www.rplumber.io/docs/routing-and-input.html#static-file-handler\">a static file server</a>). If you <a href=\"https://www.rplumber.io/docs/hosting.html\">host</a> the <strong>plumber</strong> API on a public server, you will be able to enjoy a similar service as Updog.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-video-recording-of-my-talk\">The video recording of my talk</h2>\n\n<p>RStudio is currently uploading <a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2019\">all conference videos</a>. If you are interested, my talk is already there:</p>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://resources.rstudio.com/rstudio-conf-2019/pagedown-creating-beautiful-pdfs-with-r-markdown-and-css\"><img src=\"https://embedwistia-a.akamaihd.net/deliveries/f936ea9db9c32a27a3262d640ea1810fc319ddab.jpg?image_play_button_size=2x&amp;amp;image_crop_resized=960x540&amp;amp;image_play_button=1&amp;amp;image_play_button_color=4287c7e0\" alt=\"pagedown Creating beautiful PDFs with R Markdown and CSS - Yihui Xie\" /></a></p>\n\n<p>Many other people have written blog posts about this conference (again, see <a href=\"https://github.com/kbroman/RStudioConf2019Slides\">Karl&rsquo;s repo</a>). I have read most of them, and they are all beautifully written (I&rsquo;m particularly impressed by Brooke Watson&rsquo;s hand-drawing skills). I feel very glad and proud to learn that people found this conference super friendly, inclusive, welcoming, and helpful. Many thanks to those who made this conference such a positive experience, and looking forward to rstudio::conf(2020) in San Francisco!</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2019/01/rstudio-conf/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 25 Jan 2019 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/51764240-4c612c80-209a-11e9-9c92-ba35c4f0d802.jpg","linkMd5":"36d5b56353cf8fd09432e2ebce473d1a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn52@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-33-828_0f6237576394ce14.webp","destWidth":2048,"destHeight":650,"sourceBytes":71350,"destBytes":108086,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/51764240-4c612c80-209a-11e9-9c92-ba35c4f0d802.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn52@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-33-828_0f6237576394ce14.webp","https://embedwistia-a.akamaihd.net/deliveries/f936ea9db9c32a27a3262d640ea1810fc319ddab.jpg?image_play_button_size=2x&amp;image_crop_resized=960x540&amp;image_play_button=1&amp;image_play_button_color=4287c7e0":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn95@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-20-01-212_22a034ec2dca3830.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973440},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On Cosmetic Changes in Pull Requests","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/cosmetic-changes/","description":"\n        <p>Programmers, including myself, make cosmetic changes in code all the time, such as adding or deleting white spaces or blank lines, or re-wrapping lines, or adding <code>{ }</code> to single-line <code>if</code> statements. That is usually fine. However, when making changes in other people&rsquo;s code and sending pull requests on Github, I suggest you refrain from introducing cosmetic changes. There are two reasons:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>Such changes require unnecessary attention of the pull request reviewer. If you don&rsquo;t know if the reviewer is busy or not, always assume he/she is extremely busy, so the best strategy is to bring the really minimal set of changes, and exclude all non-essential changes (<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/bite-sized-pull-requests/\">or save them for later</a>).</p></li>\n\n<li><p>The author of the package may not really like the style of the cosmetic changes. This is worse. People can argue to death on style. Such changes are often red herrings and can quickly lead to <a href=\"https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bikeshedding\">bikeshedding</a>. Unless you are going to take over the maintainership of a package, I recommend that you follow the style of the main author(s). Even if they use <code>=</code> for assignments, which makes absolutely no sense to you, you just use <code>=</code> (you can complain about it by yourself in your own room if that makes you feel better). FWIW, <a href=\"https://github.com/Robinlovelace/geocompr/issues/319\">I use <code>&lt;-</code> when I contribute to a package</a> if its author uses <code>&lt;-</code>. I don&rsquo;t think there is any need to argue about it, and I wish people could be more tolerant with each other&rsquo;s styles.</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>In summary, the whole point is &ldquo;recognize the priorities&rdquo;. Getting the pull request merged is more important, so you want to save the reviewer time. It is more important for the package maintainers to be happy so they can carry on (bear in mind that they will also have to maintain the code you contributed <em>after you have left</em>), so you follow their own styles.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/drag-cat.gif\" alt=\"I love pull requests, but usually you won't stay in my bathtub for long\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/cosmetic-changes/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 09 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/drag-cat.gif","linkMd5":"67f501ec24468aafa4f06f500fe3a598","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn79@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-36-886_dad90d4bcefee63d.webp","destWidth":317,"destHeight":198,"sourceBytes":1046813,"destBytes":824792,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/drag-cat.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn79@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-36-886_dad90d4bcefee63d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973440},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"On Marketing (on Social Media)","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/social-media-marketing/","description":"<p>Both Mara and Hadley disagreed with me <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/\">when I talked about my concerns on marketing</a> last month. Marketing was not the main topic of that post, and I want to clarify my thoughts in this post.</p> \n<h2 id=\"the-medium-is-the-message\">The medium is the message</h2> \n<p>I’m not against marketing. I do it myself, too. I definitely agree with Mara’s comment that meritocracy is a fantasy:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>I think there’s a tendency to imagine a platonic ideal of meritocracy in the absence of something we call “marketing,” which (in reality) doesn’t really exist.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>My actual problem is more with the social media than with marketing. When I said “the room” was loud enough, I meant the social media:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>[…] marketing more or less depends on how loud you can shout out and whether you are able to keep raising your voice in a room that has already been fairly loud (or using more emojis).</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>There is too much to see on the social media. Too much “breaking” news. Too many pictures of dogs and cats. Too much Trump. To market your software package, you are forced to “raise your voice” or use all sorts of bells and whistles (e.g., attach images or GIFs) unless you have a fairly big number of followers.</p> \n<p>I’d be very interested in a service that can help us filter the content on social media. I don’t think social media platforms like Twitter and Facebook care about whether we are able to read what we really want to read. On the contrary, I believe they want to train us to be buried in, and addicted by, chaos.</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_medium_is_the_message\">McLuhan described</a> the “content” of a medium as a juicy piece of meat carried by the burglar to distract the watchdog of the mind. I feel social media platforms are exactly such burglars. To clarify, I’m not saying that users who generated the content are burglars. Users are simply victims.</p> \n<h2 id=\"two-great-examples-of-marketing-on-social-media\">Two great examples of marketing on social media</h2> \n<p>If I were to follow only one person on Twitter, I’d follow <a href=\"https://twitter.com/dataandme\">Mara @dataandme</a>.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:If-I-were-to-sub\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/social-media-marketing/#fn:If-I-were-to-sub\">1</a></sup> She is a great curator, promoting an extremely wide range of interesting resources about R every day. If I were still relatively new to R, and had enough free time to look for things to learn, I’m sure I’d benefit a lot from her tweets. The great thing about Mara is that she actively markets for people with low voices, so they don’t have to scream by themselves. <del>My last wish for Mara is to completely drop the (already very occasional) use of “ICYMI” :) In case you missed it, just miss it. <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fear_of_missing_out\">FOMO</a> is rather unhealthy in my opinion.</del> (Update 24 hours later: <a href=\"https://twitter.com/dataandme/status/1078850366492561409\">She did it! “ICYMI” was replaced by #repeatTweet.</a> Yeah! Thanks, Mara!)</p> \n<p>Another great example of marketing on Twitter is <a href=\"https://twitter.com/wearerladies\">@WeAreRLadies</a>. I don’t know who came up with this idea of “Rotating Curators”. It must be a genius. This is the best marketing idea I have ever seen: people (R ladies) take turns to use the same Twitter handle to promote their own or other people’s interesting work.</p> \n<h2 id=\"deep-marketing\">Deep marketing</h2> \n<p><a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/dependency-winner/#comment-4192835297\">As Hadley pointed out</a>, I do a lot of marketing myself, but I often do it differently. In particular, I deliberately avoid using Twitter myself. It is only my personal choice, and I don’t mean you should do it, too. In fact, I believe most people would actually benefit more from marketing on social media than through other ways.</p> \n<p>After I finished the <strong>bookdown</strong> project and the corresponding book in 2016, I started to think about this question:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>If I were to tweet (announce) only once or twice a year about my work, what would I post?</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>This partially explains my decreasing number of tweets per year—if I don’t have anything of importance to say, I just keep quiet. To me, the marginal utility of marketing on social media has been diminishing. I feel more and more people are engaged in social media. It has become a <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Ocean_Strategy\">red ocean</a>. I don’t want to spend time on thinking about how to do marketing more effectively there as the bar keeps raising, and I’d rather save the effort to do the one thing (or two) well every year. On the other hand, thanks to many friends like <a href=\"https://twitter.com/juliasilge/status/1062575146404274176\">Julia Silge</a> and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/cpsievert/status/950416948634021893\">Carson Sievert</a>, I only need to focus on writing blog posts, and they will pick some to spread. The crafty Yihui clearly knows that marketing through one’s own mouth is not the best idea. Therefore staying (relatively) quiet on Twitter is the rational choice for me. I guess most other people don’t have my luxury, hence should not follow my marketing strategy.</p> \n<p>My so-called deep marketing strategy is “deep work + passive marketing”. This has an important prerequisite: I have been famous enough. I can invest most of my time into the work itself, and people will surely discover it sooner or later. Even if I’m excited about the work, I’d hold the desire to share it myself, because I want it to pass someone else’s independent test first. <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/influence-depth-or-breadth/\">I strongly prefer depth over breadth</a> in terms of making an impact. I’d be much happier if only a single user finds my work useful and uses it for a decade than when ten thousand users are excited for ten seconds.</p> \n<h2 id=\"slow-marketing\">Slow marketing</h2> \n<p>After we build a cool product, we often feel the urge to tell people in the next minute. I usually hold this desire, too. Instead, I slowly collect cool use cases of this product in the coming years. Hadley described the <strong>bookdown</strong> website <a href=\"https://bookdown.org\">bookdown.org</a> as one of my marketing initiatives. He was absolutely correct. I keep collecting books written in <strong>bookdown</strong> for marketing both my own package and other people’s books. However, I won’t share the individual books every time I find one (usually I find them on Twitter but won’t tweet or retweet). I want to take a single chance to impress <strong>bookdown</strong> users. That is, when they open the website and see the long list of free books.</p> \n<p>Blogging is also a slow way of marketing. It is slow in two senses: posts are often slow to write and also slow to read. By writing slowly, you (as the author) have a better chance to speculate. A possible by-product is that you may do a better job of marketing. By reading slowly, your readers know more deeply about your work and you. Of course, there will be readers who don’t prefer reading blog posts because of their length. Personally I don’t consider them my target audience.<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:But-I-admit-my-p\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/social-media-marketing/#fn:But-I-admit-my-p\">2</a></sup> The audience who are easy to get may also be easy to lose.</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://twitter.com/martinjhnhadley/status/1074961327414743042\">As I mentioned to Martin John Hadley</a> a few days ago, I don’t often announce new releases of packages I maintain (the main exception is the initial CRAN releases). New releases typically contain bug fixes and new features. I feel not many people are interested in knowing the bug fixes, and I often don’t have many groundbreaking new features in these releases, so I’d just let those who really care about the new releases dig out the new stuff slowly by themselves. Sometimes it is worth leaving small exercises to users (<a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/questions/53820938#comment94526023_53820938\">Dason is an expert</a>). Is that a kind of the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IKEA_effect\">IKEA effect</a>?</p> \n<h2 id=\"what-i-didn-t-do-well-marketing-for-other-people\">What I didn’t do well: marketing for other people</h2> \n<p>A lot of people in the R community have helped me promote my work. I feel very grateful to them. Honestly, I haven’t done much in return, and I definitely owe the community some marketing effort. Occasionally I write about some cool hackers in my blog, and try to help people find jobs. As a master of marketing, sometimes I use <a href=\"https://pagedown.rbind.io/html-resume\">very subtle ways</a> to market for <a href=\"https://pagedown.rbind.io/html-letter\">other people</a>.</p> \n<p>As I was writing <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/code-appendix/\">the post</a> about Alex and Lucy’s methods of putting code in the appendix in September, I came up with a book idea. I was hoping to put together a book about those cool hackers, to show how one could be moderately successful as an ordinary human, because I don’t believe the chicken soup that all people can be wildly and unconditionally successful. I hope the community would benefit from these examples. That said, I have already had two other books lined up for 2019, so I really have no idea when I’ll be able to work on this one.</p> \n<h2 id=\"conclusion\">Conclusion</h2> \n<p>Is marketing necessary? Absolutely. Do you have to use social media? It depends. The take-home message? Do good work first. Then try to be creative in marketing.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/duck-dog-mask.gif\" alt=\"Be a good duck first. Then wear a dog mask to impress others.\" /></p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:If-I-were-to-sub\">If I were to subscribe to only one RSS feed about R, I’d subscribe to <a href=\"https://rweekly.org\">R Weekly</a>. The team has done a fantastic job of handpicking the best new stuff related to R every week. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/social-media-marketing/#fnref:If-I-were-to-sub\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:But-I-admit-my-p\">But I admit my posts can be wordy and rambling. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/social-media-marketing/#fnref:But-I-admit-my-p\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/12/social-media-marketing/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 26 Dec 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/duck-dog-mask.gif","linkMd5":"080a63eaf6e0fa28883d971a3d51555f","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn7@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-304_5895cc688678435c.webp","destWidth":440,"destHeight":479,"sourceBytes":100785,"destBytes":33898,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/duck-dog-mask.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn7@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-35-304_5895cc688678435c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973440},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Reflections on 25+ Years of \"50 Years of Data Science\"","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/25-years-of-data-science/","description":"\n        \n\n<p><strong>Note</strong>: This is a guest post and <em>not</em> written by Yihui. Its author desires to remain anonymous. If anyone would like to contact the author, please contact me (Yihui). And if any statistical sleuths out there determine the author&rsquo;s identity, please refrain from revealing it. Thank you.</p>\n\n<hr />\n\n<blockquote>\n<p><em>When I was a child<br />\nI caught a fleeting glimpse<br />\nOut of the corner of my eye<br />\nI turned to look but it was gone<br />\nI cannot put my finger on it now<br />\nThe child is grown<br />\nThe dream is gone<br />\nI have become comfortably numb</em></p>\n\n<p>&mdash; &ldquo;Comfortably Numb&rdquo; by Pink Floyd (1979)</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<h2 id=\"introduction\">Introduction</h2>\n\n<p>I was lucky to attend the RStudio Conference this year, and even luckier to be invited to an impromptu Iowa State University Statistics alumni dinner on Saturday, February 3. Full disclosure: I am not an ISU alumnus, but Di Cook granted me that privilege for the night. During the dinner, I wanted to mention something from the 2016 useR! Conference at Stanford, and I asked Yihui Xie if he was there. My intended benign conversation starter raised a sore point that Yihui explained in his post <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/02/career-crisis/\">My Early Career Crisis (2014 - 2015)</a>. Yes, I am that guy, that &ldquo;Someone at the table recalled that I missed the useR! 2016 conference at Stanford due to visa issues.&rdquo; The next day, Yihui emailed me to ask me to think about posting my anecdotes on famous statisticians. And now, more than six months later, I&rsquo;m finally able to put some of my thoughts on paper. Maybe there will be others posts someday too.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"david-donoho\">David Donoho</h2>\n\n<p>The anecdotal story I wanted to tell at the dinner was meeting <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Donoho\">David Donoho</a> at the 2016 useR! Conference Welcome Reception on Tuesday, June 28, 2016. I was trying to overcome my INTJ Myers-Briggs Type Indicator and &ldquo;work the room&rdquo; as best as possible for a math-stats geek with the aid of wine, when I crossed paths with what appeared to be a younger wavy blond-haired, tanned skin California surfer dude-type - not quite <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fast_Times_at_Ridgemont_High\">Jeff Spicoli</a>, but maybe one of the guys in the van - wearing a blue suit and glasses, and I was struck by the apparent dichotomy. Then I looked at his name tag and saw &ldquo;David Donoho&rdquo; and immediately made the connection to the paper &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science&rdquo; (2015-09-15 version), which I devoured the moment I found it posted online. I had no idea who David Donoho was, but I <a href=\"http://paperswelove.org/\">loved the paper</a>, mainly because it referenced landmark data science papers by four of my statistical heroes:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>John Tukey: &ldquo;The Future of Data Analysis&rdquo; (1962)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>John Chambers: &ldquo;Greater or Lesser Statistics: A Choice for Future Research&rdquo; (1993)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Leo Breiman: &ldquo;Statistical Modeling: The Two Cultures&rdquo; (2001)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>William S. Cleveland: &ldquo;Data Science: An Action Plan for Expanding the Technical Areas of the Field of Statistics&rdquo; (2001)</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>So, I just had to corner Donoho, tell him how much I liked his paper, and try to play a game of 20 Questions. As I recall, my opening question was: did you talk to Tukey, Chambers, Breiman, and Cleveland before writing the paper to get their input or perspective? Donoho&rsquo;s reply was something like, &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve known them for years and worked with them.&rdquo; This dumbfounded me - how could a guy who looked so much younger than me possibly work with that generation of statisticians, whose work I studied in grad school nearly 30 years earlier? I just had to pry and asked him how that was even possible [as soon as I could check Wikipedia, I learned that Donoho is 7 years OLDER than me - Palo Alto water must spring forth from the Fountain of Youth]. The details are fuzzy at this point, but Donoho explained that he went to Princeton for his undergraduate degree and worked as a statistical programmer for John Tukey and so was able to work with John Chambers and Bill Cleveland at Bell Labs. I tried to articulate my fan-boy admiration of the Princeton-Bell Labs-Institute for Advanced Study <a href=\"https://hbr.org/1998/11/clusters-and-the-new-economics-of-competition\">innovation cluster</a>, but I&rsquo;m afraid the wine and warm, dry air were too good. Then I asked how he knew Leo Breiman, and Donoho told me he worked at the University of California at Berkeley while Breiman was there. And then he told me an anecdote that blew my mind: he was sitting in a Berkeley wine bar with <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ross_Ihaka\">Ross Ihaka</a> and said to him something like: &ldquo;You know what you should do - rewrite S using a Lisp dialect, like Scheme.&rdquo;</p>\n\n<p>Dang! Not only did Donoho personally know and work with the statisticians whose papers, books, and methods I&rsquo;ve read and utilized for nearly 30 years, he put in motion thoughts that led to the creation of R, which I use almost every day! While I certainly didn&rsquo;t know Tukey, Chambers, Breiman, and Cleveland personally like Donoho did, they were key inspirations over my 25+ years of &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science&rdquo;.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"john-tukey\">John Tukey</h2>\n\n<p>From what I can determine, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Tukey\">John Tukey</a> spent his entire career simultaneously at Princeton and Bell Labs, and, as his Wiki page attests, his influence on both world-class institutions and the rest of the world was amazing. The more I think about it, the more impressed I am that a very young David Donoho was able to work for John Tukey while attending Princeton in the mid-1970s - was that due to happenstance or planned? I never even heard of Tukey until his name kept popping up in my grad school classes, especially in my Design Of Experiments (DOE) and Exploratory Data Analysis (EDA) classes. My EDA course was based on two books:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p><em>Applications, Basics, and Computing of Exploratory Data Analysis</em> (ABCs of EDA) by Paul F. Velleman and David C. Hoaglin</p></li>\n\n<li><p><em>Graphical Methods for Data Analysis</em> (GMDA) by John M. Chambers, William S. Cleveland, Beat Kleiner, and Paul A. Tukey (all Bell Labs staff)</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>The ABCs of EDA was dedicated to John W. Tukey, and both Velleman and Hoaglin were PhD students of Tukey&rsquo;s at Princeton. To implement Tukey&rsquo;s techniques, the book was filled with Basic code and Fortran code, which I knew fairly well at the time - along with Pascal - from my undergraduate studies; however, for the sake of expedience we were able to use Minitab and Minitab macros to do the examples and exercises.</p>\n\n<p>GMDA was dedicated to &ldquo;To our parents,&rdquo; while its Preface stated:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p><em>Without the influence of John Tukey on statistics, this book would probably never have been written. His many contributions to graphical methods, his insights into the role good plots can play in statistics and his general philosophy of data analysis have shaped much of the approach presented here. Directly and indirectly, he is responsible for much of the richness of graphical methods available today.</em></p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>So, the name Tukey was ubiquitous for me during that period of my life, and I kept asking myself, &ldquo;Who is this guy?&rdquo; It was only later that I learned that the creator, head, and adviser for my statistics program was one of Tukey&rsquo;s PhD students at Princeton, so maybe that explains some of it. Somewhere along the way, I acquired copies of two of Tukey&rsquo;s books <em>Exploratory Data Analysis</em> (1977) and <em>Exploring Data Tables, Trends and Shapes</em> (1985) edited by David Hoaglin, Frederick Mosteller, and John Tukey, which I use as references from time to time. I even made attempts to reproduce examples using S-PLUS and R, but those attempts kept getting derailed. But I hope someday, maybe in retirement, I can work through those two books cover-to-cover.</p>\n\n<p>Donoho marks the birth of Data Science with the publication of Tukey&rsquo;s &ldquo;The Future of Data Analysis&rdquo; paper in 1962, I assume because Tukey implored statisticians to reduce their focus on statistical theory and engage with the entire data analysis process. True confession: this was similar to my thinking when I decided to attend a relatively unknown applied statistics graduate program that offered an industrial co-op position instead of well-known theoretical graduate programs (and likely a Research Assistant or Teaching Assistant position), after being demotivated by theory without application as an undergraduate. Then Tukey reviewed three tools that I don&rsquo;t recall encountering in books or classes:</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>Full Normal Rejection (FUNOR)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Full Normal Modification (FUNOM)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Freeing Interaction Line from the Error Term (FILLET)</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>Are there R packages for these?</p>\n\n<p>I think the best part of the 67 page paper is near the end, which I believe is worth repeating for today&rsquo;s Data Scientists, so they can be confident they have the right attitudes, unlike when I was initially learning and practicing statistics:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>We need to face up to more realistic problems</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We need to face up to the necessarily approximate nature of useful results in data analysis</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We need to face up to the need for collecting the results of actual experience with specific data-analytic techniques</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We need to face up to the need for iterative procedures in data analysis</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We need to face up to the need for both indication and conclusion in the same analysis</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We need to face up to the need for a free use of ad hoc and informal procedures in seeking indications</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We need to face up to the face that, as we enter into new fields or study new kinds of procedures, it is natural for indication procedures to grow up before the corresponding conclusion procedures do so</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We must face up to the need for a double standard in dealing with error rates, whether significance levels or lacks of confidence</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We must face up to the fact that, in any experimental science, our certainty about what will happen in a particular situation does not usually come from directly applicable experiments or theory, but rather comes mainly through analogy between situations which are not know to behave similarly</p></li>\n\n<li><p>We need to give up the vain hope that data analysis can be founded upon a logico-deductive system like Euclidean plan geometry (or some form of the propositional calculus) and to face up to the fact that data analysis is intrinsically an empirical science</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<h2 id=\"john-chambers\">John Chambers</h2>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Chambers_(statistician)\">John Chambers</a> worked at Bell Labs, was one of the original authors of S, and later moved to Stanford. As mentioned in the previous section, I first encountered the name John Chambers from <em>Graphical Methods for Data Analysis</em> (GMDA) in my EDA class. GMDA showcased the S language&rsquo;s graphical methods, and our class used Minitab and S on a Unix server to work through the examples and exercises, so this was my first exposure to the S language. This book turned me on to solving problems by visualizing the data. For example, years later I taught a basic statistics class for my employer, and I encouraged students to bring their own data for live problem-solving sessions (I called it &ldquo;Stump the Chump, er, Statistician,&rdquo; daring them to challenge me in front of the class). One student had a vexing factory problem, and I recommended a simple normal probability plot, which exhibited several vertical lines of data points, indicating the same values to 6+ decimal places were occurring, a statistical impossibility. I asked him to check if they were successive values, and sure enough they were, suggesting the same value was stuck in the buffer and subsequent values were ignored. This simple graph solved the problem in a few minutes that no one else had solved after collecting data for a long period of time. This was just one of many examples of solving problems by organizing and visualizing the data, without requiring any statistical modeling or Design Of Experiments (often after I was really hoping to take the next step and design and analyze a complex DOE).</p>\n\n<p>GMDA became one of those &ldquo;go to&rdquo; books where the pages are marked up with pencil scribblings and highlighting, the spine is broken, and pages are about to fall out if I&rsquo;m not careful. Its methods were especially useful when encountering &ldquo;real world&rdquo; data - some from Bell Labs and others used for S, S-PLUS and R examples - that never seemed to follow the normal distribution, unlike the data sets in most other statistics textbooks. Concurrent with my studies, I was lucky to have a co-op assignment that gave me access to an AT&amp;T 3B2 Unix box, so I took advantage of it and wrote some programs with S and Ratfor (Rational Fortran - Fortran with curly braces).</p>\n\n<p>After I graduated I joined a SAS shop for two years. Unlike the wide variety of statistical tools and computing systems available at grad school and my co-op job, we only had two ways to do statistics: mainframe SAS and PC SAS. Still, it had a role in my S journey. My supervisor had budget money left over near the the end of the fiscal year and asked me for ideas on how to spend it, explaining that if he didn&rsquo;t spend it, his budget might get cut. I told him that as a co-op, I used software called STAR (Statistical Reliability Analysis), developed at Bell Labs by Bill Meeker, a professor at Iowa State University and world-renowned reliability statistics expert. Based on Meeker&rsquo;s reputation and desperate to spend money fast, my boss told me to order it. Because I put in the order, AT&amp;T Bell Laboratories sent me a complimentary copy of <em>The New S Language: A Programming Environment for Data Analysis and Graphics</em> by Becker, Chambers, and Wilks (1988) with a thank you letter dated 06/19/1990 and a tape drive containing the software. Well, I couldn&rsquo;t install software from a tape; this required IT support. I put in a request. Nothing. No response, not even an estimate of when they might get to it. But we met the objective: spend our entire budget before the end of the fiscal year.</p>\n\n<p>After two years of being a frustrated SAS programmer and mastering the SAS macro language - purely to reduce the amount of SAS code for trivial, repetitive tasks and hand it off to administrative staff - and never getting STAR installed, I left to join a much smaller firm. We didn&rsquo;t have much data, and when we did, I had no statistical software to analyze it and struggled writing statistical programs in C. I heard that S was ported from Unix to Windows and renamed S-PLUS, so I purchased a copy. With the purchase of S-PLUS, I received a second copy of <em>The New S Language</em> (now softcover) and a copy of <em>Statistical Methods with S</em>, edited by Chambers and Trevor Hastie. Chambers also wrote or co-wrote chapters 1-5 and appendix A. Later I attended a 1-week training course at StatSci, the creator of S-PLUS, in Seattle, WA in 1993. And I have been using S-PLUS and R ever since, but not as continuously as I hoped. I&rsquo;m still using <em>Statistical Methods with S</em> as a reference, and I also purchased Chamber&rsquo;s <em>Programming with Data: a Guide to the S Language</em> (1998) and <em>Software for Data Analysis: Programming with R</em> (2008). I briefly met Chambers at the 2014 useR! Conference at UCLA and asked about how to obtain data sets from <em>Statistical Methods with S</em>, but he told me they are now property of TIBCO, the current owners of S-PLUS - blocked again! [Strangely, at least to me, while cleaning up files on my PC, I found artifacts from the 2001 S-PLUS conference I attended in Philadelphia, PA, and I discovered that John Chambers was an invited speaker, but I have no recollection of that.]</p>\n\n<p>Many years later, finding and reading &ldquo;Greater or Lesser Statistics: A Choice for Future Research&rdquo; (1993) based on Donoho&rsquo;s inclusion in &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science,&rdquo; a theme emerged of dividing statistics into two groups:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>Greater Statistics: everything related to <em>learning from data</em>, from the first planning or collection to the last presentation or report</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Lesser Statistics: the body of specifically statistical methodology that has evolved with the profession - roughly, statistics as defined by texts, journals, and doctoral dissertations</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>This puts a name on Tukey&rsquo;s 1962 recommendation to reduce focus on theory - Lesser Statistics - and increase focus on the entire data analysis process - Greater Statistics. Chambers cautioned, &ldquo;If statisticians remain aloof, others will act. Statistics will lose.&rdquo; This is happening now.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"leo-breiman\">Leo Breiman</h2>\n\n<p><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leo_Breiman\">Leo Breiman</a> was referenced in &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science&rdquo; for his 2001 paper &ldquo;Statistical Modeling: The Two Cultures.&rdquo; This paper has been continually referenced in Internet posts since it was published. Breiman was a professor at UCLA, then a consultant, then a professor again at UC Berkeley, where he worked with Donoho. As I recall, I first heard his name in 1988, when I was a co-op statistician and assigned to a cube that had the 1984 book <em>Classification and Regression Trees</em> (CART) by Breiman, Friedman, Olshen and Stone sitting on its shelf. I scanned the book and was fascinated by its potential to use the data itself to discover relationships hidden in multivariate data. So I asked around and learned that the company received the book when they purchased the CART software. I investigated how to run it on a time share computer, tried to recreate an example with available data, but nothing happened - I received no output or feedback on errors or file location. So I called IT support, explained the situation, and was mentally crushed after being told they didn&rsquo;t support the software and could not help me. I was beginning to spot a trend: whenever statistical software was involved, there was rarely support from IT. So I had to learn to figure out how to install statistical software myself or give up if I didn&rsquo;t have system administration rights. I needed a Statistician&rsquo;s <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serenity_Prayer\">Serenity Prayer</a>.</p>\n\n<p>Around ten years after discovering CART and being blocked from using it, a former professor of mine recommended <em>Modern Applied Statistics with S</em> (MASS) by Bill Venables and Brian Ripley. I immediately bought it (Second Edition at that time), and it quickly become my &lsquo;Bible,&rsquo; and I bought the Third and Fourth Editions as soon as they were published. Because of MASS, I learned about the <code>tree</code> method and cross-validation to do something similar to CART. I started using <code>tree</code> whenever I could to see if it would identify explanatory variables or interactions. I also learned about Alternating Conditional Expectations (ACE) by Breiman from MASS, and I often applied it, along with the Additivity And Variance Stabilization (AVAS) method, to messy data sets to find transformations that provide a better fit to the data. Due to a great deal of luck, involving the reputation of Bill Meeker at Iowa State University being one of the keynote speakers, I was able to attend the 2001 S-PLUS conference in Philadelphia, PA. As soon as I received authorization to register, I signed up for Brian Ripley&rsquo;s Data Mining tutorial, and took advantage of the opportunity to pick his brain about topics in MASS. When I started to ask about <code>tree</code>, he said, &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t use that - it&rsquo;s flawed. Use <code>rpart</code> instead.&rdquo;</p>\n\n<p>The very next day, I attended a tutorial by Frank Harrell and met Beth Atkinson, the co-developer of <code>rpart</code> with Terry Therneau. Even today, the CRAN page for <code>rpart</code> states it is, &ldquo;An implementation of most of the functionality of the 1984 book by Breiman, Friedman, Olshen and Stone.&rdquo; Based on Ripley&rsquo;s advice, I have used <code>rpart</code> ever since, and I think it&rsquo;s fair to say I&rsquo;ve used CART for the last 17 years, but I wish it was longer.</p>\n\n<p>When &ldquo;Statistical Modeling: the Two Cultures&rdquo; was published in 2001 and kept popping up in Internet posts, I knew I had to read it. I learned that Breiman was involved in the development of several other techniques, like Bagging and Boosting. But I assume Donoho included this paper in &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science&rdquo; because Bremen recommend statisticians focus less on theory and more on data, like Tukey did 40 years earlier. But Breimen was far more explicit than Turkey when he stated &ldquo;98% of all statisticians&rdquo; belong to the Data Modeling Culture (similar to Chamber&rsquo;s Lesser Statistics) and &ldquo;2% of statisticians, many in other fields&rdquo; belong to The Algorithmic Modeling Culture (similar to Chamber&rsquo;s Greater Statistics), and that imbalance resulted in three major opportunity costs:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>Led to irrelevant theory and questionable scientific conclusions</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Kept statisticians from using more suitable algorithmic models</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Prevented statisticians from working on exciting new problems</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>I found <a href=\"https://projecteuclid.org/euclid.ss/1009213726\">&ldquo;A Conversation with Leo Breiman&rdquo;</a>, and he was even more explicit when asked to give advice to students studying statistics:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p><em>I&rsquo;m torn in a way because what I might even tell them is, &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t go into statistics,&rdquo; My feeling is, to some extent, that academic statistics may have lost its way&hellip; but probably in the end, I would say, &ldquo;Take statistics, but remember that the great adventure of statistics is in gathering and using data to solve interesting and important real world problems.&rdquo;</em></p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Sure sounds like Data Science to me.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"bill-cleveland\">Bill Cleveland</h2>\n\n<p>Like John Chambers, I was introduced to <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_S._Cleveland\">Bill Cleveland</a> from <em>Graphical Methods for Data Analysis</em> (GMDA) and <em>Statistical Models with S</em>, where he co-wrote Chapter 8: Local Regression Models. And his papers were often referenced in Edward Tufte&rsquo;s visualization books with respect to pie charts and small multiples. I emailed him sometime after 2001 to ask about data sets to reproduce examples in GMDA. He didn&rsquo;t have the data sets available but recommended his other books <em>Visualizing Data</em> (1993) and <em>The Elements of Graphing Data</em> (1994), where the data sets were available on his web page. So I purchased them both and read <em>The Elements of Graphing Data</em> cover-to-cover, thanks to Andrew Gelman making his <a href=\"http://andrewgelman.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/06/communication_course_outline.pdf\">Communicating Data and Statistics course syllabus</a> available online. And as a S-PLUS user since 1993, I used Trellis graphics whenever base graphics were insufficient and then used the <strong>lattice</strong> version after switching to R (but I use <code>ggplot2</code> for most graphics now). And I use loess/lowess all the time, especially now with <strong>ggplot2</strong> <code>geom_smooth()</code> making it so easy.</p>\n\n<p>Cleveland worked at Bell Labs with Tukey, Chambers and Donoho (as an undergrad student at Princeton), then he moved to Purdue University, which I only discovered when I was wandering the halls of their statistics department circa 2012, and found him in his office. I made it a point to check if he was available every time I visited Purdue for a project I managed there and had a few face-to-face conversations. Around the same time I attended the 2012 useR! Conference at Vanderbilt University in Nashville, TN, and attended the RHIPE tutorial. Cleveland was gracious with his time and offered support to help me use RHIPE on a Linux cluster, but once again, I was blocked by IT.</p>\n\n<p>Cleveland&rsquo;s paper &ldquo;Data Science: An Action Plan for Expanding the Technical Areas of the Field of Statistics&rdquo; was published in 2001, like Breiman&rsquo;s &ldquo;Statistical Modeling&rdquo; paper. The title alone qualifies it as landmark paper in the evolution of the term &ldquo;Data Science.&rdquo; I must have read it around the time it was published, because I found it in a folder of my 2001 S-PLUS conference artifacts while cleaning up file cabinets around 2010. But since I had no recollection, the term must not have resonated with me at that time; however, I had already re-read it several times before &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science&rdquo; was published. More than helping to establish the term <em>Data Science</em>, the paper&rsquo;s six technical areas and recommended time allocations define the discipline better than other sources, which mostly list skills, not tasks:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>Multidisciplinary Investigations: data analysis collaborations in a collection of subject matter areas (25%)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Models and Methods for Data: statistical models; methods of model building; methods of estimation and distribution based on probabilistic inference (20%)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Computing with Data: hardware systems; software systems; computational algorithms (15%)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Pedagogy: curriculum planning and approaches to teaching for elementary school, secondary school, college, graduate school, continuing education, and corporate training (15%)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Tool Evaluation: surveys of tools in use in practice, surveys of perceived needs for new tools, and studies of the processes for developing new tools (5%)</p></li>\n\n<li><p>Theory: foundations of data science; general approaches to models and methods, to computing with data, to teaching, and to tool evaluation; mathematical investigations of models and methods, of computing with data, of teaching, and of evaluation (20%)</p></li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>All of my Data Scientist job responsibilities can be mapped into those six areas, and I have used them to define my goals and time allocations whenever possible (still a work in progress). So in that respect, it has greatly influenced my life, surely more than any other paper, book, class, conference, tutorial, or workshop.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"closing-comments\">Closing Comments</h2>\n\n<p>Data Science - I could not put my finger on it until I read &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science.&rdquo; In retrospect, it was my reasoning for studying math and statistics instead of &ldquo;hard core&rdquo; sciences, hypothesizing that solving future, complex problems would require analysis of large, multivariate data sets, not just theory and logic; however, I could never fully articulate my thoughts to myself, let alone others. And &ldquo;50 Years of Data Science&rdquo; helped me realize that my Data Science journey parallels my statistical programming journey from S to S-PLUS to R over the last 30 years - with excursions for Pascal, Fortran, Ratfor, SAS, C, VBA for Excel, Minitab, and Statistica. For me, Data Science and S/S-PLUS/R are intertwined, inseparable.</p>\n\n<p>Around 20 years ago, I attended a seminar by a well-known industrial statistician whose main message was that to be a better statistician, one should learn more about other fields. I took that advice to heart - maybe too much - and spent years and years learning more about engineering, business, and finance, hoping that would help me &ldquo;get my foot in the door&rdquo; and apply statistics to a wider variety of problems, or, as Tukey put it, get <a href=\"http://ww2.amstat.org/about/statisticiansinhistory/index.cfm?fuseaction=biosinfo&amp;BioID=14\">to play in everyone&rsquo;s backyard</a>. But that never happened (a topic for another post?). And given my limited time and attention span, I neglected continuing to learn what I really enjoyed: math, statistics, and programming. In retrospect, this was bad advice, at least for me. I now realize I should have learned MORE math, statistics, and programming, and that would have enabled me to contribute more to other fields by applying advanced statistical techniques to solve problems, rather than knowing more about other fields and applying generic, basic statistical techniques. I now wish I had learned that about myself much, much sooner.</p>\n\n<p>Data Science is not a fad; it is a long evolution of statistical practice, what statistics should have been if &ldquo;we&rdquo; listened to Tukey 50 years ago. It took 50 years for Tukey&rsquo;s ideas to become mainstream. And because the statistics community did not lead, as Chambers warned in 1993, others have assumed control. I saw this coming based on my experiences with Total Quality Management (TQM) and Six Sigma, and I hoped Data Science would be different, but it seems to be too late. Personally, however, it has been a huge benefit. When I was a child, I caught a fleeting glimpse of the future out of the corner of my eye. With so many distractions, bad advice, and being blocked by Information Technology, I often just gave up and coasted along. Thanks to Data Science, I have new motivation and renewed energy to purse what I enjoy - and avoided becoming comfortably numb.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/25-years-of-data-science/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 07 Aug 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"c83632c20eb8643c82f4018f75b540db","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Anonymous","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973509},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Don't Use Spaces or Underscores in File Paths; Use Dashes Instead","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/space-pain/","description":"\n        <p>Recently I received a few bug reports about the PDF generation in R Markdown. Eventually I figured out one of the reasons: spaces in filenames. For example, in <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/rmarkdown/issues/1285\">rstudio/rmarkdown#1285</a>, <code>xelatex</code> failed to compile the <code>.tex</code> file because there was a space in a folder name (but <code>pdflatex</code> succeeded). From this issue, you can also see how many replies it takes to get <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/09/the-minimal-reprex-paradox/\">a reproducible example</a>.</p>\n\n<p>I had been bothered by spaces for long enough before (not only in LaTeX), so I had developed a habit of not using spaces in any of my filenames. When I need to separate words, I always use dashes (<code>-</code>) instead of other characters such as underscores (<code>_</code>) or dots (<code>.</code>). I have no idea why spaces are bad in file paths in LaTeX. In HTML, spaces in file paths are often encoded to <code>%20</code>. In command line, when a path contains spaces, it has to be quoted (e.g., <code>rm &quot;abc def.txt&quot;</code>). For underscores, they are special characters in LaTeX (e.g., in the math mode, an underscore indicates a subscript). By comparison, dashes are much safer everywhere, and they are not special characters in HTML or LaTeX.</p>\n\n<p>For the same reason, I always recommend <strong>knitr</strong> and R Markdown users to use dashes in chunk labels instead of spaces or underscores, e.g., this is good:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-markdown\">```{r, hello-world, fig.width=8}\n```\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>These could be bad chunk labels:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-markdown\">```{r, hello world, fig.width=8}\n```\n\n```{r, hello_world, fig.width=8}\n```\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Chunk labels will be used to name figure files (if any plots are generated in the code chunk), so you have to use more portable characters in the labels. Usually I only use letters a-z, A-Z, numbers 0-9, and dashes. If your code chunk won&rsquo;t generate any plots, it should not matter which characters you used in the label, and <strong>knitr</strong> itself puts no restrictions on that.</p>\n\n<p>Sigh. Technology.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/dump-computer.gif\" alt=\"Sigh. Technology.\" /></p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/space-pain/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 15 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/dump-computer.gif","linkMd5":"00a4c6e93590ac2e08b7414d022e18a5","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn68@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-34-796_8eec5bf3be9d8e9a.webp","destWidth":464,"destHeight":258,"sourceBytes":837069,"destBytes":241926,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://slides.yihui.org/gif/dump-computer.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn68@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-34-796_8eec5bf3be9d8e9a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973463},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"My Biggest Regret in the knitr Package","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/biggest-regret-knitr/","description":"\n        <p>The <strong>knitr</strong> package was written in late 2011, when I knew little about character encodings. I still don&rsquo;t know much about them today, but I have had enough pain. At RStudio, I have become the (self-nominated) &ldquo;Character Encoding Ambassador&rdquo;, mainly because I&rsquo;m the only native Chinese in the company, and Chinese characters are multibyte. It is common that problems related to character encodings are reported by Chinese users. If we can fix these problems, chances are problems for other languages will disappear at the same time.</p>\n\n<p>The default <code>knitr::knit(..., encoding = getOption(&quot;encoding&quot;))</code> was the biggest mistake in <strong>knitr</strong> in retrospect. Now I regret and hate it day and night. I used this default encoding because it is what all base R functions do when reading or writing files. For example, take a look at the help page <code>?file</code>. The default means the system native encoding (<code>native.enc</code>), which is <em>a total mess</em> on Windows. Different languages have different default character encodings (e.g., Simplified Chinese uses <code>GBK</code>). On *nix, the default encoding is often UTF-8.</p>\n\n<p>If Windows didn&rsquo;t introduce all these different encodings, I think half of programmers in the world could spend three months on the beach enjoying the sunshine every year. FWIW, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/matarillo/status/931050504779526145\">I have heard rumors</a> about the UTF-8 support in a certain future version of Windows. Even with that, I guess it will take several more years to clean up the mess.</p>\n\n<p><a href=\"http://utf8everywhere.org\">UTF-8 works everywhere</a>, but it takes courage for software developers to force Windows users to use UTF-8. Over the years, I have been admiring the decision of Pandoc&rsquo;s author, John MacFarlane: UTF-8 in, and UTF-8 out. Most other software packages chose to compromise to the system native encoding by default. The compromise brings endless headache to developers, but the gain for users is at most marginal, if not nil. If users are forced to use UTF-8 consistently, the life of developers will be much easier, and the hassle for users is actually minimal: users only need to choose the UTF-8 encoding when reading or saving a file. Actually if the file editor gives users an option to set the encoding globally, the hassle will be none. For example, in RStudio, you can set the default text encoding to UTF-8:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48278362-e3f79d00-e412-11e8-8d53-be409861928f.png\" alt=\"Set UTF-8 as the default encoding in RStudio\" /></p>\n\n<p>Needless to say, if you haven&rsquo;t set this option on Windows, I won&rsquo;t be your friend.</p>\n\n<p>If you don&rsquo;t use a language with multibyte characters, you probably won&rsquo;t feel the pain. This issue has wasted us so much time, as <a href=\"https://shrektan.com/post/2018/05/07/cant-bear-with-encoding-any-longer/\">Xianying Tan wrote</a> earlier this year (in Chinese). He has been trying to fix these encoding problems in various R packages like myself in the past, e.g., <a href=\"https://github.com/trestletech/plumber/pull/312\">in <strong>plumber</strong></a>, <strong>roxygen2</strong>, <strong>devtools</strong>, <strong>data.table</strong>, <strong>odbc</strong>, and so on.</p>\n\n<p>Both <strong>knitr</strong> and <strong>rmarkdown</strong> default to the native encoding due to my initial blind decision, but since <strong>bookdown</strong>, I have started enforcing UTF-8. All of <strong>bookdown</strong>, <strong>blogdown</strong>, <strong>xaringan</strong>, <strong>tinytex</strong>,and my packages since 2016 only support UTF-8 based on two simple helper functions in the <strong>xfun</strong> package: <code>xfun::read_utf8()</code> and <code>xfun::write_utf8()</code>. Before then, I was a little worried if this would offend any users. The fact is, I haven&rsquo;t received any complaints about UTF-8 in these packages.</p>\n\n<p>If you plan to write a package that involves file input/output, I&rsquo;d strongly recommend that you support, and only support UTF-8. The UTF-8 assumption can save you a lot of time guessing and debugging.</p>\n\n<p>Someday I&rsquo;ll force <strong>knitr</strong> and <strong>rmarkdown</strong> into the UTF-8 world, too (perhaps in <strong>knitr</strong> and <strong>rmarkdown</strong> v2.0), but this will certainly take a long time to happen.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/biggest-regret-knitr/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 09 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48278362-e3f79d00-e412-11e8-8d53-be409861928f.png","linkMd5":"58bde1f466eced05645b6db6b3340f91","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn28@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-33-686_62a9d17b710a38d6.webp","destWidth":596,"destHeight":589,"sourceBytes":43070,"destBytes":22998,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48278362-e3f79d00-e412-11e8-8d53-be409861928f.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn28@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-19-33-686_62a9d17b710a38d6.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973443},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Down?
