<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2020/02/down-not-my-fault/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 12 Feb 2020 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/D9IBxqNUwAA1As7?format=png&name=small","linkMd5":"9eb750d56d319d42f5c68a9967d1f7ff","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn87@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-35-095_7677ce989432f529.webp","destWidth":542,"destHeight":164,"sourceBytes":15598,"destBytes":8562,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/D9IBxqNUwAA1As7?format=png&name=small":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn87@2020_2/2020/08/25/02-19-35-095_7677ce989432f529.webp","https://pbs.twimg.com/media/EPpyn7IVUAAtXr2?format=jpg&name=large":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn43@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-20-01-366_8c6d5ce7c3390e85.webp","https://pbs.twimg.com/media/EQNM2IQU0AAXbD-?format=png&name=900x900":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn35@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-20-00-849_2f103e86998541ea.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973437},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"The User-Developer Spectrum in the R Ecosystem","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/user-developer/","description":"\n        <p>Roger Peng wrote <a href=\"https://simplystatistics.org/2018/07/12/use-r-keynote-2018/\">a very thoughtful post</a> to reflect on the evolution of the R ecosystem after his keynote at UseR! 2018. It was a great read to me (I&rsquo;d rate it as the best post so far in Simply Statistics this year). In particular, the &ldquo;user-developer&rdquo; spectrum struck me. It didn&rsquo;t surprise me, but I had never consciously thought about this notion. Clearly, the R ecosystem has been moving to the user&rsquo;s end on the spectrum, and Tidyverse is an outstanding example. To understand <code>tapply()</code>, you have to wear a programmer&rsquo;s shoes. To understand <code>group_by()</code>, you pretty much only need to understand English.</p>\n\n<p>This reminds me of LaTeX v.s. Markdown (I wish I had more time to update <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2013/10/markdown-or-latex/\">an old post I wrote in 2013</a>). An analogy to the &ldquo;user-developer spectrum&rdquo; is the &ldquo;<em>author-typesetter spectrum</em>&ldquo;: Markdown tends to make you an author, and LaTeX tends to turn you into a typesetter. Although I have been preaching Markdown, I found a couple of months ago that I had never been able to explain the philosophy better than <a href=\"https://twitter.com/jent103/status/998440668484722688\">Jennifer Thompson</a>:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DdssM0XV0AIR0C5.jpg#border\" alt=\"A tweet by Jennifer Thompson on the Markdown philosophy\" /></p>\n\n<p>Markdown is for 90% of the results from 10% of the effort. Very well said.</p>\n\n<p>I don&rsquo;t mean a certain end on the spectrum is wrong. There is nothing wrong with a programming or authoring language being designed for developers or typesetters. There is nothing wrong if you want more control, as long as you think it is worth your time. Different people have different priorities. I just happen to prefer the user/author&rsquo;s end on the spectrum, based on the belief that the number of users is almost surely much larger than that of developers. I enjoy my own life as a developer, but I don&rsquo;t think most other users want to do the same thing as I do every day.</p>\n\n<p>BTW, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/johnmyleswhite/status/1017773947360874497\">John Myles White was skeptical</a> about a few points in Roger&rsquo;s post. One of his doubts was:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>I also find this odd: &ldquo;If R seems a bit confusing, disorganized, and perhaps incoherent at times, in some ways that&rsquo;s because so is data analysis.&rdquo; If anything, the reason tidyverse succeeded was precisely because it wasn&rsquo;t incoherent, but that didn&rsquo;t make it worse for analysis.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I tend to agree with John that R was incoherent not because data analysis was so. My theory is that R does not have a <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benevolent_dictator_for_life\">BDFL</a>, and perhaps features/changes in base R are not implemented as pull requests (I&rsquo;m not really sure; maybe they have something similar in SVN). Besides, I guess most active R core developers are more on the developer&rsquo;s end of the spectrum. The Tidyverse does not have a nominal BDFL, either, but we all know &ldquo;one of these developers is not like others&rdquo;. He cares deeply, deeply about data analysis, and has simple yet sound frameworks for data analysis (e.g., Tidy Data). I think that should explain the much better coherence.</p>\n\n<p>Interestingly enough, Python&rsquo;s BDFL <a href=\"https://mail.python.org/pipermail/python-committers/2018-July/005664.html\">just chose to step down yesterday</a> after an exhausting fight over <a href=\"https://www.python.org/dev/peps/pep-0572/\">a proposal</a>. I wonder what this will mean to the development of Python.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/user-developer/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 13 Jul 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DdssM0XV0AIR0C5.jpg#border","linkMd5":"298582bd41947b7b253f1e61f86aca02","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn71@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-35-226_274c2a64ca4e5687.webp","destWidth":1200,"destHeight":899,"sourceBytes":106259,"destBytes":69100,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://pbs.twimg.com/media/DdssM0XV0AIR0C5.jpg#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn71@2020_6/2020/08/25/02-19-35-226_274c2a64ca4e5687.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973459},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Things are Getting Better and Better","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/","description":"<p>Last month <a href=\"https://twitter.com/_R_Foundation/status/1050791571514413057\">I suddently learned</a> that the R Core Team started a blog earlier this year. And of course, I was even more excited that the blog was based on <strong>blogdown</strong>. Given that the official R Project website had used <code>&lt;iframe&gt;</code> for nearly two decades, I was very delighted that they were finally embracing the newer world-wide web step by step.</p> \n<p>Although blogs and mailing lists are both open to the public, I think blogs are more public. For developers, there are always difficult decisions to make, and oftentimes there is no way to please everyone, especially when making breaking changes. In such cases, I think a public explanation helps a lot, and most users <a href=\"https://twitter.com/orchid00/status/1055512092558741504\">will appreciate</a> the transparency, even if the change brings them trouble. BTW, I, for one, fully support dropping the support for <code>if (x)</code> where <code>length(x) &gt; 1</code> in base R. <a href=\"https://developer.r-project.org/Blog/public/2018/10/12/conditions-of-length-greater-than-one/index.html\">The post by Tomas Kalibera</a> is a great example of demonstrating how careful R core is and how challenging it is to make breaking changes. Without revealing these efforts behind the scenes, users might think it was an arbitrary change.</p> \n<p>Next I’ll be waiting for <a href=\"http://r.789695.n4.nabble.com/Why-R-project-source-code-is-not-on-Github-td4695779.html\">base R to move to GIT or Github</a>. I’ll make sure to eat healthy food every day and exercise regularly to be able to witness that glorious moment in the unknown future. FWIW, LyX has moved from SVN to GIT in 2012,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Unfortunately-th\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/#fn:Unfortunately-th\">1</a></sup> and Python has moved in 2017. For R, the friction <a href=\"http://r.789695.n4.nabble.com/R-CMD-check-warning-about-compiler-warning-flags-td4747304.html\">still seems to be huge</a>.</p> \n<p>Anyway, here are some other recent things that have made me feel the world is becoming a better place:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><p>Although I was thoroughly disappointed (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/ALCarriquiry/status/954789308757659648\">so were others</a>) by the ASA guidelines on promotion and tenure (P+T) evaluation of faculty members published in January this year, I was extremely delighted to see <a href=\"https://simplystatistics.org/2018/10/11/guest-post-galin-jones-on-criteria-for-promotion-and-tenture-in-bio-statistics-departments/\">a post by the Director of Statistics at University of Minnesota</a> last month, in which he mentioned some new criteria for P+T that would recognize the importance of many other things (such as software contribution and novel teaching methods). That was so open-minded. If I were not so satisfied with my current job, I would…</p></li> \n <li><p>I have talked about Stack Overflow a few times in my blog. I still see users who are reluctant or afraid to ask questions there, but I believe it is becoming a lot better than before. When <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.blog/2018/08/07/get-to-know-our-new-code-of-conduct/\">their new Code of Conduct was published</a> in August, I stood up and applauded for ten minutes in front of my computer. Is it a perfect place for Q&amp;A now? Of course not, but there is no point of pursuing perfectionism, and it is not possible for a site with so many users, either. All I need to see is the desire to keep improving, which I have already seen. There are still people who downvote without explanations or vote to close an appropriate question, but now I can feel the positive energy from other people when I complain:</p> <p><a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/53064555/559676\"><img src=\"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48227387-d33e1d00-e367-11e8-857f-dfdc0620acce.png#border\" alt=\"People upvoting my comment on a post that was downvoted inappropriately\" /></a></p></li> \n <li><p>One of my life dreams has been (more or less) fulfilled: making it easier for students to write dissertations with R Markdown and <strong>bookdown</strong>. As I mentioned in <a href=\"https://community.rstudio.com/t/16394\">the announcement of the <strong>bookdown</strong> contest winners</a>, I was excited to see <a href=\"https://github.com/ryanpeek/aggiedown/network/members\">a network graph</a> like this on Github:</p> <p><img src=\"https://community.rstudio.com/uploads/default/original/2X/f/fe99e9903e1c7ffc1c0b2c85518fc45fd8b13013.png\" alt=\"The network graph of the thesisdown package\" /></p> <p>At the same time, publishers seem to be more and more willing to <a href=\"https://twitter.com/StatGarrett/status/1052642829745496064\">allow authors to host free versions of their books online</a>. As I said in an earlier post, I believe <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/08/bookdown-crc/\">we could tear down the paywall</a>. Just like any other revolutions in history, it takes time and requires patience.</p></li> \n <li><p>This is really old news, but I still want to mention it. I <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/commit/b5ef66b7a\">started experimenting</a> with other languages in <strong>knitr</strong> in 2012, and added a TODO item for the Python support. Five years later, thanks to my genius colleague Kevin Ushey,<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:If-only-I-had-mo\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/#fn:If-only-I-had-mo\">2</a></sup>\n   <!-- --> <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/knitr/commit/b2d892974\">the dream had finally become true</a> and I was able to remove the five-year-old TODO. I’d say that the pull request <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/reticulate/pull/107\">rstudio/reticulate#107</a> was one of the most important PRs for <strong>knitr</strong> in its history, not to mention how awesome the <strong>reticulate</strong> package itself is.</p></li> \n <li><p>I feel the “tyranny of email” eventually started to go away from my inbox. This year I have received multiple emails telling me that they knew <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2017/08/so-gh-email/\">I wanted them to</a> ask questions on public forums such as Stack Overflow or RStudio Community, so they did so before emailing me with the links to their questions in the public. I really appreciate it. Even prestigious and busy professors like Dr. Frank Harrell <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/q/48208457/559676\">would ask questions on Stack Overflow</a> instead of emailing me. How cool is that?</p></li> \n <li><p>As I mentioned recently, Rmd files will no longer be rendered as normal Markdown to HTML on Github. Github will display Rmd files in their source format (i.e., plain text). I had been waiting for this for a couple of years. It seemed that <a href=\"https://twitter.com/yutannihilat_en/status/1053877401606742018\">I didn’t</a> annoy <a href=\"https://twitter.com/datavisitor/status/1054800395019513856\">too many users</a>. Or perhaps people who hated it didn’t want to speak up… BTW, when Github was acquired by Microsoft a few months ago, I felt an earthquake in the world of programmers. I don’t want to comment on this, but at least it turned out that I was able to contact Github through <code>support@github.com</code> and they were stilling listening. The world probably didn’t become worse.</p></li> \n <li><p>The list could go on and on, but I want to stop here by mentioning the most inspiring post of the year in my eyes: <a href=\"http://hookedondata.org/The-Importance-of-Sponsorship/\"><em>The Importance of Sponsorship</em></a>, written by Emily Robinson nine months ago. If everyone (senior or not) in the community (tech or not) could read this post and follow its suggestions, I believe the world will become better much more quickly, with people of all levels growing more quickly.</p></li> \n</ol> \n<p>In summary, all the worlds of the academia, Stack Overflow, Github, college students, book authors/readers, R users who also use Python, and myself<sup class=\"footnote-ref\" id=\"fnref:Recently-my-olde\"><a rel=\"footnote\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/#fn:Recently-my-olde\">3</a></sup> are becoming better. Of course, there are always challenging problems to be solved ahead. We shall calm down and carry on.</p> \n<div class=\"footnotes\"> \n <hr /> \n <ol> \n  <li id=\"fn:Unfortunately-th\">Unfortunately they chose to host a GIT server by themselves. I had <a href=\"https://www.mail-archive.com/lyx-devel@lists.lyx.org/msg173179.html\">a failed attempt</a> in convincing them of using Github in 2012. It was a super long email thread, which <a href=\"https://www.mail-archive.com/lyx-devel@lists.lyx.org/msg198746.html\">I bumped five years later</a> after I saw Python moved to Github, but no one in the LyX developer team except Scott seemed to be interested at all. My major point is, it is not about <em>this</em> team of developers. It is always about <a href=\"http://www.quotationspage.com/quote/38805.html\">the future generations</a>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/#fnref:Unfortunately-th\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:If-only-I-had-mo\">If only I had more time to write about this guy… He doesn’t blog often, but his posts are often meticulous. His <a href=\"https://kevinushey.github.io/blog/2018/02/21/string-encoding-and-r/\">current last post</a> even motivated an R core member to update the documentation of <code>file()</code>, which was later called “blog post driven documentation” <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JennyBryan/status/966734915847864320\">by Jenny Bryan</a>. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/#fnref:If-only-I-had-mo\">↩</a></li> \n  <li id=\"fn:Recently-my-olde\">Recently, my older son has praised me for the first time (since he was born) for the meal I cooked. Just another example of my world becoming better. <a class=\"footnote-return\" href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/#fnref:Recently-my-olde\">↩</a></li> \n </ol> \n</div> \n<script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/getting-better/';</script>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 08 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48227387-d33e1d00-e367-11e8-857f-dfdc0620acce.png#border","linkMd5":"6180acedae0586b0a1e7447ebd95f7f4","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn19@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-34-621_5df035934a3158e9.webp","destWidth":737,"destHeight":376,"sourceBytes":137253,"destBytes":49640,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/163582/48227387-d33e1d00-e367-11e8-857f-dfdc0620acce.png#border":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn19@2020_5/2020/08/25/02-19-34-621_5df035934a3158e9.webp","https://community.rstudio.com/uploads/default/original/2X/f/fe99e9903e1c7ffc1c0b2c85518fc45fd8b13013.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn7@2020_4/2020/08/25/02-20-00-685_5b5f7c6d439bccac.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973443},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Timezones, and Worse, Daylight Saving Time","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/timezones-dst/","description":"\n        <p>Does it really matter if the sun reaches its zenith everywhere on the globe around 12pm? I don&rsquo;t think so. Compared with the endless and huge trouble caused by timezones, the benefit of the sun reaching its zenith at 12pm is just nothing. Australians don&rsquo;t change the months (otherwise it would lead to &ldquo;month-zones&rdquo;) to make sure the summer always starts in June. Why do different areas of the world have to change their time to match the position of the sun? I&rsquo;d be totally fine if the noon is at 3am in my place. Wouldn&rsquo;t that be so much nicer if when we say a time, it is the same time across the globe? No more calculating &ldquo;local&rdquo; times or adjusting clocks. I wish <a href=\"https://twitter.com/MMaechler/status/977906576341204994\">Martin Maechler&rsquo;s dream</a> could come true someday.</p>\n\n<p>One nice thing about the time in China is that the whole country shares the same time, from the very east to the very west, even though it means that 8am is still completely dark in the west.</p>\n\n<p>In the US, there are four timezones (I&rsquo;m aware of two more, Alaska and Hawaii), which is enough trouble, as in most other countries. There is an even worse thing: the daylight saving time (which was one of the cultural shocks I had when I first came here).</p>\n\n<p>If I were able to vote, I&rsquo;d vote for a president who promises to cancel DST, or better, timezones and the Fahrenheit scale, or even better, non-UTF8 character encodings! How much time have we wasted on those worthless inconsistencies?</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/03/timezones-dst/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 26 Mar 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"c520c1fda61d096cddfd465b90648bbc","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Yihui Xie","publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973462},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Some of My JS Tricks to Enhance the HTML Output of Markdown","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/md-js-tricks/","description":"\n        \n\n<p>Just because Markdown is so simple (well, &ldquo;<a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/hard-markdown/\">simple</a>&rdquo;), there will definitely be useful features missing. I want to share some of my relatively simple JS tricks to fill the gaps. Please note that I&rsquo;m only talking about the HTML output, and the Markdown flavor can be arbitrary (it doesn&rsquo;t have to be Pandoc&rsquo;s Markdown). In fact, all tricks below are general-purpose and will work on any web pages, no matter if the pages are generated from Markdown or not.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"how-to-work-with-mathjax\">How to work with MathJax</h2>\n\n<p>The MathJax support in most Markdown flavors is missing. The only exception is Pandoc&rsquo;s Markdown. If you use R Markdown (which is based on Pandoc), you can ignore this trick. I have explained my trick of making MathJax work with any flavors of Markdown in detail <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/07/latex-math-markdown/\">in a previous post</a>. The relevant JS code is in <a href=\"https://github.com/rbind/yihui/blob/master/static/js/math-code.js\">math-code.js</a> on my website. This is the most complicated trick in this post, although it is only 20 lines of code.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"how-to-make-links-open-in-new-tabs-i-e-target-blank\">How to make links open in new tabs (i.e., <code>target=&quot;_blank&quot;</code>)</h2>\n\n<p>Most Markdown renderers translate <code>[text](url)</code> to <code>&lt;a href=&quot;url&quot;&gt;text&lt;/a&gt;</code>, which means links will be opened in the current tab or window. I have explained my trick of <a href=\"https://yihui.org/en/2018/09/target-blank/\">making links open in new tabs</a> in a previous post, too.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"how-to-add-anchor-links-to-section-headings\">How to add anchor links to section headings</h2>\n\n<p>When a page has mutiple sections, it can be very helpful to generate an anchor link for each section heading, because we can send other people the links to specific sections. Basically, we just need to add an <code>&lt;a&gt;</code> tag inside a heading with the attribute <code>href</code> pointing to the ID of the heading:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-js\">(function() {\n  var h, hs = document.querySelectorAll('h1, h2, h3');\n  for (var i = 0; i &lt; hs.length; i++) {\n    h = hs[i];\n    if (h.id === '') continue;\n    h.innerHTML += ' &lt;span class=&quot;anchor&quot;&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;#' + h.id +\n      '&quot;&gt;#&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/span&gt;';\n  }\n})();\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>You can hide the anchor links by default, and show them only when the mouse is over a heading:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-css\">.anchor {\n  display: none;\n}\n:hover &gt; .anchor {\n  display: inline;\n}\n</code></pre>\n\n<h2 id=\"how-to-generate-tooltips-for-images\">How to generate tooltips for images</h2>\n\n<p>Images on the web often have an <code>alt</code> attribute, which stands for &ldquo;alternate text&rdquo;. This is often invisible to readers <em>without</em> vision problems, but important for users who have to use screen readers (such as blind people). Without the <code>alt</code> text, they will have no idea what an image means. The text is also displayed with the image cannot be loaded.</p>\n\n<p>Since this attribute is invisible to users with the normal vision, it actually also has a problem. Sometimes you don&rsquo;t know what exactly the author tries to express just by looking at an image. You may have a better idea by reading the text. You could right click on the image, &ldquo;Inspect Element&rdquo;, and look at the <code>&lt;img&gt;</code> tag in the source, but that&rsquo;s a bit hassle and not everyone knows how the use the Developer Tools of modern web browsers.</p>\n\n<p>My trick is to add the <code>title</code> attribute to <code>&lt;img&gt;</code> (if not already specified), whose value is identical to the <code>alt</code> text. The <code>title</code> attribute will give you a tooltip, which you can read as you mouseover the image. XKCD is a typical example. The code <a href=\"https://github.com/rbind/yihui/blob/master/static/js/alt-title.js\">alt-title.js</a> is simple enough:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-js\">(function() {\n  var img, imgs = document.getElementsByTagName('img');\n  for (var i = 0; i &lt; imgs.length; i++) {\n    img = imgs[i];\n    if (!img.title) img.title = img.alt;\n  }\n})();\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>An example:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"https://db.yihui.org/images/thick-book.jpg\" alt=\"Trying to figure out the possible reasons why my girlfriend is angry\" /></p>\n\n<p>Note that with Pandoc&rsquo;s Markdown, you can generate figure captions, so the tooltips may not be necessary. With other Markdown flavors, <code>![text](url)</code> is often translated to <code>&lt;img src=&quot;url&quot; alt=&quot;text&quot; /&gt;</code>.</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"how-to-center-images-in-paragraphs\">How to center images in paragraphs</h2>\n\n<p>When an image (<code>&lt;img&gt;</code>) is the only child element of a paragraph (<code>&lt;p&gt;</code>), chances are you want to center it. Except with Pandoc&rsquo;s Markdown, you cannot really align images with Markdown syntax. I have written a JS script <a href=\"https://github.com/rbind/yihui/blob/master/static/js/center-img.js\">center-img.js</a> to automatically center images on web pages. It is slightly tricky because I considered both of these cases:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-html\">&lt;!-- Markdown: ![](...) --&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;\n  &lt;img src=&quot;...&quot; /&gt;\n&lt;/p&gt;\n\n&lt;!-- Markdown: [![](...)](...) --&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;\n  &lt;a href=&quot;...&quot;&gt;&lt;img src=&quot;...&quot; /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;\n&lt;/p&gt;\n\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>I have also considered centering <code>&lt;embed&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;object&gt;</code>, although these elements are rarer in Markdown&rsquo;s output (no native Markdown syntax for them).</p>\n\n<h2 id=\"how-to-right-align-quote-footers\">How to right-align quote footers</h2>\n\n<p>If you need to use quotes in Markdown, everything will be left-aligned by default, but you may want the footer (the source of the quote or the person who said the words) to be right-aligned.</p>\n\n<p>My trick, <a href=\"https://github.com/rbind/yihui/blob/master/static/js/right-quote.js\">right-quote.js</a>, is to check the last paragraph of a quote. If it starts with the em-dash (&mdash;), I&rsquo;ll apply the CSS style <code>text-aligh: right;</code> to it. Here  is an example:</p>\n\n<pre><code class=\"language-md\">&gt; UNIX was not designed to stop its users from doing stupid things,\n&gt; as that would also stop them from doing clever things.\n\n&gt; --- Doug Gwyn\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>Output:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n<p>UNIX was not designed to stop its users from doing stupid things,\nas that would also stop them from doing clever things.</p>\n\n<p>&mdash; Doug Gwyn</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I&rsquo;m not a JavaScript expert. My code above may be naive and inefficient. I have found JavaScript a lot of fun to play with, even only with selectors like <code>document.getElementsByTagName()</code> or <code>document.querySelectorAll()</code> and simple for-loops. I used to think I couldn&rsquo;t live without fancy libraries like jQuery, but later realized vanilla JavaScript could actually do a lot of things. I hope these tricks could be useful to web amateurs like me.</p>\n\n        \n        <script>location.href='https://yihui.org/en/2018/11/md-js-tricks/';</script>\n        \n      ","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 09 Nov 2018 00:00:00 +0000","feedId":13021,"bgimg":"https://db.yihui.org/images/thick-book.jpg","linkMd5":"e676309a101719fac86642b691fda333","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn1@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-34-016_2602a368fe52315c.webp","destWidth":433,"destHeight":634,"sourceBytes":44594,"destBytes":33992,"author":"Yihui Xie","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://db.yihui.org/images/thick-book.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn1@2020_3/2020/08/25/02-19-34-016_2602a368fe52315c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598321973457},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 10:19:33","title":"Naming Software Packages with Common Words","link":"https://yihui.org/en/2019/04/naming-common-words/","description":"\n        <p>Andrew Gelman <a href=\"https://statmodeling.stat.columbia.edu/2019/04/29/we-shouldntve-called-it-stan-i-shouldve-listened-to-bob-and-hadley/\">regretted the name &ldquo;Stan&rdquo;</a>, because it is a common word, which makes it hard to find relevant results on Google. Actually I was taught the same lesson by Hadley when I first created the <strong>knitr</strong> package in 2011. Fortunately I listened. In the very beginning, I simply named it &ldquo;knit&rdquo;, which is certainly not very &ldquo;Googleable&rdquo;. Appending &ldquo;R&rdquo; after it made it much easier and &ldquo;neater&rdquo; to search through Google. In the early years, I had a couple of other packages named after common words, such as <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/animation\"><strong>animation</strong></a> and <a href=\"https://github.com/yihui/fun\"><strong>fun</strong></a>. These packages were obviously difficult to find via Google, although on the other hand, I guess they are not really important packages, so users won&rsquo;t think of finding them.</p>\n\n<p>In the following years, I tried to stick to this rule of using unique words when naming packages, e.g., <strong>bookdown</strong>, <strong>blogdown</strong>, <strong>xaringan</strong>, and <strong>tinytex</strong>. There is no ambiguity for these packages in Google results now. When I started working on <strong>pagedown</strong> last year, I spent some time on searching for different names on Google, and initially decided to use the name <strong>leafdown</strong>, because it seemed to be unique enough, and &ldquo;leaf&rdquo; also has the meaning of &ldquo;page&rdquo;. The first name that came to my mind was actually <strong>pagedown</strong>, because it was such an obvious choice (an R Markdown package for paged documents). I didn&rsquo;t want to use this name for the obvious reason. However, JJ suggested that I go with the name <strong>pagedown</strong>. His defense was from the experience of &ldquo;Shiny&rdquo;. Shiny is such a common word, yet after a couple of years, Google started to put our Shiny <a href=\"https://www.google.com/search?q=shiny\">on the first page of search results</a>.</p>\n\n<p>Before I made <strong>pagedown</strong> public on Github, there was another <strong>pagedown</strong> project from StackExchange on Github. I though I&rsquo;d never be able to compete with StackExchange in the Google search. To my great surprise, our <strong>pagedown</strong> became the first item in the Google search results within three months!</p>\n\n<p>A much more extreme example is the <a href=\"https://github.com/rstudio/DT\"><strong>DT</strong></a> package. The name is only two letters. I&rsquo;d never imagine it could be found via Google. However, even with the two letters, <strong>DT</strong> has made its way to the first page of Google search results.</p>\n\n<p>I guess the new lesson learned here is that a common word as a software package name is probably fine, but you&rsquo;d better avoid words related to the pop culture or the entertainment industry. If you search for a word on Google and Google shows you YouTube videos or images, it is likely to be a bad idea.</p>\n\n        \n
